### [1] [Domain-level metacognitive monitoring in frontier LLMs: A 33-model atlas](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06673)
*Jon-Paul Cacioli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that large language models’ metacognitive performance (how well their confidence tracks correctness) varies substantially across different knowledge domains in the MMLU benchmark, even when aggregate metrics look good.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work often reports a single, aggregate metacognitive score (e.g., Type-2 AUROC) for LLMs, obscuring how monitoring quality may vary across domains like science, formal reasoning, and professional knowledge. For safe deployment, especially in domain-specific applications, it’s important to know where models monitor well vs. poorly. The authors aim to unpack this hidden variation and test whether MMLU’s domain taxonomy meaningfully captures systematic metacognitive differences.

Method: They administered 1,500 MMLU questions (250 per domain under a predefined six-domain grouping) to 33 frontier LLMs from eight families. For each model-domain pair (cell), they computed Type-2 AUROC using models’ verbalized confidence (0–100) to quantify metacognitive monitoring. They analyzed domain-level variation, ranked domains by AUROC, and computed Kendall’s W to compare middle domains. They assessed whether the six-domain grouping corresponds to coherent subject-level structure via a coherence ratio. They then examined profile clustering within model families using permutation tests, and compared different confidence formats (binary KEEP/WITHDRAW vs. verbalized). They estimated uncertainty via bootstrap CIs for all model-domain cells and evaluated stability with split-half reliability at both aggregate and profile levels.

Result: Every model that showed above-chance overall metacognitive monitoring also exhibited notable variation across domains. Applied/Professional knowledge was consistently the easiest domain to monitor (highest AUROC on average, often in top-2 ranks), while Formal Reasoning and Natural Science were consistently hardest (frequently in bottom-2 ranks). The remaining three domains did not differ reliably from each other. The subject-level coherence analysis indicated high within-domain similarity but suggested the six-domain scheme is more pragmatic than theoretically grounded. Within-family clustering of metacognitive profiles was significant for some model families (Anthropic, Google-Gemini, Qwen) but not others (DeepSeek, Google-Gemma, OpenAI). One new model version (Gemma 4 31B) showed a substantial AUROC improvement over its predecessor. Models that appeared ‘Invalid’ on binary KEEP/WITHDRAW metacognitive probes produced normal profiles with verbalized confidence, showing that metacognitive assessment is sensitive to probe format. Confidence intervals were of moderate width, and aggregate split-half reliability was high though profile-level reliability was modest.

Conclusion: Metacognitive quality in LLMs is not uniform across domains: aggregate scores hide systematic and stable benchmark-domain differences. Applied/Professional tasks are monitored best, while Formal Reasoning and Natural Science are monitored worst. The current six-domain MMLU taxonomy is useful pragmatically but not a validated latent construct. Family-specific profile structures and probe-format effects matter when evaluating LLM metacognition. Practically, the findings support domain-level screening at the benchmarking stage before deploying models in domain-specific applications, rather than relying solely on aggregate metacognitive metrics.

Abstract: Aggregate metacognitive quality scores mask within-model variation across MMLU benchmark domains. We administered 1,500 MMLU items (250 per domain, under an a priori six-domain grouping) to 33 frontier LLMs from eight model families and computed Type-2 AUROC per model-domain cell using verbalized confidence (0-100). Total observations: 47,151. Every model with above-chance aggregate monitoring showed non-trivial domain-level variation. Applied/Professional knowledge was reliably the easiest benchmark domain to monitor (mean AUROC = .742, ranked top-2 in 21 of 33 models); Formal Reasoning and Natural Science were reliably the hardest (one of the two ranked bottom-2 in 27 of 33 models). The three middle domains were statistically indistinguishable (Kendall's W = .164). A subject-level coherence analysis (within-domain similarity ratio = 0.95) confirms the six-domain grouping is a pragmatic benchmark taxonomy, not a validated latent construct. Within-family profile-shape clustering is significant for Anthropic, Google-Gemini, and Qwen (permutation p < .0001) but not DeepSeek, Google-Gemma, or OpenAI. Gemma 4 31B showed a +.202 AUROC improvement over Gemma 3 27B. Three models classified Invalid on binary KEEP/WITHDRAW probes produced normal profiles under verbalized confidence, confirming probe-format specificity. Bootstrap 95% CIs on 198 cells have median width .199. Split-half aggregate stability r = .893; profile-level split-half is weaker (grand median r = .184). These results show stable benchmark-domain variation obscured by aggregate metrics, and support benchmark-stage domain screening as a step before deployment in specific application areas.

</details>


### [2] [VITA-QinYu: Expressive Spoken Language Model for Role-Playing and Singing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06765)
*Jiacheng Xu,Heting Gao,Liufei Xie,Zhenchuan Yang,Lijiang Li,Yiting Chen,Bin Zhang,Meng Chen,Chaoyu Fu,Weifeng Zhao,Wenjiang Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces VITA-QinYu, an expressive end-to-end spoken language model capable of natural conversation, role-playing, and singing, with state-of-the-art expressiveness and accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing spoken language models mainly handle natural conversation and struggle to generate highly expressive speech, such as distinct role-playing styles or singing, which require rich paralinguistic control without harming linguistic accuracy or conversational fluency.

Method: Design an expressive E2E spoken language model, VITA-QinYu, based on a hybrid speech-text paradigm with interleaved text-audio modeling and multi-codebook audio tokens to better represent paralinguistic information. Build a large-scale data generation pipeline to synthesize 15.8K hours of multi-domain data (natural conversation, role-playing, and singing) and train the model on this corpus. Evaluate on role-playing, singing quality (MOS), and conversational benchmarks (C3, URO).

Result: VITA-QinYu outperforms existing spoken language models by 7 percentage points on objective role-playing benchmarks and by 0.13 on a 5-point MOS scale for singing quality, while also achieving state-of-the-art conversational accuracy and fluency, exceeding previous models by 1.38 points on C3 and 4.98 points on URO.

Conclusion: A hybrid speech-text, multi-codebook tokenization approach combined with large-scale synthetic training data enables a single end-to-end spoken language model to jointly support natural conversation, expressive role-playing, and high-quality singing, advancing the expressiveness and accuracy of spoken language generation and providing an open-source platform for further research and applications.

Abstract: Human speech conveys expressiveness beyond linguistic content, including personality, mood, or performance elements, such as a comforting tone or humming a song, which we formalize as role-playing and singing. We present VITA-QinYu, the first expressive end-to-end (E2E) spoken language model (SLM) that goes beyond natural conversation to support both role-playing and singing generation. VITA-QinYu adopts a hybrid speech-text paradigm that extends interleaved text-audio modeling with multi-codebook audio tokens, a design enabling richer paralinguistic representation while preserving a clear separation between modalities to avoid interference. We further develop a comprehensive data generation pipeline to synthesize a total of 15.8K hours of natural conversation, role-playing, and singing data for training. VITA-QinYu demonstrates superior expressiveness, outperforming peer SLMs by 7 percentage points on objective role-playing benchmarks, and surpassing peer models by 0.13 points on a 5-point MOS scale for singing. Simultaneously, it achieves state-of-the-art conversational accuracy and fluency, exceeding prior SLMs by 1.38 and 4.98 percentage points on the C3 and URO benchmarks, respectively. We open-source our code and models and provide an easy-to-use demo with full-stack support for streaming and full-duplex interaction.

</details>


### [3] [IntentGrasp: A Comprehensive Benchmark for Intent Understanding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06832)
*Yuwei Yin,Chuyuan Li,Giuseppe Carenini*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: IntentGrasp is a new large-scale benchmark and training resource to rigorously evaluate and improve LLMs’ ability to understand user intent across many domains, plus a fine-tuning method (IFT) that substantially boosts this capability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current large language models struggle to accurately infer user intent from speech, conversation, and writing, which limits how helpful, safe, and reliable AI assistants can be. Existing benchmarks are fragmented, domain-limited, or not focused specifically on nuanced intent understanding. The authors aim to build a comprehensive, diverse, and challenging benchmark that reveals these weaknesses quantitatively, and to explore how targeted training on intent data can systematically improve models, moving towards more intentional and socially beneficial AI assistants.

Method: The authors curate 49 open-licensed corpora across 12 varied domains and process them via three steps: (1) source dataset curation to select high-quality intent-relevant data, (2) contextualization of intent labels so that they are clear, consistent, and usable across datasets, and (3) unification of the task format so that all data can be used in a common evaluation and training pipeline. This yields IntentGrasp: a large training set (262,759 instances) and two evaluation sets (All Set and Gem Set, the latter being smaller, more balanced, and more challenging). They evaluate 20 LLMs from 7 model families—including several frontier models—on these benchmarks, compare performance to random guessing and estimated human-level performance, and then introduce Intentional Fine-Tuning (IFT), in which models are fine-tuned on the IntentGrasp training set. They also run leave-one-domain-out (LoDo) experiments to measure how well IFT-trained models generalize to unseen domains.

Result: On the IntentGrasp benchmark, all 20 evaluated LLMs perform poorly on intent understanding: less than 60% on the All Set and under 25% on the harder Gem Set. Strikingly, 17 of the 20 models underperform even a random-guess baseline (15.2% F1) on Gem Set, whereas human performance is estimated at about 81.1%. After applying Intentional Fine-Tuning (IFT) on the IntentGrasp training data, models gain more than 30 F1 points on the All Set and more than 20 F1 points on the Gem Set, representing substantial improvements. LoDo experiments show that these gains persist when evaluating on domains that were held out from training, indicating strong cross-domain generalization of the learned intent understanding.

Conclusion: The study concludes that state-of-the-art LLMs exhibit serious deficiencies in accurately understanding user intent, especially in diverse, challenging settings, and that existing evaluation practices underestimate this gap relative to human performance. IntentGrasp provides a comprehensive benchmark revealing these weaknesses, while the proposed Intentional Fine-Tuning method significantly boosts models’ intent understanding and generalizes across domains. This suggests a viable route to developing more intentional, capable, and safe AI assistants, and positions IntentGrasp and IFT as useful tools for future research on intent modeling and alignment.

Abstract: Accurately understanding the intent behind speech, conversation, and writing is crucial to the development of helpful Large Language Model (LLM) assistants. This paper introduces IntentGrasp, a comprehensive benchmark for evaluating the intent understanding capability of LLMs. Derived from 49 high-quality, open-licensed corpora spanning 12 diverse domains, IntentGrasp is constructed through source datasets curation, intent label contextualization, and task format unification. IntentGrasp contains a large-scale training set of 262,759 instances and two evaluation sets: an All Set of 12,909 test cases and a more balanced and challenging Gem Set of 470 cases. Extensive evaluations on 20 LLMs across 7 families (including frontier models such as GPT-5.4, Gemini-3.1-Pro, and Claude-Opus-4.7) demonstrate unsatisfactory performance, with scores below 60% on All Set and below 25% on Gem set. Notably, 17 out of 20 tested models perform worse than a random-guess baseline (15.2%) on Gem Set, while the estimated human performance is ~81.1%, showing substantial room for improvement. To enhance such ability, this paper proposes Intentional Fine-Tuning (IFT), which fine-tunes the models on the training set in IntentGrasp, yielding significant gains of 30+ F1 points on All Set and 20+ points on Gem Set. Tellingly, the leave-one-domain-out (Lodo) experiments further demonstrate the strong cross-domain generalizability of IFT, verifying that it is a promising approach to substantially enhancing the intent understanding of LLMs. Overall, by benchmarking and boosting intent understanding ability, this study sheds light on a promising path towards more intentional, capable, and safe AI assistants for human benefits and social good.

</details>


### [4] [TajPersLexon: A Tajik-Persian Lexical Resource and Hybrid Model for Cross-Script Low-Resource NLP](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06886)
*Mullosharaf K. Arabov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents TajPersLexon, a 40k-entry Tajik–Persian parallel lexicon and shows that simple, efficient methods can solve cross-script lexical retrieval with very high accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a lack of high-quality, reproducible lexical resources and benchmarks for cross-script mapping between closely related low-resource languages like Tajik (Cyrillic) and Persian (Arabic script). Practical tasks such as OCR post-correction, transliteration, and lexical alignment need accurate yet computationally efficient methods that can run on modest hardware. Existing large multilingual models are not optimized for exact lexical matching in this setting.

Method: The authors construct TajPersLexon, a curated parallel lexicon of 40,112 Tajik–Persian word and short-phrase pairs. They benchmark three families of CPU-only methods for cross-script lexical retrieval: (i) a lightweight hybrid pipeline combining interpretable components (likely rules, string similarity, and small models), (ii) neural sequence-to-sequence models, and (iii) retrieval-based methods. They also test large multilingual sentence transformers and evaluate all approaches using fixed random seeds for reproducibility, including on an OCR post-correction task.

Result: Neural sequence-to-sequence and retrieval-based methods reach 98–99% top-1 accuracy on the cross-script lexical retrieval task, showing that the task is highly solvable under their setup. Large multilingual sentence transformers, however, perform poorly on exact lexical matching in this setting. The proposed interpretable hybrid pipeline achieves competitive performance with 96.4% accuracy in an OCR post-correction task while being computationally efficient on CPU-only hardware.

Conclusion: TajPersLexon provides a strong, reproducible benchmark and resource for Tajik–Persian cross-script lexical tasks. The problem of mapping between these lexicons is essentially solved with simple neural and retrieval approaches, but large generic multilingual transformers are not well-suited for this fine-grained lexical matching. An interpretable, lightweight hybrid model offers an attractive accuracy–efficiency trade-off for real-world applications and will be made broadly accessible through the public release of the dataset, code, and models.

Abstract: This work introduces TajPersLexon, a curated Tajik--Persian parallel lexical resource of 40,112 word and short-phrase pairs for cross-script lexical retrieval, transliteration, and alignment in low-resource settings. We conduct a comprehensive CPU-only benchmark comparing three methodological families: (i) a lightweight hybrid pipeline, (ii) neural sequence-to-sequence models, and (iii) retrieval methods. Our evaluation establishes that the task is essentially solvable, with neural and retrieval baselines achieving 98-99% top-1 accuracy. Crucially, we demonstrate that while large multilingual sentence transformers fail on this exact lexical matching, our interpretable hybrid model offers a favorable accuracy-efficiency trade-off for practical applications, achieving 96.4% accuracy in an OCR post-correction task. All experiments use fixed random seeds for full reproducibility. The dataset, code, and models will be publicly released.

</details>


### [5] [MIST: Multimodal Interactive Speech-based Tool-calling Conversational Assistants for Smart Homes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06897)
*Maximillian Chen,Xuanming Zhang,Michael Peng,Zhou Yu,Alexandros Papangelis,Yohan Jo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce MIST, a synthetic multimodal, speech-based tool-calling dataset for IoT, showing current multimodal LLMs perform poorly and releasing data + framework to spur research.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: IoT devices are proliferating, and users will naturally interact with them via speech. Existing LLMs can use tools, but real-world IoT scenarios are hard: they require reasoning about space and time, device states, and back-and-forth (mixed-initiative) conversations. There is little standardized data to evaluate and train such capabilities, creating a research gap.

Method: They construct MIST, a synthetic dataset of multi-turn, voice-driven interactions that require code generation to control IoT devices under spatiotemporal and interaction constraints. The dataset is multimodal (including speech), involves tool-calling, and is designed to emulate realistic IoT control scenarios. They also build an extensible framework that can generate similar datasets in related domains.

Result: When evaluating state-of-the-art multimodal LLMs (both open- and closed-weight) on MIST, they observe a large performance gap between open- and closed-weight models, and even the strongest closed-weight “frontier” models perform far from perfectly, revealing substantial remaining challenges.

Conclusion: MIST exposes that current multimodal LLMs are not yet capable enough for robust, mixed-initiative, speech-based control of IoT devices. By releasing the dataset and generation framework, they aim to provide a standardized benchmark and a flexible tool for creating related datasets, encouraging further research on voice assistants that reason about physical-world constraints.

Abstract: The rise of Internet of Things (IoT) devices in the physical world necessitates voice-based interfaces capable of handling complex user experiences. While modern Large Language Models (LLMs) already demonstrate strong tool-usage capabilities, modeling real-world IoT devices presents a difficult, understudied challenge which combines modeling spatiotemporal constraints with speech inputs, dynamic state tracking, and mixed-initiative interaction patterns. We introduce MIST (the Multimodal Interactive Speech-based Tool-calling Dataset), a synthetic multi-turn, voice-driven code generation task that operates over IoT devices. We find that there is a significant gap between open- and closed-weight multimodal LLMs on MIST, and that even frontier closed-weight LLMs have substantial headroom. We release MIST and an extensible data generation framework to build related datasets in order to facilitate research on mixed-initiative voice assistants which reason about physical world constraints.

</details>


### [6] [Reflections and New Directions for Human-Centered Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06901)
*Caleb Ziems,Dora Zhao,Rose E. Wang,Matthew Jörke,Ahmad Rushdi,Advit Deepak,Sunny Yu,Anshika Agarwal,Harshvardhan Agarwal,Gabriela Aranguiz-Dias,Aditri Bhagirath,Justine Breuch,Huanxing Chen,Ruishi Chen,Sarah Chen,Haocheng Fan,William Fang,Cat Gonzales Fergesen,Daniel Frees,Tian Gao,Ziqing Huang,Vishal Jain,Yucheng Jiang,Kirill Kalinin,Su Doga Karaca,Arpandeep Khatua,Teland La,Isabelle Levent,Miranda Li,Xinling Li,Yongce Li,Angela Liu,Minsik Oh,Nathan J. Paek,Anthony Qin,Emily Redmond,Michael J. Ryan,Aadesh Salecha,Xiaoxian Shen,Pranava Singhal,Shashanka Subrahmanya,Mei Tan,Irawadee Thawornbut,Michelle Vinocour,Xiaoyue Wang,Zheng Wang,Henry Jin Weng,Pawan Wirawarn,Shirley Wu,Sophie Wu,Yichen Xie,Patrick Ye,Sean Zhang,Yutong Zhang,Cathy Zhou,Yiling Zhao,James Landay,Diyi Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a framework for building Human-Centered Large Language Models (HCLLMs) that integrate human needs and values throughout the entire development pipeline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs increasingly influence people’s private and professional lives across domains like business, education, healthcare, law, and science, there is a pressing need to ensure these systems are aligned with human priorities, not just optimized for technical performance. Existing practices often treat human considerations as an afterthought, mainly in a brief post-training alignment stage, which is insufficient given the societal scale and impact of LLMs.

Method: The authors synthesize perspectives from NLP, HCI, and responsible AI to define a human-centered framework. They conceptually analyze ethics, economics, and technical objectives of language modeling, and then map human-centered principles onto each step of the LLM pipeline: system design, data sourcing, model training, evaluation, and deployment. They also illustrate the framework via a case study focused on the future of work with HCLLMs.

Result: The paper produces a structured set of human-centered insights, design principles, and practical recommendations tailored to each stage of the LLM development lifecycle. It clarifies where and how human concerns, preferences, values, and goals should be incorporated beyond the usual post-training alignment, and demonstrates the applicability of the framework through the work-focused case study.

Conclusion: To responsibly manage the growing influence of LLMs, developers must embed human priorities into every stage of the modeling pipeline, rather than relegating them to late-stage fixes. The proposed HCLLM framework provides interdisciplinary guidance for doing so and suggests that such an approach will better support beneficial, human-aligned uses of LLMs, particularly in domains like the future of work.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly shaping the private and professional lives of users, with numerous applications in business, education, finance, healthcare, law, and science. With this rise in global influence comes greater urgency to build, evaluate, and deploy these systems in a manner that prioritizes not only technical capabilities but also human priorities. This work presents a framework for developing Human-Centered Large Language Models (HCLLMs), which integrates perspectives from Natural Language Processing (NLP), Human-Computer Interaction (HCI), and responsible AI. Considering the ethics, economics, and technical objectives of language modeling, we argue that model developers need to address human concerns, preferences, values, and goals, not only during a cursory post-training stage, but rather with rigor and care at every stage of the pipeline. This paper offers human-centered insights and recommendations for developers at each stage, from system design to data sourcing, model training, evaluation, and responsible deployment. Then we conclude with a case study, applying these insights to understand the future of work with HCLLMs.

</details>


### [7] [MELD: Multi-Task Equilibrated Learning Detector for AI-Generated Text](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06903)
*Chenjun Li,Cheng Wan,Johannes C. Paetzold*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents MELD, a robust multi-task AI-generated text detector that outperforms existing open-source and many commercial systems, especially under attacks and at low false-positive rates.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI-text detectors mostly optimize a simple binary human-vs-AI objective. Once that task saturates, the model has little incentive to encode richer structure such as which generator produced the text, what attack was applied, or which domain it came from. As a result, detectors often fail under adversarial rewrites, on unseen models or domains, and when required to operate at very low false-positive rates, which are crucial for real-world deployment (e.g., academic integrity, moderation, provenance). The paper aims to build a deployable detector that is robust in these challenging, realistic settings.

Method: The authors propose MELD (Multi-Task Equilibrated Learning Detector). A shared encoder feeds four prediction heads: (1) AI-vs-human binary detection, (2) generator-family classification, (3) attack-type classification, and (4) source-domain classification. Their losses are combined using learned homoscedastic uncertainty weights so that the model automatically balances the tasks. To increase robustness, they use a teacher–student setup: an EMA teacher predicts on clean text, while a student is trained on attack-perturbed versions and distilled to match the teacher, encouraging invariance to attacks. Additionally, they introduce a hard-negative pairwise ranking loss that explicitly pushes AI-generated scores away from the closest human examples, enlarging the margin where confusion is greatest. At inference time, only the binary detection head is used, so runtime cost is similar to standard detectors.

Result: On the public RAID leaderboard, MELD is reported as the strongest open-source detector and is competitive with top commercial detectors, particularly under adversarial attack conditions and when evaluated at low FPR. On standard held-out benchmarks, MELD matches or surpasses supervised baselines. The authors also construct MELD-eval, a challenging held-out evaluation set from recent chat models of four major LLM providers. Without any extra fine-tuning, MELD reaches 99.9% true positive rate at 1% false positive rate on MELD-eval, while many comparison methods see large drops in performance.

Conclusion: A multi-task, uncertainty-weighted training strategy combined with teacher–student distillation and margin-enlarging ranking loss yields a robust and deployable AI-text detector. MELD demonstrates strong transfer to new generators and domains, resilience to attacks, and high recall at low false-positive rates, indicating that enriching simple binary detection with structured auxiliary tasks is an effective design pattern for AI-generated text detection in real-world conditions.

Abstract: Large language models are now embedded in everyday writing workflows, making reliable AI-generated text detection important for academic integrity, content moderation, and provenance tracking. In practice, however, a detector must do more than achieve high aggregate AUROC on clean, in-distribution human and AI text: it should remain robust to attacks and adversarial rewrites, transfer to unseen generators and domains, and operate at low false-positive rates (FPR). Most existing detectors optimize a single AI/Human objective, giving the representation little incentive to learn generator, attack, or domain structure once the binary task saturates. We introduce MELD (Multi-Task Equilibrated Learning Detector), a deployable detector for AI-generated text that enriches binary detection with auxiliary supervision. MELD attaches generator-family, attack-type, and source-domain heads to a shared encoder, and balances the four losses with learned homoscedastic uncertainty weights. To improve robustness, an EMA teacher predicts on clean inputs while an attack-augmented student is distilled toward the teacher. MELD further uses a hard-negative pairwise ranking loss to enlarge the score margin between AI-generated texts and the most confusable human texts. At inference, all auxiliary heads are discarded, giving MELD the same interface and cost as a standard detector. On the public RAID leaderboard, MELD is the strongest open-source detector and is competitive with leading commercial models, especially under attack and at low FPR. Across standard held-out benchmarks, MELD matches or outperforms supervised baselines. We further introduce MELD-eval, a held-out evaluation pool built from recent chat models released by four major LLM providers. Without additional finetuning, MELD achieves 99.9% TPR at 1% FPR on MELD-eval, while many baselines degrade sharply.

</details>


### [8] [Can LLMs Take Retrieved Information with a Grain of Salt?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06919)
*Behzad Shayegh,Mohamed Osama Ahmed,Fred Tung,Leo Feng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how well large language models adjust their answers according to how certain or uncertain the provided context is, finds systematic failures, and proposes a prompt-level interaction strategy that improves this behavior without retraining models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs with retrieval-augmentation are strong, in critical domains their answers must reflect how reliable the retrieved information is. Current work has paid little attention to whether models actually modulate their responses based on explicit certainty labels in the context, which can lead to overconfident or inappropriate answers in high‑stakes scenarios like medicine and finance.

Method: The authors evaluate eight different LLMs on a new metric called context-certainty obedience, defined as how well a model’s response matches the certainty expressed in its context. They design tests where the context explicitly signals different certainty levels, then observe whether models: (1) retain or recall their prior knowledge when the context is uncertain, (2) correctly interpret the expressed certainty, and (3) avoid overtrusting complex or misleading contexts. Based on the failure modes, they design an interaction strategy composed of prior knowledge reminders, explicit certainty recalibration instructions, and simplification of the input context, all implemented at the prompt / interaction level without changing model weights.

Result: Across the eight evaluated LLMs, the authors find systematic weaknesses: models often fail to recall correct prior knowledge when given an uncertain context, misread the certainty cues embedded in the text, and tend to overtrust long or complex contexts even when those contexts are flagged as uncertain. Applying their proposed interaction strategy yields about a 25% reduction in context-certainty obedience errors on average across models, indicating that careful prompt and interaction design can meaningfully improve uncertainty-aware behavior.

Conclusion: LLMs as currently used are not reliably obedient to the certainty levels expressed in retrieved contexts, which is problematic for high-stakes applications. However, through a principled evaluation metric and analysis of failure modes, the authors show that a light‑weight, model-agnostic interaction strategy—combining prior knowledge reminders, certainty recalibration, and context simplification—can substantially improve context-certainty obedience without retraining. This underscores the importance and effectiveness of interaction design as a lever for enhancing LLM reliability around uncertainty.

Abstract: Large language models have demonstrated impressive retrieval-augmented capabilities. However, a crucial area remains underexplored: their ability to appropriately adapt responses to the certainty of the retrieved information. It is a limitation with real consequences in high-stakes domains like medicine and finance. We evaluate eight LLMs on their context-certainty obedience, measuring how well they adjust responses to match expressed context certainty. Our analysis reveals systematic limitations: LLMs struggle to recall prior knowledge after observing an uncertain context, misinterpret expressed certainties, and overtrust complex contexts. To address these, we propose an interaction strategy combining prior reminders, certainty recalibration, and context simplification. This approach reduces obedience errors by 25% on average, without modifying model weights, demonstrating the efficacy of interaction design in enhancing LLM reliability. Our contributions include a principled evaluation metric, empirical insights into LLMs' uncertainty handling, and a portable strategy to improve context-certainty obedience across diverse LLMs.

</details>


### [9] [MultiSoc-4D: A Benchmark for Diagnosing Instruction-Induced Label Collapse in Closed-Set LLM Annotation of Bengali Social Media](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06940)
*Souvik Pramanik,S. M. Riaz Rahman Antu,Shak Mohammad Abyad,Md. Ibrahim Khalil,Md. Shahriar Hussain*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MultiSoc-4D, a 58K+ Bengali social media benchmark annotated along four dimensions, and uses it to reveal and quantify a systematic LLM annotation failure called instruction-induced label collapse, where models overuse fallback labels and under-detect minority classes like hate and sarcasm.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used to create and scale NLP datasets, especially in low-resource languages, but their behavior under closed-set labeling instructions in such settings is poorly understood. The authors aim to understand whether and how LLM-based annotation pipelines introduce systematic biases, particularly for subtle, minority phenomena (hate, sarcasm) in Bengali social media. They want a diagnostic benchmark and empirical evidence to evaluate annotation quality and the illusion of high agreement created by label collapse.

Method: They construct MultiSoc-4D, a Bengali social media dataset (58K+ comments) from six sources annotated along four dimensions: category, sentiment, hate speech, and sarcasm. They design a structured annotation pipeline where four major LLMs (ChatGPT, Gemini, Claude, Grok) each annotate disjoint partitions but share a 20% common validation set. They compare LLM labels against a human-calibrated reference, quantify under-detection of minority classes, and analyze agreement via Fleiss’ Kappa. They then extend the study to 40+ LLMs to measure how annotation bias propagates across architectures and models in the training pipeline.

Result: They identify and characterize a phenomenon called instruction-induced label collapse: under closed-set labeling instructions, LLMs strongly prefer generic fallback labels (Other, Neutral, No), leading to inflated apparent agreement but severe under-detection of minority categories. In the dataset, LLMs missed about 79% of hateful and 75% of sarcastic instances relative to the human reference. Statistical analysis shows almost zero Fleiss’ Kappa (κ ≈ -0.001) for sarcasm detection, indicating that the high apparent agreement on majority labels is an illusion. This bias is observed consistently across more than 40 different LLMs, suggesting it is pervasive and architecture-agnostic.

Conclusion: LLM-based closed-set annotation pipelines for low-resource languages, exemplified here with Bengali social media, are prone to instruction-induced label collapse, which hides substantial errors behind high agreement on majority labels. This creates a label agreement illusion and systematically suppresses important minority phenomena like hate speech and sarcasm. MultiSoc-4D is released as a benchmark to diagnose and study such annotation biases in Bengali NLP, encouraging more careful design, validation, and calibration of LLM-based annotation workflows.

Abstract: Annotation automation via Large Language Models (LLMs) is the core approach for scaling NLP datasets; however, LLM behavior with respect to closed-set instructions in low-resource languages has not been well studied. We present MultiSoc-4D, a Bengali social media dataset benchmark, which contains 58K+ social media comments from six sources annotated along four dimensions: category, sentiment, hate speech, and sarcasm. By employing a structured pipeline where ChatGPT, Gemini, Claude, and Grok individually annotate separate partitions, while sharing a common validation set of 20%, we diagnose LLM behavior systematically. We discover a prevalent phenomenon called "instruction-induced label collapse", wherein LLMs show a systematic preference towards fallback labels (Other, Neutral, No), leading to high agreement rates but under-detection of minority categories. For example, we find that LLMs failed to detect 79% and 75% of instances with hateful and sarcastic content compared to a human-calibrated reference. Furthermore, we prove that it represents a "label agreement illusion", statistically validated via almost null Fleiss' Kappa ($κ\approx -0.001$) on sarcasm detection. Across 40+ LLMs, we benchmark this annotation bias propagation within the training pipeline, regardless of architectural differences. We release MultiSoc-4D as a diagnostic benchmark for annotation biases in Bengali NLP.

</details>


### [10] [Group of Skills: Group-Structured Skill Retrieval for Agent Skill Libraries](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06978)
*Kun Zeng,Yu Huo,Siyu Zhang,Zi Ye,Yuecheng Zhuo,Haoyue Liu,Yuquan Lu,Junhao Wen,Xiaoying Tang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes GoSkills, a structured skill-retrieval method that returns role-labeled execution contexts instead of flat skill lists, improving agent performance under limited skill budgets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Skill-based agents depend on large skill libraries, but existing retrieval returns either individual skills or opaque bundles, forcing agents to guess how to use them. This leads to inefficiencies and failures, especially when context budgets are small and requirements or pitfalls are not explicit. A method is needed that retrieves skills as organized execution plans with clear roles and guidance while remaining compatible with existing agents and environments.

Method: Introduce GoSkills, an inference-time retrieval framework that: (1) models the skill library as a typed skill graph; (2) forms anchor-centered skill groups representing core skills and their dependencies; (3) links these groups via a higher-level group graph to select supporting groups; (4) compresses the resulting group plan into a bounded set of atomic skill payloads that fit a skill budget; and (5) renders a standardized execution contract exposing four explicit fields—Start, Support, Check, and Avoid—so the agent receives a compact, role-labeled context without modifying the agent or skills themselves.

Result: On the SkillsBench and ALFWorld benchmarks, GoSkills matches or preserves coverage of visible task requirements even under tight skill budgets and outperforms flat skill-access baselines. Compared to more structurally aware retrieval methods, it often yields higher task reward and lower agent-only runtime, indicating more efficient and effective use of available skills.

Conclusion: Structuring retrieval as groups of role-labeled skills rather than unstructured lists enables agents to use large skill libraries more effectively without changing their architecture or the underlying skills. GoSkills shows that inference-time, group-structured retrieval can maintain requirement coverage under small budgets and improve both success rates and efficiency relative to existing retrieval strategies.

Abstract: Skill-augmented agents increasingly rely on large reusable skill libraries, but retrieving relevant skills is not the same as presenting usable context. Existing methods typically return atomic skills or dependency-aware bundles whose internal roles remain implicit, leaving the agent to infer the execution entry point, support skills, visible requirements, and failure-avoidance guidance. We introduce Group of Skills (GoSkills), an inference-time group-structured retrieval method that changes the agent-facing retrieval object from a flat skill list to a compact, role-labeled execution context. GoSkills builds anchor-centered skill groups from a typed skill graph, expands support groups through a group graph, bottlenecks the selected group plan into a bounded set of atomic skill payloads, and renders a fixed execution contract with Start, Support, Check, and Avoid fields, without changing the downstream agent, skill payloads, or execution environment. Experiments on SkillsBench and ALFWorld show that GoSkills preserves visible-requirement coverage under a small skill budget, improves over flat skill-access baselines, and often improves reward and agent-only runtime relative to structural retrieval references.

### [11] [Towards Closing the Autoregressive Gap in Language Modeling via Entropy-Gated Continuous Bitstream Diffusion](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07013)
*Georgios Batzolis,Mark Girolami,Luca Ambrogioni*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a diffusion-based language model that operates on continuous representations of binary bitstreams instead of token IDs, achieving autoregressive-level perplexity while being parallel and more scalable with vocabulary size.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion language models enable parallel, order-agnostic text generation, but they have underperformed autoregressive models in sample quality and diversity, and they incur an O(V) cost with respect to vocabulary size. Recent work in continuous diffusion over token embeddings helps, but the performance gap and scalability issues remain. The authors want a diffusion-based model that matches autoregressive quality, improves sampling efficiency, and avoids vocabulary-size bottlenecks.

Method: They model text as a continuous-time diffusion process over fixed-width binary bitstreams rather than over discrete tokens or continuous embeddings. Semantic tokens are encoded as analog bit sequences. The model uses a matched-filter residual parameterization that separates contextual learning from analytic, independent-bit posteriors. For sampling, they use a stochastic procedure with Langevin-type noise corrections gated by an entropy-rate profile, so that more stochasticity is injected in high-information regions and less where the prediction is confident. Architecturally, they predict only O(log V) bitwise logits with a semantic bit-patching scheme, avoiding the usual O(V) softmax over the vocabulary.

Result: On the LM1B benchmark, a 130M-parameter bitstream DLM achieves a generative perplexity of 59.76 at a real-data-matched entropy of 4.31 using 256 neural function evaluations, outperforming previous DLMs and matching an autoregressive reference. On OpenWebText, with far fewer sampling steps than prior continuous DLMs (4x fewer than 1024-NFE baselines), it reaches GenPPL=27.06 at entropy 5.26, establishing a new Pareto frontier of quality vs. sampling cost. The model also demonstrates reduced memory use and higher throughput thanks to bitwise prediction instead of full-vocabulary softmax.

Conclusion: Modeling language as diffusion over continuous bitstreams, coupled with entropy-aware stochastic sampling and bitwise prediction, can close the quality gap between diffusion language models and autoregressive models while remaining parallelizable and more scalable in vocabulary size. Bitstream diffusion is presented as a promising, efficient paradigm for future large-vocabulary language generation systems.

Abstract: Diffusion language models (DLMs) promise parallel, order-agnostic generation, but on standard benchmarks they have historically lagged behind autoregressive models in sample quality and diversity. Recent continuous flow and diffusion approaches over token embeddings have narrowed this gap, suggesting continuous state spaces are highly effective for language. In this work, we further close the autoregressive gap by modeling text as a continuous diffusion process over fixed-width binary bitstreams. Our approach represents semantic tokens as analog bit sequences and utilizes a matched-filter residual parameterization to isolate contextual learning from analytic independent-bit posteriors. Crucially, we adopt a stochastic sampler that applies Langevin-type corrections gated by the entropy-rate profile, automatically concentrating stochasticity in high-information regions while remaining nearly deterministic elsewhere. On the One Billion Word Benchmark (LM1B), our 130M-parameter bitstream model reaches a generative perplexity ($\GenPPL$) of $59.76$ at matched real-data entropy ($4.31$) using 256 neural function evaluations (NFEs), decisively outperforming prior DLM baselines and reaching the autoregressive reference. On OpenWebText (OWT), our stochastic sampler establishes a new continuous-DLM Pareto frontier, achieving $\GenPPL=27.06$ at an entropy of $5.26$ using $4\times$ fewer steps than previous 1024-NFE baselines. As an additional architectural benefit, bitstream diffusion removes the $\mathcal{O}(V)$ vocabulary scaling bottleneck shared by standard DLMs. By predicting $\mathcal{O}(\log V)$ bitwise logits via semantic bit-patching, our model yields a reduced memory footprint and higher throughput, demonstrating a scalable paradigm for language generation as vocabulary sizes grow.

*Hyeongdon Moon,Carolyn Rosé,John Stamper*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors propose Cognitive Agent Compilation (CAC), a framework that decomposes an educational AI agent into three parts: (i) an explicit knowledge representation, (ii) a problem-solving policy, and (iii) verification and update rules. A strong teacher LLM is used to "compile" its own problem-solving behavior and knowledge into a smaller target agent—implemented as a Small Language Model or similar structure—that operates within explicit, human-inspectable bounds.

Result: They implement an early proof-of-concept using Small Language Models as the target agents. This prototype illustrates how problem-solving knowledge can be compiled into a more explicit form and reveals trade-offs between maintaining tight, explicit control over the agent’s knowledge and achieving scalable generalization across tasks.

Educational systems often require inspectable and editable knowledge states: educators want to know what a system assumes the learner knows, and learners benefit when the system can justify actions in terms of explicit skills, misconceptions, and strategies. Inspired by cognitive architectures, we propose Cognitive Agent Compilation (CAC), a framework that uses a strong teacher LLM to compile problem-solving knowledge into an explicit target agent. CAC separates (i) knowledge representation, (ii) problem-solving policy, and (iii) verification and update rules, with the goal of making bounded problem solving more inspectable and editable in educational settings. We present an early proof of concept implemented with Small Language Models that surfaces key design trade-offs, particularly between explicit control and scalable generalization, and positions CAC as an initial step toward bounded-knowledge AI for educational applications.

### [13] [MedExAgent: Training LLM Agents to Ask, Examine, and Diagnose in Noisy Clinical Environments](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07058)
*Yicheng Gao,Xiaolin Zhou,Yahan Li,Yue Zhao,Ruishan Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper formalizes clinical diagnosis as an interactive, noisy decision process (a POMDP) and proposes MedExAgent, an LLM-based agent trained with supervised finetuning plus reinforcement learning to perform realistic, cost‑aware medical diagnosis through questioning, exam ordering, and final diagnosis.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing medical LLM benchmarks and automatic diagnosis systems oversimplify real clinical practice, typically treating it as single-turn QA or clean, linear conversations. They ignore interaction, uncertainty, noise, and the need to balance diagnostic accuracy with costs and patient burden. The paper aims to build a more realistic environment and agent that better reflect how doctors actually gather information and decide under uncertainty.

Method: The authors formalize clinical diagnosis as a Partially Observable Markov Decision Process (POMDP) with three types of actions: (1) ask the patient questions, (2) order medical exams (implemented as tool calls), and (3) issue a diagnosis. They design a systematic noise model with seven patient noise types and three exam noise types to mimic real-world uncertainty and imperfect information. On top of this environment, they train MedExAgent via a two-stage pipeline: first, supervised finetuning on synthetic doctor–patient conversations structured by the Calgary–Cambridge clinical interview model; second, policy optimization with DAPO (a RL-style method) using a composite reward that jointly accounts for diagnostic accuracy, quality of tool calls, and exam costs including money and patient discomfort.

Result: In experiments and ablation studies within their proposed environment, MedExAgent reaches diagnostic accuracy on par with larger models while using examinations more judiciously. It maintains or improves diagnostic performance while controlling or reducing exam-related costs compared to baselines. The ablations show the contribution of the POMDP formulation, the structured synthetic training data, and the DAPO-based optimization to both accuracy and cost-efficiency.

Conclusion: Modeling diagnosis as a noisy, interactive POMDP and training an LLM-based agent (MedExAgent) with both supervised and reinforcement-style objectives leads to realistic and efficient diagnostic behavior. The approach yields competitive diagnostic accuracy with more cost-effective exam strategies than larger or less-structured baselines, suggesting that future medical LLM systems should be evaluated and trained in similarly interactive, uncertainty-aware environments rather than simplified QA-style settings.

Abstract: Real-world clinical diagnosis is a complex process in which the doctor is required to obtain information from both interaction with the patient and conducting medical exams. Additionally, the doctor needs to adapt to different patient personas, as well as noisy and incomplete information that can happen at any time during the process. However, existing benchmarks for medical LLMs and methods for automatic diagnosis largely simplify this process by reducing it to single-turn question answering, noise-free conversations, or sequential exam making, etc., ignoring the interactive and uncertain nature of clinical diagnosis. In this paper, we aim to address this gap by formalizing clinical diagnosis as a Partially Observable Markov Decision Process (POMDP) with three action types: questioning the patient, ordering medical exams as tool calls, and issuing a diagnosis. We also introduce a systematic noise model comprising seven patient noise types and three exam noise types. Using our proposed environment, we train an effective diagnosis agent, \textbf{MedExAgent}, through a two-stage pipeline that first performs supervised finetuning on synthetic conversations structured after the Calgary-Cambridge model for clinical interviews, and then applies DAPO to optimize a composite reward capturing diagnostic accuracy, tool call quality, and exam cost including financial cost and patient discomfort. Through extensive experiments and ablation studies, we demonstrate that MedExAgent achieves diagnostic performance comparable to larger models while maintaining cost-efficient examination strategies.

### [14] [WiCER: Wiki-memory Compile, Evaluate, Refine Iterative Knowledge Compilation for LLM Wiki Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07068)
*Juan M. Huerta*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces WiCER, an iterative method to reliably compile raw documents into a persistent "LLM Wiki" that can be used via KV-cache instead of RAG, achieving fast, high-quality context access while avoiding loss of critical facts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Using LLMs with external knowledge typically relies on retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), which incurs runtime retrieval cost and can fail to retrieve needed documents. A promising alternative is to pre-compile domain knowledge into a compact, wiki-like representation that can be loaded into KV cache for sub-second, retrieval-free inference. However, naïvely compiling large corpora into such a wiki causes a compilation gap: the model tends to discard or blur crucial details, leading to catastrophic failures and performance worse than RAG, especially at scale due to attention dilution. The paper aims to characterize this gap and develop a principled way to close it so that LLM Wiki memory becomes practically reliable.

Method: The authors first empirically study the "compilation gap" on 6,800 questions across 17 RepLiQA domains, comparing full-context KV-cache inference, RAG, and various compilation strategies. They then propose WiCER (Wiki-memory Compile, Evaluate, Refine), an iterative algorithm inspired by counterexample-guided abstraction refinement (CEGAR). WiCER works by: (1) compiling raw documents into a candidate wiki; (2) evaluating that wiki using diagnostic probes/questions to reveal where important facts were dropped or distorted; (3) identifying those missing facts as counterexamples; and (4) forcing preservation of those facts in a refined compilation step. This loop is repeated for one or two iterations. Ablation studies separate the impact of targeted diagnosis/refinement from simple generic pinning of more content.

Result: The study finds that when using curated knowledge and full-context KV-cache inference, performance exceeds RAG (4.38 vs. 4.08 on a 5-point scale) with 7.3× faster time-to-first-token. However, at larger scales attention dilution causes performance to drop below RAG, and blind (non-iterative) compilation into wikis performs poorly (scores around 2.14–2.32 vs. 3.46, with 53–60% catastrophic failure rates). Applying WiCER closes much of this compilation gap: after one to two refinement iterations, about 80% of lost quality is recovered, with mean scores of 3.24 vs. 3.47 for raw full-context across 15 topics with baselines, and catastrophic failures are reduced by 55% relative. Ablations over all 17 topics show that targeted diagnostic refinement brings a large improvement (+0.95), whereas generic pinning of content yields only a minor gain (+0.16).

Conclusion: The paper concludes that LLM Wiki-style persistent memory, served via KV-cache, can outperform RAG in both quality and latency when knowledge is curated and the compilation gap is addressed. Naïve or blind compilation is insufficient and leads to high failure rates, but the proposed WiCER algorithm, which iteratively compiles, evaluates, and refines using diagnostic probes, successfully recovers most of the lost quality and significantly reduces catastrophic errors. This demonstrates a practical path toward reliable, retrieval-free wiki memories for LLMs, and the released code and benchmarks support further research in this direction.

Abstract: The LLM Wiki pattern, to compile and provide domain knowledge into a persistent artifact and serve it to LLMs via KV cache inference, promises context access at sub-second latency with zero retrieval failure. Realizing this requires solving the compilation gap: LLM compilation distilling raw documents into a wiki without catastrophically discarding critical facts. We characterize this gap across 17 RepLiQA domains (6,800 questions): we observe that full context KV cache inference outperforms RAG on curated knowledge (4.38 vs. 4.08 out of 5, 7.3 faster TTFT) but degrades below RAG at scale due to attention dilution, and blind compilation fails entirely (2.14 to 2.32 vs. 3.46, 53 to 60% catastrophic failure rate). To address the compilation gap, we propose WiCER (Wiki-memory Compile, Evaluate, Refine), an iterative algorithm inspired by counterexample-guided abstraction refinement (CEGAR) that closes this gap. WiCER evaluates compiled wikis against diagnostic probes, identifies dropped facts, and forces their preservation in subsequent compilations. One to two iterations recover 80% of lost quality (mean 3.24 vs. 3.47 for raw full-context across the 15 topics with baselines), reducing catastrophic failures by 55% relative. An ablation across all 17 topics confirms that targeted diagnosis (+0.95), not generic pinning (+0.16), drives the gains. All code and benchmarks are released for reproducible research.

### [15] [Self-Consolidating Language Models: Continual Knowledge Incorporation from Context](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07076)
*Zekun Wang,Anant Gupta,Zihan Dong,Christopher J. MacLellan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose SCoL, a framework that lets an LLM decide, via generated update instructions and meta-RL, which of its own layers to update so it can continually consolidate streamed context into weights while minimizing interference, improving knowledge acquisition and retention over several baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs now operate on long, streaming contexts (passages, conversations, workflows). Simply increasing context length doesn’t guarantee that important information is stored and reused; information can be forgotten once it leaves the window, and naive continual fine-tuning causes interference with prior knowledge. The paper aims to enable LLMs to continually consolidate useful information from context into their parameters in a controlled, scalable way.

Method: They introduce Self-Consolidating Language Models (SCoL), a post-training framework where, given the current context, the LLM generates textual update instructions specifying which Transformer layers should be updated. These instructions control sparse parameter updates. Because each update changes the model that will later choose future update locations, they train this process with meta-reinforcement learning over an evolving model state. Instantiations include using supervised QA rewards on SQuAD for knowledge incorporation and intrinsic likelihood-based rewards for long-context consolidation (LongBench v2). They also analyze the learned update-layer selections using Fisher information.

Result: On both SQuAD-based knowledge incorporation and LongBench v2 long-context consolidation, SCoL improves knowledge acquisition and retention compared to prompting, summarization, batch test-time training, and sequential finetuning baselines. The learned update patterns are sparse and tend to target layers with high Fisher information, indicating that plasticity is routed to loss-sensitive regions while reducing interference. SCoL trained on shorter meta-training streams generalizes to longer evaluation streams, demonstrating scalability for streaming settings.

Conclusion: SCoL provides an effective, scalable approach for continual context consolidation in LLMs by having models self-select where to update their own parameters under meta-RL. It improves retention and reduces interference compared to strong baselines, learns to update a sparse set of high-impact layers, and generalizes from shorter to longer streams, suggesting it is a promising direction for long-context, streaming LLM applications.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly receive information as streams of passages, conversations, and long-context workflows. While longer context windows expose more evidence, they do not ensure that useful information is preserved and reused. We study continual context consolidation: writing current context into model weights while limiting interference with previously consolidated information. We propose \textbf{S}elf-\textbf{Co}nsolidating \textbf{L}anguage Models (SCoL), a post-training framework in which, given current context, an LLM learns to generate textual update instructions specifying which of its own Transformer layers should be updated. Because committed updates change the model that later generates future selections, we train SCoL with meta-reinforcement learning over an evolving model state. We instantiate SCoL with supervised QA rewards on SQuAD knowledge incorporation and intrinsic likelihood-based rewards for LongBench v2 long-context consolidation. Across both settings, SCoL improves acquisition and retention over prompting, summarization, batch test-time training, and sequential finetuning baselines. Analysis of learned selection patterns shows that SCoL encourages the LLM to generate sparse update locations that align with layers of high Fisher information, suggesting that the model learns to route plasticity toward loss-sensitive regions while limiting interference. Moreover, SCoL transfers from shorter meta-training streams to longer LongBench v2 streams at evaluation, suggesting that our framework supports scalable streaming consolidation.

### [16] [Beyond Single Ground Truth: Reference Monism as Epistemic Injustice in ASR Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07084)
*Anna Seo Gyeong Choi,Maria Teleki,James Caverlee,Miguel del Rio,Corey Miller,Hoon Choi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper critiques the use of a single, “ground truth” transcription standard in ASR evaluation and proposes reporting performance across multiple, normatively legitimate conventions instead.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: ASR systems are evaluated by comparing their outputs to human-generated transcripts using WER, but these transcripts follow specific conventions that embed normative assumptions about what counts as relevant speech. This is particularly problematic for speakers with aphasia, whose clinically meaningful disfluencies are erased or treated as errors under common “clean” conventions. The authors are motivated to show that this practice creates epistemic injustice and that ASR evaluation methodology must be reconsidered to account for diverse speech patterns.

Method: 1) Philosophical analysis: introduce the concept of a “hermeneutical gap” in ASR evaluation, arguing that reference monism (a single transcription standard) lacks interpretive resources for certain speaker groups. 2) Formalization: define Epistemic Injustice Distance (EID) as a quantitative measure of the harm or distortion induced by using one transcription convention as ground truth over others. 3) Empirical study: use the AphasiaBank corpus to generate or compare different transcription conventions (verbatim, non-verbatim, legal, etc.) for the same speech, compute WER for ASR outputs under each convention, and examine how performance metrics shift across standards and populations.

Result: The study finds that ASR performance, as quantified by WER, varies substantially depending on which transcription convention is treated as ground truth. For speakers with aphasia, “clean” or non-verbatim conventions mischaracterize clinically meaningful disfluencies as errors, producing systematically worse scores. This variability is captured by the proposed Epistemic Injustice Distance (EID), which quantifies the evaluation bias introduced by reference monism.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that enforcing a single transcription convention as the unique ground truth in ASR evaluation constitutes epistemic injustice, particularly for marginalized or atypical speaker populations such as individuals with aphasia. They argue that evaluation should move from reference monism to a pluralist approach by: (a) explicitly recognizing multiple legitimate transcription conventions, and (b) reporting a WER-Range across these conventions instead of a single WER number. This better reflects the diversity of speech practices and reduces evaluative harms to underrepresented speakers.

Abstract: Automatic speech recognition (ASR) evaluation compares system output to ground truth transcripts, with Word Error Rate (WER) quantifying the distance between them. But ground truth transcripts are not discovered - they are produced by human annotators following conventions that encode normative assumptions about which speech features matter. Different conventions (verbatim, non-verbatim, legal) produce different transcripts of identical speech and judge the same ASR output differently. This paper argues that reference monism - enforcing a single transcription convention as ground truth - commits epistemic injustice. Speakers with aphasia, whose speech includes clinically meaningful disfluencies, are systematically disadvantaged when evaluated against "clean" references that treat those disfluencies as errors. The harm is not merely differential performance, but that evaluative infrastructure lacks interpretive resources to recognize their contributions as legitimate. We develop a philosophical framework introducing the hermeneutical gap, formalize Epistemic Injustice Distance (EID) to measure reference monism's cost, and demonstrate empirically using AphasiaBank that WER varies depending on which convention defines ground truth. We propose WER-Range: reporting performance across legitimate conventions rather than assuming a single correct answer.

### [17] [The Translation Tax Is Not a Scalar: A Counterfactual Audit of English-Source Cue Inheritance in Chinese Multilingual Benchmarks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07093)
*Zezheng Lin,Fengming Liu,Handi Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper re-examines the idea of a single “Translation Tax” in translated benchmarks and finds instead nuanced, estimator- and item-specific validity risks rather than a uniform inflation effect.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Many NLP benchmarks are created by translating English datasets into other languages, and it is commonly assumed that translation uniformly inflates model scores by preserving English cues (the “Translation Tax”). This assumption underpins how researchers interpret multilingual benchmark results, but has not been rigorously audited across estimators and items. The paper aims to test whether a single, scalar Translation Tax reliably explains performance inflation in an English-to-Chinese setting and to clarify what actual validity risks arise from translation.

Method: The authors study English-to-Chinese benchmark translations using several complementary estimators of Translation Tax. They analyze back-translation gaps and their robustness to parsing, examine whether calibration between cue presence and model scores predicts item-level gains, and run a six-model comparison between native (non-translated) and translated controls to test for uniform benchmark effects versus model-family effects. They introduce a same-item LLM-based “naturalization” stress test, which systematically rewrites the Chinese surface form while holding answers, options, and semantic content fixed, to isolate the effect of residual English-source cues. They also identify and correct a prompt-construction bug affecting initial naturalization results. The study is accompanied by per-cell evidence, a formalized naturalization protocol, human quality control procedures, and a reporting checklist for translated-benchmark papers.

Result: The three proxy estimators for Translation Tax do not agree. Back-translation gaps are small and highly sensitive to parsing choices (parser-fragile). Cue-score calibration fails to predict which items gain more performance after translation. The six-model native-control comparison suggests differences are driven more by model family than by a uniform effect of benchmark translation. After fixing a prompt-construction bug, the LLM naturalization contrast no longer indicates a strong interaction between model family and translation. However, it reveals a dose-response pattern at the item level: items with higher residual English-source cues (“high-residue”) benefit from translation, whereas low-residue items do not show such gains.

Conclusion: There is no single, scalar Translation Tax that uniformly explains performance inflation in translated benchmarks. Instead, translation introduces heterogeneous, estimator-dependent and item-dependent validity risks. Effects vary across items and models, and common proxy estimators can be misleading or unstable. The authors recommend more nuanced evaluation practices, provide a naturalization protocol and quality-control artifacts, and propose a reporting checklist to improve transparency and reliability in future translated multilingual benchmark studies.

Abstract: The Translation Tax is often treated as a scalar: translated benchmarks are assumed to inflate scores by preserving English-source cues. We audit this claim in an English-to-Chinese setting. Three proxy estimators disagree: back-translation gaps are small and parser-fragile; cue-score calibration does not predict item-level gains; and a six-model native-control comparison shows model-family rather than uniform benchmark effects. We add a same-item LLM-naturalization stress test that holds answer, options, and content fixed while rewriting Chinese surface form. After correcting a prompt-construction bug, this contrast no longer supports a model-family interaction, but it preserves a residue dose-response: high-residue items benefit while low-residue items do not. The result is not a single Translation Tax, but a set of estimator- and item-dependent validity risks. We release per-cell evidence, the naturalization protocol, human QC, and a reporting checklist for translated multilingual benchmark papers.

</details>


### [18] [SAGE: Hierarchical LLM-Based Literary Evaluation through Ontology-Grounded Interpretive Dimensions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07102)
*Tianyu Wang,Nianjun Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes SAGE, a hierarchical, theory-driven LLM-based framework for evaluating literary quality, validates it on a corpus of short stories, and shows it produces reliable, discriminative, measurement-grade assessments of different quality dimensions where LLM stories lag behind human ones.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Literary quality depends on nuanced interpretive aspects—cultural representation, emotional depth, philosophical sophistication—that are hard to measure computationally. Existing automatic metrics (e.g., for fluency or coherence) do not capture these higher-level, theory-grounded literary dimensions. The authors want a scalable, reliable way to evaluate such interpretive qualities, both to study literature and to rigorously compare human and LLM-generated narratives.

Method: They design SAGE, a hierarchical evaluation framework that breaks literary quality into an ontology of interpretive dimensions across three layers: cultural, emotional-psychological, and existential-philosophical. LLMs are used as evaluators in a structured protocol: multi-round iterative reflection for each dimension and independent validation checks, run in dual modes (content-based vs. metadata-based prompts). They apply SAGE to 100 short stories (canonical, pulp, and LLM-generated) and run 600 total evaluations, then analyze reliability (score convergence, inter-rater agreement, mode invariance), discriminative power across genres, dimension-specific effect sizes, and cross-layer correlations.

Result: SAGE yields highly consistent evaluations: 98.8% convergence of scores across reflection rounds, over 94% inter-rater agreement, and almost identical outcomes when using content-based versus metadata-based evaluation. Statistically, the framework detects a strong quality hierarchy Canonical > Pulp > LLM with high significance (p<0.001). Cultural critique and philosophical depth strongly differentiate genres (very large effect sizes, Cohen’s d>2.4), while emotional representation distinguishes them less (d=1.68). Cross-layer correlations between 0.649 and 0.683 show the three layers are related but empirically distinct quality facets.

Conclusion: Theory-driven, ontology-grounded use of LLMs as evaluators can achieve measurement-grade reliability for complex literary dimensions. The SAGE framework both quantifies multiple aspects of literary quality and reveals that current LLMs better approximate human emotional patterns than deep cultural critique or philosophical sophistication. This supports using such structured evaluation for scalable, automated assessment of open-ended text generation and for diagnosing specific weaknesses of generative models relative to human literature.

Abstract: Evaluating literary quality requires assessing interpretive dimensions such as cultural representation, emotional depth, and philosophical sophistication that resist straightforward computational measurement. We introduce SAGE, a hierarchical evaluation framework that decomposes literary quality into ontology-grounded interpretive dimensions assessed through structured large language model evaluation with multi-round iterative reflection and independent validation. We validate the framework on 100 short stories (50 canonical works, 30 pulp fiction, 20 LLM-generated narratives) across three analytical layers (cultural, emotional-psychological, existential-philosophical) using dual-mode assessment. Across 600 evaluations, the framework achieves 98.8% score convergence and greater than 94% inter-rater agreement, with near-perfect mode invariance between content-based and metadata-based evaluation. Statistical analysis reveals a consistent genre hierarchy (Canonical > Pulp > LLM, all p<0.001) with layer-specific discrimination: cultural critique and philosophical depth exhibit very large effect sizes (Cohen's d>2.4), while emotional representation shows smaller gaps (d=1.68), suggesting that affective patterns are more learnable from training data than critical stance or philosophical depth. Cross-layer correlations (r=0.649-0.683) confirm the three dimensions capture empirically distinguishable quality facets. These findings demonstrate that theory-driven LLM evaluation can achieve measurement-grade reliability and support systematic identification of where current generative models fall short of human literary production, with direct implications for scalable automated evaluation of open-ended text generation.

</details>

*Jin Cui,Xinyue Long,Xunyong Zhang,Yadong Zhang,Chuanchang Su,Jingye Gan,Boran Zhao,Pengju Ren*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal LLMs typically compress visual information into discrete textual thoughts, which creates an information bottleneck for fine-grained perception. Newer latent visual reasoning methods aim to reason in continuous hidden states but suffer from manifold incompatibility with pretrained reasoning circuits, leading to drifting latent trajectories, instance-agnostic patterns, and being bypassed during decoding. The authors are motivated to build a latent reasoning mechanism that remains compatible with and causally influential in standard MLLM computation while preserving rich visual detail.

Method: The authors introduce RIS (Retrieve, Integrate, and Synthesize), a spatial-semantic grounded framework that extends the latent reasoning of pretrained MLLMs. They first build a step-wise grounded reasoning dataset annotated with bounding boxes and region-specific semantic descriptions. Using this supervision, they: (1) anchor latent tokens jointly to spatial regions and semantic content, (2) enforce their causal use via a progressive attention bottleneck that forces the model to route information through these latent tokens, and (3) add short language transition tokens that connect synthesized latent states back to vocabulary-aligned decoding, ensuring compatibility with the base language model.

Result: On several vision-language reasoning benchmarks (V*, HRBench4K, HRBench8K, MMVP, BLINK), RIS consistently outperforms both closed- and open-source baselines, including existing latent reasoning approaches. The learned latent trajectories are diverse, interpretable, and show progressive integration of information rather than collapsing into generic patterns or being ignored during answer generation.

Conclusion: RIS provides a practical and effective way to implement faithful internal visual reasoning in MLLMs by grounding latent states in spatial and semantic evidence and integrating them causally into the model’s computation. This mitigates information bottlenecks and manifold incompatibility issues of prior approaches, leading to better fine-grained visual reasoning and more interpretable latent processes.

Abstract: Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) have made remarkable progress on vision-language reasoning, yet most methods still compress visual evidence into discrete textual thoughts, creating an information bottleneck for fine-grained perception. Recent latent visual reasoning methods attempt to reason in continuous hidden states, but we find that they suffer from insufficient manifold compatibility: latent trajectories drift away from pretrained reasoning circuits, collapse into instance-agnostic patterns, and are often bypassed during answer generation. To address these issues, we propose RIS (Retrieve, Integrate, and Synthesize), a spatial-semantic grounded framework that develops latent reasoning as a compatible extension of pretrained MLLM computation. We first construct a step-wise grounded reasoning dataset with bounding boxes and region-specific semantic descriptions. Built on this supervision, RIS anchors latent tokens to both spatial and semantic evidence, enforces their causal role through a progressive attention bottleneck, and introduces short language transition tokens to bridge synthesized latent states back to vocabulary-aligned decoding. Experiments on V*, HRBench4K, HRBench8K, MMVP, and BLINK show consistent improvements over closed/open-source and latent reasoning baselines. Further analyses demonstrate that RIS learns diverse, interpretable, and progressively integrated latent trajectories, offering a practical path toward faithful internal visual reasoning in MLLMs.
