<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing surveys of computer-use agents focus on methods, benchmarks, platforms, or security threats in isolation and typically equate reliability with task success. However, in real deployments, agents interact with complex software ecosystems where reliability also depends on perception accuracy, planning stability, authority and permissions, tool usage, memory handling, and runtime oversight. There is a gap in understanding how capabilities, authority exposure, failures, and control mechanisms are structurally related across an agent’s architecture and lifecycle.

Method: The paper develops a conceptual architecture-lifecycle framework. Architecturally, it decomposes CUAs into three coupled layers—Perception, Decision, and Execution—that map software observations to authority-bearing actions. From a lifecycle perspective, it analyzes four stages—Creation, Deployment, Operation, and Maintenance—describing how priors are learned, tools and permissions are bound, runtime trajectories are exercised, and assurance must be preserved under drift. Using this dual lens, it systematizes existing systems, benchmarks, and security/privacy studies, and identifies where failures are introduced versus where they become observable.

Result: Applying the framework, the authors organize representative CUA systems and studies according to architectural layers and lifecycle stages, clarifying the relationships between capability formation, authority binding, failure modes, and oversight mechanisms. They identify recurring points of intervention for control, monitoring, and assurance, and distinguish visibility of failures (where they manifest) from their root enabling conditions (where they are introduced). OpenClaw is discussed as a public motivating example of an open deployment pattern, but not evaluated as a full internal case study.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that deployment-grounded reliability for CUAs requires integrated consideration of architecture (perception–decision–execution) and lifecycle (creation–deployment–operation–maintenance). It highlights several open challenges: establishing robust and controllable grounding of CUAs in real software environments, preserving constraints over long horizons, safely binding and managing authorities and permissions, defending at runtime in mixed-trust settings, protecting privacy in memory and logging, and maintaining continual assurance under system and environment drift.

Thearchitectural view analyzes Perception, Decision, and Executionas coupled layers that transform software observations intoauthority-bearing actions, The lifecycle view examines Creation.Deployment, Operation, and Maintenance as stages in which priorsare learned, tools and permissions are bound, runtime trajecto.ries are stressed, and assurance must be preserved under drift.Using this lens, the analysis synthesizes representative systems,benchmarks, and security/privacy studies; distinguishes wherefailures become visible from where their enabling conditions areintroduced, and maps recurring intervention surfaces for controloversight, and assurance. OpenClaw is used only as a public moti.vating example of an open deployment pattern, not as a verifedinternal case study. ### [21] [Beyond LoRA vs. Full Fine-Tuning: Gradient-Guided Optimizer Routing for LLM Adaptation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07111)
*Haozhan Tang,Xiuqi Zhu,Xinyin Zhang,Boxun Li,Virginia Smith,Kevin Kuo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies when to use full fine-tuning versus LoRA for LLMs and proposes a unified method (MoLF) that mixes both to get consistently strong performance across tasks and models, plus an efficient variant that only uses LoRA experts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a practical and conceptual tension in fine-tuning LLMs: full fine-tuning offers maximum flexibility but is expensive and prone to overfitting, while LoRA is parameter-efficient and often competitive or better because many tasks lie in a low-rank subspace. However, always choosing one static strategy (all FFT or all LoRA) is limiting, since different tasks and model regions may benefit from different levels of adaptation. The authors aim to design a single framework that can smoothly interpolate between FFT and LoRA, adapt to task needs during training, and still work under memory constraints.

Method: The authors empirically compare FFT and standard LoRA on several diverse tasks (SQL generation, medical question answering, and counterfactual knowledge injection) across three smaller LLMs (Gemma-3-1B, Qwen2.5-1.5B, Qwen2.5-3B) to expose when each method excels. Based on this, they propose MoLF (Mixture of LoRA and Full Fine-Tuning), which treats FFT and LoRA as two experts. At the optimizer level, MoLF dynamically routes gradient updates between full-weight updates and LoRA updates so both receive precise gradient information and the model can continuously shift between the regimes during training. For memory-limited settings, they introduce MoLF-Efficient, which keeps the base model weights frozen but allows routing between two LoRA experts of potentially different ranks to approximate the flexibility of FFT+LoRA while remaining lightweight.

Result: Across all evaluated settings, MoLF either outperforms or stays within 1.5% of whichever of FFT or LoRA alone is better, indicating that it robustly tracks the stronger method without paying a large performance penalty. MoLF-Efficient, despite freezing the base model, substantially outperforms previous adaptive LoRA techniques, with gains up to 20% on factual tasks and 9% on medical QA and SQL benchmarks.

Conclusion: The work concludes that neither pure full fine-tuning nor pure LoRA is universally optimal, and that a dynamic combination of both offers a better trade-off between flexibility, performance, and efficiency. MoLF provides a general mechanism to navigate between FFT and LoRA during training by routing gradients at the optimizer level, achieving near-best-of-both-worlds performance. The lighter MoLF-Efficient variant shows that even in tight memory budgets, adaptive mixing of different LoRA configurations can significantly outperform existing adaptive LoRA baselines.

Abstract: Recent literature on fine-tuning Large Language Models highlights a fundamental debate. While Full Fine-Tuning (FFT) provides the representational plasticity required for high-entropy knowledge injection, Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) can match or surpass FFT performance because many tasks only require updates in a low-rank space and benefit from LoRA's additional regularization. Through empirical evaluation across diverse tasks (SQL, Medical QA, and Counterfactual Knowledge) and varying language models (Gemma-3-1B, Qwen2.5-1.5B, and Qwen2.5-3B), we verify both trends and demonstrate that relying solely on either static architecture is structurally limited. To address this challenge, we propose a Mixture of LoRA and Full (MoLF) Fine-Tuning, a unified framework that enables continuous navigation between both training regimes. MoLF dynamically routes updates between FFT and LoRA at the optimizer level to ensure that exact gradient signals are available to both experts throughout training, yielding stable training dynamics. For memory-constrained environments, we also introduce MoLF-Efficient, which freezes base weights and only routes updates among a pair of LoRA experts of potentially varying rank. Our evaluations show that MoLF either improves on or stays within $1.5\%$ of the better of FFT and LoRA across all settings, while MoLF-Efficient outperforms prior adaptive LoRA approaches by up to $20\%$ on Fact and $9\%$ on Med and SQL.

</details>


### [22] [Region4Web: Rethinking Observation Space Granularity for Web Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07134)
*Donguk Kwon,Dongha Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Region4Web and PageDigest, two components that let web agents observe web pages at the level of functional regions rather than individual elements, yielding shorter observations and better task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing web agents observe pages at the same fine-grained, element-level as their action space. This forces agents to repeatedly infer high-level page structure from low-level element signals, which is inefficient and may hinder understanding. The authors want to make the functional organization of a page explicit in the observation space to improve efficiency and performance.

Method: They define web-page observation at the granularity of functional regions—coherent parts of a page that each serve a distinct purpose—rather than individual elements. They propose Region4Web, which reorganizes the accessibility tree (AXTree) of a web page into functional regions using hierarchical decomposition and semantic abstraction. They also propose PageDigest, a web-specific inference pipeline that converts this region-level representation into a compact, persistent per-page digest that is fed to the actor agent across interaction steps.

Result: On the WebArena benchmark, using PageDigest significantly reduces the length of observations provided to the agent while simultaneously improving task success rates. This improvement holds across multiple backbone LLMs with different capacities and across established agent architectures, indicating robustness and generality of the approach.

Conclusion: Structuring web-agent observations around functional regions instead of raw elements yields a more compact and informative representation of web pages. This region-level observation scheme improves web-agent performance while reducing observation size, suggesting that explicit functional organization is a better basis for page-state understanding than element-level processing alone.

Abstract: Web agents perceive web pages through an observation space, yet its granularity has remained an underexamined design choice. Existing work treats observation at the same element-level granularity as the action space, leaving the page's functional organization implicit and forcing the agent to infer it from element-level signals at every step. We argue observation should instead operate at the granularity of functional regions, parts of the page that each serve a distinct purpose. We propose Region4Web, a framework that reorganizes the AXTree into functional regions through hierarchical decomposition and semantic abstraction, exposing the page's functional organization as the basis for page state understanding. Moreover, we propose PageDigest, a web-specific inference pipeline that delivers this region-level observation to the actor agent as a compact per-page digest that persists across steps. On the WebArena benchmark, PageDigest substantially reduces observation length while improving overall task success rate across diverse backbone large language models (LLMs) and established agent methods, regardless of backbone capacity. These results show that operating at the granularity of functional regions delivers a more compact and informative basis for the actor agent than element-level processing alone.

### [23] [Structural Rationale Distillation via Reasoning Space Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07139)
*Jialin Yang,Jiankun Wang,Jiajun Wu,Henry Leung,Jiayu Zhou,Steve Drew*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces D-RPC, a method that improves distillation of reasoning from large to small language models by compressing and reusing high-level reasoning paths, yielding more consistent and efficient supervision and better performance than existing distillation approaches.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In distilling reasoning abilities from large language models (teachers) into smaller ones (students), existing methods rely on teacher-generated chain-of-thought or freeform rationales. For similar problems, these rationales can differ greatly in structure, style, and strategy, creating noisy and inconsistent supervision. This variability makes it hard for the student model to internalize stable reasoning patterns and limits the efficiency of distillation, especially given token costs. The authors aim to reduce this supervision noise and improve generalization while keeping token usage low.

Method: The authors propose Distillation through Reasoning Path Compression (D-RPC). They maintain a compact, dynamically updated bank of reusable high-level reasoning paths (abstract templates or strategies). For each training question, D-RPC retrieves the most relevant reasoning path from this bank and conditions the teacher LLM to follow that path when generating its rationale. This enforces consistency across similar problems while retaining diversity through multiple paths for different problem types. The method includes a retrieval mechanism for selecting paths and a procedure for updating and maintaining the path bank. They further conduct a PAC-Bayes analysis to study the trade-off between the size of the reasoning-path bank and its coverage of problem types, deriving a generalization bound that suggests an optimal intermediate bank size.

Result: Experimentally, D-RPC is evaluated on five math and commonsense reasoning benchmarks using two different student models. Compared to baselines—chain-of-thought distillation, unconstrained/freeform rationale generation, direct answer distillation without rationales, and structured-supervision/template-based methods—D-RPC consistently yields higher student performance. It also uses fewer tokens than template-heavy structured methods due to the compact high-level paths. Ablation studies show that bank size critically affects performance, and the optimal size predicted by the PAC-Bayes analysis matches empirical findings.

Conclusion: Constraining teacher LLMs to generate rationales via a compressed bank of reusable reasoning paths can significantly improve the distillation of reasoning capabilities into smaller models. D-RPC reduces supervision entropy (noise), achieves better generalization and benchmark performance than standard chain-of-thought and other distillation baselines, and does so with improved token efficiency. The theoretical PAC-Bayes analysis not only explains the observed trade-off between bank size and coverage but also guides practical choices for configuring the reasoning-path bank.

Abstract: When distilling reasoning from large language models (LLMs) into smaller ones, teacher rationales for similar problems often vary wildly in structure and strategy. Like a chef who makes the same dish differently each time, this inconsistency burdens the student with noisy supervision that is hard to internalize. We propose Distillation through Reasoning Path Compression (D-RPC), which constrains the teacher to follow a compact, dynamically maintained bank of reusable high-level reasoning paths. For each training question, D-RPC retrieves the most relevant path and conditions the teacher to follow it, producing rationales that are consistent across similar problems yet diverse enough to cover different problem types. A PAC-Bayes analysis formalizes the resulting trade-off between bank size and coverage: smaller banks reduce supervision entropy but risk coverage gaps, and the generalization bound identifies an optimal intermediate size confirmed by our ablations. Across five math and commonsense reasoning benchmarks with two student models, D-RPC consistently outperforms chain-of-thought distillation, freeform rationale generation, direct distillation, and structured-supervision baselines, while using fewer tokens than template-heavy alternatives.

</details>


### [24] [Beyond Reasoning: Reinforcement Learning Unlocks Parametric Knowledge in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07153)
*Wanli Yang,Hongyu Zang,Junwei Zhang,Wenjie Shi,Du Su,Jingang Wang,Xueqi Cheng,Fei Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether reinforcement learning (RL) can improve large language models’ direct factual recall (parametric knowledge) in a tightly controlled QA setup, and finds substantial gains driven by reweighting existing knowledge rather than learning new facts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While RL has recently excelled at improving LLM reasoning (e.g., chain-of-thought, planning), it is unclear if RL also helps with simple factual recall of knowledge already stored in model parameters. Prior work often mixes reasoning, memorization, prompt engineering, or data leakage, making it difficult to isolate the effect of RL on pure recall. Understanding whether RL can unlock latent knowledge without additional supervision or data is important for more efficient LLM training and deployment.

Method: The authors use a controlled closed-book, zero-shot, one-hop QA setting without chain-of-thought, where the model answers factual questions directly. They train with only binary correctness rewards (right/wrong) and ensure fact-level train-test deduplication to avoid contamination. RL is applied to multiple model families across various factual QA benchmarks. They compare RL to standard training and inference-time baselines, and perform mechanistic and data-attribution analyses: examining how probability mass over answers shifts and which training examples contribute most to performance gains, including tracking questions whose correct answers are extremely low probability in pre-RL samples.

Result: Across three model families and several factual QA datasets, RL produces about 27% average relative performance gains on factual QA, outperforming both standard supervised training baselines and inference-time methods. Analysis shows that RL mainly increases the probability of correct answers that the model already “knows” at low probability, rather than introducing new facts. Correct answers move from the low-probability tail into consistently generated greedy outputs. Data attribution indicates that examples whose answers never appear in 128 pre-RL samples (only ~18% of the training set) account for ~83% of the overall gain, because occasional rare correct rollouts during RL training are strongly reinforced.

Conclusion: RL can substantially enhance direct factual recall in LLMs, even without chain-of-thought or explicit reasoning supervision. Its main role in this setting is to unlock and reweight latent parametric knowledge instead of acquiring new facts, by reinforcing rare correct generations on hard questions. This broadens the perceived utility of RL for LLMs from primarily improving reasoning to also serving as an effective mechanism for turning implicit, low-probability knowledge into reliably accessible answers.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning (RL) has achieved remarkable success in LLM reasoning, but whether it can also improve direct recall of parametric knowledge remains an open question. We study this question in a controlled zero-shot, one-hop, closed-book QA setting with no chain-of-thought, training only on binary correctness rewards and applying fact-level train-test deduplication to ensure gains reflect improved recall rather than reasoning or memorization. Across three model families and multiple factual QA benchmarks, RL yields ~27% average relative gains, surpassing both training- and inference-time baselines alike. Mechanistically, RL primarily redistributes probability mass over existing knowledge rather than acquiring new facts, moving correct answers from the low-probability tail into reliable greedy generations. Our data-attribution study reveals that the hardest examples are the most informative: those whose answers never appear in 128 pre-RL samples (only ~18% of training data) drive ~83% of the gain, since rare correct rollouts still emerge during training and get reinforced. Together, these findings broaden the role of RL beyond reasoning, repositioning it as a tool for unlocking rather than acquiring latent parametric knowledge.

In this paper, we introduce \textbf{CLIPer}(\textbf{Cl}assifier-guided \textbf{I}nference-time \textbf{Per}sonalization), a lightweight personalization approach that leverages a classifier model to steer LLM generation dynamically to different user preferences at inference time. Our method eliminates the need for extensive fine-tuning, inducing negligible additional computational overhead while enabling more controllable and nuanced personalization across single and multi-dimensional preferences. Comprehensive empirical analyses demonstrate the scalability and effectiveness of our approach in delivering personalized language generation.

### [26] [Rethinking Experience Utilization in Self-Evolving Language Model Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07164)
*Weixiang Zhao,Yingshuo Wang,Yichen Zhang,Yanyan Zhao,Yu Zhang,Yang Wu,Dandan Tu,Bing Qin,Ting Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes ExpWeaver, a method for using past experience in self-evolving LLM agents only when needed during reasoning, leading to better performance than always-on or one-shot experience injection.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most self-evolving agents focus on how to build and store experience but use rigid strategies for when to apply that experience at runtime, typically always or only at initialization. The authors suspect that more adaptive, selective use of experience during decision-making could yield better performance and more efficient reasoning.

Method: They introduce ExpWeaver, which keeps existing experience construction mechanisms intact but changes runtime utilization: experience is exposed as an optional resource that the agent can invoke during reasoning when additional guidance seems useful. They test ExpWeaver across four different agent frameworks, seven LLM backbones, and three environment types. They also perform reinforcement learning to encourage beneficial usage patterns and conduct analyses of usage patterns, causal ablations, and entropy to understand when and why experience is invoked.

Result: ExpWeaver consistently outperforms other experience utilization strategies in all tested settings. RL training amplifies the desirable selective usage behavior. Analyses show that the agent tends to call on experience at key decision points and especially when its reasoning uncertainty (entropy) is higher, and that these calls causally contribute to improved outcomes.

Conclusion: Adaptive, selective utilization of stored experience during decision-making is a crucial but underexplored design dimension for self-evolving agents. Rather than only optimizing what experience to store, researchers should also focus on how and when that experience is integrated into the reasoning process. ExpWeaver demonstrates that exposing experience as an optional, on-demand resource can significantly improve agent performance.

Abstract: Self-evolving agents improve by accumulating and reusing experience from past interactions. Existing work has largely focused on how experience is constructed, represented, and updated, while paying less attention to how experience should be used during runtime decision-making. As a result, most agents rely on rigid usage strategies, either injecting experience once at initialization or at every step, without considering whether it is needed for the current decision. This paper studies experience utilization as a critical design dimension of self-evolving agents. We ask whether agents benefit from interweaving experience use with decision-making, so that experience is invoked only when additional guidance is needed. To examine this question, we introduce {ExpWeaver}, a lightweight instantiation that leaves experience construction unchanged and modifies only runtime utilization by exposing experience as an optional resource during reasoning. Across four representative frameworks, seven LLM backbones, and three types of environments, ExpWeaver consistently achieves the best performance among different utilization strategies. Reinforcement learning experiments further show that this behavior can be amplified through training. Usage-pattern, causal ablation, and entropy-based analyses reveal that ExpWeaver enables agents to invoke experience selectively, at beneficial decision points, and under higher reasoning uncertainty. Overall, our findings call for a shift from merely studying \emph{what} experience to store toward understanding \emph{how} and \emph{when} experience should enter decision-making.

### [27] [A Reproducible Multi-Architecture Baseline for Token-Level Chinese Metaphor Identification under the MIPVU Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07170)
*Yufeng Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper builds and compares strong neural baselines for word-level metaphor detection in Chinese using the PSU Chinese Metaphor Corpus, finding a Chinese MelBERT variant works best and releasing code/resources for reproducible research.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While metaphor detection has advanced in English, Chinese remains under-studied, especially under the MIPVU annotation framework at token level. There is only one widely available annotated Chinese corpus (PSU CMC) and no clear, reproducible baselines or tailored architectures leveraging Chinese-specific resources. The authors aim to fill this gap and provide a standard reference point for future work.

Method: They use the PSU Chinese Metaphor Corpus and compare three model families: (i) fine-tuning a strong Chinese encoder model, RoBERTa-wwm-ext-large; (ii) adapting MelBERT—a metaphor-specific architecture—to Chinese by constructing a large basic-meaning resource from the Modern Chinese Dictionary (MCD7) and integrating it into the model; and (iii) fine-tuning a 9B-parameter Qwen3.5 model with QLoRA as an instruction-tuned generative system for token-level labeling under various prompt formats. They train and evaluate all models across five random seeds and analyze performance differences, including component-wise ablations within MelBERT and different task formulations for Qwen.

Result: MelBERT configured to use only the MIP (basic vs contextual meaning) channel yields the best test positive F1 (0.7281 ± 0.0050), slightly outperforming full MelBERT and clearly outperforming plain RoBERTa encoders (0.7142 ± 0.0121). The Qwen3.5-9B generative baseline, fine-tuned with QLoRA, lags behind encoder models by about 11 F1 points (0.6157 ± 0.0113), especially in recall. The SPV channel in MelBERT contributes little useful signal in Chinese; Qwen’s underperformance is tied to discrete output decisions and some poor task/prompt designs rather than raw model capacity. They also report coverage statistics for the new MCD7-based resource (about 71.5% of PSU CMC vocabulary).

Conclusion: A Chinese adaptation of MelBERT that focuses on MIP-style basic-vs-contextual meaning is currently the strongest baseline for token-level metaphor detection on PSU CMC, while additional SPV-style signals bring limited gains in Chinese. Large instruction-tuned generative LMs like Qwen3.5 can be fine-tuned for this task but currently trail well-optimized encoder models, largely due to recall and prompt-format issues. The released resources—dictionary-based basic-meaning embeddings, data splits, per-seed outputs, and training scripts—provide a reproducible, shared benchmark to drive future research in Chinese metaphor identification.

Abstract: Metaphor is pervasive in everyday language, yet token-level computational identification of metaphor-related words in Chinese under the MIPVU framework remains under-explored relative to English. This paper presents a reproducible multi-architecture baseline for token-level metaphor identification on the PSU Chinese Metaphor Corpus (PSU CMC), the only widely available MIPVU-annotated Chinese corpus. We systematically compare three model families: (i) encoder fine-tuning with Chinese RoBERTa-wwm-ext-large; (ii) MelBERT adapted to Chinese using a newly constructed basic-meaning resource derived from the Modern Chinese Dictionary, 7th edition (MCD7), comprising 74,823 entries with 71.51% PSU CMC vocabulary coverage; and (iii) Qwen3.5-9B fine-tuned with QLoRA as an instruction-tuned generative baseline. Across five fixed seeds, MelBERT MIP-only achieves the strongest performance at 0.7281 +/- 0.0050 test positive F1, marginally above MelBERT Full (0.7270 +/- 0.0069) and clearly above plain RoBERTa (0.7142 +/- 0.0121). The Qwen QLoRA generative configuration trails encoder baselines by approximately 11 F1 points (0.6157 +/- 0.0113). Three findings merit attention: (1) the SPV channel of MelBERT does not contribute reliable positive signal in Chinese, consistent with the dominance of conventional metaphor; (2) the Qwen-encoder gap is concentrated in recall, reflecting the discrete-commitment limitation of generative output; (3) several Qwen task formulations fail due to format design rather than model capacity. We release all split manifests, per-seed outputs, the MCD7 basic-meaning embedding pipeline, and training scripts to serve as a common reference for future Chinese metaphor identification research.

</details>


### [28] [Topology-Enhanced Alignment for Large Language Models: Trajectory Topology Loss and Topological Preference Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07172)
*Yurui Pan,Ke Xu,Bo Peng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce topology-aware alignment methods for LLMs that regularize hidden-state trajectories using 0D persistent homology, improving SFT and DPO-style training over standard regularizers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing SFT and RLHF/DPO alignment methods focus on local token-level likelihoods or scalar rewards and ignore the global geometry of hidden representations. The authors argue that generation is a trajectory in semantic space and that leveraging topological structure (via persistent homology) could yield more globally consistent and controllable alignment, beyond ad‑hoc regularizers.

Method: 1) For supervised fine-tuning, they propose Trajectory Topology Loss (TTL). They treat embeddings from prompts and gold answers as points in a shared hidden space, run a 0D persistent homology algorithm on this mixed point cloud to identify “prompt-answer bridges” (important connectivity components), and then train the model so that its actual update direction during generation follows these bridges rather than arbitrary directions.
2) For preference-based alignment, they introduce Topological Preference Optimization (TPO). For each topic, they build semantic preference vectors in an intermediate hidden layer that capture the direction from rejected to chosen responses. They then constrain the model so that, during DPO-style training, the improvement direction between rejected and preferred responses aligns with these topic-specific preference vectors. A dynamic weighting schedule balances the standard DPO loss and the new TPO loss.
3) They compare against non-topological regularizers such as per-example, nearest-neighbor, and random trajectory constraints.

Result: On Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct trained with UltraChat and Anthropic HH-RLHF datasets, both TTL (for SFT) and TPO (for DPO) yield consistent gains over strong baselines on automatic preference metrics and evaluations by LLM judges. They also report that these topology-based objectives do not worsen, and sometimes improve, toxicity metrics. These results indicate the added topological constraints lead to more aligned behavior than comparable non-topological regularization schemes.

Conclusion: Incorporating persistent homology and trajectory geometry into alignment offers a promising path for more globally coherent and controllable LLM behavior. By shaping hidden-state trajectories according to topological structures—prompt-answer bridges for SFT and topic-specific preference vectors for DPO—the proposed TTL and TPO methods outperform standard regularizers while maintaining safety, suggesting that topological information is a useful, previously underexploited signal for LLM alignment.

Abstract: Alignment of large language models (LLMs) via SFT and RLHF/DPO typically ignores the global geometry of the representation space, relying instead on local token likelihoods or scalar scores. We view generation as tracing a semantic trajectory in hidden space and propose a topology-enhanced alignment framework that regularizes these trajectories using 0-dimensional persistent homology. First, for SFT, we introduce Trajectory Topology Loss (TTL). Treating prompt and gold-answer embeddings as a mixed point cloud, we use a 0D persistent homology algorithm to extract "prompt-answer bridges." TTL aligns the model's actual update direction with these topological bridges rather than arbitrary directions. Second, for DPO, we propose Topological Preference Optimization (TPO). TPO constructs topic-specific semantic preference vectors and aligns the improvement direction between rejected and chosen responses with these vectors in an intermediate hidden layer. We also introduce a dynamic weighting scheme to balance DPO and TPO losses. Evaluating on Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct using UltraChat and Anthropic HH-RLHF, our topology-enhanced objectives consistently outperform strong non-topological baselines (e.g., per-example, nearest-neighbor, random regularizers) on automatic preference metrics and LLM-judge evaluations, while maintaining or improving toxicity. Results show persistent homology and trajectory geometry offer a promising direction for controllable alignment.

*Yimin Wang,Jiahao Qiu,Xuan Qi,Xinzhe Juan,Jingzhe Shi,Zelin Zhao,Hongru Wang,Shilong Liu,Mengdi Wang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Full LLM agents are powerful but slow and expensive, while many real-world queries are easy enough for a single LLM call. There is a need for an effective router that can decide when simple inference suffices and when the heavier agent is needed, especially in cold-start scenarios with limited data.

Method: BoundaryRouter is a training-free routing framework. It first runs both a lightweight LLM and a full agent on a shared seed set to build a compact experience memory. At inference, it retrieves similar past cases and uses rubric-guided reasoning over these examples to decide whether to answer via direct LLM inference or escalate to the agent. No additional training is required; it relies on behavioral experience and prompt-based reasoning.

Result: On the newly introduced RouteBench benchmark, which includes in-domain, paraphrased, and out-of-domain routing scenarios, BoundaryRouter reduces inference time by 60.6% relative to always using the agent, while increasing performance by 28.6% over always using direct LLM inference. It also outperforms prompt-only and retrieval-only routing baselines by 37.9% and 8.2% on average, respectively.

Conclusion: A training-free, experience- and rubric-guided router can effectively decide between lightweight LLM inference and full agent execution, yielding substantial latency savings and quality gains. BoundaryRouter provides a practical solution for cold-start routing and a strong baseline on the proposed RouteBench benchmark.

Abstract: LLM agents achieve strong performance on complex reasoning tasks but incur high latency and compute cost. In practice, many queries fall within the capability boundary of cutting-edge LLMs and do not require full agent execution, making effective routing between LLMs and agents a key challenge. We study the problem of routing queries between lightweight LLM inference and full agent execution under realistic cold-start settings. To address this, we propose BoundaryRouter, a training-free routing framework that uses early behavioral experience and rubric-guided reasoning to decide whether to answer a query with direct LLM inference or escalate to an agent. BoundaryRouter builds a compact experience memory by executing both systems on a shared seed set and retrieves similar cases at inference time to guide routing decisions. To evaluate this method, we introduce RouteBench, a benchmark covering in-domain, paraphrased, and out-of-domain route settings. Experiments show that BoundaryRouter reduces inference time by 60.6% compared to the agent while improving performance by 28.6% over direct LLM inference, outperforming prompt-based and retrieval-only routing by an average of 37.9% and 8.2%, respectively.

### [30] [The Text Uncanny Valley: Non-Monotonic Performance Degradation in LLM Information Retrieval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07186)
*Zekai Tong,Ruiyao Xu,Aryan Shrivastava,Chenhao Tan,Ari Holtzman*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that large language models have a U-shaped drop-and-recovery in detection accuracy when text is progressively broken by inserting spaces inside words, revealing a hidden failure regime on noisy inputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most LLM benchmarks assume clean, well-formed text, but real-world inputs are often noisy, misspelled, or oddly spaced. The authors want to understand how such word-boundary corruption impacts an LLM’s ability to detect specific targeted information, and to uncover systematic failure modes that current benchmarks miss.

Method: They corrupt text by inserting whitespace within words at varying rates, fragmenting words into pieces. They evaluate LLMs’ ability to detect targeted information under these perturbations and plot performance as a function of insertion rate. They then test a hypothesized mode transition—between word-level and character-level processing—through four experiments: (1) using in-context learning examples to see if performance can be restored in the worst region; (2) regularizing the perturbation pattern to see whether enforcing structure lessens the U-shape; (3) replicating the phenomenon on a math reasoning task across models of different strength; and (4) analyzing tokenization entropy relative to performance minima to probe underlying tokenization dynamics.

Result: Performance exhibits a U-shaped curve (“Text Uncanny Valley”) as insertion rate increases: accuracy initially drops, reaches a valley, then recovers at high fragmentation. In-context learning does not fix performance at the valley bottom. Making perturbations more regular significantly flattens or reduces the U-shape. For a math reasoning task, the U-shape appears for Gemini 3.0 Flash but not for stronger models, suggesting tasks less dependent on exact lexical form or models with more robust reasoning show a weaker effect. Tokenization entropy peaks before F1 reaches its minimum, aligning with the idea of a conflicted regime where word-level and character-level processing are both ineffective.

Conclusion: LLMs exhibit a specific, previously unrecognized failure mode when word boundaries are corrupted: a Text Uncanny Valley where models transition ineffectively between word-level and character-level processing. This cannot be easily fixed via in-context examples and is mitigated by more structured perturbations or by tasks/models less tied to lexical alignment. The work highlights that clean-text benchmarks overlook critical vulnerabilities in realistic, noisy-input scenarios and suggests future evaluation and model design must account for such regime transitions.

Abstract: Existing Large Language Model (LLM) benchmarks primarily focus on syntactically correct inputs, leaving a significant gap in evaluation on imperfect text. In this work, we study how word-boundary corruption affects how LLMs detect targeted information. By inserting whitespace characters within words to break them into fragments, LLMs' detection accuracy follows a U-shaped curve with the increase in insertion rate. We refer to this curve as the Text Uncanny Valley. To explain such observation, we propose a mode transition hypothesis: LLMs operate in a word-level mode for near-normal text and a character-level mode for heavily fragmented text, with the valley marking the disordered transition where neither mode is effective. Four experiments and one analysis are consistent with this account: in-context learning fails to rescue valley-bottom performance; regularizing the perturbation substantially reduces the U-shape; a math reasoning task replicates the U-shape for Gemini 3.0 Flash but not for stronger models, suggesting the effect is attenuated when tasks rely less on exact lexical alignment; and tokenization entropy peaks before the F1 minimum, consistent with a regime-conflict interpretation. These findings reveal a failure mode invisible to clean-text benchmarks yet directly relevant to any deployment scenario involving noisy or uncurated text inputs.

### [32] [Hallucination Detection via Activations of Open-Weight Proxy Analyzers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07209)
*Akshita Singh,Prabesh Paudel,Siddhartha Roy*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a small “reader” model that analyzes generated text to detect hallucinations, achieving strong, size-robust performance across multiple architectures and outperforming prior work on key benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hallucinations in large language models are a major safety and reliability issue, especially for closed-source generators (e.g., API models) where internal signals are inaccessible. Existing detection methods often depend on access to the generating model’s internals or do not generalize well across model families and sizes. The authors aim to build a detector that (1) works for any generator, open or closed, (2) leverages transformer internal mechanisms in a principled way, and (3) remains accurate even with relatively small models.

Method: They introduce a proxy-analyzer framework: a separate, small, locally hosted open-weight model reads the already-generated text (and source documents) and uses its own internal activations to detect hallucinations at the token level. They engineer 18 features inspired by transformer computation, including residual stream norms, per-head attention to the source document, token-level entropy, MLP activation patterns, logit-lens trajectories, and three new grounding statistics that measure how well tokens are supported by the source. A stacking ensemble is then trained on these features using 72,135 labeled examples from five hallucination datasets. They evaluate seven different analyzer architectures, ranging from 0.5B to 9B parameters (Qwen2.5, Gemma-2, Pythia, LLaMA-3 variants), to test robustness to model choice and size.

Result: Across all seven analyzer models, they consistently outperform ReDeEP’s token-level AUC of 0.73 on the RAGTruth benchmark by 7.4–10.3 percentage points. Qwen2.5-7B attains an F1 score of 0.717, slightly surpassing ReDeEP’s 0.713, while even the smallest Qwen2.5-0.5B achieves 0.706 F1. Performance across analyzers clusters tightly: token-level AUC varies by only 2.3 percentage points despite an 18× spread in parameter counts. A 3B-parameter LLaMA analyzer even outperforms its 8B counterpart on RAGTruth. Their evaluation on RAGTruth and LLM-AggreFact—both containing outputs from multiple generator families—indicates that the detector generalizes across different underlying LLMs and is not tuned to a specific generator.

Conclusion: A small, separate proxy model can reliably detect hallucinations in LLM outputs by exploiting its own internal transformer features, without needing access to the generating model’s internals. The proposed 18-feature framework and ensemble classifier generalize well across analyzer architectures and sizes, outperform prior state-of-the-art baselines like ReDeEP, and show that larger analyzers are not necessarily better. This supports the viability of lightweight, model-agnostic hallucination detectors that can be deployed locally alongside any text generator, including closed-source APIs.

Abstract: We introduce a proxy-analyzer framework for detecting hallucinations in large language models. Instead of looking inside the generating model, our system reads already-generated text through a small locally hosted open-weight model and spots hallucinations using the reader's own internal activations. This works just as well when the generator is a closed API like GPT-4 as when it is any open-weight model. We built eighteen features grounded in how transformers process text, covering residual stream norms, per-head source-document attention, entropy, MLP activations, logit-lens trajectories, and three new token-level grounding statistics. We trained a stacking ensemble on 72,135 samples from five hallucination datasets. We tested across seven analyzer architectures from 0.5 billion to 9 billion parameters: Qwen2.5 at 0.5B and 7B, Gemma-2 at 2B and 9B, Pythia at 1.4B, and LLaMA-3 at both 3B and 8B. Across all seven, we consistently beat ReDeEP's token-level AUC of 0.73 on RAGTruth by 7.4 to 10.3 percentage points. Qwen2.5-7B reached an F1 of 0.717, just above ReDeEP's 0.713, while Qwen2.5-0.5B hit 0.706. The most striking finding is how tightly all seven models cluster: AUC spans only 2.3 percentage points across an eighteen-fold difference in model size. Even more surprising, our 3B LLaMA outperforms our 8B LLaMA on RAGTruth, showing that bigger is not always better even within the same model family. Both RAGTruth and LLM-AggreFact include outputs from multiple LLM families, so our results are not skewed toward any particular generator.

### [33] [Reformulating KV Cache Eviction Problem for Long-Context LLM Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07234)
*Tho Mai,Joo-Young Kim*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes LaProx, an output-aware, layer-wise KV cache eviction strategy that approximates attention output to assign globally comparable token importance scores, enabling aggressive KV cache compression (down to 5%) with minimal performance loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context inference in LLMs requires storing large Key-Value (KV) caches for attention, causing substantial memory and runtime overhead. Existing eviction methods mainly use local attention weights and treat heads independently, ignoring value vectors, output projections, and inter-head interactions, which leads to suboptimal token removal decisions. The authors aim to design a more principled, output-aware method that can make globally consistent eviction decisions to substantially reduce KV cache usage while preserving model quality.

Method: They reformulate KV cache eviction as an approximation problem for the layer output, rather than a head-wise attention-weight heuristic. They explicitly model the matrix multiplication between attention maps and projected value states, capturing both value representations and inter-head dependencies. Based on this, they define a token contribution metric that reflects each token's impact on the final layer output. Using these globally comparable importance scores, they propose LaProx, a unified, layer-wise eviction strategy that selects which tokens to retain or discard across all heads and positions to best approximate the original output under a cache budget.

Result: On 19 datasets from LongBench and Needle-In-A-Haystack benchmarks, LaProx maintains strong model performance while keeping only 5% of the KV cache, significantly reducing memory and runtime overhead. It consistently outperforms prior KV eviction baselines under various configurations. In extreme compression regimes, LaProx achieves up to 2x reduction in accuracy loss compared with state-of-the-art competitors, and it does so with minimal additional computational overhead.

Conclusion: Output-aware, layer-wise modeling of the interaction between attention maps and projected values enables more accurate estimation of token importance than local, head-wise attention-based heuristics. The proposed LaProx strategy provides a unified way to assign globally comparable importance scores and select tokens model-wide, enabling substantial KV cache compression with minimal degradation in performance. This shows that principled approximation of attention outputs is an effective direction for efficient long-context LLM inference.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) support long-context inference but suffer from substantial memory and runtime overhead due to Key-Value (KV) Cache growth. Existing KV Cache eviction methods primarily rely on local attention weights, neglecting the influence of value representations, output projection, and inter-head interactions. In this work, we reformulate KV Cache eviction from a conventional head-wise, weight-averaging approach into an output-aware, layer-wise matrix multiplication approximation problem. We introduce LaProx, a novel eviction strategy that explicitly models the multiplicative interaction between attention maps and projected value states to accurately quantify token contributions while accounting for inter-head dependencies. Building on this metric, we propose the first unified eviction strategy that assigns globally comparable importance scores to tokens, enabling model-wide selection instead of local, head-wise decisions. Experimental results across 19 datasets on long-context benchmarks LongBench and Needle-In-A-Haystack demonstrate that our approach maintains model performance with only 5\% of the KV cache and consistently outperforms prior works across all configurations. Notably, our method achieves up to 2$\times$ accuracy loss reduction under extreme compression scenarios compared to existing state-of-the-art baselines with minimal overhead.

### [34] [Teaching Language Models to Think in Code](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07237)
*Hyeon Hwang,Jiwoo Lee,Jaewoo Kang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ThinC, a framework where code—not natural language—does almost all the mathematical reasoning, leading to better performance than existing tool-integrated reasoning systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing tool-integrated reasoning (TIR) for math uses natural language as the main reasoning medium and code as an auxiliary tool, typically for verification. This setup has issues: natural language intermediate steps are error-prone, code is used only after-the-fact, and the division of labor between NL and code is blurry. The authors want a system where reasoning is more reliable, more structured, and more clearly grounded in executable computations.

Method: They propose ThinC (Thinking in Code), where the model performs only a short initial natural-language planning step and then conducts essentially all subsequent reasoning via code blocks. These code blocks are chained only through their execution outputs, so the reasoning state is carried by program state rather than NL narration. To realize this, they distill 12.2k code-centric reasoning trajectories from a strong teacher model. They then train two models, ThinC-1.7B and ThinC-4B, first with supervised fine-tuning on these trajectories and then with reinforcement learning to further refine reasoning behavior. They evaluate on several competition-level math benchmarks and analyze how much the final answers are grounded in interpreter outputs and how the model behaves when code execution fails.

Result: ThinC-4B consistently outperforms all baselines that use traditional tool-integrated reasoning across five challenging math benchmarks, and it even surpasses a much larger model, Qwen3-235B-A22B-Thinking. Empirically, 99.2% of ThinC’s final answers are directly grounded in code execution results, demonstrating that the model truly relies on code as the main reasoning medium. The system also shows robust behavior in the face of code execution failures, recovering without reverting to intermediate natural-language reasoning.

Conclusion: Using code as the primary reasoning substrate, with only minimal initial natural-language planning, yields more accurate and reliable mathematical problem-solving than standard TIR approaches. ThinC demonstrates that small models trained on code-centric trajectories can outperform larger models that rely on interleaved NL and tool usage. The strong grounding of answers in interpreter outputs and the ability to recover from execution errors suggest that code-centric reasoning is a promising direction for future reasoning-oriented language models.

Abstract: Tool-integrated reasoning (TIR) has emerged as a dominant paradigm for mathematical problem solving in language models, combining natural language (NL) reasoning with code execution. However, this interleaved setup has three key limitations: code often acts as a post-hoc verifier, intermediate NL computations are error-prone, and NL and code play overlapping rather than clearly distinct roles. We propose ThinC (Thinking in Code), a framework in which code itself serves as the reasoner rather than as a tool invoked by NL. A ThinC trajectory begins with a brief NL planning step, after which all reasoning unfolds through code blocks connected only by their execution outputs. We distill 12.2k code-centric trajectories from a teacher model and train ThinC-1.7B and ThinC-4B with supervised fine-tuning followed by reinforcement learning. ThinC-4B consistently outperforms every TIR baseline on five competition-level math benchmarks and even surpasses the much larger Qwen3-235B-A22B-Thinking. Further analysis shows that ThinC reasons through code: 99.2% of its final answers are grounded in interpreter output, and the model recovers reliably from code execution failures without intermediate NL reasoning. Our code and models will be released soon.

### [35] [SpecBlock: Block-Iterative Speculative Decoding with Dynamic Tree Drafting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07243)
*Weijie Shi,Qiang Xu,Fan Deng,Yaguang Wu,Jiarun Liu,Yehong Xu,Hao Chen,Jia Zhu,Jiajie Xu,Xiangjun Huang,Jian Yang,Xiaofang Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: SpecBlock is a new speculative decoding drafter that generates blocks of dependent tokens at once to speed up LLM inference more efficiently than prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing speculative decoding drafters either maintain path dependence but are expensive because they run once per tree depth, or are cheap but produce low-quality, often-rejected draft paths due to independence between predicted positions. There is a need for a drafting strategy that keeps path dependence (for accuracy) while significantly reducing drafting cost and better using verifier budgets.

Method: SpecBlock introduces a block-iterative drafting scheme where each drafter forward generates K sequential, dependency-preserving positions (a block). The draft tree is expanded by repeatedly adding these blocks. Path dependence is carried (1) within blocks via a layer-wise shift that feeds the previous token’s hidden state into each decoder layer, and (2) across blocks by allowing new blocks to start from any token in the previous block, inheriting its hidden state. A co-trained ranking head dynamically allocates branching factors per position instead of using a fixed top-k. Training uses a valid-prefix mask to drop loss terms for later tokens when earlier ones in the block are incorrect. At deployment, a cost-aware bandit uses verifier feedback to decide when to update the drafter to maximize net throughput gains considering adaptation cost.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that SpecBlock provides 8–13% higher average speedup than EAGLE-3 while using only 44–52% of its drafting FLOPs, and that the online, cost-aware adaptation mechanism further improves the speedup advantage to 11–19% over EAGLE-3.

Conclusion: Block-iterative drafting with explicit path-dependence propagation and adaptive branching yields more efficient speculative decoding than prior autoregressive or parallel drafters. SpecBlock reduces drafting cost while improving end-to-end inference speed, and its cost-aware online adaptation continues to refine the drafter in deployment for additional efficiency gains.

Abstract: Speculative decoding accelerates LLM inference by drafting a tree of candidate continuations and verifying it in one target forward. Existing drafters fall into two camps with opposite weaknesses. Autoregressive drafters such as EAGLE-3 preserve dependence along each draft path but call the drafter once per tree depth, making drafting a non-trivial share of per-iteration latency. Parallel drafters cut drafter calls by predicting multiple future positions in one forward, but each position is predicted without seeing the others, producing paths the verifier rejects. In this paper, we propose SpecBlock, a block-iterative drafter that combines path dependence with cheap drafting. Each drafter forward produces K dependent positions and we call this a block. The draft tree grows through repeated block expansions. Two mechanisms explicitly carry path dependence to keep later draft positions accurate. Within each block, a layer-wise shift carries the previous position's hidden state into every decoder layer. Across blocks, each new block can start from any position of the previous block, inheriting its hidden state to extend the path. To spend verifier budget where acceptance is likely, a co-trained rank head replaces the fixed top-k tree by allocating per-position branching during drafting. To avoid training the drafter on prefixes it never produces at inference, a valid-prefix mask drops the loss at later positions once an earlier one is wrong. Beyond static drafting, a cost-aware bandit at deployment uses free verifier feedback to update the drafter selectively, only when the expected throughput gain exceeds the update cost. Experiments show that SpecBlock improves mean speedup by 8-13% over EAGLE-3 at 44-52% of its drafting cost, and cost-aware adaptation extends this lead to 11-19%.

</details>


### [36] [PaT: Planning-after-Trial for Efficient Test-Time Code Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07248)
*Youngsik Yoon,Sungjae Lee,Seockbean Song,Siwei Wang,Wei Chen,Jungseul Ok*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Planning-after-Trial (PaT), an adaptive test-time computation policy for LLM-based code generation that only triggers an expensive planning procedure after a verification failure, improving the cost–performance trade-off compared to always planning beforehand.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods for scaling test-time computation in LLMs often use a rigid Planning-before-Trial (PbT) strategy: they always perform an up-front planning phase before attempting code generation. This wastes computation on problems that are already easy for the model, leading to unnecessary cost and latency. The authors aim to design a more efficient test-time policy that allocates extra reasoning and planning computation only when it is truly needed, thus improving the compute–performance Pareto frontier.

Method: The authors introduce Planning-after-Trial (PaT), an adaptive policy for code generation. First, a relatively cost-efficient model attempts to solve the problem and the generated code is checked via a verifier (e.g., test cases). Only if this first attempt fails does the system invoke a more powerful planner model to guide further attempts. This design naturally supports a heterogeneous configuration where a smaller, cheaper model does routine generation and a larger, more capable model is selectively used for planning. They evaluate PaT across different benchmarks and LLM families, comparing it with standard Planning-before-Trial and homogeneous large-model baselines in terms of performance and inference cost.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks and model families, PaT improves the cost–performance Pareto frontier relative to existing PbT approaches. Using a heterogeneous setup (small model for generation, large model for planning), the method achieves performance comparable to a large homogeneous model while cutting inference cost by about 69%.

Conclusion: Adaptive allocation of test-time computation via Planning-after-Trial can substantially reduce inference cost for LLM-based code generation without sacrificing accuracy. By invoking an expensive planner only when verification fails and leveraging heterogeneous model configurations, PaT offers a more efficient and flexible alternative to rigid Planning-before-Trial policies, advancing the overall cost–performance trade-off for test-time scaling.

Abstract: Beyond training-time optimization, scaling test-time computation has emerged as a key paradigm to extend the reasoning capabilities of Large Language Models (LLMs). However, most existing methods adopt a rigid Planning-before-Trial (PbT) policy, which inefficiently allocates test-time compute by incurring planning overhead even on directly solvable problems. We propose Planning-after-Trial (PaT), an adaptive policy for code generation that invokes a planner only upon verification failure. This adaptive policy naturally enables a heterogeneous model configuration: a cost-efficient model handles generation attempts, while a powerful model is reserved for targeted planning interventions. Empirically, across multiple benchmarks and model families, our approach significantly advances the cost-performance Pareto frontier. Notably, our heterogeneous configuration achieves performance comparable to a large homogeneous model while reducing inference cost by approximately 69\%.

</details>


### [37] [From 0-Order Selection to 2-Order Judgment: Combinatorial Hardening Exposes Compositional Failures in Frontier LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07268)
*Hanmeng Liu,Shichao Weng,Xiulai Liu,Zhicai Zhang,Anli Yan,Xiaozhang Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces LogiHard, a framework that turns simple multiple-choice questions into higher-order logical judgment tasks, greatly increasing reasoning difficulty while preserving validity and enabling adaptive testing.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multiple-choice reasoning benchmarks quickly saturate as models improve, and are often contaminated by training data, making static evaluations unreliable. Ad-hoc methods to make questions harder (paraphrasing, perturbations) mainly change surface form and can break logical validity, failing to truly stress-test advanced reasoning models. There is a need for a principled, validity-preserving way to systematically increase reasoning difficulty and to control it precisely across models and domains.

Method: The authors propose LogiHard, a formal transformation framework that converts 0-order selection tasks (choose one answer) into 2-order logical judgment tasks (e.g., evaluating combinations, entailment/consistency across options), thereby increasing required reasoning steps. They integrate Item Response Theory (IRT) into a computerized adaptive testing (CAT) setup to control difficulty with fewer items than static benchmarks. They build LogiHard-2k by first using a 9-dimensional analysis of model thinking traces to cognitively rank high-stakes exam questions, selecting high-difficulty items, and then applying combinatorial transformations to create hardened logical reasoning questions. They then test multiple state-of-the-art LLMs on these transformed items and perform zero-shot transfer of the transformations to MMLU.

Result: Across twelve strong LLMs, accuracy on combinatorially hardened questions drops substantially by 31–56% compared with the original questions. The analysis reveals specific failure patterns, including multi-select failure (difficulty in handling questions requiring multiple correct answers or combinations) and early exit bias (prematurely committing to an answer without fully exploring combinations), behaviors not observed in human test-takers. When the same transformation is applied zero-shot to the MMLU benchmark, model accuracy drops from 89.84% to 42.86% (a 47% degradation), showing that the method generalizes across domains while preserving item validity.

Conclusion: LogiHard provides a principled, validity-preserving way to substantially increase the reasoning difficulty of multiple-choice benchmarks by transforming them into higher-order logical judgment tasks. The approach, combined with IRT-based adaptive testing, offers finer difficulty control with fewer items and reveals a domain-agnostic combinatorial reasoning gap in current LLMs. The observed performance drop is attributed not to knowledge limitations but to a training-induced deficit in completeness verification over combinatorial spaces, suggesting that current training paradigms under-emphasize systematic combinatorial reasoning and exhaustive checking.

Abstract: Multiple-choice reasoning benchmarks face dual challenges: rapid saturation from advancing models and data contamination that undermines static evaluations. Ad-hoc hardening methods (paraphrasing, perturbation) attempt to increase difficulty but sacrifice logical validity for surface complexity, falling short to challenge advanced reasoning models. We present LogiHard, a formal framework that deterministically transforms 0-order selection into 2-order logical judgment, which significantly increases the thinking overhead and reasoning steps. The framework integrates Item Response Theory (IRT) for computerized adaptive testing (CAT), enabling precise difficulty control with fewer questions than static benchmarks. We instantiate LogiHard-2k, a logical reasoning dataset constructed by cognitively ranking high-stakes examination questions via 9-dimensional analysis of model thinking traces, followed by combinatorial transformation of high-difficulty items. Evaluation across twelve state-of-the-art models reveals an accuracy degradation ranging from 31% to 56% on combinatorially hardened questions. LLMs suffer from the multi-select failure and early exit bias, which are not shared by human testees. Zero-shot transfer to MMLU demonstrates 47% accuracy degradation (89.84% to 42.86%), confirming applicability across domains with provable validity preservation. The consistent aggregate degeneration is domain-agnostic and stems not from knowledge deficits but from a combinatorial reasoning gap, reflecting a training-induced completeness-verification deficit.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Indirect prompt injection is a critical vulnerability for LLM systems that use external tools or retrieved documents. Existing defenses are mostly monolingual and do not adequately address multilingual scenarios like English-Bangla, where attack styles and linguistic properties differ. There is a need for scalable, language-extensible defenses that work well cross-lingually and can be integrated into real systems without retraining huge models for every new language.

Method: They propose MIPIAD, a modular defense pipeline. The core neural component is XLPID, a sequence classifier based on Qwen2.5-1.5B fine-tuned with LoRA for prompt-injection detection. They combine this with classical lexical features using TF-IDF and SVM, then perform validation-tuned ensembling via late fusion, stacking, and gradient boosting to combine neural and lexical predictions. They also leverage NLLB-200 to facilitate extension to more than 200 languages and design the pipeline so that the multilingual backbone can be retargeted without architecture changes.

Result: On their benchmark, simple lexical models already perform well (TF-IDF+SVM achieves F1=0.77). The hybrid ensemble of XLPID and TF-IDF reaches the best overall F1 score of 0.9205, while a Boosting Ensemble achieves the highest AUROC of 0.9378. Ensemble approaches consistently narrow the performance gap between English and Bangla compared to neural-only models, indicating better cross-lingual robustness. The framework is empirically validated on English and Bangla but architecturally supports many more languages through NLLB-200 and the multilingual backbone.

Conclusion: MIPIAD demonstrates that combining lightweight neural classifiers with strong lexical baselines through careful ensembling yields a robust, cross-lingual defense against indirect prompt injection. Lexical cues remain powerful, and ensembles both boost overall accuracy and reduce cross-lingual disparities. While experiments are limited to English and Bangla, the design is extensible to many languages, making it a promising foundation for scalable multilingual prompt-injection defenses in retrieval-augmented and tool-using LLM systems.

Abstract: Indirect prompt injection remains a persistent weakness in retrieval-augmented and tool-using LLM systems, and the problem becomes harder to characterise in multilingual settings. We present MIPIAD, a defense framework evaluated on English and Bangla that combines a sequence classifier fine-tuned from Qwen2.5-1.5B via LoRA (XLPID), TF-IDF lexical features, and validation-tuned ensembling through late fusion, stacking, and gradient boosting. The framework is evaluated on a synthetic benchmark built from BIPIA(Yi et al., 2023) templates spanning five task families -- email, table, QA, abstract, and code-comprising over 1.43 million generated samples, with train and test splits using mutually exclusive attack categories. Across the experiments, lexical signals prove strong (TF-IDF+SVM F1=0.77), and the hybrid XLPID+TF-IDF ensemble achieves the best overall F1 (0.9205) while the Boosting Ensemble achieves the best AUROC (0.9378). Ensemble methods consistently reduce the English-Bangla cross-lingual gap relative to standalone neural models. The pipeline is designed for extensibility: NLLB-200 supports over 200 languages and XLPID's multilingual backbone can be retargeted to additional languages without architectural changes; empirical validation is currently limited to English and Bangla

### [39] [Understanding Performance Collapse in Layer-Pruned Large Language Models via Decision Representation Transitions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07271)
*Boyu Shi,Chang Liu,ChuanBao Gao,Xu Yang,Xin Geng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies why layer pruning causes sudden performance collapse in large language models and explains it via decision dynamics rather than hidden representations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Layer pruning is a popular approach to speed up LLMs, but it often causes abrupt performance drops that current representation-based explanations cannot fully account for. The authors aim to understand the underlying mechanism behind this collapse in a principled way, especially for multiple-choice tasks.

Method: They introduce a decision-centric framework using multiple-choice tasks, defining two metrics—Decision Margin (how confidently the model prefers the correct option over others) and Option Frequency (how often each option is selected across instances). They apply an Iterative Pruning procedure, progressively removing layers and observing how these decision metrics evolve layer by layer, to identify where and how the final decision forms in the network.

Result: They discover a distinct decision transition boundary in the network that splits it into two functional stages. In the earlier Silent Phase, the model is not yet able to output the correct answer; in the later Decisive Phase, the correct answer starts to dominate. Surprisingly, pruning layers in the Decisive Phase barely harms performance, while pruning layers in the Silent Phase leads to abrupt and severe accuracy collapse.

Conclusion: The critical mechanism behind pruning-induced collapse is the disruption of the Silent Phase, which is essential for enabling the later decision transition. Consequently, safe and efficient layer pruning must preserve the integrity of the Silent Phase and can more aggressively target the Decisive Phase with limited performance loss.

Abstract: Layer pruning efficiently reduces Large Language Model (LLM) computational costs but often triggers sudden performance collapse. Existing representation-based analyses struggle to explain this mechanism. We propose studying pruning through decision representation. Focusing on multiple-choice tasks, we introduce two metrics, Decision Margin and Option Frequency, and an Iterative Pruning method to analyze layer-wise decision dynamics. Our findings reveal a sharp decision transition that partitions the network into two stages: a Silent Phase, where the model cannot yet predict the correct answer, and a Decisive Phase, where the correct prediction emerges. We also find that pruning the Decisive Phase has minimal impact, whereas pruning the Silent Phase triggers immediate performance collapse, highlighting its extreme sensitivity to structural changes. Therefore, we conclude that pruning-induced collapse stems from disrupting the Silent Phase, which prevents the critical decision transition from occurring.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing medical LLM evaluations mostly use static, single-turn cases where all information is given at once, which does not reflect the real clinical workflow of iteratively gathering information, ordering tests, and updating diagnoses. This mismatch causes current LLMs to fail in realistic active diagnosis scenarios, exposing a gap in their ability to reason under partial and evolving evidence.

Method: The authors systematically analyze LLM behavior in multi-turn diagnostic tasks and identify key failure modes. They then propose MedAction, a tree-structured distillation pipeline where an LLM interacts with a simulated clinical environment to generate diverse multi-turn diagnostic trajectories. They introduce two knowledge-graph-based automatic metrics—Disease Trajectory Consistency (DTC) and Reasoning-Action Consistency (RAC)—to filter and retain only high-quality trajectories. Using this process, they build the MedAction-32K dataset of 32,681 multi-turn trajectories from 2,896 PubMed Central (PMC) clinical cases and fine-tune an 8B-parameter model on this data.

Result: The resulting MedAction-32K dataset captures high-quality, diverse, multi-turn diagnostic processes. An 8B model fine-tuned on this dataset achieves state-of-the-art performance among open-source medical LLMs on MedR-Bench and on a new, challenging multi-turn benchmark, MedAction-300-Hard.

Conclusion: Training medical LLMs on carefully curated, multi-turn diagnostic trajectories that emphasize evidence-grounded test ordering and belief updating substantially improves their active diagnosis capabilities. MedAction and MedAction-32K address a core deficiency of current medical LLMs—reasoning under partial, evolving information—and push the performance frontier of open-source medical LLMs on realistic clinical reasoning tasks.

Abstract: Most existing LLM diagnoses are evaluated on static, single-turn settings where complete patient information is provided upfront, an oversimplification of real clinical practice. We study active diagnosis: the real-life clinical process of starting from initial observation, ordering tests, interpreting results, and updating a differential diagnosis across multiple turns. Through systematic analysis, we identify three recurring failure modes in current LLMs: ungrounded test ordering, unreliable diagnostic update, and degraded multi-turn coherence. Together, these failures reveal a core deficit: existing medical training data teaches models to reason from complete information but not to act under evolving, partial evidence. To address this gap, we introduce MedAction, a tree-structured distillation pipeline that synthesizes diverse and high-quality multi-turn diagnostic trajectories via LLM-environment interaction. We propose two knowledge-graph-grounded metrics to filter trajectory quality: Disease Trajectory Consistency (DTC), which tracks whether the model's hypothesis converges toward the correct diagnosis, and Reasoning-Action Consistency (RAC), which verifies that belief updates are driven by gathered evidence. Using this pipeline, we construct MedAction-32K, a dataset of 32,681 trajectories from 2,896 PMC cases. Fine-tuning an 8B model on MedAction-32K achieves state-of-the-art performance among open-source models on both MedR-Bench and our curated MedAction-300-Hard benchmark, pushing the edge for open-source medical LLMs.
