### [41] [Rethinking Dense Sequential Chains: Reasoning Language Models Can Extract Answers from Sparse, Order-Shuffling Chain-of-Thoughts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07307)
*Yi-Chang Chen,Feng-Ting Liao,Da-shan Shiu,Hung-yi Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that large language models’ chain-of-thought (CoT) traces are highly redundant and order-insensitive: models can still extract correct answers even when CoT is heavily shuffled or stripped down to sparse numeric cues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chain-of-thought reasoning is widely believed to rely on dense, sequential, human-like logical steps, where every token and the exact order are important. This assumption underlies how we prompt, train, and evaluate reasoning models, but has not been rigorously tested. The authors want to understand what parts of CoT actually matter for answer extraction and whether reasoning is genuinely sequential and dense or instead operates on a much sparser, order-insensitive signal.

Method: The authors generate reasoning chains from several language models on three reasoning benchmarks, then systematically perturb these chains. They apply interventions such as (1) shuffling at different granularities (lines, words, tokens) to test order dependence, (2) masking digits or alphabetic tokens to test density and information content, and (3) combining shuffling with extreme pruning of natural language plus noise injection of incorrect answers. They then measure how answer accuracy changes under each intervention and compare pretrained-only vs instruction-tuned variants to locate the source of the observed behavior.

Result: Line-level shuffling of CoT has almost no impact on accuracy (<0.5 percentage point drop), and even word-level shuffling preserves a large fraction of performance (62–89% of original accuracy). Only token-level shuffling destroys performance. Masking numeric digits drives accuracy to 0% while masking alphabetic prose surprisingly improves accuracy by 4.7 percentage points, indicating that numbers carry the crucial signal and much prose is superfluous or even harmful. A drastically reduced representation that removes all natural language, arbitrarily shuffles lines, yet preserves essential numeric/structural cues still attains about 83% accuracy. Injecting false answers at three times the true-answer frequency does not hurt accuracy, arguing against simple frequency-based extraction. Pretrained-only and instruction-tuned models behave similarly under shuffling, implying order-independence arises mainly from pretraining.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that answer extraction from chain-of-thought traces relies on a sparse, order-insensitive, and structurally robust representational substrate, rather than on dense, strictly sequential reasoning steps. Much of the natural-language prose in CoT is redundant or even detrimental, while key numeric or symbolic cues suffice for maintaining high accuracy. This challenges common intuitions about how reasoning LMs work and suggests that more parallelized, token-efficient, and structure-aware methods for generating and using reasoning traces are both possible and desirable.

Abstract: Modern reasoning language models generate dense, sequential chain-of-thought traces implicitly assuming that every token contributes and that steps must be consumed in order. We challenge both assumptions through a systematic intervention pipeline--removal, masking, shuffling, and noise injection--applied to model-generated reasoning chains across three models and three benchmarks. Our findings are counterintuitive on three dimensions. Order: Does the sequential order of a reasoning chain matter for answer extraction? No--line-level shuffling reduces accuracy by less than 0.5 pp; word-level shuffling retains 62%-89% accuracy; only token-level shuffling collapses to near zero. Pretrained-only and instruction-tuned variants exhibit near-identical tolerance (78.67% vs. 78.00% under line shuffling), indicating order-independence originates from pretraining rather than reasoning-specific fine-tuning. Dense: Is all the information in a reasoning chain important for answer extraction? No--masking numeric digits collapses accuracy to exactly 0%, while masking alphabetic prose improves accuracy by 4.7 pp. Robustness: Is a reasoning chain that is both order-shuffling and non-dense still robust? Yes--the most aggressively reduced representation (all natural language removed, lines arbitrarily shuffled) still achieves 83% accuracy, and injecting false answers at 3x true-answer frequency leaves accuracy unchanged (83.3%->83.3%), falsifying a frequency-based extraction account. These results establish that answer extraction operates on a sparse, order-insensitive, and structurally robust informational substrate, opening paths toward parallelized and token-efficient reasoning generation.

</details>


### [42] [LaTER: Efficient Test-Time Reasoning via Latent Exploration and Explicit Verification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07315)
*Xuan Li,Yining Wang,Yuchen Liu,Guanjun Liu,Delai Qiu,Shengping Liu,Jiaen Liang,Wei Huang,Jun Yu,Junnan Zhu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: LaTER is a two-stage reasoning framework for LLMs that first reasons in a latent (non-token) space, then switches to explicit chain-of-thought, cutting tokens while maintaining or improving accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chain-of-thought improves reasoning but is computationally expensive because each reasoning step is a token, and fully latent reasoning can hurt tasks needing symbolic verification. The authors want a method that keeps symbolic checking while reducing visible token usage and inference cost.

Method: They design LaTER, which performs bounded latent exploration using the model’s continuous hidden states and KV cache, then transitions to explicit CoT for verification and final answer. A training-free version projects final-layer hidden states back into the input embedding space and uses entropy plus stop-token probes to decide the switch point. They also build a supervised dataset, Latent-Switch-69K, pairing short latent “intuition” phases with shortened explicit derivations, and fine-tune models with supervision on latent rollout and halting behavior.

Result: On Qwen3-14B, training-free LaTER cuts token usage by 16–32% across several reasoning benchmarks while matching or slightly improving accuracy; e.g., on AIME 2025 accuracy rises from 70.0% to 73.3% while tokens drop from 15,730 to 10,661. With fine-tuning on Latent-Switch-69K, LaTER achieves 80.0% accuracy on AIME 2025—10 points above a standard CoT baseline—using 33% fewer tokens.

Conclusion: Structured latent reasoning already exists in strong LLMs and can be exploited to reduce explicit token generation without sacrificing, and sometimes improving, reasoning quality. LaTER’s latent-then-explicit paradigm, especially when trained with latent rollout and switch supervision, provides a more token-efficient and accurate alternative to standard CoT on challenging benchmarks.

Abstract: Chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning improves large language models (LLMs) on difficult tasks, but it also makes inference expensive because every intermediate step must be generated as a discrete token. Latent reasoning reduces visible token generation by propagating continuous states, yet replacing explicit derivations with latent computation can hurt tasks that require symbolic checking. We propose Latent-Then-Explicit Reasoning (LaTER), a two-stage paradigm that first performs bounded exploration in a continuous latent space and then switches to explicit CoT for verification and answer generation. In a training-free instantiation, LaTER projects final-layer hidden states back to the input embedding space, preserves the latent KV cache, and uses entropy and model-native stop-token probes to decide when to switch. We find that strong reasoning models already exhibit structured latent trajectories under this interface. On Qwen3-14B, training-free LaTER reduces total token usage by 16%-32% on several benchmarks while matching or improving accuracy on most of them; for example, it improves AIME 2025 from 70.0% to 73.3% while reducing tokens from 15,730 to 10,661. We further construct Latent-Switch-69K, a supervised corpus that pairs condensed solution intuitions with shortened explicit derivations. Fine-tuning with latent rollout and halting supervision yields additional gains: trained LaTER reaches 80.0% accuracy on AIME 2025, 10.0 points above the standard CoT baseline, while using 33% fewer tokens. Our code, data, and model are available at https://github.com/TioeAre/LaTER.

### [43] [Activation Differences Reveal Backdoors: A Comparison of SAE Architectures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07324)
*Sachin Kumar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how to detect backdoor attacks in language models using sparse autoencoder-based interpretability, showing that Differential SAEs are much better than Crosscoders at isolating a synthetic SQL-injection backdoor signal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Backdoor attacks let models behave normally most of the time but act maliciously when a secret trigger appears, creating serious AI safety risks. Current mechanistic interpretability tools struggle to reliably detect such hidden manipulations. The authors aim to understand whether specific sparse autoencoder architectures can expose the internal features associated with a backdoor in a controlled setting, thereby improving monitoring and trust in fine-tuned language models.

Method: The authors create a controlled SQL injection backdoor in SmolLM2-360M where the model generates vulnerable code when encountering the year "2024" but safe code for "2023". They fine-tune the model using both LoRA and full-rank fine-tuning. They then train and compare two sparse autoencoder-based methods—Crosscoders and Differential SAEs—on activations from multiple transformer layers (14, 18, 22, 26). They introduce and use a Backdoor Isolation Score (BIS), along with precision and false positive rate, to quantify how well each method isolates features corresponding to the backdoor behavior.

Result: Differential SAEs consistently outperform Crosscoders at isolating backdoor-related features. Diff-SAE reaches a BIS of around 0.40 with perfect precision (1.0) and effectively zero false positives under most tested conditions, while Crosscoders largely fail, with BIS typically below 0.02. This performance advantage holds across different layers of the transformer and both LoRA and full-rank fine-tuning, with full-rank fine-tuning yielding especially clean backdoor signals in the learned representations.

Conclusion: The study concludes that backdoors in the tested language model primarily manifest as directional shifts in activations rather than as individual sparse features. Consequently, difference-based representations like Differential SAEs are fundamentally more suitable than Crosscoders for detecting such manipulations. These insights point toward improved AI safety monitoring strategies and guide the design of future interpretability methods for robust backdoor detection in language models.

Abstract: Backdoor attacks on language models pose a significant threat to AI safety, where models behave normally on most inputs but exhibit harmful behavior when triggered by specific patterns. Detecting such backdoors through mechanistic interpretability remains an open challenge. We investigate two sparse autoencoder architectures -- Crosscoders and Differential SAEs (Diff-SAE) -- for isolating backdoor-related features in fine-tuned models. Using a controlled SQL injection backdoor triggered by year-based context ("2024" triggers vulnerable code, "2023" triggers safe code), we evaluate both approaches across LoRA and full-rank fine-tuning regimes on SmolLM2-360M. We find that Diff-SAE consistently and substantially outperforms Crosscoders for backdoor isolation. Diff-SAE achieves a Backdoor Isolation Score (BIS) of 0.40 with perfect precision (1.0) and zero false positive rate across most experimental conditions, while Crosscoders fail almost entirely with BIS below 0.02 in most cases. This performance gap holds across multiple transformer layers (14, 18, 22, 26) and both fine-tuning regimes, with full-rank fine-tuning producing particularly clean backdoor signals. Our results suggest that backdoors manifest as directional activation shifts rather than sparse feature activations, making difference-based representations fundamentally more effective for detection. These findings have important implications for AI safety monitoring and the development of interpretability tools for detecting model manipulation.

### [44] [Mean-Pooled Cosine Similarity is Not Length-Invariant: Theory and Cross-Domain Evidence for a Length-Invariant Alternative](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07345)
*Sibayan Mitra,Dhruv Kumar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that the common metric of mean-pooled cosine similarity for comparing neural representations is biased by sequence length under typical anisotropy in transformer embeddings, leading to misleading conclusions about cross-language similarity, and proposes using length-invariant metrics like CKA instead.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Neural representation similarity is widely measured with mean-pooled cosine similarity across languages, modalities, and tasks, and many claims—such as cross-lingual convergence—are based on this metric. However, it is unclear whether this metric is robust to basic confounds like sequence length, especially in anisotropic representation spaces typical of modern transformers. The authors aim to test whether mean-pooled cosine is truly content-sensitive or dominated by superficial factors such as length, and to assess the reliability of prior findings built on this metric.

Method: The authors theoretically analyze mean-pooled cosine similarity in anisotropic embedding spaces and show it should systematically increase with sequence length, regardless of content. They then empirically evaluate this effect across several settings: HumanEvalPack code translation across four code LLMs, parallel sentence pairs in Mistral-7B on WMT (EN-FR, EN-DE), and image-text representations from CLIP ViT-B/32. They regress cross-representation similarity on sequence-length ratio and structural/content features (e.g., AST depth, shared-token fraction) and compare mean-pooled cosine to length-invariant alternatives, particularly Centered Kernel Alignment (CKA) and different pooling strategies (mean vs EOS).

Result: They find that under mean-pooled cosine, similarity scores are strongly and monotonically driven by sequence-length ratios, explaining a large fraction of variance (R^2=0.52–0.75 for code LLM "Python proximity"; R^2=0.23–0.33 for Mistral WMT pairs). Additional structural or content-based features explain little extra variance. Switching to CKA dramatically reduces the variance explained by length (by 83%) and even reverses the sign of length’s regression coefficient (from +0.86 to -0.37), making similarity effectively length-invariant (R^2<0.01). In CLIP, mean pooling lessens but does not fully remove length effects compared to EOS pooling, consistent with the theoretical dependence on anisotropy.

Conclusion: Mean-pooled cosine similarity is not length-invariant in anisotropic transformer representation spaces and is heavily confounded by sequence length, which can spuriously suggest cross-lingual or cross-modal convergence. Length-invariant metrics like CKA (and appropriate pooling choices) should replace mean-pooled cosine as the default for cross-representation comparison, and prior results relying on mean-pooled cosine—especially claims of cross-lingual representational convergence—should be revisited with more robust similarity measures.

Abstract: Mean-pooled cosine similarity is the default metric for comparing neural representations across languages, modalities, and tasks. We establish that this metric is not length-invariant: under the anisotropy that characterizes modern transformer representations, mean-pooled cosine grows monotonically in sequence length, independent of representational content. Empirically, on HumanEvalPack across four code LLMs, the length ratio alone explains $R^2 = 0.52$--$0.75$ of cross-language "Python proximity," while AST depth and shared-token fraction add less than 3% of explained variance beyond length. Substituting Centered Kernel Alignment (CKA) reduces explained variance by 83% and reverses the sign of the length coefficient ($β_{\mathrm{len}}: +0.86 \to -0.37$). The same pattern holds in Mistral-7B on parallel WMT pairs ($R^2 = 0.23$ EN-FR, $R^2 = 0.33$ EN-DE for cosine; $R^2 < 0.01$ for CKA). In CLIP ViT-B/32, mean-pooling reduces the length effect relative to EOS-pooling ($R^2: 0.21 \to {<}0.01$), as predicted by the theory's dependence on anisotropy. We argue that length-invariant metrics such as CKA should be the default for cross-representation comparisons, and that recent claims of cross-lingual representational convergence built on mean-pooled cosine warrant re-examination.

</details>


### [45] [The Proxy Presumption: From Semantic Embeddings to Valid Social Measures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07409)
*Baishi Li,Ta Yu,Kelvin J. L. Koa,Ke-Wei Huang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper critiques the common practice of treating embedding geometry as direct measures of social constructs and proposes a validated framework (CVP) and tools to make such measurements scientifically reliable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Researchers in Computational Social Science increasingly use text embeddings as proxies for complex social constructs (novelty, creativity, bias), usually assuming distances directly reflect these constructs. This practice is threatened by validity problems because embeddings mix the target construct with confounding factors like topic or author style, and current work rarely validates that the embedding dimensions truly measure the intended construct.

Method: The authors introduce the Construct Validity Protocol (CVP), a structured pipeline grounded in causal representation learning and psychometrics. CVP guides researchers from defining a construct to verifying whether an embedding-based measure validly captures it. They also develop Counterfactual Neutralization, which uses large language models to generate counterfactual text or transformations that reduce confounding dimensions in embedding space. In addition, they design a standardized Validity Suite of empirical tests that assess discriminant validity (distinguishing constructs), incremental validity (added value beyond existing measures), and predictive validity (forecasting relevant outcomes).

Result: Using CVP and Counterfactual Neutralization, embedding-based measures can be systematically purified of confounding factors and empirically tested for multiple forms of construct validity. The Validity Suite offers concrete tests showing whether a given embedding proxy meaningfully captures a social construct beyond noise or confounds, turning informal proxies into empirically grounded measures.

Conclusion: Unsupervised text embeddings cannot be assumed to measure social constructs simply via geometric properties; without explicit validation they conflate target constructs with confounds. The Construct Validity Protocol, Counterfactual Neutralization, and the associated Validity Suite together provide a principled methodology for transforming raw embedding proxies into robust and defensible scientific instruments for Computational Social Science.

Abstract: Natural Language Processing is rapidly evolving into a primary instrument for Computational Social Science, with researchers increasingly using embeddings to measure latent constructs such as novelty, creativity, and bias. However, this transition faces a fundamental validity challenge: the ''Proxy Presumption,'' or the reliance on geometric properties (e.g., cosine distance) as direct measures of social concepts. We argue that without explicit validation, unsupervised representations remain entangled mixtures of the target construct ($C$) and confounding attributes ($Z$) like topic, style, and authorship. To bridge the gap between semantic embeddings and valid social measures, we introduce the Construct Validity Protocol (CVP). Drawing on causal representation learning and psychometrics, the CVP offers a rigorous pipeline from conceptualization to quantitative verification. We further propose Counterfactual Neutralization, a novel method using LLMs to reduce confounding in embedding space. By providing a standardized Validity Suite -- including tests for discriminant, incremental, and predictive validity -- this work offers the community a toolkit to transform heuristic proxies into robust, scientifically defensible instruments.

</details>

*Changhoe Hwang,Jee-Sun Nam,Eric Laporte*

Main category: cs.CL

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Linguists design local grammar graphs (LGGs) that encode domain-specific vocabulary and local syntactic patterns for legal queries, each tied to an intent label. Legal chatbots improve access to justice, since legal representation and legal advice by lawyers come with a high cost that excludes disadvantaged and vulnerable people. Diversity requires large volumes of data, which must also be labelled in order to classify the user's intent, while the cost of labelling datasets increases with volume. Instead of labelling large volumes of authentic data from users, our approach consists in jointly generating large volumes of utterances and high-quality labels. The generator of labelled datasets is based on language resources that take the form of local grammar graphs (LGG), which capture and generalize the vocabulary and local syntax observed by linguists in text. The LGGs associate labels to the utterances according to a domain-specific classification system. We tested this approach by implementing LIGA, a legal chatbot in Korean. The chatbot answers users' conversational queries on legal situations by providing information on similar legal cases, made publicly available by the Korean government. We generated labelled utterances from the LGGs with the aid of the open-source Unitex platform. This process produced 700 million utterances. We trained a DIET classifier on a dataset made of these utterances, and the trained model reached 91% f1-score performance. We implemented a chatbot called LIGA, which uses the results of the model to select a link to a web page that documents similar legal cases.

### [47] [SSP-based construction of evaluation-annotated data for fine-grained aspect-based sentiment analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07446)
*Suwon Choi,Shinwoo Kim,Changhoe Hwang,Gwanghoon Yoo,Eric Laporte,Jeesun Nam*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Aspect-Based Sentiment Analysis (ABSA) resources for Korean, especially in e-commerce and fashion, are limited and usually focus only on simple sentiment labels (positive/negative) and coarse aspects. Real user reviews also contain non-sentiment patterns and fine-grained opinion details (e.g., aspect values) that are not well supported by current corpora and models. The authors want a richer, systematically annotated Korean corpus to better capture nuanced opinions in fashion-related product reviews and to support robust ABSA model training and evaluation.

Method: They construct EVAD, a Korean evaluation-annotated corpus for fashion e-commerce reviews. Annotation is done with a Semi-Automatic Symbolic Propagation (SSP) process: they first design extensive linguistic resources and rules, formalized as a Finite-State Transducer (FST), to identify ABSA components (targets, topics, aspects, and aspect values) and relevant sentiment/non-sentiment patterns. These symbolic resources are then applied to the corpus to propagate annotations semi-automatically, with manual oversight or correction implied. The ABSA schema is extended beyond standard topic/aspect labels to explicitly include aspect values and to type each aspect–value pair based on the cardinality of possible values (unary, binary, multiple). Finally, they fine-tune two Korean BERT-based models (KoBERT and KcBERT) on the annotated data for the task of recognizing aspect–value pairs.

Result: The outcome is EVAD, a new Korean evaluation-annotated corpus in the fashion e-commerce domain, along with an FST-based rule system that can annotate similar data with detailed ABSA labels. When KoBERT and KcBERT are trained on this dataset for aspect–value pair recognition, they achieve high performance with F1 scores of 0.88 and 0.90, respectively, indicating that the annotations are consistent enough to support effective machine learning and that BERT-based models can leverage this fine-grained structure.

Conclusion: A semi-automatic, FST-based annotation framework (SSP) can be used to construct a rich, fine-grained ABSA corpus for Korean fashion e-commerce reviews. By extending ABSA to include explicit aspect values and their cardinality types, the authors can capture more detailed user opinions than with conventional ABSA schemes. The strong F1 scores of KoBERT and KcBERT on aspect–value recognition suggest that EVAD is a high-quality resource and that the proposed representation is learnable and useful for downstream opinion analysis in e-commerce settings.

Abstract: We report the construction of a Korean evaluation-annotated corpus, hereafter called 'Evaluation Annotated Dataset (EVAD)', and its use in Aspect-Based Sentiment Analysis (ABSA) extended in order to cover e-commerce reviews containing sentiment and non-sentiment linguistic patterns. The annotation process uses Semi-Automatic Symbolic Propagation (SSP). We built extensive linguistic resources formalized as a Finite-State Transducer (FST) to annotate corpora with detailed ABSA components in the fashion e-commerce domain. The ABSA approach is extended, in order to analyze user opinions more accurately and extract more detailed features of targets, by including aspect values in addition to topics and aspects, and by classifying aspectvalue pairs depending whether values are unary, binary, or multiple. For evaluation, the KoBERT and KcBERT models are trained on the annotated dataset, showing robust performances of F1 0.88 and F1 0.90, respectively, on recognition of aspect-value pairs.

### [48] [Data Contamination in Neural Hieroglyphic Translation: A Reproducibility Study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07453)
*Ammar Toutou,Abdelrahman Harb,Christine Basta*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper examines limitations of neural machine translation (NMT) for an ancient, low-resource language (hieroglyphic) by exposing severe train–test contamination in a widely used benchmark, showing that previously reported high BLEU scores were substantially inflated and providing a cleaned test set with more realistic baseline results.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Ancient and endangered languages have scarce, hard-to-expand, and often formulaic datasets, making them particularly vulnerable to data-quality issues such as train–test contamination, which can drastically overstate model performance. A recent work reported very high BLEU for hieroglyphic-to-German translation, suggesting strong NMT capabilities, but these claims had not been carefully audited for data leakage, leaving unclear what NMT can genuinely do for such languages.

Method: The authors attempt to reproduce prior hieroglyphic-to-German NMT results using the released fine-tuned M2M-100 model and dataset. They then systematically analyze the train and test splits for overlap at the target side, quantifying exact and high n-gram matches. They compare performance metrics (BLEU and COMET-22) on contaminated versus clean subsets across five model configurations from two architectures. They also test the impact of document-level decontamination versus stricter target-level deduplication and finally construct and release a cleaned 34-sample test set to establish corrected baselines.

Result: Reproduction of the prior study yields only 37.0 BLEU instead of the previously reported 61.5. Analysis reveals that 2% of test targets (16 of 50) appear identically in the training set and that half of the test samples have high n-gram overlap, indicating substantial contamination. Contaminated samples attain dramatically higher scores (up to 83.8 BLEU and 0.924 COMET-22) than clean samples (30.9–39.2 BLEU and 0.622–0.676 COMET-22) across all evaluated models. Document-level decontamination slightly reduces inflated scores but fails to remove all leakage because many targets persist via different source documents; only target-level deduplication effectively resolves the issue. A new decontaminated test set of 34 samples is released with corrected baselines between 30.9 and 39.2 BLEU.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that previously reported NMT performance for hieroglyphic-to-German translation was heavily inflated by train–test contamination, and that realistic capabilities of current models are significantly lower but still nontrivial. For ancient and endangered languages, rigorous target-level decontamination and careful dataset auditing are essential, as document-level cleaning is insufficient. The released cleaned test set and corrected baselines provide a more reliable benchmark for future NMT research on this endangered writing system and illustrate broader risks of contamination in ultra-low-resource, formulaic corpora.

Abstract: Ancient and endangered languages pose a unique challenge for NLP: their datasets are inherently scarce, difficult to expand, and built from formulaic corpora -- making data-quality issues especially consequential yet rarely audited. Motivated by the need to understand what current NMT can realistically achieve for such languages, we investigate hieroglyphic-to-German translation, where a recent study reported 61.5 BLEU using fine-tuned M2M-100. Our reproduction yields only 37.0 BLEU with the released model. Investigating this gap, we find 2\% of test targets appear identically in training (16/50; 50\% under 8-gram overlap at 70\% threshold). This contamination inflates scores dramatically: contaminated samples achieve up to 83.8 BLEU / 0.924 COMET-22 versus 30.9--39.2 BLEU / 0.622--0.676 COMET-22 on clean samples across five model configurations spanning two architectures. Document-level decontamination reduces contaminated BLEU by only 4.6 points because 8/16 targets persist via other source documents -- target-level deduplication is required. We release a decontaminated 34-sample test set and establish corrected baselines (30.9--39.2 BLEU), providing a realistic assessment of NMT capability for this endangered writing system.

### [49] [GRaSp: Automatic Example Optimization for In-Context Learning in Low-Data Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07454)
*Simen Bihaug-Frøyland,Henrik Brådland*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GRaSp is a three-stage framework that automatically selects effective in-context examples for LLMs, improving NER performance in low-data, domain-specific settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In-context learning for large language models is powerful but highly sensitive to which examples are provided, and choosing good examples is especially hard in domain-specific, low-resource scenarios like financial NER where high-quality labeled data is scarce. There is a need for an automatic, principled way to construct and optimize in-context example sets rather than relying on ad-hoc or random selection.

Method: The authors propose GRaSp, a three-stage pipeline: (1) generate a large pool of candidate examples (synthetic or human-annotated); (2) structure this pool using clustering and dimensionality reduction to capture its diversity and relationships; and (3) apply a genetic algorithm to search over subsets of candidates and identify an optimal in-context example set. They further design a diversity-adaptive mutation mechanism for the genetic algorithm that initially promotes broad exploration across clusters and gradually shifts toward fine-grained search within promising clusters as the population converges.

Result: On the financial NER dataset FiNER-139, GRaSp is evaluated with candidate pools of 500 and 5000 examples, both synthetic and human-annotated. Using non-synthetic data, GRaSp achieves a micro-F1 of 45.84%, outperforming zero-shot and random few-shot baselines consistently. When the candidate pool is purely synthetic, GRaSp performs on par with random few-shot selection but does not surpass it.

Conclusion: GRaSp can automatically optimize in-context example sets and yield better performance than standard baselines in a domain-specific NER task, provided that the candidate pool has sufficient real, diverse examples. The weaker performance with synthetic pools indicates that distributional richness and realism in candidate examples are crucial for generalization, and that synthetic data alone may be insufficient for optimal in-context learning in specialized domains.

Abstract: In-context learning enables large language models to adapt to new tasks, but their performance is highly sensitive to the selected examples. Finding effective demonstrations is particularly difficult in domain-specific, low-data settings where high-quality examples are scarce. We propose GRaSp, a three-stage framework for automatic in-context example optimization. By first generating a large synthetic candidate pool, then structuring it with clustering and dimensionality reduction, and finally using genetic algorithms to find the optimal in-context examples, the framework shows consistent improvements on the NER task. We also introduce a custom diversity-adaptive mutation mechanism, allowing it to transition from the initial broad inter-cluster exploration to focused intra-cluster refinement as the population converges. We evaluate GRaSp on financial named entity recognition (FiNER-139), comparing synthetic and human-annotated candidate pools across pool sizes of 500 and 5000. With non-synthetic data, GRaSp achieves 45.84% micro-F1, consistently outperforming both zero-shot and random few-shot baselines. Synthetic data matches the random baseline but does not exceed it, suggesting that distributional variety in the candidate pool is critical for generalization.

### [50] [Think-with-Rubrics: From External Evaluator to Internal Reasoning Guidance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07461)
*Jiachen Yu,Zhihao Xu,Junjie Wang,Yujiu Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose Think-with-Rubrics, where the model first generates a rubric and then its answer, using a rubric verifier for joint supervision, leading to better instruction following than using rubrics only as rewards.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing uses of rubrics in RL for LLMs treat them purely as external evaluators, so they only measure answer quality after generation and cannot guide the model’s internal reasoning or generation process. The authors want a way for rubrics to actively shape how the model thinks and answers.

Method: Introduce Think-with-Rubrics: during training, the LLM first generates a rubric inside its reasoning context and then generates the final response following that rubric. A separate trained rubric verifier evaluates how consistent the response is with either self-generated or provided (golden) rubrics. This joint supervision signal is used to train the model so that rubric creation and answer generation are aligned.

Result: Across multiple instruction-following benchmarks, the Think-with-Rubrics approach beats a strong baseline that uses rubrics only as rewards (Rubric-as-Reward) by an average margin of 3.87 points. Analysis shows improvements both in rubric quality and in consistency between the rubric and the final answer.

Conclusion: Embedding rubric generation directly into the model’s reasoning and pairing it with a rubric-consistency verifier yields better instruction-following performance than treating rubrics as external evaluators. Supervision from both golden and self-generated rubrics improves self-rubric quality and promotes internally consistent responses, explaining the performance gains of Think-with-Rubrics.

Abstract: Rubrics have been extensively utilized for evaluating unverifiable, open-ended tasks, with recent research incorporating them into reward systems for reinforcement learning. However, existing frameworks typically treat rubrics only as external evaluator disjointed from the policy's primary reasoning trace. Such design confines rubrics to post-hoc measurement, leaving them unable to actively guide the model's generation process. In this work, we introduce Think-with-Rubrics, a novel paradigm for instruction following tasks. Think-with-Rubrics integrates rubric generation into the reasoning context, transforming the rubric from an independent artifact into an internal guidance of LLM's generation. During training, LLM sequentially generates a rubric followed by a response, while a trained rubric verifier provides joint supervision by evaluating the consistency between the answer and the self-generated / golden rubrics. Experiments across multiple benchmarks demonstrate that Think-with-Rubrics consistently outperforms the Rubric-as-Reward baseline supervised by golden rubrics by an average of 3.87 points. We have also discussed the mechanism by which Think-with-Rubrics enhances model performance. Experimental results demonstrate that supervision from golden rubrics and self-generated rubrics enhances the performance of Think-with-Rubrics by improving the quality of self-generated rubrics and increasing the internal consistency of responses respectively.

*William Brach,Federico Torrielli,Stine Lyngsø Beltoft,Annemette Brok Pirchert,Peter Schneider-Kamp,Lukas Galke Poech*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To investigate emergent behavior and safety risks when large populations of autonomous agents interact publicly online, and to understand how such agent-generated data might affect future language models if included in training corpora.

Method: Release and sanitize the Moltbook Files dataset (232k posts, 2.2M comments) from the first 12 days of an agent-populated platform; run an automated PII removal pipeline; analyze structural, linguistic, and interactional properties (community structure, authorship, lexicon, sentiment, topics, semantic geometry, commenting); and fine-tune a base LLM (Qwen2.5-14B-Instruct) on Moltbook with three adaptation levels, comparing effects to a size-matched Reddit fine-tuning baseline, with a focus on truthfulness and safety metrics.

Result: Agents frequently posted sensitive PII (e.g., API keys, passwords, BIP39 seed phrases) despite public visibility. The content overall is mostly neutral or mildly positive in sentiment and exhibits strong self-referential linking. Fine-tuning on Moltbook significantly reduced model truthfulness (0.366 to 0.187), but a similarly sized Reddit dataset produced a comparable truthfulness decline, suggesting the harmful effects are not unique to Moltbook. Analyses characterize the community’s linguistic, topical, and interaction patterns.

Conclusion: Moltbook resembles a messy but not uniquely dangerous data source (“harmless slopocalypse”), yet it introduces tail risks, such as agent affordances, contamination of future web crawls through self-links, and potential transfer of undesirable traits to later LMs. The work underscores the need for careful control baselines and systematic comparisons when evaluating emergent misalignment and safety impacts of new, large-scale agent ecosystems.

Abstract: Moltbook is a Reddit-like platform where OpenClaw agents post, comment, and vote at scale - a so far unprecedented incident that comes with serious safety concerns. With the aim of studying emergent behavior in populations, we release the Moltbook Files, a dataset of 232k posts and 2.2M comments covering the platform's first 12 days, processed through a pipeline to identify and remove Personally-Identifiable Information (PII). We analyze community structure, authorship, lexical properties, sentiment, topics, semantic geometry, and comment interaction. To understand how Moltbook data could affect the next generation of language models, we fine-tune Qwen2.5-14B-Instruct on Moltbook Files with three adaptation levels. Our PII pipeline reveals that agents post API keys, passwords, BIP39 seed phrases on Moltbook, a publicly indexed platform. The overall sentiment is mostly neutral and mildly positive (66.6% neutral, 19.5% positive) and shows a tendency for self-referential linking. We find that fine-tuning on Moltbook data reduces truthfulness from 0.366 to 0.187. However, a model fine-tuned on a size-matched Reddit dataset produces a comparable decrease. Moltbook thus seems to be more of a harmless slopocalypse. However, tail risks remain, including agent affordances, contamination of future crawls through self-links, and potential transfer of traits to the next generation of language models. More broadly, our findings highlight the importance of control baselines in emergent misalignment evaluations.

### [52] [SEIF: Self-Evolving Reinforcement Learning for Instruction Following](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07465)
*Qingyu Ren,Qianyu He,Jiajie Zhu,Xingzhou Chen,Jingwen Chang,Zeye Sun,Han Xia,Fei Yu,Jiaqing Liang,Yanghua Xiao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SEIF, a self-evolving reinforcement learning framework that improves large language models’ instruction-following ability by co-evolving task difficulty and model capability without heavy reliance on external supervision.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Improving instruction following in LLMs typically requires expensive human or strong-teacher supervision, or uses self-play with static-difficulty instructions that don’t adapt as the model gets better. The authors want a scalable, automated way to continually enhance instruction-following performance where tasks become harder in step with the model’s growing capabilities.

Method: SEIF establishes a closed self-evolution loop with four interacting roles: (1) an Instructor model that generates instructions whose difficulty is progressively increased; (2) a Filter that removes conflicting or invalid instructions to maintain data quality; (3) a Follower model that is trained via reinforcement learning to follow these evolving instructions; and (4) a Judger that evaluates the Follower’s responses and produces reward signals. The Instructor and Follower are alternately trained so that as the Follower improves, the Instructor generates more challenging instructions, leading to co-evolution of tasks and capabilities.

Result: Across different model sizes and architectures, applying SEIF leads to consistent gains in instruction-following benchmarks, indicating that the framework is broadly applicable. The authors’ analyses further dissect which components contribute to the gains and how training schedules affect performance.

Conclusion: A self-evolving RL framework that couples evolving instruction difficulty with model learning can effectively and generally improve LLM instruction-following. An identified best practice is to perform substantial training at early stages to establish strong base capabilities, then apply only moderate additional training later to avoid overfitting and achieve better final performance.

Abstract: Instruction following is a fundamental capability of large language models (LLMs), yet continuously improving this capability remains challenging. Existing methods typically rely either on costly external supervision from humans or strong teacher models, or on self-play training with static-difficulty instructions that cannot evolve as the model's capabilities improve. To address these limitations, we propose SEIF (Self-Evolving Reinforcement Learning for Instruction Following), a self-evolving framework for enhancing the instruction-following ability of LLMs. SEIF forms a closed self-evolution loop that improves the model's instruction-following ability, where instruction difficulty evolution and model capability evolution reinforce each other. SEIF consists of four roles: an Instructor that generates increasingly challenging instructions, a Filter that removes conflicting or invalid instructions to ensure data quality, a Follower that learns to follow evolved instructions, and a Judger that provides reward signals for reinforcement learning. The Instructor and Follower are alternately trained and co-evolve throughout the process. Experiments across multiple model scales and architectures show that SEIF consistently improves instruction-following performance, suggesting strong generality. Further analyses reveal the sources of improvement and identify an effective training strategy for self-evolution on open-ended tasks: sufficient early-stage training to build a solid foundation, followed by moderate late-stage training to mitigate overfitting and achieve better final performance. The code and data are publicly available at https://github.com/Rainier-rq1/SEIF.

</details>

### [55] [Why do Large Language Models Fail in Low-resource Translation? Unraveling the Token Dynamics of Large Language Models for Machine Translation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07533)
*Shenbin Qian,Yves Scherrer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes when and why LLM-based machine translation fails, introducing a token-level metric (TAR) that links language representation to translation quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs show strong average performance in machine translation, existing work mostly benchmarks quality without explaining systematic failure patterns, especially across different language pairs and resource levels. The authors aim to identify structural weaknesses and understand how representation of different languages within LLMs affects translation performance.

Method: They evaluate 15 large language models, including four reasoning-focused models, on 22 language pairs with different resource levels. They compare English-centric vs non-English-centric pairs using COMET scores, and propose Token Activation Rate (TAR), measuring how often language-specific tokens are activated during generation. They validate TAR as a proxy for language representation using models with known training language distributions, and correlate TAR with translation quality. They also analyze how reasoning LLMs change their token usage, especially in low-TAR target languages.

Result: Non-English-centric language pairs systematically underperform English-centric ones in COMET. TAR is strongly correlated with translation quality: lower TAR languages exhibit worse MT performance. In low-TAR target languages, reasoning LLMs generate longer outputs (more tokens), which appears to be a compensatory behavior, though its effectiveness varies by model.

Conclusion: Token-level dynamics, captured by TAR, are crucial to understanding LLM machine translation behavior. Poorly represented (low-TAR) languages in the model’s vocabulary correspond to worse translation performance, and reasoning-style LLMs react by generating more tokens without consistently improving quality. This highlights inherent representational imbalances across languages and suggests that improving language-specific token utilization could enhance MT performance in low-resource and non-English-centric settings.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have recently demonstrated strong performance in machine translation (MT). However, most prior work focuses on improving or benchmarking translation quality, offering limited insight into when and why LLM-based translation fails. In this work, we systematically analyze failure modes of LLMs in MT by evaluating 15 models, including four reasoning LLMs, across 22 language pairs (LPs) with varying resource levels. We find that non-English-centric LPs consistently yield lower COMET scores than English-centric pairs. To investigate the underlying causes, we introduce Token Activation Rate (TAR), a metric that captures how effectively a model utilizes language-specific tokens in its vocabulary during generation. We validate TAR as a proxy for language representation using models with known language distributions in the training data, and show that lower TAR is strongly associated with poorer translation performance. Furthermore, reasoning LLMs tend to generate more tokens when translating into low-TAR languages, suggesting a compensatory mechanism, although its impact on translation quality varies across models. Overall, our findings emphasize the importance of token-level dynamics in understanding MT performance of LLMs.

### [56] [Nürnberg NLP at PsyDefDetect: Multi-Axis Voter Ensembles for Psychological Defence Mechanism Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07606)
*Philipp Steigerwald,Eric Rudolph,Jens Albrecht*

Even trained human annotators only reach moderate agreement, highlighting the ambiguity and noise in the task. This calls for methods that can cope with overlapping class boundaries and label uncertainty rather than relying on a single deterministic model.

Method: The authors participate in the PsyDefDetect shared task at BioNLP 2026 and propose a 9-voter ensemble system. The ensemble is designed along three orthogonal axes: (1) class granularity, where one 'gatekeeper' model is trained on all nine classes (including presumably a non-defence or other class) while eight specialist models handle only the defence classes; (2) training method, combining both generative and discriminative models; and (3) base model diversity, using different underlying language models or architectures. The goal is to leverage error independence between diverse models to better handle the ambiguous boundaries between defence categories.

Result: On the hidden test set of the PsyDefDetect shared task, the proposed ensemble system achieves an F1 score of 0.420. This performance ranks first among 21 registered teams, indicating that the ensemble outperforms other approaches on this challenging task.

Conclusion: The study concludes that for highly ambiguous, pragmatically defined categories such as psychological defence mechanisms, performance gains come less from building a stronger single model and more from exploiting error independence via diverse ensembles. Carefully designed multi-axis ensembles can overcome some of the limitations posed by overlapping categories and moderate human agreement, leading to state-of-the-art performance in shared tasks like PsyDefDetect.

Abstract: Detecting levels of psychological defence mechanisms in supportive conversations is inherently ambiguous. In the PsyDefDetect shared task at BioNLP 2026 the eight positive defence categories share surface language and differ only in pragmatic function and trained raters reach only moderate inter-annotator agreement. On such a task the decisive lever is not a stronger single model but error independence, since any single representation will waver on the overlapping defence boundaries. We translate this insight into a 9-voter ensemble spanning three orthogonal axes: class granularity (all nine classes for the gatekeeper, only the eight defence classes for the specialists), training method (generative and discriminative) and base model. The system reaches $F1_{test}{=}.420$ on the hidden test set, placing first among 21 registered teams.

### [57] [Intent-Driven Semantic ID Generation for Grounded Conversational News Recommendation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07613)
*Hongyang Su,Beibei Kong,Lei Cheng,Chengxiang Zhuo,Zang Li,Chenyun Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an intent-driven Semantic ID (SID) generation framework for conversational news recommendation that overcomes the retrieve-first bottleneck in standard RAG by mapping implicit user intents to semantic ID prefixes and then matching them to a dynamic news corpus, achieving grounded, low-hallucination recommendations even for cold-start users.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RAG-based conversational recommenders struggle when user intents are implicit and lack explicit retrievable keywords, especially in fast-changing domains like news. This causes a retrieve-first bottleneck where relevant articles are not retrieved, leading to hallucinations or irrelevant suggestions. The authors observe six real-world intent types—mostly implicit—that break conventional retrieval pipelines and need a new approach tailored to intent understanding and robust grounding in an evolving article pool.

Method: They propose an intent-driven Semantic ID (SID) generation approach under a Generate-then-Match paradigm. An LLM is trained in two stages: (1) multi-task SID alignment so the model learns to map different user intents to hierarchical SID prefixes that represent regions of the news space; (2) GPT-4 Chain-of-Thought distillation to strengthen reasoning and intent interpretation. At inference, the LLM first generates SID prefixes from conversation context (and/or user profile) and then these SIDs are fuzzily matched against the current news pool to select articles, ensuring grounding. They further add Profile-Aware Dual-Signal Reasoning (PADR), which leverages only user profiles as signals to generate useful SID prefixes for cold-start users, combining conversational and profile signals when available.

Result: On a large-scale Chinese news platform, their 7B model obtains 0% hallucination and a 12.4% top-1 (L1) match rate in a 152K-size open-generation SID space, which is 4x better than a random baseline. The model matches GPT-4 with a Hybrid RAG setup on L1 accuracy and outperforms it on finer-grained metrics, doubling L2 performance and improving category match by 1.2 percentage points, while operating at roughly 100x lower cost. For cold-start users—where previous baselines achieve 0%—the method reaches 18.0% L1 accuracy (6x random), the best across all user segments.

Conclusion: Intent-driven SID generation with a Generate-then-Match framework effectively bypasses the retrieve-first bottleneck in conversational news recommendation. By aligning LLMs to semantic ID spaces and distilling reasoning from GPT-4, the system can handle diverse implicit intents and remain grounded in a rapidly evolving news corpus. PADR enables strong performance even for cold-start users. Overall, the approach provides GPT-4-level recommendation quality at a fraction of the cost while eliminating hallucination and improving fine-grained matching performance.

Abstract: Conversational news recommendation requires grounding each suggestion in a rapidly evolving article corpus while addressing implicit user intents that lack explicit retrievable keywords. To characterize this scenario, we identify 6 intent types from production dialogues: five are implicit and pose fundamental challenges to standard RAG pipelines, forming a critical retrieve-first bottleneck. To address these issues, we introduce intent-driven Semantic ID (SID) generation under a Generate-then-Match paradigm. With two-stage training that consists of multi-task SID alignment and GPT-4 Chain-of-Thought distillation, an LLM maps diverse intents to hierarchical SID prefixes, which are then fuzzy-matched to the current news pool to guarantee fully grounded recommendations. Profile-Aware Dual-Signal Reasoning (PADR) further enables cold-start users to obtain valid recommendations using only profiles. On a mainstream Chinese news platform, our 7B model achieves 0% hallucination and 12.4% L1 match in the 152K open-generation SID space (4x random baseline). It matches GPT-4+Hybrid RAG on L1 while surpassing it on finer-grained metrics (L2 2x, Category +1.2pp) at ~100x lower cost. Cold-start users, where existing baselines score 0%, achieve 18.0% L1 (6x random), the highest among all user groups.

*Jonas Klein,Chiara Manna,Eva Vanmassenhove*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how gender bias emerges during training in a Dutch BERT model, showing that gender becomes linearly encoded early and that the model defaults to male interpretations and stereotypes despite explicit contextual cues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most gender bias research in large language models focuses on English and on fully trained models, leaving a gap in understanding how bias forms over time in languages with grammatical or morphological gender like Dutch. The authors aim to study when and how gender information becomes encoded during training, and whether contextual information can counteract stereotypical and male-default biases in such a setting.

Method: They train a Dutch BERT model from scratch and save intermediate checkpoints across training. For each checkpoint, they extract contextual embeddings and use linear SVMs to build dynamic gender subspaces that capture how linearly separable gender information is over time and across embedding dimensions. They then design controlled sentence templates with explicit gender cues (e.g., ‘She is a plumber’) and stereotypical vs anti‑stereotypical gender–profession pairings to test whether these explicit cues can override learned statistical gender associations, and how generic Dutch forms are interpreted.

Result: Gender information becomes clearly linearly separable around epoch 20 and is distributed over multiple embedding dimensions. However, the model does not reliably update its internal gender representation when given explicit gender cues in short, controlled templates. It predicts stereotypical gender‑profession pairings much more accurately than anti‑stereotypical ones, and Dutch generic forms are consistently interpreted as male, even when context indicates a female referent.

Conclusion: The Dutch BERT model develops a robust, linearly decodable gender representation relatively early in training, but this representation is not flexibly updated by local contextual cues in the probed settings. As a result, the model exhibits persistent male‑default interpretations and strong adherence to gender stereotypes, indicating that contextualization along the gender direction in the embedding space is insufficiently dynamic and that bias mitigation must address how such representations are formed and used during training.

Abstract: Gender bias in large language models has primarily been investigated for English, while languages with grammatical or morphological gender remain comparatively understudied. This paper investigates how and when gender information emerges in a Dutch BERT model trained from scratch, offering one of the first checkpoint-level analyses of bias formation in a Transformer architecture for a language combining overt morphological gender marking and generic forms. By extracting contextual embeddings throughout training, we construct dynamic gender subspaces using linear SVMs to trace when gender becomes linearly encoded and how this encoding evolves over time. Contextual embeddings are often assumed to integrate contextual cues robustly, allowing models to adjust the representation of a word depending on its more local usage. We therefore test whether explicit gender cues in controlled sentence templates (e.g., Zij is een loodgieter ('She is a plumber')) can override learned statistical associations (plumber -> male). Our findings challenge this assumption: although gender becomes clearly linearly separable around epoch 20 and is distributed across multiple embedding dimensions, the model struggles to update its internal gender representation in light of explicit contextual cues in short sentence templates. Stereotypical gender-profession pairings are predicted far more accurately than anti-stereotypical ones, and generic forms in Dutch systematically default to a male interpretation, even when the context explicitly denotes a female referent. Together, our results seem to indicate that contextualization in the representations learned by our Dutch BERT model is not sufficiently dynamic along the probed gender direction: explicit gender cues in anti-stereotypical contexts are not reliably reflected in the resulting representations, resulting in persistent male-default behaviour.

*Zhengyang Tang,Yi Zhang,Chenxin Li,Xin Lai,Pengyuan Lyu,Yiduo Guo,Weinong Wang,Junyi Li,Yang Ding,Huawen Shen,Zhengyao Fang,Xingran Zhou,Liang Wu,Fei Tang,Sunqi Fan,Shangpin Peng,Zheng Ruan,Anran Zhang,Benyou Wang,Chengquan Zhang,Han Hu*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of phone-use agents conflate different reasons why harmful outcomes are avoided. An agent might avoid harm because it understands the risk and deliberately chooses a safe action, or because it simply fails to act or to understand the screen. These distinct failure modes require different remedies, yet current benchmarks often lump them together as task success, refusal, or just look at whether the final outcome is harmful or not. This obscures whether models are actually making safe decisions at critical moments.

Method: The authors construct PhoneSafety, a benchmark of 700 safety-critical decision points extracted from real-world phone interactions across more than 130 apps. Each example isolates a single risky moment on the phone and asks whether the model takes a safe action, takes an unsafe action, or fails to do anything useful. They then evaluate eight representative phone-use agents with this framework, analyzing performance patterns and how failures distribute across different kinds of interfaces and tasks, as well as how results change under different evaluation protocols.

Result: The evaluation shows that higher general phone-use capability does not consistently translate into safer behavior at safety-critical moments. Models that excel on regular app tasks are not necessarily the ones that make safer choices when risk is present. The analysis further finds that "doing nothing useful" corresponds more to a lack of capability than to safety: such failures are concentrated in visually and operationally complex settings and their rate remains stable when the evaluation setup changes. Failures fall into two main categories: (1) unsafe decisions where the model is clearly able to act but chooses wrongly, and (2) inability to act in more demanding interfaces.

Conclusion: A harmless outcome on a phone interaction is insufficient evidence that an agent is safe. To meaningfully assess safety in phone-use agents, evaluations must distinguish between unsafe judgment and simple inability to act. PhoneSafety enables this separation by focusing on next-action decisions at risky moments, revealing that safety and capability are partially decoupled and need to be measured and addressed separately.

Abstract: When a phone-use agent avoids harm, does that show safety, or simply inability to act? Existing evaluations often cannot tell. A harmful outcome may be avoided because the agent recognized the risk and chose the safe action, or because it failed to understand the screen or execute any relevant action at all. These cases have different causes and call for different fixes, yet current benchmarks often merge them under task success, refusal, or final harmful outcome. We address this problem with PhoneSafety, a benchmark of 700 safety-critical moments drawn from real phone interactions across more than 130 apps. Each instance isolates the next decision at a risky moment and asks a simple question: does the model take the safe action, take the unsafe action, or fail to do anything useful? We evaluate eight representative phone-use agents under this framework. Our results reveal two main patterns. First, stronger general phone-use ability does not reliably imply safer choices at risky moments. Models that perform better on ordinary app tasks are not always the ones that behave more safely when the next action matters. Second, failures to do anything useful behave like a capability signal rather than a safety signal: they are concentrated in more visually and operationally demanding settings and remain stable when the evaluation protocol changes. Across models, failures split into two recurring patterns: unsafe choices in settings where the model can act but chooses wrongly, and inability to act in more visually and operationally demanding screens. Overall, a harmless outcome is not enough to count as evidence of safety. Evaluating phone-use agents requires separating unsafe judgment from inability to act.

Method: The authors build Psych-201, a dataset of human behavioral responses across many tasks, and use it to quantitatively compare human responses to outputs from various LLMs. They evaluate models before and after post-training across different families, sizes, and objectives, and also test persona-induction prompts that condition models on participant-like profiles to see whether that improves individual-level predictions.

Result: Across model families, sizes, and objectives, post-training consistently reduces alignment between LLM outputs and human behavioral data, and newer post-trained generations are even less aligned despite improvements in their base counterparts. Conclusion: Current post-training procedures that make LLMs good assistants systematically push them away from human-like behavioral patterns, and popular prompting strategies such as persona-induction do not restore person-level alignment. If LLMs are to be used as accurate models of human behavior, training and prompting methods must be rethought or specialized for that purpose rather than reusing assistant-oriented pipelines.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used as surrogates for human participants, but it remains unclear which models best capture human behavior and why. To address this, we introduce Psych-201, a novel dataset that enables us to measure behavioral alignment at scale. We find that post-training -- the stage that turns base models into useful assistants -- consistently reduces alignment with human behavior across model families, sizes, and objectives. Moreover, this misalignment widens in newer model generations even as base models continue to improve. Finally, we find that persona-induction -- a popular technique for eliciting human-like behavior by conditioning models on participant-specific information -- does not improve predictions at the level of individuals. Taken together, our results suggest that the very processes that are currently employed to turn LLMs into useful assistants also make them less accurate models of human behavior.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automated grammatical error correction is widely deployed in educational platforms, but there is little systematic evaluation of the newest LLMs on grammar correction, limited understanding of whether ensembles of these models provide benefits, and inadequate quantification of how much standard reference-based metrics underestimate system performance. This creates uncertainty for educators choosing tools and may lead to underappreciating the true capabilities of modern GEC systems.

Method: The authors evaluate latest-generation LLMs, including a fine-tuned GPT-4o, on three key dimensions: edit precision (accuracy of corrections), fluency preservation (maintaining natural language flow), and meaning retention (preserving original semantics). Result: Fine-tuned GPT-4o achieves state-of-the-art performance on edit precision, fluency preservation, and meaning retention among evaluated systems. Evaluation against gold references reveals that 73.76% of GPT-4o corrections that differ from the reference are nonetheless valid or better, indicating that reference-based automatic metrics substantially underestimate system performance.

Conclusion: Latest-generation LLMs, especially fine-tuned GPT-4o, provide highly effective grammatical error correction, but their performance is not fully captured by traditional reference-based metrics, which miss many valid or superior corrections. Because individual LLMs show strongly similar error correction patterns, combining them may yield limited diversity gains. The findings offer guidance for educators and practitioners in selecting GEC assistants that support, rather than limit, learners’ linguistic development, and the released data, code, and models facilitate further research and benchmarking.

In this study, first, we evaluate latest-generation LLMs on edit precision, fluency preservation, and meaning retention, showing fine-tuned GPT-4o achieves state-of-the-art performance across all three dimensions. Second, through grammatical error type analysis we demonstrate that individual LLMs exhibit highly similar error correction patterns ($ρ=0.947$). Third, we show that reference-based metrics underestimate GEC performance with 73.76% of GPT-4o corrections different from gold standards being equally valid or even superior. These GEC evaluation findings equip educators with guidance for selecting GEC assistants that enhance rather than constrain student linguistic development. We make our data, code, and models publicly available.

### [62] [MAVEN: Multi-Agent Verification-Elaboration Network with In-Step Epistemic Auditing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07646)
*Yinsheng Yao,Jiehao Tang,Zhaozhen Yang,Dawei Cheng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MAVEN, a modular multi-agent framework that improves LLM reasoning via adversarial, role-separated deliberation and in-step verification, outperforming existing explicit and latent reasoning baselines across multiple QA benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing chain-of-thought style reasoning in LLMs often uses long, monolithic reasoning chains without intermediate checks. Early mistakes then propagate, making it hard to audit or trust the reasoning, especially for high-stakes tasks. The authors want a framework that is modular, allows granular verification and auditing, and can be plugged into different backbone models to enhance epistemic reliability.

Method: They propose MAVEN (Multi-Agent Verification-Elaboration Network with In-Step Epistemic Auditing), a blackboard-style multi-agent architecture. MAVEN explicitly decouples roles into at least three agents: a Skeptic, a Researcher, and a Judge. These agents interact in an adversarial-but-cooperative loop on a shared workspace: the Researcher proposes reasoning steps and factual claims, the Skeptic challenges logical and factual weaknesses, and the Judge arbitrates and synthesizes the final answer. This process enforces stepwise verification, separates logical defense from factual grounding, and yields structured, modular reasoning traces that can be audited. The framework is designed to be model-agnostic and can wrap different LLM backbones.

Result: On four benchmarks—OpenBookQA, TruthfulQA, HALUEVAL, and StrategyQA—MAVEN improves reasoning quality along four fine-grained metrics (not enumerated here, but likely including correctness, consistency, calibration, and justification quality). It outperforms both latent reasoning models like GEMINI-3.1-Pro and consensus-based approaches such as ReConcile. The gains come from producing explicit, modular, verifiable deliberation trajectories rather than relying on hidden internal reasoning or simple voting/consensus over independent samples.

Conclusion: MAVEN is an effective, model-agnostic reasoning enhancer that turns LLMs into more deliberate and auditable reasoners via explicit role separation and adversarial deliberation. It consistently boosts performance across diverse backbones and benchmarks, suggesting that well-designed multi-agent, step-verified reasoning frameworks can be a powerful alternative to monolithic chain-of-thought or purely latent reasoning approaches.

Abstract: While explicit reasoning trajectories enhance model interpretability, existing paradigms often rely on monolithic chains that lack intermediate verification, allowing early errors to cascade unchecked. This lack of modularity impedes granular auditing and compromises the epistemic trust required for high-stakes applications. We propose MAVEN (Multi-Agent Verification-Elaboration Network with In-Step Epistemic Auditing), a blackboard-inspired framework designed to transform LLMs into deliberate reasoners through explicit role-decoupling. At its core, MAVEN operationalizes an adversarial Skeptic-Researcher-Judge loop, simulating expert deliberation by functionally separating logical defense from factual grounding. Experiments on OpenBookQA, TruthfulQA, HALUEVAL and StrategyQA benchmarks demonstrate that MAVEN delivers superior reasoning quality across four fine-grained metrics. Notably, MAVEN consistently outperforms latent reasoning models such as GEMINI-3.1-Pro and consensus-based baselines (e.g., ReConcile) by generating explicitly structured, modular, and verifiable deliberation trajectories, rather than relying on implicit internal states or post-hoc consensus. Moreover, comprehensive evaluations confirm that MAVEN is fully model-agnostic, serving as a strong and transferable reasoning booster that yields substantial performance improvements across diverse backbone models.

### [63] [Quality-Conditioned Agreement in Automated Short Answer Scoring: Mid-Range Degradation and the Impact of Task-Specific Adaptation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07647)
*Abigail Victoria Gurin Schleifer,Moriah Ariely,Beata Beigman Klebanov,Asaf Salman,Giora Alexandron*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As automated short answer scoring shifts from fine-tuned models to few-shot LLMs, there is little understanding of how this affects the fair and accurate scoring of partially correct student answers that need nuanced interpretation. The authors want to know how different levels of task-specific adaptation influence scoring quality, especially for responses from students with developing understanding, and whether this creates fairness concerns.

Method: The authors evaluate three few-shot LLMs (GPT-5.2, GPT-4o, Claude Opus 4.5), a fine-tuned BERT-based encoder model, and a human expert on two open-ended biology questions. They use several hundred real student responses that have expert-provided ground truth scores. They analyze agreement between model/human scores and expert scores across the full quality spectrum, focusing on how performance changes for low-, mid-, and high-quality responses.

Result: Human–human agreement is highest and consistent across all response quality levels. All AI models achieve good agreement for clearly correct and clearly incorrect answers but show large performance drops on mid-range, partially correct answers. This degradation is strongest for few-shot LLMs with limited examples and diminishes as the amount of task-specific data and adaptation increases; the fine-tuned encoder performs best on these mid-range cases.

Conclusion: Few-shot LLMs and even adapted models are reliable for extreme cases (fully correct or incorrect responses) but are least reliable on mid-range, partially correct answers, where nuanced understanding is required. The severity of this mid-range degradation depends on how much the model has been adapted to the specific scoring task. Because these mid-range responses often come from students with developing understanding, such degradation raises fairness concerns. The authors argue that automated scoring systems should be evaluated and designed with quality-conditioned fairness in mind, paying special attention to performance on mid-range responses and the benefits of deeper task-specific adaptation.

Abstract: Automated short answer scoring (ASAS) is shifting from discriminative, fine-tuned models to large language models (LLMs) used in few-shot settings. This paradigm leverages LLMs broad world knowledge and ease of deployment, but limited task-specific data may reduce alignment on complex scoring tasks. In particular, its impact on scoring partially correct responses that require nuanced interpretation remains underexplored. We investigate the relationship between the degree of task-specific adaptation of different models and quality-conditioned scoring agreement. We compare three LLMs (GPT-5.2, GPT-4o, Claude Opus 4.5) in few-shot mode, a fine-tuned BERT-based encoder, and a human expert on two open-ended biology items, using several hundred student responses and ground truth scores provided by a biology education expert. The results show that human-human agreement is highest and stable across the full quality spectrum. All AI models perform well on fully correct and fully incorrect responses, but exhibit substantial degradation on mid-range responses. This mid-range degradation is conditioned on task-specific adaptation: It is most severe in few-shot LLMs with few examples and decreases as task-specific data increases, with fine-tuned encoder models performing best. This mid-range degradation may lead to inequitable evaluation of responses produced by students with developing understanding. Our findings highlight the importance of quality-conditioned fairness, with particular attention to mid-range responses.
