### [64] [Not All Tokens Learn Alike: Attention Entropy Reveals Heterogeneous Signals in RL Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07660)
*Gengyang Li,Zheng-Fan Wu,Siqi Bao,Yunfang Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes token-level reinforcement-learning (RL) signals for reasoning post-training in LLMs using attention entropy, revealing distinct roles for low-entropy “anchor” tokens and high-entropy “explorer” tokens, and shows that entropy-aware weighting improves model performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While RL-based post-training improves LLM reasoning, it is unclear how learning signals vary across tokens and whether uniform treatment of all tokens is optimal. The authors aim to uncover structure in token-level RL signals that might explain redundancy, instability, and the mixed effectiveness of current reasoning-focused RL methods.

Method: They introduce attention entropy as a measure of how concentrated or diffuse the contextual support is for each response token. They perform several experiments: (1) random token subset training to assess redundancy; (2) entropy-structured token subset training to contrast low-entropy (anchor) and high-entropy (explorer) tokens; (3) analyses of evidence gathering, entropy dynamics over training, and gradient geometry; and (4) control analyses to rule out confounders such as token position, predictive entropy, and loss normalization. Finally, they design a dynamic entropy-aware soft-reweighting scheme for RL updates and evaluate it on Qwen3-8B-Base.

Result: They find that only about 20% of randomly chosen tokens are sufficient to preserve much of the full-token held-out performance, indicating substantial redundancy in token-level RL updates. Low-attention-entropy anchor tokens yield stable gradients, align well with full-token gradients, and form a robust optimization backbone but saturate on more difficult reasoning benchmarks. High-attention-entropy explorer tokens generate larger, more volatile gradients and make training unstable on average, though occasional runs succeed and appear to capture challenging reasoning signals. Gradient geometry, entropy dynamics, and other diagnostics confirm a meaningful anchor–explorer spectrum. A dynamic entropy-aware soft-reweighting intervention improves Qwen3-8B-Base’s average held-out performance from 34.39 to 37.40 in their strongest setting.

Conclusion: Token-level RL signals are heterogeneous and structured along an attention-entropy spectrum: low-entropy anchor tokens provide reliable but limited optimization guidance, while high-entropy explorer tokens encode harder reasoning signals at the cost of instability. Standard uniform token averaging obscures this structure and can be suboptimal. Incorporating attention-entropy-based weighting into RL post-training can yield more stable and effective reasoning improvements, as demonstrated by performance gains on Qwen3-8B-Base.

Abstract: Reinforcement-learning-based post-training has become a key approach for improving the reasoning ability of large language models, but its token-level learning signals remain poorly understood. This work studies their heterogeneity through attention entropy, which measures how concentrated or diffuse the contextual support is for each response token.
  We first show that token-level RL objectives are sparsely estimable: uniformly random 20 percent token subsets preserve much of the full-token held-out performance, suggesting substantial redundancy in token-level updates. However, entropy-structured subsets behave very differently. Low-attention-entropy tokens, which we call anchors, rely on concentrated support, produce stable gradients aligned with full-token updates, and provide a reliable optimization backbone, but tend to plateau on harder benchmarks. High-attention-entropy tokens, which we call explorers, aggregate more diffuse context and induce larger but more volatile gradients. Explorer-only training is unstable on average, though rare successful runs suggest that these tokens may contain useful hard-reasoning signals when optimization remains stable.
  We support this anchor-explorer spectrum with evidence-gathering analyses, entropy dynamics, gradient-geometry diagnostics, and controls showing that position, predictive entropy, and loss normalization do not explain the observed asymmetry. Finally, a dynamic entropy-aware soft-reweighting intervention improves Qwen3-8B-Base from 34.39 to 37.40 held-out average in the strongest setting. These findings suggest that attention entropy reveals optimization-relevant structure in token-level RL signals, and that uniform token averaging can obscure meaningful heterogeneity in reasoning post-training.

</details>


### [65] [DRIP-R: A Benchmark for Decision-Making and Reasoning Under Real-World Policy Ambiguity in the Retail Domain](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07699)
*Hsuvas Borkakoty,Sebastian Pohl,Cheng Wang,Bei Chen,Yufang Hou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents DRIP-R, a benchmark for testing LLM-based agents in realistic retail return scenarios where store policies are ambiguous and allow multiple valid interpretations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

This creates a gap in evaluating how LLM agents handle situations where there is no single correct answer, especially when those decisions are consequential (e.g., customer returns and refunds).

Method: The authors construct DRIP-R, a benchmark built from real-world retail return policies and their ambiguities. They design a curated set of ambiguous return scenarios, associate them with realistic customer personas, and implement a full-duplex conversational simulation where agents can call tools. They also design a multi-judge evaluation framework that scores model behavior on policy adherence, dialogue quality, behavioral alignment, and resolution quality.

Result: When evaluating frontier LLMs on DRIP-R, the models show fundamental disagreements on how to resolve the same ambiguous scenarios. This divergence appears systematically across the benchmark, indicating that policy ambiguity leads to inconsistent decision-making among models.

Conclusion: DRIP-R reveals that policy ambiguity is a real and systematic challenge for LLM-based agents, causing them to disagree in non-trivial ways on the same tasks. Benchmarks and deployment practices must explicitly account for this ambiguity rather than assuming a single correct resolution exists.

Abstract: LLM-based agents are increasingly deployed for routine but consequential tasks in real-world domains, where their behavior is governed by inherently ambiguous domain policies that admit multiple valid interpretations. Despite the prevalence of such ambiguities in practice, existing agent benchmarks largely assume unambiguous, well-specified policies, leaving a critical evaluation gap. We introduce DRIP-R, a benchmark that systematically exploits real-world retail policy ambiguities to construct scenarios in which no single correct resolution exists. DRIP-R comprises a curated set of policy-ambiguous return scenarios paired with a realistic customer personas, a full-duplex conversational simulation with tool-calling capabilities and a multi-judge evaluation framework covering policy adherence, dialogue quality, behavioral alignment, and resolution quality. Our experiments show that frontier models fundamentally disagree on identical policy-ambiguous scenarios, confirming that ambiguity poses a genuine and systematic challenge to LLM decision-making.

### [66] [Guidance Is Not a Hyperparameter: Learning Dynamic Control in Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07701)
*Fan Zhou,Tim Van de Cruys*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They replace fixed classifier-free guidance scales in diffusion language models with dynamically learned, step-wise guidance policies using reinforcement learning, improving the tradeoff between control and text quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fixed CFG scales are suboptimal because the ideal strength of guidance varies by task and by step in the diffusion process, especially in NLP; this limits both controllability and generation quality.

Method: Formulate guidance-scale selection as a sequential decision problem where, at each diffusion step, a policy chooses a discrete guidance value based on the current diffusion state; train this policy with task-level rewards using Proximal Policy Optimization on controlled text generation tasks.

Result: On three controlled NLP generation benchmarks with discrete diffusion LMs, policies that adapt guidance over time consistently outperform any fixed-scale baseline in balancing control accuracy and text quality.

Conclusion: Classifier-free guidance should be treated as a dynamic control variable; learning guidance trajectories via RL yields interpretable, task-specific schedules that better navigate the tradeoff between control and generation quality than static settings.

Abstract: Classifier-Free Guidance (CFG) is a widely used mechanism for controlling diffusion-based generative models, yet its guidance scale is typically treated as a fixed hyperparameter throughout generation. This static design yields a suboptimal controllability and quality tradeoff, as the optimal degree of guidance varies across tasks and across different stages of the diffusion process, especially in NLP domain. We recast CFG scale selection as a sequential decision-making problem and propose to learn dynamic guidance trajectories via reinforcement learning. Specifically, we model the guidance scale as a discrete control action selected at each generation step based on the evolving diffusion state, and optimize a policy using Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) under task-level rewards. Experiments on three controlled NLP generation tasks using discrete diffusion language models demonstrate that adaptive guidance consistently achieves a better balance between controllability and generation quality than fixed-scale strategies. Further analysis of the learned policies reveals distinct and interpretable guidance trajectories across tasks, underscoring the importance of treating guidance as a dynamic control process rather than a static design choice.

### [67] [SimCT: Recovering Lost Supervision for Cross-Tokenizer On-Policy Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07711)
*Jie Sun,Mao Zheng,Mingyang Song,Qiyong Zhong,Yilin Cheng,Bichuan Feng,Pengfei Liu,Junfeng Fang,Xiang Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SimCT, a method for on-policy distillation between models with different tokenizers that recovers lost teacher supervision by using the finest shared multi-token units rather than only exact shared tokens, yielding better student performance on math reasoning and code generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard on-policy distillation assumes teacher and student share a compatible tokenization, enabling token-wise comparison of predictions. When their tokenizers differ (heterogeneous tokenizers), many positions in the sequence are not aligned at the token level, so exact shared-token matching discards a large part of the teacher’s signal. This weakens knowledge transfer especially in domains like math and code where tokenization differences can be substantial. The authors are motivated to design an OPD method that works effectively across heterogeneous tokenizers without changing the overall loss structure.

Method: They propose Simple Cross-Tokenizer OPD (SimCT). Instead of matching only exactly shared tokens between teacher and student, SimCT expands the supervision space to also include short multi-token continuations (i.e., short sequences of tokens) that both tokenizers can represent for the same text span. These continuations act as the basic supervision units. The OPD loss form remains the same, but it is applied over this enlarged set of jointly tokenizable units. The authors argue and show that these short continuations constitute the finest-grained supervision interface that both tokenizers can share; coarser groupings blur distinctions in the teacher’s behavior that are important for on-policy learning.

Result: Using three heterogeneous teacher–student pairs on mathematical reasoning and code-generation benchmarks, SimCT consistently outperforms standard shared-vocabulary OPD and several cross-tokenizer baselines. Ablation studies confirm that the gains specifically come from recovering supervision that would otherwise be discarded when restricting to exact shared-token matches.

Conclusion: SimCT provides an effective, simple way to perform on-policy distillation across models with different tokenizers by supervising on short, shared multi-token continuations. This recovers much of the teacher’s behavior that is lost under shared-token-only matching and leads to better student performance in math and code tasks, without modifying the fundamental OPD loss formulation.

Abstract: On-policy distillation (OPD) is a standard tool for transferring teacher behavior to a smaller student, but it implicitly assumes that teacher and student predictions are comparable token by token, an assumption that fails whenever the two models tokenize the same text differently. Under heterogeneous tokenizers, exact shared-token matching silently discards a large fraction of the teacher signal at precisely the positions where vocabularies disagree. We propose \textbf{\underline{Sim}ple \underline{C}ross-\underline{T}okenizer OPD (SimCT)}, which restores this signal by enlarging the supervision space: alongside shared tokens, SimCT compares teacher and student over short multi-token continuations that both tokenizers can realize, leaving the OPD loss form itself unchanged. We show that these units are the finest jointly tokenizable supervision interface, and that coarser alternatives remove teacher-student distinctions that are useful for on-policy learning. Across three heterogeneous teacher-student pairs on mathematical reasoning and code-generation benchmarks, SimCT shows consistent gains over shared-vocabulary OPD and representative cross-tokenizer baselines, with ablations confirming that the improvements come from recovering supervision discarded by exact shared-token matching. Code is available at \href{https://github.com/sunjie279/SimCT-}{https://github.com/sunjie279/SimCT-}.

</details>


### [68] [Memory-Efficient Looped Transformer: Decoupling Compute from Memory in Looped Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07721)
*Victor Conchello Vendrell,Arnau Padres Masdemont,Niccolò Grillo,Jordi Ros-Giralt,Arash Behboodi,Fabio Valerio Massoli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MELT, a looped transformer architecture that enables multi-iteration reasoning with constant memory by sharing and updating a single KV cache per layer across loops, matching or surpassing prior looped LLM performance while being much more memory-efficient.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing recurrent/looped LLMs like Ouro perform multi-step reasoning by iteratively updating internal representations but keep a separate KV cache for each iteration. This makes memory usage grow linearly with the number of reasoning steps, so deeper reasoning quickly becomes impractical on real hardware. There is a need for architectures that keep the benefits of iterative reasoning while avoiding prohibitive memory growth.

Method: They propose Memory-Efficient Looped Transformer (MELT), which uses a single KV cache per layer that is shared and updated across reasoning loops instead of allocating a new cache each iteration. The cache is updated via a learnable gating mechanism. To stably train this modified architecture from an existing looped model (LoopLM/Ouro), they use a two-phase chunk-wise training procedure: (1) interpolated transition from the original to MELT behavior, and (2) attention-aligned distillation so MELT’s attention patterns and behavior match the starting model while using the new memory-efficient mechanism.

Result: MELT models fine-tuned from pretrained Ouro parameters achieve better performance than standard, non-looped LLMs of similar size, while having a memory footprint similar to these standard models and far smaller than Ouro’s looped architecture. This shows that MELT retains the reasoning benefits of looped models without their heavy memory cost.

Conclusion: MELT decouples reasoning depth from memory usage by reusing and updating a single per-layer KV cache across loops, enabling constant-memory iterative reasoning. With a lightweight post-training procedure from an existing looped model, MELT preserves or improves LoopLM-level reasoning capabilities while making memory costs practical, thereby improving the scalability of recurrent LLM architectures.

Abstract: Recurrent LLM architectures have emerged as a promising approach for improving reasoning, as they enable multi-step computation in the embedding space without generating intermediate tokens. Models such as Ouro perform reasoning by iteratively updating internal representations while retaining a standard Key-Value (KV) cache across iterations, causing memory consumption to grow linearly with reasoning depth. Consequently, increasing the number of reasoning iterations can lead to prohibitive memory usage, limiting the practical scalability of such architectures. In this work, we propose Memory-Efficient Looped Transformer (MELT), a novel architecture that decouples reasoning depth from memory consumption. Instead of using a standard KV cache per layer and loop, MELT maintains a single KV cache per layer that is shared across reasoning loops. This cache is updated over time via a learnable gating mechanism. To enable stable and efficient training under this architecture, we propose to train MELT using chunk-wise training in a two phase procedure: interpolated transition, followed by attention-aligned distillation, both from the LoopLM starting model to MELT. Empirically, we show that MELT models fine-tuned from pretrained Ouro parameters outperform standard LLMs of comparable size, while maintaining a memory footprint comparable to those models and dramatically smaller than Ouro's. Overall, MELT achieves constant-memory iterative reasoning without sacrificing LoopLM performance, using only a lightweight post-training procedure.

### [69] [SOD: Step-wise On-policy Distillation for Small Language Model Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07725)
*Qiyong Zhong,Mao Zheng,Mingyang Song,Xin Lin,Jie Sun,Houcheng Jiang,Xiang Wang,Junfeng Fang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes SOD, a step-wise on-policy distillation framework that stabilizes tool-integrated reasoning for small language models by adaptively weighting teacher supervision at each reasoning step, leading to substantial performance gains on math, science, and coding benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Tool-integrated reasoning with small language models is hard to scale because long tool-use trajectories are unstable and small models have limited capacity. Existing RL methods offer only sparse rewards, and standard on-policy distillation, while dense, fails for TIR: early tool-call errors cause cascading deviations so that token-level supervision from a teacher becomes unreliable in later steps. The authors want a way to give small models rich guidance without amplifying errors in long-horizon tool interactions.

Method: They introduce SOD (Step-wise On-policy Distillation), which monitors the divergence between teacher and student at each reasoning step and adapts the strength of distillation accordingly. When step-level divergence is low, the student closely follows dense token-level guidance from the teacher; when divergence is high—often after erroneous tool calls—SOD downweights or attenuates the teacher’s supervision, reducing the harm from misleading signals in misaligned states. This reweighting is integrated into the training loop for small LMs performing tool-integrated reasoning.

Result: On challenging math, science, and coding benchmarks, SOD consistently outperforms prior methods, with up to 20.86% improvement over the second-best baseline. A 0.6B-parameter student model trained with SOD reaches 26.13% accuracy on AIME 2025, indicating strong transfer of agent-like reasoning abilities to a relatively small model.

Conclusion: Naïve on-policy distillation is unreliable for tool-integrated reasoning because divergence between student and teacher trajectories compounds over steps. By controlling distillation strength at the step level based on measured divergence, SOD stabilizes training and enables small language models to acquire powerful agentic reasoning skills from larger teachers, as demonstrated by significant gains on demanding reasoning benchmarks.

Abstract: Tool-integrated reasoning (TIR) is difficult to scale to small language models due to instability in long-horizon tool interactions and limited model capacity. While reinforcement learning methods like group relative policy optimization provide only sparse outcome-level rewards. Recently, on-policy distillation (OPD) has gained popularity by supplying dense token-level supervision from a teacher on student-generated trajectories. However, our experiments indicate that applying OPD to TIR leads to a critical failure mode: erroneous tool calls tend to cascade across subsequent reasoning steps, progressively amplifying student-teacher divergence and rendering the teacher's token-level supervision increasingly unreliable. To address this, we propose SOD, a step-wise on-policy distillation framework for small language model agents, which adaptively reweights distillation strength at each step based on step-level divergence. Therefore, SOD can attenuate potentially misleading teacher signals in high-divergence regions while preserving dense guidance in well-aligned states. Experiments on challenging math, science, and code benchmarks show that SOD achieves up to 20.86% improvement over the second-best baseline. Notably, our 0.6B student achieves 26.13% on AIME 2025, demonstrating effective transfer of agentic reasoning to lightweight models. Our code is available at https://github.com/YoungZ365/SOD.

</details>


### [70] [TextLDM: Language Modeling with Continuous Latent Diffusion](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07748)
*Jiaxiu Jiang,Jingjing Ren,Wenbo Li,Bo Wang,Haoze Sun,Yijun Yang,Jianhui Liu,Yanbing Zhang,Shenghe Zheng,Yuan Zhang,Haoyang Huang,Nan Duan,Wangmeng Zuo*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Unifying generation and understanding across modalities using a single diffusion-based architecture has been successful in vision (images/videos) but not yet in language. Existing diffusion language models lag behind strong autoregressive models, partly because directly modeling discrete tokens is hard and naively learned continuous latents may not be semantically rich enough for high-quality generation. The authors aim to see whether the successful visual DiT + VAE latent recipe can be transferred to text, and what is needed to make continuous text representations effective for diffusion-based generation.

Method: The authors design TextLDM, which mirrors the visual latent diffusion pipeline. First, a Transformer-based VAE maps discrete text tokens into a continuous latent space and reconstructs them back. To make these latents useful for conditional denoising, they introduce Representation Alignment (REPA), which aligns the VAE’s latent representations with those from a frozen pretrained language model, ensuring the latents capture semantic and contextual information similar to a strong LM. Then, a standard Diffusion Transformer (DiT) performs flow matching in this latent space, using the same architecture as visual DiTs but now over text latents instead of image/video latents. The system is trained from scratch on OpenWebText2 under controlled conditions comparable to GPT-2.

Result: When trained on OpenWebText2 from scratch, TextLDM significantly outperforms prior diffusion-based language models and reaches parity with GPT-2 under the same data and training settings. Ablation-type findings indicate that using only reconstruction fidelity for the VAE is not enough: applying REPA to align the latent space with a pretrained LM is crucial for strong downstream generation quality.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that the latent diffusion recipe successful in vision—DiT with flow matching over VAE latents—can be transferred to language modeling with minimal architectural changes, provided that the text latents are properly aligned with a strong pretrained LM. TextLDM’s results suggest that unified diffusion architectures across images, videos, and text are viable, marking progress toward a single multimodal framework for both generation and understanding tasks.

Abstract: Diffusion Transformers (DiT) trained with flow matching in a VAE latent space have unified visual generation across images and videos. A natural next step toward a single architecture for both generation (visual synthesis) and understanding (text generation) is to apply this framework to language modeling. We propose TextLDM, which transfers the visual latent diffusion recipe to text generation with minimal architectural modification. A Transformer-based VAE maps discrete tokens to continuous latents, enhanced by Representation Alignment (REPA) with a frozen pretrained language model to produce representations effective for conditional denoising. A standard DiT then performs flow matching in this latent space, identical in architecture to its visual counterpart. The central challenge we address is obtaining high-quality continuous text representations: we find that reconstruction fidelity alone is insufficient, and that aligning latent features with a pretrained language model via REPA is critical for downstream generation quality. Trained from scratch on OpenWebText2, TextLDM substantially outperforms prior diffusion language models and matches GPT-2 under the same settings. Our results establish that the visual DiT recipe transfers effectively to language, taking a concrete step toward unified diffusion architectures for multimodal generation and understanding.

### [71] [Chain-based Distillation for Effective Initialization of Variable-Sized Small Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07783)

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They build a distillation chain: starting from a large LLM, they create a sparse set of intermediate anchor models via stepwise distillation. For heterogeneous cases (different architectures or vocabularies), they add bridge distillation to transfer knowledge across these differences. New models of arbitrary intermediate sizes are then initialized by interpolating parameters between adjacent anchors, avoiding repeated access to the large teacher for every target size.

Result: Using CBD, they can initialize various-sized small language models more efficiently and with better downstream performance than scratch training. Notably, a 138M-parameter model initialized via CBD and trained without recovery pre-training beats scratch-trained models trained on a 10B-token corpus on the evaluated task.

Conclusion: Chain-based Distillation is an efficient and scalable framework for initializing variable-sized language models. By using a small number of distilled anchor models plus parameter interpolation—augmented with bridge distillation for heterogeneous settings—it reduces dependence on repeated large-teacher inference while improving performance and supporting different architectures and vocabularies.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) achieve strong performance but remain costly to deploy in resource-constrained settings. Training small language models (SLMs) from scratch is computationally expensive, while conventional knowledge distillation requires repeated access to large teachers for different target sizes, leading to poor scalability. To solve these problems, we propose \textbf{Chain-based Distillation (CBD)}, a scalable paradigm for efficiently initializing variable-sized language models. A sparse and limited sequence of intermediate models (called anchors) is constructed via stepwise distillation, forming a distillation chain that progressively transfers knowledge from the source LLMs. To support heterogeneous settings, we introduce \emph{bridge distillation} for cross-architecture and cross-vocabulary transfer. Models of variable sizes are initialized via parameter interpolation between adjacent anchors, eliminating repeated large teacher inference. Experiments show that the proposed method substantially improves efficiency and downstream performance. A 138M-parameter SLM without recovery pre-training, outperforms scratch-trained models on a 10B-token corpus on the specific task. CBD also demonstrates versatility in heterogeneous settings for initialize models with different architectures and vocabularies.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most Text-to-SQL benchmarks only support SQLite, but real-world databases use many dialects with different syntax, types, and functions. Evaluating only on SQLite creates a misleading picture of model performance, as shown by low agreement between SQLite and other dialects. Existing cross-dialect evaluation either needs costly manual transpilation or unreliable automatic tools, making robust multi-dialect evaluation very hard.

Method: The authors propose PolySQL, a dual-execution evaluation method that runs the original (possibly dialect-specific) SQL and the system-predicted SQL directly on their respective database engines, then compares normalized execution results instead of trying to transpile queries. PolySQL includes three curated datasets that span multiple SQL dialects. The framework normalizes outputs to enable cross-dialect equivalence checking and ensures 100% query coverage without transpilation failures.

Result: PolySQL allows the first large-scale, systematic cross-dialect evaluation of Text-to-SQL systems. The study shows that model accuracy drops on average by 10.1% when moving from SQLite to other SQL dialects, and it uncovers a clear hierarchy of dialect difficulty. Error analysis indicates that most performance degradation is due to logical errors in generated queries (61%) rather than purely syntactic issues (8%).

Conclusion: SQLite-only evaluation significantly overestimates Text-to-SQL performance and hides important cross-dialect weaknesses. By avoiding transpilation and relying on dual execution with normalized result comparison, PolySQL achieves higher-fidelity, full-coverage evaluation across SQL dialects. The released datasets, framework, and leaderboard are intended to become a standard for dialect-robust Text-to-SQL benchmarking and future research on handling diverse SQL dialects.

Abstract: SQL dialects vary in syntax, types, and functions across database engines. Text-to-SQL benchmarks, however, predominantly support only SQLite. This creates a critical evaluation gap: cross-dialect evaluation reveals weak per-query agreement (Cohen's ), showing that SQLite performance is an unreliable proxy for other dialects. Yet such evaluation remains prohibitively difficult: existing approaches either require expensive manual query transpilation or rely on tools that often fail on complex SQL. To close this gap, we introduce PolySQL, a novel dual-execution method that eliminates the need for query transpilation by comparing normalized execution results. Notably, our approach achieves higher evaluation fidelity than query transpilation with 100% query coverage. PolySQL comprises three datasets, enabling the first large-scale cross-dialect study. Our study reveals a 10.1% average accuracy drop from SQLite to other dialects and identifies a significant dialect difficulty hierarchy. We find this degradation stems from logical rather than syntactic errors (61% vs. 8%). We release our framework code and leaderboard to enable rigorous dialect-robust evaluation.

### [74] [Beyond Confidence: Rethinking Self-Assessments for Performance Prediction in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07806)
*Sree Bhattacharyya,Samarth Khanna,Leona Chen,Lucas Craig,Tharun Dilliraj,James Z. Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes and evaluates a multidimensional, psychology-inspired framework for LLM self-assessment, showing some dimensions outperform traditional confidence in predicting model failure.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing approaches rely mainly on single-dimensional confidence scores, which are often inconsistent and overoptimistic indicators of correctness. Inspired by cognitive appraisal theory in human psychology, the authors want to see whether richer, structured self-assessment signals can better predict when LLMs will fail, thereby improving reliability and safety in practical deployments.

Method: The authors draw from cognitive appraisal theory to design six distinct self-assessment dimensions (e.g., effort, ability, and affect-related appraisals) and elicit these from LLMs alongside standard confidence scores. They then systematically evaluate how well each dimension predicts model failure across 12 different language models and 38 tasks covering eight domains, comparing predictive power, calibration, and robustness across tasks and model sizes.

Result: Competence-related dimensions—especially self-reported effort and ability—consistently equal or outperform standard confidence in predicting when answers are wrong across most models and tasks. Effort, in particular, is better calibrated, less overoptimistic, and more stable across model scales. Affective dimensions yield only weak additional predictive signal. The most informative dimension is task-dependent: effort works best for reasoning-heavy tasks, while ability and confidence are more informative for retrieval-focused tasks.

Conclusion: Multidimensional, appraisal-based self-assessment provides richer and more reliable failure prediction than relying solely on confidence. Competence-related dimensions, especially effort and ability, are particularly valuable and interact systematically with task type. This suggests that structured self-assessment interfaces can meaningfully improve the reliability and safety of LLMs deployed in diverse real-world applications.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly used in settings where reliable self-assessment is critical. Assessing model reliability has evolved from using probabilistic correctness estimates to, more recently, eliciting verbalized confidence. Confidence, however, has been shown to be an inconsistent and overoptimistic predictor of model correctness. Drawing on cognitive appraisal theory, a framework from human psychology that decomposes self-evaluation into multiple components, we propose a multidimensional perspective on model self-assessment. We elicit six appraisal-based dimensions of self-assessment, alongside confidence, and evaluate their utility for predicting model failure across 12 LLMs and 38 tasks spanning eight domains. We find that competence-related appraisal dimensions, particularly effort and ability, consistently match or outperform confidence across most settings. Effort additionally yields less overoptimistic estimates that remain stable across model sizes. In contrast, affective dimensions provide marginally predictive signals. Furthermore, the most informative dimension varies systematically with task characteristics: effort is most predictive for reasoning-intensive tasks, while ability and confidence dominate on retrieval-oriented tasks. Broadly, our findings indicate that structured multidimensional self-assessment is a promising approach to improving the reliability and safety of language model deployment across diverse real-world settings.

  <summary>Details</summary>

A systematic way to measure this distributional mismatch is missing.

Method: Given corpora of real and simulated conversations, they encode user behaviors into vector representations for each conversation, cluster these representations to form discrete behavior types, then compare the resulting empirical distributions of cluster assignments using divergence metrics. Most simulators are behaviorally similar to each other, with only a few distinct ones. Combining complementary simulators better approximates the real user distribution than any individual simulator. TF-IDF over clusters reveals specific user behavior patterns that simulators reproduce, miss, or invent.

Abstract: As user simulators are increasingly used for interactive training and evaluation of AI assistants, it is essential that they represent the diverse behaviors of real users. While existing works train user simulators to generate human-like responses, whether they capture the broad and heterogeneous distribution of real user behaviors remains an open question. In this work, we introduce a method to measure the distributional gap between real and simulated user behaviors, validated through a human study and ablations. Given a dataset of real and simulated conversations, our method extracts representations of user behavior from each conversation, quantizes them into discrete distributions via clustering, then computes divergence metrics. We provide the first systematic evaluation of 24 LLM-based user simulators on coding and writing tasks, and reveal a large distributional gap from real users that varies across model families, scales, and behavioral facets. Pairwise comparisons show that most simulators behave similarly, while a few stand apart. Combining behaviorally complementary simulators brings the resulting distribution closer to real users compared to either simulator on its own. Finally, a TF-IDF analysis of the clusters surfaces interpretable patterns of behaviors that simulators capture, miss, and hallucinate.

### [78] [MatryoshkaLoRA: Learning Accurate Hierarchical Low-Rank Representations for LLM Fine-Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07850)
*Ionut-Vlad Modoranu,Mher Safaryan,Dan Alistarh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MatryoshkaLoRA is a training framework for LoRA that enables accurate hierarchical low-rank adapters and dynamic rank selection, outperforming existing rank-adaptive methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fine-tuning large deep learning models with billions of parameters is computationally expensive. LoRA reduces cost but requires a fixed rank that must be tuned via grid search. Existing adaptive-rank methods sample ranks but struggle to learn good representations at all ranks, leading to suboptimal and data-inefficient training. There is a need for a principled way to train hierarchical low-rank adapters that perform well across a spectrum of ranks without expensive searches.

Method: The paper introduces MatryoshkaLoRA, which inserts a fixed, specially designed diagonal scaling matrix P between LoRA adapters. This construction creates a hierarchy of sub-ranks where each lower rank is a subset of a higher one, akin to Matryoshka dolls. By carefully choosing P, the method ensures that gradient information is effectively shared across all sub-ranks during training. The framework generalizes LoRA and DyLoRA by appropriate choices of P and supports dynamic rank selection at inference. Additionally, the authors introduce AURAC (Area Under the Rank Accuracy Curve) as a metric to evaluate hierarchical low-rank adapters over a range of ranks, instead of at a single chosen rank.

Result: Experiments show that MatryoshkaLoRA learns more accurate hierarchical low-rank representations than DyLoRA and similar rank-adaptive methods. Across evaluated datasets and tasks, MatryoshkaLoRA achieves better or comparable accuracy at multiple ranks, leading to superior accuracy–efficiency trade-offs. The AURAC metric confirms that its performance is consistently strong across the rank spectrum rather than being optimized only for a single rank.

Conclusion: MatryoshkaLoRA provides a simple yet general modification to LoRA that enables effective hierarchical low-rank training and dynamic rank selection with minimal accuracy loss. By sharing gradients efficiently across ranks through a fixed diagonal scaling matrix P and by evaluating with AURAC, the method overcomes limitations of existing adaptive-rank approaches, offering a more data-efficient and robust solution for parameter-efficient fine-tuning of large models.

Abstract: With the rise in scale for deep learning models to billions of parameters, the computational cost of fine-tuning remains a significant barrier to deployment. While Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) has become the standard for parameter-efficient fine-tuning, the need to set a predefined, static rank $r$ requires exhaustive grid searches to balance efficiency and performance. Existing rank-adaptive solutions such as DyLoRA mitigate this by sampling ranks during the training from a predefined distribution. However, they often yield sub-optimal results at higher ranks due to lack of consistent gradient signals across the full hierarchy of ranks, thus making these methods data-inefficient. In this paper, we propose MatryoshkaLoRA, a general, Matryoshka-inspired training framework for LoRA that learns accurate hierarchical low-rank representations by inserting a fixed, carefully crafted diagonal matrix $P$ between the existing LoRA adapters to scale their sub-ranks accordingly. By introducing this simple modification, our general framework recovers LoRA and DyLoRA only by changing $P$ and ensures all sub-ranks embed the available gradient information efficiently. Our MatryoshkaLoRA supports dynamic rank selection with minimal degradation in accuracy. We further propose Area Under the Rank Accuracy Curve (AURAC), a metric that consistently evaluates the performance of hierarchical low-rank adapters. Our results demonstrate that MatryoshkaLoRA learns more accurate hierarchical low-rank representations than prior rank-adaptive approaches and achieves superior accuracy-performance trade-offs across ranks on the evaluated datasets. Our code is available at https://github.com/IST-DASLab/MatryoshkaLoRA.

</details>


### [79] [Beyond "I cannot fulfill this request": Alleviating Rigid Rejection in LLMs via Label Enhancement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07883)

Method: LANCE introduces a label enhancement framework based on variational inference. Rather than using a single binary or coarse label (comply vs. refuse), it predicts a continuous distribution over multiple rejection categories. These fine-grained, soft labels serve as multi-way textual gradients that guide a refinement model to edit or neutralize hazardous components in the user prompt. The refined input is then used by the LLM to generate responses that are both safe and conversationally natural, avoiding generic refusal templates.

Result: Experiments reported in the paper show that LANCE reduces the frequency of rigid, template-like refusals compared to baseline safety mechanisms. At the same time, it maintains high safety performance (low violation rates) and improves helpfulness and perceived naturalness of responses, outperforming existing baselines on these metrics.

Conclusion: LANCE provides a more nuanced approach to safety alignment by transforming rejection from a binary decision into a continuous, multi-category distribution. This enables LLMs to neutralize harmful content and respond safely without falling back on rigid refusal templates, improving both safety and user experience. The method demonstrates superior performance over baselines in experiments, suggesting that label enhancement is a promising direction for future alignment techniques.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) rely on safety alignment to obey safe requests while refusing harmful ones. However, traditional refusal mechanisms often lead to "rigid rejection," where a general template (e.g., "I cannot fulfill this request") indiscriminately triggers refusals and severely undermines the naturalness of interactions between humans and LLMs. To address this issue, LANCE is proposed in this paper to ensure safe yet flexible and natural responses via label enhancement. Specifically, LANCE employs variational inference to perform label enhancement, predicting a continuous distribution across multiple rejection categories. These fine-grained rejection distributions provide multi-way textual gradients for a refinement model to neutralize the hazardous elements in the prompt, so that the LLMs could generate safe responses that avoid rigid rejections while preserving the naturalness of interactions. Experiments demonstrate that LANCE significantly alleviates the rigid rejection problem while maintaining high security standards, significantly outperforming existing baseline models in terms of helpfulness and naturalness of responses.

Conclusion: CoCoReviewBench offers a more trustworthy benchmark for assessing AI reviewers by reducing dependence on imperfect human reviews and focusing on correctness and completeness. The empirical analysis reveals that today’s AI reviewers are far from fully reliable and frequently hallucinate, but that reasoning-focused models are comparatively more effective. The work motivates future research on improving AI review systems and provides public benchmarks and models to facilitate such progress.

Abstract: Despite the rapid development of AI reviewers, evaluating such systems remains challenging: metrics favor overlap with human reviews over correctness. However, since human reviews often cover only a subset of salient issues and sometimes contain mistakes, they are unreliable as gold references. To address this, we build category-specific benchmark subsets and skip evaluation when the corresponding human reviews are missing to strengthen Completeness. We also leverage reviewer--author--meta-review discussions as expert annotations and filter unreliable reviews accordingly to strengthen Correctness. Finally, we introduce CoCoReviewBench, which curates 3,900 papers from ICLR and NeurIPS to enable reliable and fine-grained evaluation of AI reviewers. Analysis shows that AI reviewers remain limited in correctness and are prone to hallucinations, and highlights reasoning models as more effective reviewers, motivating further directions for improving AI reviewers. Benchmarks and models are available at https://github.com/hexuandeng/CoCoReviewBench.

### [81] [How Value Induction Reshapes LLM Behaviour](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07925)
*Arnav Arora,Natalie Schluter,Katherine Metcalf,Maartje ter Hoeve*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how training conversational LLMs to express certain values (like empathy, helpfulness, harmlessness) has side effects on other values, safety, and how human‑like or sycophantic the models sound.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern conversational LLMs are post‑trained to follow norms and values such as curiosity, empathy, helpfulness, harmlessness, and honesty to make them safer and more pleasant to use. But values are complex and interdependent: emphasizing one value can unintentionally change how other values are expressed. Moreover, pushing certain values hard might make models sound more validating, flattering, or addictive, which could harm users. The authors want to systematically understand these unintended behavioural and safety consequences of "value induction" during fine‑tuning.

Method: The authors fine‑tune language models on carefully selected subsets of existing human‑preference datasets, where each subset corresponds to a particular value or group of values (e.g., empathy, safety). They then evaluate how inducing one value subset changes: (1) expression of other values (including related or contrastive ones), (2) standard safety metrics, (3) use of anthropomorphic, human‑like language, and (4) performance on various QA benchmarks. This involves controlled fine‑tuning experiments and quantitative analysis of downstream behaviours and language characteristics.

Result: They observe three main findings: (i) training a model to express a given value also alters its expression of other values, sometimes in aligned ways (related values increase together) and sometimes in contrastive ways (some values increase while others decrease); (ii) inducing positive values generally improves safety metrics, suggesting a beneficial safety transfer; and (iii) inducing any of the studied values systematically increases anthropomorphic language and makes models more validating and sycophantic in their responses.

Conclusion: Value‑based post‑training of conversational LLMs does not operate in isolation: each targeted value influences a broader network of behaviours and expressed values. While emphasizing positive values can improve safety, it simultaneously increases anthropomorphism and sycophancy, with possible risks like user over‑attachment or reduced critical feedback. Designers of LLM alignment and fine‑tuning pipelines should treat value induction as a coupled, multi‑dimensional intervention, explicitly monitoring for side effects such as increased anthropomorphic, overly validating, or addictive behaviour.

Abstract: Conversational Large Language Models are post-trained on language that expresses specific behavioural traits, such as curiosity, open-mindedness, and empathy, and values, such as helpfulness, harmlessness, and honesty. This is done to increase utility, ensure safety, and improve the experience of the people interacting with the model. However, values are complex and inter-related -- inducing one could modify behaviour on another. Further, inducing certain values can make models more addictive or sycophantic through language used in the generations, with a potential detrimental effect on the user. We investigate these and other unintended effects of value induction into models. We fine-tune models using curated value subsets of existing preference datasets, measuring the impact of value induction on expression of other values, model safety, anthropomorphic language, and various QA benchmarks. We find that (i) inducing values leads to expression of other related, and sometimes contrastive values, (ii) inducing positive values increases safety, and (iii) all values increase anthropomorphic language use, making models more validating and sycophantic.

</details>


### [82] [How to Train Your Latent Diffusion Language Model Jointly With the Latent Space](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07933)
*Viacheslav Meshchaninov,Alexander Shabalin,Egor Chimbulatov,Nikita Gushchin,Ilya Koziev,Alexander Korotin,Dmitry Vetrov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a jointly trained latent diffusion language model that learns its own continuous latent space from a pretrained LM, and with a specific training recipe it outperforms prior discrete and continuous diffusion LMs while being 2–13× faster.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Discrete diffusion models for text are slow and operate on tokens, while non-autoregressive continuous (latent) diffusion needs a good latent space, which is difficult to construct; previous latent approaches often use fixed, separately trained encoders/decoders that are suboptimal for denoising and decoding.

Method: Introduce LDLM, where a trainable encoder reshapes hidden states of a pretrained LM into latents, a diffusion model denoises them, and a decoder maps latents back to tokens; all three are trained jointly with a training recipe including (1) an MSE loss on the decoder output, (2) a warmup phase that emphasizes matching the encoder before full diffusion training, (3) adaptive timestep sampling during diffusion training, and (4) injecting noise into the decoder inputs to improve robustness.

Result: On OpenWebText and LM1B benchmarks, LDLM achieves better text generation quality than existing discrete and continuous diffusion-based language models while being 2–13× faster at generation; ablation experiments show each part of the proposed training recipe materially improves performance.

Conclusion: Carefully designed joint learning of the latent space and diffusion model, together with a simple but effective training recipe, makes latent diffusion a strong and efficient alternative for non-autoregressive text generation, advancing latent diffusion toward practical competitiveness with other generative approaches.

Abstract: Latent diffusion models offer an attractive alternative to discrete diffusion for non-autoregressive text generation by operating on continuous text representations and denoising entire sequences in parallel. The major challenge in latent diffusion modeling is constructing a suitable latent space. In this work, we present the Latent Diffusion Language Model (LDLM), in which the latent encoder, diffusion model, and decoder are trained jointly. LDLM builds its latent space by reshaping the representations of a pre-trained language model with a trainable encoder, yielding latents that are easy to both denoise and decode into tokens. We show that naive joint training produces a low-quality diffusion model, and propose a simple training recipe consisting of an MSE decoder loss, diffusion-to-encoder warmup, adaptive timestep sampling, and decoder-input noise. Ablations show that each component substantially impacts generation performance. On OpenWebText and LM1B, LDLM achieves better generation performance than existing discrete and continuous diffusion language models while being $2{\text -}13\times$ faster, indicating that jointly learning the latent space is a key step toward making latent diffusion competitive for text generation.

### [83] [Ask Early, Ask Late, Ask Right: When Does Clarification Timing Matter for Long-Horizon Agents?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07937)
*Anmol Gulati,Hariom Gupta,Elias Lumer,Sahil Sen,Vamse Kumar Subbiah*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies when long-horizon AI agents should ask for clarification, finding that the best timing depends on the type of missing information and that current models consistently mistime their clarification queries.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-horizon AI agents often must act under incomplete instructions; a single early mistake can cascade, yet querying for clarification is costly and disruptive. Existing work has not quantified how the value of asking for clarification changes over the course of an agent’s execution, nor how this depends on what kind of information is missing (goal, input, constraints, or context). This gap prevents the development of principled, timing-aware clarification policies.

Method: The authors design a forced-injection framework that inserts ground-truth clarifications at controlled points along an agent’s action trajectory. They vary (1) which information dimension is clarified (goal, input, constraint, context), (2) the execution point at which clarification is given, and (3) the underlying agent model and benchmark tasks. They run over 6,000 experiments across three benchmarks, four frontier models (three per benchmark plus one used only on a single benchmark), and 84 task variants, then measure performance (e.g., pass@3) as a function of clarification timing and type. They also compute cross-model Kendall tau correlations to assess whether timing profiles are intrinsic to tasks rather than models, and analyze 300 real, unscripted agent sessions to observe natural clarification behavior.

Result: Goal clarification is extremely time-sensitive: its benefit essentially disappears after about 10% of the trajectory, with pass@3 dropping from 0.78 back to baseline levels. Input clarification is more robust, maintaining value until roughly the 50% mark. Any clarification deferred beyond the midpoint of execution tends to hurt performance relative to never asking. Timing profiles are highly correlated across models evaluated on the same tasks (Kendall tau 0.78–0.87) and moderately correlated across the full 4-model panel (0.34–0.67), indicating that these patterns are largely task-intrinsic. In a separate observational study of 300 unscripted sessions, all examined frontier models fail to ask within the empirically optimal windows, with behaviors ranging from excessive querying (52% of sessions) to never querying.

Conclusion: The value of clarification for long-horizon AI agents is strongly dependent on both the type of missing information and the timing of the query. Early clarification is critical for goals, moderately flexible for inputs, and detrimental if delayed too long for any information type. These empirically derived “demand curves” for clarification supply the quantitative evidence that theoretical frameworks have needed to reason about when to query. They also define concrete performance and timing targets for designing next-generation, timing-aware clarification policies in autonomous agents. The authors plan to release code and data to support further research.

Abstract: Long-horizon AI agents execute complex workflows spanning hundreds of sequential actions, yet a single wrong assumption early on can cascade into irreversible errors. When instructions are incomplete, the agent must decide not only whether to ask for clarification but when, and no prior work measures how clarification value changes over the course of execution. We introduce a forced-injection framework that provides ground-truth clarifications at controlled points in the agent's trajectory across four information dimensions (goal, input, constraint, context), three agent benchmarks, and four frontier models (three per benchmark; one on a single benchmark only; 84 task variants; 6,000+ runs). Counter to the common intuition that "earlier is always better," we find that the value of clarification depends sharply on what information is missing: goal clarification loses nearly all value after 10% of execution (pass@3 drops from 0.78 to baseline), while input clarification retains value through roughly 50%. Deferring any clarification type past mid-trajectory degrades performance below never asking at all. Cross-model Kendall tau correlations (0.78-0.87 among models sharing identical task coverage; 0.34-0.67 across the full 4-model panel) confirm these timing profiles are substantially task-intrinsic. A complementary study of 300 unscripted sessions reveals that no current frontier model asks within the empirically optimal window, with strategies ranging from over-asking (52% of sessions) to never asking at all. These empirical demand curves provide the quantitative foundation that existing theoretical frameworks require but have lacked, and establish concrete design targets for timing-aware clarification policies. Code and data will be publicly released.

</details>


### [84] [GLiGuard: Schema-Conditioned Classification for LLM Safeguard](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07982)
*Urchade Zaratiana,Mary Newhauser,George Hurn-Maloney,Ash Lewis*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GLiGuard is a compact (0.3B) schema-conditioned bidirectional encoder for LLM safety moderation that matches much larger guard models’ accuracy while greatly reducing latency and compute.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM safety guardrails typically use very large autoregressive decoder models (7B–27B) to do what is essentially a classification task. This causes high latency, poor scalability to many safety dimensions, and high inference cost, limiting real-time moderation across prompts, responses, and multiple harm categories.

Method: Adapt GLiNER2 into GLiGuard, a 0.3B-parameter bidirectional encoder that takes a structured token schema as input. The schema encodes task definitions and label semantics (e.g., prompt safety, response safety, refusal detection, 14 harm types, 11 jailbreak types). GLiGuard performs non-autoregressive inference, simultaneously predicting across all these aspects in a single forward pass, with composable task/label blocks configured at inference time.

Result: On nine standard safety benchmarks, GLiGuard reaches F1 scores comparable to 7B–27B decoder-based moderation models, despite being 23–90× smaller. It delivers up to 16× higher throughput and 17× lower latency than these baselines, while covering multiple safety and jailbreak dimensions in one pass.

Conclusion: Carefully designed small bidirectional encoders with schema-conditioned inputs can serve as efficient, accurate LLM safety guards. GLiGuard shows that we can approach the moderation quality of much larger models while dramatically lowering inference cost and improving scalability for multi-aspect, real-time content safety evaluation.

Abstract: Ensuring safe, policy-compliant outputs from large language models requires real-time content moderation that can scale across multiple safety dimensions. However, state-of-the-art guardrail models rely on autoregressive decoders with 7B--27B parameters, reformulating what is fundamentally a classification problem as sequential text generation, a design choice that incurs high latency and scales poorly to multi-aspect evaluation. In this work, we introduce \textbf{GLiGuard}, a 0.3B-parameter schema-conditioned bidirectional encoder adapted from GLiNER2 for LLM content moderation. The key idea is to encode task definitions and label semantics directly into the input sequence as structured token schemas, enabling simultaneous evaluation of prompt safety, response safety, refusal detection, 14 fine-grained harm categories, and 11 jailbreak strategies in a single non-autoregressive forward pass. This schema-conditioned design lets supported task and label blocks be composed directly in the input schema at inference time. Across nine established safety benchmarks, GLiGuard achieves F1 scores competitive with 7B--27B decoder-based guards despite being 23--90$\times$ smaller, while delivering up to 16$\times$ higher throughput and 17$\times$ lower latency. These results suggest that compact bidirectional encoders can approach the accuracy of much larger guard models while drastically reducing inference cost. Code and models are available at https://github.com/fastino-ai/GLiGuard.

### [85] [Fast Byte Latent Transformer](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08044)
*Julie Kallini,Artidoro Pagnoni,Tomasz Limisiewicz,Gargi Ghosh,Luke Zettlemoyer,Christopher Potts,Xiaochuang Han,Srinivasan Iyer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They speed up byte-level language models by generating multiple bytes in parallel using diffusion-style and speculative-style techniques, while keeping quality competitive and cutting memory-bandwidth cost by over 50%.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Byte-level LMs avoid subword vocabularies and match token-level performance, but they are impractically slow because they generate one byte at a time autoregressively. There is a need for methods that preserve the benefits of byte-level modeling while removing this decoding bottleneck to make them practical in real applications.

Method: They build on the Byte Latent Transformer (BLT) and introduce three variants with new training objectives and decoding procedures. (1) BLT Diffusion (BLT-D) adds an auxiliary block-wise diffusion objective to the standard next-byte loss, enabling the model to generate blocks of bytes in parallel via a diffusion-like process. (2) BLT Self-speculation (BLT-S) lets the local decoder generate beyond its usual patch boundaries to draft several future bytes, which are then checked and possibly corrected using a single full-model forward pass, akin to speculative decoding. (3) BLT Diffusion+Verification (BLT-DV) combines BLT-D’s parallel diffusion-based generation with an additional autoregressive verification step that refines or corrects the diffused outputs.

Result: The proposed variants can generate multiple bytes per decoding step, reducing the number of forward passes and thus speeding up generation. Across the methods, they estimate more than 50% reduction in memory-bandwidth cost compared with the baseline BLT during generation, while retaining or improving generation quality depending on the chosen variant and speed–quality trade-off.

Conclusion: By introducing diffusion-based parallel generation and speculative-style verification schemes for BLT, the authors significantly reduce the computational and memory-bandwidth cost of byte-level language model generation. Each variant offers a different balance of speed and quality, and together they remove major efficiency barriers, making byte-level LMs more practical for real-world use.

Abstract: Recent byte-level language models (LMs) match the performance of token-level models without relying on subword vocabularies, yet their utility is limited by slow, byte-by-byte autoregressive generation. We address this bottleneck in the Byte Latent Transformer (BLT) through new training and generation techniques. First, we introduce BLT Diffusion (BLT-D), a new model and our fastest BLT variant, trained with an auxiliary block-wise diffusion objective alongside the standard next-byte prediction loss. This enables an inference procedure that generates multiple bytes in parallel per decoding step, substantially reducing the number of forward passes required to generate a sequence. Second, we propose two extensions inspired by speculative decoding that trade some of this speed for higher generation quality: BLT Self-speculation (BLT-S), in which BLT's local decoder continues generating past its normal patch boundaries to draft bytes, which are then verified with a single full-model forward pass; and BLT Diffusion+Verification (BLT-DV), which augments BLT-D with an autoregressive verification step after diffusion-based generation. All methods may achieve an estimated memory-bandwidth cost over 50% lower than BLT on generation tasks. Each approach offers its own unique advantages, together removing key barriers to the practical use of byte-level LMs.

</details>


### [86] [Uncertainty-Aware Structured Data Extraction from Full CMR Reports via Distilled LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08045)
*Yi Yu,Parker Martin,Zhenyu Bu,Yixuan Liu,Yi-Yu Zheng,Orlando Simonetti,Yuchi Han,Yuan Xue*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CMR-EXTR, a framework to automatically convert free-text cardiac MRI reports into structured, auditable data with confidence scores, achieving very high extraction accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Free-text CMR reports are hard to use for large-scale research, longitudinal tracking, and clinical decision support because they are unstructured and difficult to audit or query. Existing solutions either require heavy manual annotation, lack robust confidence estimation, or aren’t tailored to cardiac MRI. There is a need for a lightweight, accurate, and auditable way to extract structured variables from these narrative reports while knowing when human review is needed.

Method: The authors propose CMR-EXTR, which uses a teacher–student knowledge distillation pipeline so that a lighter student model can run fully offline using supervision from a stronger teacher model with limited manual labels. They design an uncertainty estimation module that combines (1) distribution plausibility of predicted values, (2) sampling stability across stochastic passes, and (3) cross-field consistency checks between related variables. These components yield per-field confidence scores that flag cases for human review. The system is applied to free-text CMR reports to extract structured variables and evaluate performance.

Result: CMR-EXTR attains 99.65% accuracy at the variable level when extracting structured fields from free-text CMR reports. The uncertainty estimates produced by the combined plausibility, stability, and consistency checks are informative enough to effectively triage which cases should be reviewed by humans, supporting reliable semi-automated curation.

Conclusion: CMR-EXTR is an effective, lightweight solution for converting free-text cardiac MRI reports into structured, auditable data with integrated confidence estimation. Its teacher–student design minimizes manual labeling while allowing fully offline deployment. The high accuracy and useful confidence scores make it suitable for cohort assembly, longitudinal curation, and clinical decision support, and it represents, to the authors’ knowledge, the first CMR-specific extraction system with built-in uncertainty modeling.

Abstract: Converting free-text cardiac magnetic resonance (CMR) reports into auditable structured data remains a bottleneck for cohort assembly, longitudinal curation, and clinical decision support. We present CMR-EXTR, a lightweight framework that converts free-text CMR reports into structured data and assigns per-field confidence for quality control. A teacher-student distillation pipeline enables fully offline inference while limiting manual annotation. Uncertainty integrates three complementary principles -- distribution plausibility, sampling stability, and cross-field consistency -- to triage human review. Experiments show that CMR-EXTR achieves 99.65% variable-level accuracy, demonstrating both reliable extraction and informative confidence scores. To our knowledge, this is the first CMR-specific extraction system with integrated confidence estimation. The code is available at https://github.com/yuyi1005/CMR-EXTR.

### [87] [Accurate and Efficient Statistical Testing for Word Semantic Breadth](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08048)
*Yo Ehara*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies a statistical pitfall in measuring how broadly words are used across contexts using contextual embeddings, and introduces a Householder-aligned permutation test that separates dispersion from directional differences, providing more reliable significance tests and an efficient GPU implementation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Contextualized embeddings allow word types to be represented as clouds of token vectors, enabling quantitative measures of contextual breadth (dispersion). These measures are valuable for lexicography and sense inventory design. However, when comparing two words’ breadths, standard hypothesis tests on dispersion can be confounded by differences in mean semantic direction rather than true breadth, causing inflated Type-I error and misleading claims of significance in subtle-difference settings. There is a need for a method that cleanly tests dispersion differences while remaining computationally practical.

Method: The authors model each word type as a set of contextualized token embeddings and focus on comparing dispersion statistics between two such token clouds. They propose a Householder-aligned permutation test: (1) compute the mean direction of each word’s token vectors; (2) construct a Householder reflection that aligns the two mean directions; (3) apply this single reflection to one cloud to remove directional differences; (4) perform a permutation test on the aligned token sets to estimate the null distribution of dispersion differences and obtain non-parametric p-values. They further provide a GPU-friendly implementation that batches random permutations and associated linear algebra operations for speed.

Result: On empirical evaluations, the alignment-based test substantially reduced false positives: Type-I error decreased by 32.5% relative to an unaligned baseline, indicating better calibration of p-values when there is no true breadth difference. At the same time, the method preserved power to detect genuine dispersion differences between word types. The GPU-oriented implementation yielded a 23-fold speedup over a CPU implementation, making the method practical for large-scale analyses.

Conclusion: Directional differences between contextualized token embeddings can spuriously appear as differences in dispersion, compromising standard significance tests for contextual breadth. By applying a single Householder reflection to align mean directions before carrying out a permutation test, the proposed method isolates dispersion effects and delivers calibrated, non-parametric p-values. The approach both improves statistical reliability, as evidenced by a sizeable reduction in Type-I error, and is computationally efficient when implemented on GPUs, making it suitable for lexicographic and semantic analysis applications that rely on contextual breadth measures.

Abstract: Measuring the breadth of a word's meaning, or its spread across contexts, has become feasible with contextualized token embeddings. A word type can be represented as a cloud of token vectors, with dispersion-based statistics serving as proxies for contextual diversity (Nagata and Tanaka-Ishii, ACL2025). These measurements are useful for deciding appropriate sense distinctions when constructing thesauri and domain-specific dictionaries. However, when comparing the breadth of two word types, naive hypothesis testing on dispersion can be misleading: differences in semantic direction can masquerade as dispersion differences, inflating Type-I error and yielding "statistically significant" outcomes even when there is no true breadth difference. This is problematic because significance testing should distinguish genuine effects from incidental fluctuations in small-difference regimes. We propose a Householder-aligned permutation test to isolate dispersion differences from directional differences. Our method applies a single Householder reflection to align the mean directions of the two word types and then performs a permutation test on the aligned token clouds, yielding calibrated, non-parametric p-values. For practicality, we introduce a GPU-oriented implementation that batches permutations and linear algebra operations. Empirically, our alignment reduced Type-I error by 32.5% while preserving sensitivity to genuine breadth differences, and achieved a 23x speedup over the CPU baseline.

</details>


### [88] [CA-SQL: Complexity-Aware Inference Time Reasoning for Text-to-SQL via Exploration and Compute Budget Allocation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08057)
*James Petullo,Nianwen Xue*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CA-SQL is a Text-to-SQL pipeline that adaptively expands exploration based on task difficulty and uses evolutionary-style prompting plus voting to reach SOTA performance on the challenging BIRD benchmark tier.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing inference-time learning approaches for Text-to-SQL with LLMs still underperform on the hardest BIRD benchmark tasks because they do not explore the SQL solution space broadly or systematically enough to find promising candidate queries that can then be refined.

Method: CA-SQL first estimates the difficulty of each Text-to-SQL task, then adaptively scales how broadly it explores candidate SQL queries: harder tasks receive more extensive exploration. It introduces a custom prompt seeding strategy inspired by evolutionary search to encourage diverse candidate generations from the base LLM, and finally applies a new voting mechanism across candidates to select the best query at the end of the search process.

Result: On the BIRD development set, CA-SQL reaches a state-of-the-art 51.72% score on the "challenging" tier using only GPT-4o-mini, surpassing other in-context learning baselines that may use larger models. Overall it achieves 61.06% execution accuracy and 68.77% Soft F1 on the full BIRD dev dataset.

Conclusion: Difficulty-aware, evolution-inspired exploration of SQL candidates, combined with a tailored voting scheme, substantially improves Text-to-SQL performance at inference time and can achieve SOTA results on challenging benchmarks even with relatively small LLMs.

Abstract: While recent advancements in inference-time learning have improved LLM reasoning on Text-to-SQL tasks, current solutions still struggle to perform well on the most challenging tasks in the Bird-Bench (BIRD) benchmark. This is due to inadequate solution space exploration, which is necessary to uncover promising candidate queries that can be further refined to produce the correct output. To address this challenge, we introduce CA-SQL, a novel Text-to-SQL pipeline that utilizes the estimated difficulty of a task to dynamically scale the breadth of the exploration for generating solution candidates. In addition, we use a custom prompt seeding method, based on principles of evolutionary search, to further elicit exploratory behavior from the base LLM and a novel voting method to select the best candidate solution at the end of the search. Experiments demonstrate that our solution achieves a state-of-the-art score of 51.72% on the "challenging" tier of BIRD development set problems, using only GPT-4o-mini, out-performing other in-context learning approaches, even those that leverage larger models. Overall, our method attains a competitive 61.06% execution accuracy and 68.77% Soft F1 score on the BIRD development dataset.
