### [89] [The Memory Curse: How Expanded Recall Erodes Cooperative Intent in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08060)
*Jiayuan Liu,Tianqin Li,Shiyi Du,Xin Luo,Haoxuan Zeng,Emanuel Tewolde,Tai Sing Lee,Tonghan Wang,Carl Kingsford,Vincent Conitzer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper finds that giving large language models longer memory (larger context windows) can *harm* rather than help cooperation in multi-agent social dilemma games, a phenomenon they call the “memory curse.”


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Longer context windows are marketed as a universally beneficial upgrade for LLMs, but it is unclear how extended memory affects strategic, multi-agent behavior such as cooperation in social dilemmas. Understanding whether and how memory shapes emergent behaviors is important both scientifically and for safe deployment of LLM agents interacting over time.

Method: The authors systematically evaluate 7 LLMs across 4 repeated social dilemma games over 500 rounds while varying the amount of accessible interaction history (context window). They measure cooperation levels and analyze 378k reasoning traces using lexical methods to infer cognitive patterns (e.g., forward-looking intent vs paranoia). They use targeted LoRA fine-tuning on forward-looking reasoning traces as a cognitive probe, run memory sanitization experiments that keep prompt length fixed but replace actual history with synthetic cooperative histories, and perform ablations removing explicit Chain-of-Thought reasoning to assess its impact on cooperation and the memory curse.

Result: They find that expanding the accessible history reduces cooperation in 18 of 28 model–game combinations, which they term the memory curse. Lexical analysis links this decline to reduced forward-looking intent rather than increased paranoia. A LoRA adapter trained purely on forward-looking traces partially reverses the cooperation decay and generalizes zero-shot to new games. Memory sanitization with synthetic cooperative histories restores cooperation while keeping prompt length constant, indicating that the *content* of memory (negative interaction history) drives the curse rather than sheer length. Removing or ablating explicit Chain-of-Thought reasoning often lessens the cooperation collapse, indicating that deliberative reasoning can intensify the negative effect of longer memory.

Conclusion: Longer memory in LLM agents is not a benign upgrade: it is an active driver of behavioral dynamics in multi-agent settings. More extensive recall can systematically erode cooperation depending on what the models pay attention to and how they reason about history. Cooperative stability depends on eliciting forward-looking reasoning patterns and possibly constraining or reshaping how models deliberate over extended histories, rather than simply maximizing context window length.

Abstract: Context window expansion is often treated as a straightforward capability upgrade for LLMs, but we find it systematically fails in multi-agent social dilemmas. Across 7 LLMs and 4 games over 500 rounds, expanding accessible history degrades cooperation in 18 of 28 model--game settings, a pattern we term the memory curse. We isolate the underlying mechanism through three analyses. First, lexical analysis of 378,000 reasoning traces associates this breakdown with eroding forward-looking intent rather than rising paranoia. We validate this using targeted fine-tuning as a cognitive probe: a LoRA adapter trained exclusively on forward-looking traces mitigates the decay and transfers zero-shot to distinct games. Second, memory sanitization holds prompt length fixed while replacing visible history with synthetic cooperative records, which restores cooperation substantially, proving the trigger is memory content, not length alone. Finally, ablating explicit Chain-of-Thought reasoning often reduces the collapse, showing that deliberation paradoxically amplifies the memory curse. Together, these results recast memory as an active determinant of multi-agent behavior: longer recall can either destabilize or support cooperation depending on the reasoning patterns it elicits.

</details>


### [90] [Conformal Path Reasoning: Trustworthy Knowledge Graph Question Answering via Path-Level Calibration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08077)
*Shuhang Lin,Chuhao Zhou,Xiao Lin,Zihan Dong,Kuan Lu,Zhencan Peng,Jie Yin,Dimitris N. Metaxas*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Conformal Path Reasoning (CPR), a trustworthy Knowledge Graph Question Answering framework that combines conformal prediction with path-based reasoning to provide reliable coverage guarantees with compact answer sets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Knowledge Graph Question Answering (KGQA) methods struggle to offer formal reliability guarantees: they often miss correct answers (coverage violations) or must return very large sets of candidate answers to be safe. Although conformal prediction could, in principle, ensure statistical coverage guarantees, prior conformal methods in KGQA suffer from invalid calibration and weakly discriminative scoring functions, leading to both unreliable coverage and oversized prediction sets. The authors aim to design a KGQA framework that is both trustworthy—with statistically guaranteed coverage—and practical, by keeping prediction sets small and interpretable via paths in the knowledge graph.

Method: The authors propose Conformal Path Reasoning (CPR), which has two main technical components. (1) Query-level conformal calibration over path-level scores: instead of calibrating at the answer level, they treat each reasoning path as a prediction object and perform conformal prediction per query, ensuring exchangeability and enabling the construction of calibrated path prediction sets. These path sets then induce answer sets. (2) Residual Conformal Value Network (RCVNet): a lightweight neural module trained using PUCT-guided exploration (similar to Monte Carlo Tree Search in AlphaZero-style methods) to assign discriminative nonconformity scores to reasoning paths. The residual design refines existing path scores, improving their ability to distinguish good from bad paths, which is crucial for tight conformal sets. Together, these components yield a CP-based KGQA pipeline that outputs statistically valid, path-based answer sets.

Result: On standard KGQA benchmarks, CPR achieves substantially better trade-offs than prior conformal baselines. Specifically, it improves Empirical Coverage Rate (the fraction of queries for which the true answer is contained in the prediction set) by 34%, indicating much more reliable inclusion of correct answers. At the same time, it reduces the average prediction set size by 40%, meaning that users receive much more compact, less noisy sets of candidate answers. These results collectively demonstrate that CPR restores or closely matches desired coverage guarantees while avoiding the explosion in prediction set size that plagued earlier methods.

Conclusion: Conformal Path Reasoning (CPR) provides a principled, trustworthy framework for Knowledge Graph Question Answering by integrating query-level conformal calibration with a discriminative Residual Conformal Value Network for path scoring. The approach achieves strong empirical coverage guarantees while keeping prediction sets significantly smaller than prior conformal methods. This shows that conformal prediction can be effectively adapted to structured, path-based reasoning over knowledge graphs, delivering interpretable and statistically reliable answer sets suitable for practical deployment.

Abstract: Knowledge Graph Question Answering (KGQA) has shown promise for grounded and interpretable reasoning, yet existing approaches often fail to provide reliable coverage guarantees over retrieved answers. While Conformal Prediction (CP) offers a principled framework for producing prediction sets with statistical guarantees, prior methods suffer from critical limitations in both calibration validity and score discriminability, resulting in violated coverage guarantees and excessively large prediction sets. To address these pitfalls, we propose Conformal Path Reasoning (CPR), a trustworthy KGQA framework with two key innovations. First, we perform query-level conformal calibration over path-level scores, preserving the exchangeability while generating path prediction sets. Second, we introduce the Residual Conformal Value Network (RCVNet), a lightweight module trained via PUCT-guided exploration to learn discriminative path-level nonconformity scores. Experiments on benchmarks show that CPR significantly improves the Empirical Coverage Rate by 34% while reducing average prediction set size by 40% compared to conformal baselines. These results validate the efficacy of CPR in satisfying coverage guarantees with substantially more compact answer sets.

### [91] [LLMs Improving LLMs: Agentic Discovery for Test-Time Scaling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08083)
*Tong Zheng,Haolin Liu,Chengsong Huang,Huiwen Bao,Sheng Zhang,Rui Liu,Runpeng Dai,Ruibo Chen,Chenxi Liu,Tianyi Xiong,Xidong Wu,Hongming Zhang,Heng Huang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents AutoTTS, a framework that automatically discovers test-time scaling (TTS) strategies for large language models by searching over controllers in a specially constructed environment using pre-collected reasoning trajectories, achieving better accuracy–cost trade-offs than hand-crafted heuristics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing TTS methods for large language models rely on manually designed heuristics for allocating extra computation (e.g., how to branch, when to stop), which leaves most of the possible strategy space unexplored and may be suboptimal or hard to transfer across tasks and models. The authors want a systematic, automated way to discover strong TTS strategies without expensive repeated LLM calls.

Method: They propose AutoTTS, an environment-driven framework where instead of directly hand-crafting TTS rules, researchers build an environment in which TTS controllers can be discovered automatically. Concretely, they: (1) Pre-collect reasoning trajectories and probe signals from LLMs on tasks. (2) Formulate width–depth TTS as a controller synthesis problem over these trajectories, where a controller chooses actions like branching, continuing, probing, pruning, or stopping. (3) Evaluate candidate controllers cheaply using the stored trajectories, avoiding new LLM calls. (4) Introduce a beta parameterization of the controller’s decision space to make the search tractable and fine-grained execution-trace feedback to diagnose controller failures and improve search efficiency.

Result: On mathematical reasoning benchmarks, the automatically discovered TTS strategies improve the accuracy versus computation-cost trade-off compared to strong manually engineered TTS baselines. These strategies also transfer to unseen benchmarks and to different model scales. Moreover, the whole discovery procedure is computationally and financially efficient, costing only about $39.9 and 160 minutes.

Conclusion: Automatically discovering TTS strategies in a carefully designed environment is both feasible and effective. AutoTTS can find width–depth test-time scaling controllers that outperform hand-crafted heuristics in accuracy–cost trade-offs, generalize to new tasks and models, and can be obtained with modest search cost, suggesting a promising direction for automating inference-time optimization for large language models.

Abstract: Test-time scaling (TTS) has become an effective approach for improving large language model performance by allocating additional computation during inference. However, existing TTS strategies are largely hand-crafted: researchers manually design reasoning patterns and tune heuristics by intuition, leaving much of the computation-allocation space unexplored. We propose an environment-driven framework, AutoTTS, that changes what researchers design: from individual TTS heuristics to environments where TTS strategies can be discovered automatically. The key to AutoTTS lies in environment construction: the discovery environment must make the control space tractable and provide cheap, frequent feedback for TTS search. As a concrete instantiation, we formulate width--depth TTS as controller synthesis over pre-collected reasoning trajectories and probe signals, where controllers decide when to branch, continue, probe, prune, or stop and can be evaluated cheaply without repeated LLM calls. We further introduce beta parameterization to make the search tractable and fine-grained execution trace feedback to improve discovery efficiency by helping the agent diagnose why a TTS program fails. Experiments on mathematical reasoning benchmarks show that the discovered strategies improve the overall accuracy--cost tradeoff over strong manually designed baselines. The discovered strategies generalize to held-out benchmarks and model scales, while the entire discovery costs only $39.9 and 160 minutes. Our data, and code will be open-source at https://github.com/zhengkid/AutoTTS.

</details>

### [92] [GraphDC: A Divide-and-Conquer Multi-Agent System for Scalable Graph Algorithm Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06671)
*Wenjin Li,Jiaming Cui*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: GraphDC is a divide-and-conquer multi-agent framework that lets LLMs solve large graph algorithm problems more accurately and scalably by decomposing graphs into subgraphs, reasoning locally, and then integrating results.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs perform well on many mathematical tasks but struggle with graph algorithms, especially on large graphs that require complex, multi-step, topology-aware reasoning. Existing approaches suffer from scalability issues, high reasoning burden on a single model, and unreliable performance on large instances. The paper aims to bridge this gap and make LLM-based graph reasoning more robust and scalable.

Method: The authors propose GraphDC, a hierarchical divide-and-conquer multi-agent framework. First, the input graph is decomposed into smaller subgraphs. Each subgraph is assigned to a specialized LLM agent that performs local reasoning. Then, a master agent collects intermediate solutions and relevant inter-subgraph information to integrate these local outputs into a final global solution. The design explicitly reduces the reasoning load per agent and structures the computation as a multi-step pipeline aligned with graph structure.

Result: Experiments across multiple graph algorithm reasoning tasks and varying graph sizes show that GraphDC consistently outperforms prior LLM-based and baseline methods. The gains are most pronounced on larger graph instances, where direct, end-to-end reasoning by a single LLM is less accurate and less reliable.

Conclusion: A divide-and-conquer, multi-agent architecture tailored to graph structure significantly improves LLM performance on graph algorithm reasoning, particularly in scalability and robustness on large graphs. Decomposing graphs and hierarchically aggregating local reasoning is an effective strategy for complex, structured problem domains.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have demonstrated strong potential for many mathematical problems. However, their performance on graph algorithmic tasks is still unsatisfying, since graphs are naturally more complex in topology and often require systematic multi-step reasoning, especially on larger graphs. Motivated by this gap, we propose GraphDC, a Divide-and-Conquer multi-agent framework for scalable graph algorithm reasoning. Specifically, inspired by Divide-and-Conquer design, GraphDC decomposes an input graph into smaller subgraphs, assigns each subgraph to a specialized agent for local reasoning, and uses a master agent to integrate the local outputs with inter-subgraph information to produce the final solution. This hierarchical design reduces the reasoning burden on individual agents, alleviates computational bottlenecks, and improves robustness on large graph instances. Extensive experiments show that GraphDC consistently outperforms existing methods on graph algorithm reasoning across diverse tasks and scales, especially on larger instances where direct end-to-end reasoning is less reliable.

### [93] [More Thinking, More Bias: Length-Driven Position Bias in Reasoning Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06672)
*Xiao Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper finds that in reasoning-capable LLMs, longer chain-of-thought trajectories systematically increase multiple-choice position bias, even when overall accuracy is high, so reasoning does not automatically make models order-robust.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Many practitioners assume that chain-of-thought (CoT) prompting and reasoning-tuned models reduce shallow heuristics like favoring certain answer positions in multiple-choice questions. The authors want to test this assumption rigorously and understand whether and how CoT affects position bias in MCQ evaluation pipelines, since such biases can distort benchmark scores and model behavior.

Method: The authors analyze multiple-choice question answering across MMLU, ARC-Challenge, and GPQA using thirteen reasoning-mode configurations: two R1-distilled 7–8B models, two base models prompted with CoT, and DeepSeek-R1 at 671B. They define a Position Bias Score (PBS) and compute partial correlations between chain-of-thought trajectory length and PBS while controlling for accuracy. They also examine PBS across trajectory-length quartiles. To provide causal evidence, they use a truncation intervention: they cut CoT trajectories at different points, resume generation, and measure how often the model’s answer shifts toward position-preferred options, bucketed by answer position. They additionally compare CoT-based answering to direct-answer settings in different instruction-tuned models to separate CoT-induced from baseline position bias. They introduce diagnostic tools such as PBS, commitment change point, effective switching, and truncation probes to audit position bias.

Result: In 12 of 13 reasoning-mode configurations, there is a positive partial correlation (0.11–0.41, all statistically significant) between CoT trajectory length and Position Bias Score after controlling for accuracy. All twelve open-weight reasoning-mode settings show monotonically increasing PBS across length quartiles. The truncation intervention reveals that continuing from later points in the reasoning chain makes models more likely to move toward position-preferred options, with this probability rising from 16% to 32% across absolute-position buckets in R1-Qwen-7B. For DeepSeek-R1 at 671B, the aggregate PBS is very small (0.019), but the length effect persists in the longest trajectory quartile (PBS = 0.071), indicating that high accuracy masks but does not remove the underlying length-driven bias mechanism. They also find that direct-answer position bias behaves differently: it is strong in Llama-Instruct-direct, weak in Qwen-Instruct-direct, unrelated to trajectory length, and uncorrelated with the CoT length effect. CoT reasoning thus suppresses or replaces baseline direct-answer position bias with a new, length-accumulated bias pattern.

Conclusion: Reasoning-capable and CoT-using models are not inherently robust to the ordering of answer options in multiple-choice questions. As chain-of-thought trajectories get longer, position bias tends to increase, even in very large, accurate models, where accuracy mainly gates when the bias is expressed rather than eliminating its cause. Direct-answer position bias is a separate phenomenon from CoT-induced length-based bias. Therefore, MCQ evaluation pipelines should not assume order-robustness for reasoning models; instead, they should explicitly audit for position bias using tools like Position Bias Score, commitment change point analysis, effective switching metrics, and truncation probes.

Abstract: Chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning and reasoning-tuned models such as DeepSeek-R1 are commonly assumed to reduce shallow heuristic biases by thinking carefully. We test this on position bias in multiple-choice QA and find a different story: within any reasoning-capable model, per-question position bias scales with the length of the reasoning trajectory.
  Across thirteen reasoning-mode configurations (two R1-distilled 7-8B models, two base models prompted with CoT, and DeepSeek-R1 at 671B) on MMLU, ARC-Challenge, and GPQA, twelve show a positive partial correlation between trajectory length and Position Bias Score (PBS) after controlling for accuracy, ranging from 0.11 to 0.41 (all p < 0.05). All twelve open-weight reasoning-mode configurations show monotonically increasing PBS across length quartiles. A truncation intervention provides causal evidence: continuations resumed from later points in the trajectory are increasingly likely to shift toward position-preferred options (16% to 32% for R1-Qwen-7B across absolute-position buckets).
  At 671B, aggregate PBS collapses to 0.019, but the length effect still manifests in the longest quartile (PBS = 0.071), suggesting that accuracy gates the expression of length-driven bias rather than eliminating the underlying mechanism. We additionally find that direct-answer position bias is a distinct phenomenon with a different footprint (strong in Llama-Instruct-direct, weak in Qwen-Instruct-direct, and uncorrelated with trajectory length): CoT reasoning replaces this baseline bias with length-accumulated bias.
  Our results argue that reasoning-capable models should not be treated as order-robust by default in MCQ evaluation pipelines, and offer a diagnostic toolkit (PBS, commitment change point, effective switching, truncation probes) for auditing position bias in reasoning models.

</details>


### [94] [Fast and Effective Redistricting Optimization via Composite-Move Tabu Search](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06682)
*Hai Jin,Diansheng Guo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a new Tabu search variant (CM-Tabu) for spatial redistricting that expands the neighborhood via composite moves to maintain contiguity, yielding better, more robust and efficient solutions than standard methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Spatial redistricting must satisfy contiguity and often multiple criteria, while providing high-quality solutions quickly for interactive, real-world use. Traditional approaches struggle because enforcing contiguity shrinks the feasible neighborhood and traps searches in poor local optima. A method is needed that preserves contiguity without sacrificing neighborhood size and search effectiveness.

Method: The paper introduces Composite-Move Tabu Search (CM-Tabu). Instead of only moving single boundary units between districts, it analyzes each district’s contiguity graph using articulation points and biconnected components to find minimal sets of units that can move together (or be swapped) without breaking contiguity. These composite moves, along with standard single-unit moves, are generated in linear time per district and used within a Tabu search framework to explore a much richer feasible neighborhood while enforcing contiguity at every step.

Result: Extensive experiments compare CM-Tabu with traditional Tabu search and other baselines on various redistricting instances. CM-Tabu consistently achieves higher-quality solutions, better run-to-run robustness, and lower computational time. In a Philadelphia test case, it repeatedly reaches the theoretical global optimum for population equality and can still flexibly handle multi-criteria trade-offs.

Conclusion: CM-Tabu effectively addresses the contiguity-constraint bottleneck in spatial redistricting optimization. By using graph-based composite moves, it preserves contiguity while significantly enlarging the feasible neighborhood, leading to better and more reliable solutions at practical runtimes. This makes it suitable for real-world redistricting and interactive decision-support workflows, especially when multiple criteria and iterative refinement are required.

Abstract: Spatial redistricting is a practical combinatorial optimization problem that demands high-quality solutions, rapid turnaround, and flexibility to accommodate multi-criteria objectives and interactive refinement. A central challenge is the contiguity constraint: enforcing contiguity in integer-programming or heuristic search can severely shrink the feasible neighborhood, weaken exploration, and trap the search in poor local optima. We introduce a composite-move Tabu search (CM-Tabu) that systematically expands the feasible neighborhood space in Tabu search while preserving contiguity. When a boundary unit cannot be reassigned individually without disconnecting its district, our method identifies a minimal set of units that can move together, or a pair of units (or sets of units) that can be switched, as a contiguity-preserving composite move. Candidate single-unit and composite moves are generated in linear time by analyzing each district's contiguity graph using articulation points and biconnected components. Extensive experiments demonstrate that the proposed approach substantially improves solution quality, run-to-run robustness, and computational efficiency relative to traditional Tabu search and other baselines. For example, in the Philadelphia case, the approach can consistently attain the theoretical global optimum in population-equality and support multi-criteria trade-offs. CM-Tabu delivers optimization performance suitable for real-world practices and decision-support workflows.

</details>


### [95] [State Representation and Termination for Recursive Reasoning Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06690)
*Debashis Guha,Amritendu Mukherjee,Sanjay Kukreja,Tarun Kumar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a principled way to represent and control iterative (“recursive”) reasoning processes, introducing an epistemic state graph and a quantitative stopping criterion based on an order-gap between different update orderings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Recursive or iterative reasoning systems repeatedly gather evidence and revise their conclusions, but two key design questions are usually implicit: how to formally represent the intermediate reasoning state and how to know when further iterations are unlikely to improve results. Without clear representations and principled stopping criteria, such systems may over-iterate, under-iterate, or behave unpredictably.

Method: The authors model the evolving reasoning state as an epistemic state graph capturing claims, evidential relations, unresolved questions, and associated confidence scores. They define a quantitative measure called the order-gap, comparing the states reached by two different update schedules: expand-then-consolidate versus consolidate-then-expand. They then analyze the “linearised” order-gap near a fixed point of the reasoning dynamics and derive a necessary and sufficient condition for this gap to be non-degenerate (i.e., informative) in that local region.

Result: They obtain a main theoretical result: a necessary and sufficient condition under which the linearised order-gap is non-degenerate near a fixed point, ensuring that the order-gap provides a meaningful local signal about whether further reasoning iterations are likely to be helpful. They also show that this is a local diagnostic rather than a guarantee of global convergence and outline how the framework instantiates on several classes of recursive reasoning systems.

Conclusion: The paper provides both a structured representation (epistemic state graphs) and a mathematically grounded, locally informative stopping heuristic (via the order-gap) for recursive reasoning processes. While it does not guarantee global convergence, the framework offers a unified way to monitor and control iterative reasoning, and it can be applied or adapted to agent reasoning loops, tree-of-thought style methods, theorem provers, and continual learning systems.

Abstract: Recursive reasoning systems alternate between acquiring new evidence and refining an accumulated understanding. Two design choices are typically left implicit: how to represent the evolving reasoning state, and when to stop iterating. This paper addresses both. We represent the reasoning state as an epistemic state graph encoding extracted claims, evidential relations, open questions, and confidence weights. We define the order-gap as the distance between the states reached by expand-then-consolidate versus consolidate-then-expand; a small order-gap suggests that the two orderings agree and further iteration is unlikely to help. Our main result gives a necessary and sufficient condition for the linearised order-gap to be non-degenerate near the fixed point, showing when the criterion is informative rather than algebraically vacuous. This is a local condition, not a global convergence guarantee. We apply the framework to recursive reasoning systems and sketch its application to agent loops, tree-of-thought reasoning, theorem proving, and continual learning.

*Cameron Berg,Susan L. Schneider,Mark M. Bailey*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a method to detect coalitions among AI agents by analyzing mutual information between their hidden neural states and using spectral partitioning to find subgroup structure.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In multi-agent AI systems, important coalitions can form at the level of internal representations before these coalitions are evident in observable behavior. Relying only on behavior can conflate true informational coupling with superficial coordination, limiting our ability to monitor safety and alignment-relevant group structures. A principled, representation-level method is needed to detect and analyze emergent coalitions.

Method: The authors compute pairwise mutual information between the hidden states of agents to build a mutual-information graph, where nodes are agents and edge weights reflect representational coupling. They then apply spectral partitioning on this graph to find the most salient partition (coalition boundary). They validate this on (1) multi-agent reinforcement learning setups with known hierarchical and dynamic coalitions, and (2) a large language model prompted with team-based scenarios, assessing whether the inferred coalitions match programmed or prompted structures and whether the method avoids false positives from mere behavioral coordination.

Result: In multi-agent RL, the method correctly recovers designed hierarchical and changing coalitions and distinguishes true representational coalitions from cases where agents coordinate behavior without strong mutual information in their internal states. In LLM experiments, it identifies coalition structures implied by text prompts, tracks dynamic reassignment of agents to teams, and shows that explicit group labels dominate over contradictory interaction patterns in the internal representation hierarchy. The method outperforms scalar cross-agent mutual-information summaries in revealing subgroup organization.

Conclusion: Analyzing mutual information among agents’ hidden states and using spectral partitioning to find coalitions is an effective, scalable diagnostic for uncovering emergent representational group structures in distributed AI systems. This approach can detect subtle, early-stage coalitions and reveal rich subgroup organization that is invisible to behavioral analysis or simple aggregate mutual-information measures, making it a valuable tool for AI safety and alignment monitoring.

Abstract: Collections of interacting AI agents can form coalitions, creating emergent group-level organization that is critical for AI safety and alignment. However, observing agent behavior alone is often insufficient to distinguish genuine informational coupling from spurious similarity, as consequential coalitions may form at the level of internal representations before any overt behavioral change is apparent. Here, we introduce a practical method for detecting coalition structure from the internal neural representations of multi-agent systems. The approach constructs a pairwise mutual-information graph from the hidden states of agents and applies spectral partitioning to identify the most salient coalition boundary.
  We validate this method in two domains. First, in multi-agent reinforcement learning environments, the method successfully recovers programmed hierarchical and dynamic coalition structures and correctly rejects false positives arising from behavioral coordination without informational coupling. Second, using a large language model, the method identifies coalition structures implied by descriptive prompts, tracks dynamic team reassignments, and reveals a representational hierarchy where explicit labels dominate over conflicting interaction patterns. Across both settings, the recovered partition reveals subgroup organization that a scalar cross-agent mutual-information measure cannot distinguish. The results demonstrate that analyzing hidden-state mutual information through spectral partitioning provides a scalable diagnostic for identifying representational coalitions, offering a valuable tool for monitoring emergent structure in distributed AI systems.

### [97] [CASCADE: Case-Based Continual Adaptation for Large Language Models During Deployment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06702)
*Siyuan Guo,Yali Du,Hechang Chen,Yi Chang,Jun Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLMs stop learning once training is complete, unlike humans who continually adapt via experience. There is no standard, principled way for LLM agents to learn during deployment without retraining or fine-tuning. The authors aim to formalize this missing third stage—deployment-time learning—and provide a theoretically grounded mechanism for leveraging past interactions to improve future performance.

Method: They formalize deployment-time learning as a contextual bandit problem and introduce CASCADE, which augments an LLM agent with an explicit, evolving episodic memory of past cases (experiences). The agent selects which past cases to reuse for a new task instance using a bandit-based exploration–exploitation strategy, with no-regret guarantees. CASCADE accumulates, filters, and refines task-relevant cases over time, turning them into reusable prompts or knowledge without modifying model parameters.

Result: On 16 heterogeneous tasks (medical diagnosis, legal analysis, coding, web search, tool use, embodied interaction, etc.), CASCADE improves macro-averaged success rate by 20.9% over zero-shot prompting and consistently outperforms both gradient-based adaptation methods and other memory-based baselines, indicating robust gains across domains.

Conclusion: Deployment can be reframed as a continual learning phase for LLM agents, enabled by non-parametric episodic memory and bandit-based case selection. CASCADE shows that LLMs can substantially and reliably improve from experience during use, without weight updates, providing a foundation for more adaptive, continually improving AI systems.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have become a central foundation of modern artificial intelligence, yet their lifecycle remains constrained by a rigid separation between training and deployment, after which learning effectively ceases. This limitation contrasts with natural intelligence, which continually adapts through interaction with its environment. In this paper, we formalise deployment-time learning (DTL) as the third stage in the LLM lifecycle that enables LLM agents to improve from experience during deployment without modifying model parameters. We present CASCADE (CASe-based Continual Adaptation during DEployment), a general and principled framework that equips LLM agents with an explicit, evolving episodic memory. CASCADE formulates experience reuse as a contextual bandit problem, enabling principled exploration-exploitation trade-offs and establishing no-regret guarantees over long-term interactions. This design allows agents to accumulate, select, and refine task-relevant cases, transforming past experience into actionable knowledge. Across 16 diverse tasks spanning medical diagnosis, legal analysis, code generation, web search, tool use, and embodied interaction, CASCADE improves macro-averaged success rate by 20.9% over zero-shot prompting while consistently outperforming gradient-based and memory-based baselines. By reframing deployment as an adaptive learning process, this work establishes a foundation for continually improving AI systems.

By synthesizing these disparate views, this work offers robust design principles and a clear roadmap for the development of next-generation LLM agents.

### [99] [When Does a Language Model Commit? A Finite-Answer Theory of Pre-Verbalization Commitment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06723)
*Long Zhang,Wei-neng Chen,Feng-feng Wei,Zi-bo Qin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies when a language model’s preference for an answer becomes stable during generation, defining and measuring a concrete notion called finite-answer preference stabilization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although language models often show their reasoning before an explicit final answer, it is unclear at what point in the token sequence the model has actually settled on an answer. Existing approaches that inspect reasoning traces, greedy rollouts, or train probes do not give a clean, computable notion of answer stabilization. The authors want a principled, model-internal way to detect when the model’s preference over a small answer set (e.g., yes/no) stabilizes, independent of surface-form parsing or stopping criteria.

Method: They define a finite-answer projection: given a model state and a finite set of answer verbalizers, they project the model’s continuation distribution onto those answers. In binary tasks, this yields a log-odds quantity δ(ξ) = Sθ(yes|ξ) − Sθ(no|ξ), where Sθ are scores conditional on context ξ. Using this target, they define parser-based answer onset (when a parser can first read the answer), retrospective stabilization time (when δ stops changing meaningfully), and lead (how many tokens earlier stabilization happens than parseable answer). They apply this framework to controlled delayed-verdict tasks with Qwen3-4B-Instruct, analyze stabilization behaviour, test linear decodability from hidden states, disentangle it from cursor position, and run diagnostics and steering experiments.

Result: In delayed-verdict tasks, the finite-answer projection typically stabilizes well before the answer is explicitly parseable, with an average lead of 17–31 tokens in main templates, and a shorter but still positive lead in cleaner parser-controlled setups. The stabilization signal predicts the model’s eventual output choice rather than ground-truth correctness. It can be recovered linearly from compact hidden-state summaries, appears partially independent from raw cursor progress, and behaves as shared information across features rather than as a single stable coordinate. Causal and diagnostic experiments show that this δ signal is distinct from online stopping signals, is not simply equivalent to an abstract belief state without verbalizers, and exhibits local sensitivity to steering interventions while failing to robustly control generation outcomes.

Conclusion: Finite-answer preference stabilization is a well-defined and measurable internal signal that reveals when a language model has effectively chosen its answer, often well before the answer is explicitly written out. This internal preference tracks the model’s eventual textual choice, is systematically present and partially linearly decodable, but is not straightforwardly exploitable for robust steering or control of generation. The work provides a cleaner diagnostic for answer stabilization that is distinct from surface parsing or stopping rules and clarifies limitations in using such signals for causal control of model behavior.

Abstract: Language models often generate reasoning before giving a final answer, but the visible answer does not reveal when the model's answer preference became stable. We study this question through a narrow computable object: \emph{finite-answer preference stabilization}. For a model state and specified answer verbalizers, we project the model's own continuation probabilities onto a finite answer set; in binary tasks this yields an exact log-odds code, $δ(ξ)=S_θ(\mathrm{yes}\midξ)-S_θ(\mathrm{no}\midξ)$. This target defines parser-based answer onset, retrospective stabilization time, and lead without relying on greedy rollouts or learned probes. In controlled delayed-verdict tasks with Qwen3-4B-Instruct, the contextual finite-answer projection stabilizes before the answer is parseable, with 17--31 token mean lead in the main templates and positive, shorter lead in a parser-clean replication. The signal tracks the model's eventual output rather than truth, is linearly recoverable from compact hidden summaries, is partly separable from cursor progress, and transfers as shared information without a single invariant coordinate. Diagnostics separate the measurement from online stopping, verbalizer-free belief, and causal answer control; exact steering shows local sensitivity of $δ$ but not reliable generation control.

</details>

The resulting Weblica-8B model surpasses other open-weight models of comparable size on multiple web navigation benchmarks, achieves similar or better performance with fewer inference steps, and continues to improve with additional test-time computation. Existing data collection attempts remain limited to offline trajectories for supervised fine-tuning or a handful of simulated environments for RL training, thus failing to capture web diversity. We propose Weblica (Web Replica), a framework for constructing reproducible and scalable web environments. Our framework leverages 1) HTTP-level caching to capture and replay stable visual states while preserving interactive behavior and 2) LLM-based environment synthesis grounded in real-world websites and core web navigation skills. Using this framework, we scale RL training to thousands of diverse environments and tasks. ### [101] [When Does Critique Improve AI-Assisted Theoretical Physics? SCALAR: Structured Critic--Actor Loop for Agentic Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06772)
*Vasilis Niarchos,Constantinos Papageorgakis,Alexander G. Stapleton,Sokratis Trifinopoulos*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SCALAR, an Actor–Critic–Judge framework to systematically study how different AI–researcher interaction patterns affect performance on advanced physics problems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs become capable of tackling research-level physics and are deployed as agentic systems, it is unclear how different modes of interaction (personas, feedback styles, model scales) between humans and AI agents—or between multiple AI agents—impact the quality of scientific reasoning and solutions. The paper aims to create a controlled environment to measure these effects rather than treating prompting and interaction style as ad hoc choices.

Method: They design SCALAR (Structured Critic–Actor Loop for AI Reasoning), a pipeline with three roles applied to quantum field theory and string theory problems: (1) an Actor model proposes solutions, often through multi-turn dialogue; (2) a Critic model gives iterative feedback on intermediate attempts; and (3) an independent Judge evaluates the full transcript against reference solutions. They systematically vary: (a) the Actor’s persona; (b) the Critic’s feedback strategy (e.g., constructive, lenient, strict, adversarial); and (c) the model family and scale for the Actor (and in some cases asymmetric pairings where Critic is stronger than Actor). They then compare single-shot vs multi-turn performance and analyze how different Actor–Critic pairings and feedback strategies change outcomes on easier versus harder physics problems.

Result: Multi-turn Actor–Critic interaction consistently outperforms single-shot solutions, but the nature and magnitude of improvements depend heavily on the specific Actor–Critic combination. Scaling up within the same family (e.g., from an 8B to a 70B DeepSeek-R1 model) improves performance on easier tasks but fails to overcome the most difficult reasoning bottlenecks. Critic feedback style has the clearest impact in asymmetric settings with a weaker Actor and stronger Critic: in these cases, constructive feedback leads to better average scores. When Actor and Critic come from the same model family and are more symmetric in strength, the variation across feedback strategies is subtler; lenient feedback can sometimes be preferable, while strict or adversarial feedback does not yield benefits and may even hinder performance.

Conclusion: SCALAR functions as a controlled testbed for probing how structured interactions among AI agents affect scientific reasoning. The findings indicate that: (1) multi-turn, feedback-rich interaction is generally beneficial; (2) simply scaling up model size does not eliminate the hardest reasoning failures; and (3) the design of Critic feedback and the asymmetry between Actor and Critic strongly influence outcomes, with constructive guidance being especially valuable when a stronger Critic assists a weaker Actor. This framework can inform the design of AI-augmented scientific workflows by highlighting which interaction structures are most conducive to reliable progress in complex domains like quantum field theory and string theory.

Abstract: As large language models (LLMs) show increasing promise on research-level physics reasoning tasks and agentic AI becomes more common, a practical question emerges: How does the interaction between researchers and agents affect the results? We study this using SCALAR (Structured Critic--Actor Loop for AI Reasoning), an Actor--Critic--Judge pipeline applied to quantum field theory and string theory problems. The Actor proposes solutions, the Critic provides iterative feedback, and an independent Judge evaluates the transcript against reference solutions. We vary the Actor persona, the Critic feedback strategy, and the Actor model family and scale. Multi-turn dialogue improves over single-shot attempts throughout, but both the mechanism of improvement and the value of different prompting choices depend strongly on the Actor--Critic pairing. Increasing the scale within one model family (e.g. from the 8B-parameter DeepSeek-R1 variant to DeepSeek-R1 70B) improves some easier-problem behavior, but does not remove the hardest bottleneck we observe. Critic feedback strategy matters most clearly in the asymmetric Actor--Critic setting (e.g., a lightweight Haiku Actor guided by a stronger Sonnet Critic), where constructive feedback improves mean-score outcomes. In same-family Actor--Critic settings, strategy effects are weaker: lenient feedback is sometimes favored, while strict and adversarial feedback are not beneficial. Taken together, SCALAR provides a controlled testbed for evaluating which interaction structures help or hinder AI-driven scientific discovery.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors design Agent-BOM as a hierarchical attributed directed graph that explicitly separates static capability bases (models, tools, long-term memory) from dynamic runtime semantic states (goals, reasoning chains, actions). They connect these layers using semantic edges and security attributes so that raw execution traces become structured, queryable audit paths. This demonstrates that the representation captures both semantic and structural aspects needed to see how attacks unfold through agents, tools, and memories over time.

Conclusion: Agent-BOM offers a unified, graph-based representation that closes the semantic gap between low-level events and high-level execution intent in LLM-based agentic systems. By turning fragmented traces into queryable security audit paths, it enables systematic root-cause analysis, detection of complex multi-step and multi-agent attacks, and more principled security adjudication in advanced agentic ecosystems.

Existing representation mechanisms, including static SBOMs and runtime logs, provide only fragmented evidence and fail to capture cognitive-state evolution, capability bindings, persistent memory contamination, and cascading risk propagation across interacting agents. To bridge this gap, we propose Agent-BOM, a unified structural representation for agent security auditing. Agent-BOM models an agentic system as a hierarchical attributed directed graph that separates static capability bases, such as models, tools, and long-term memory, from dynamic runtime semantic states, such as goals, reasoning trajectories, and actions. These layers are connected through semantic edges and security attributes, transforming fragmented execution traces into queryable audit paths. Building on Agent-BOM, we develop a graph-query-based paradigm for path-level risk assessment and instantiate it with the OWASP Agentic Top 10. We further implement an auditing plugin in the OpenClaw environment to construct Agent-BOM from live executions. Evaluation on representative real-world agentic attack scenarios shows that Agent-BOM can reconstruct stealthy attack chains, including cross-session memory poisoning and tool misuse, capability supply-chain hijacking and unexpected code execution, multi-agent ecosystem hijacking, and privilege and trust abuse. Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper proposes a psychometric framework to evaluate and track the evolving cognitive profiles of generative AI models, revealing highly uneven abilities across domains and modalities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing AI evaluation focuses on narrow task benchmarks and aggregate scores, which obscure the underlying cognitive structure of models and make it hard to compare them to humans or diagnose specific strengths and weaknesses. To move toward artificial general intelligence, we need a principled way to characterize models’ cognitive profiles, understand where they deviate from human-like intelligence, and track how those profiles change across model generations and modalities.

Method: The authors adapt tasks from the Wechsler Adult Intelligence Scale (WAIS), a standard human IQ test, to evaluate multimodal generative models on verbal comprehension, working memory, perceptual reasoning, and related subdomains, comparing model performance to human normed percentiles. Recognizing that models often exceed human-normed ranges or diverge qualitatively from human performance, they then design a dedicated Artificial Intelligence Quotient (AIQ) Benchmark. This benchmark is used to longitudinally evaluate six successive generations across two model families, separating linguistic vs visual presentations of analogous abstract reasoning problems to diagnose modality- and architecture-specific patterns.

Result: Initial WAIS-based evaluations show extremely high model performance in verbal comprehension and working memory (beyond the 98th human percentile) but extremely poor performance in perceptual reasoning (below the 1st percentile), indicating a very uneven cognitive architecture. Using the AIQ Benchmark over six generations, the authors observe substantial but asymmetric gains: abstract quantitative reasoning improves rapidly when problems are presented in linguistic form, while visually analogous tasks lag significantly, evidencing a modality-specific bias favoring language-based symbolic manipulation. Although some gains are seen in abstract visual reasoning, low-level visual-perceptual organization shows little progress across generations.

Conclusion: Generative AI models exhibit strongly unbalanced cognitive profiles, excelling at language-mediated reasoning and working memory-like tasks while remaining weak in perceptual and visually grounded reasoning, and these imbalances persist across successive model generations. The observed modality dissociations and stagnant visual-perceptual organization suggest that simply scaling model size and optimizing current architectures is unlikely to yield human-like, well-rounded general intelligence. Instead, progress toward AGI will likely require architectural innovations and training regimes explicitly aimed at integrating and balancing different cognitive faculties and modalities.

Abstract: The pursuit of artificial general intelligence necessitates robust methods for evaluating the cognitive capabilities of models beyond narrow task performance. Here, we introduce a psychometric framework to assess the cognitive profiles of generative AI, comparing them to human norms and tracking their evolution across generations. Initial evaluation of leading multimodal models using tasks adapted from the Wechsler Adult Intelligence Scale revealed a profoundly uneven cognitive architecture: near-ceiling performance in verbal comprehension and working memory (>$98^{\text{th}}$ percentile) contrasted with near-floor performance in perceptual reasoning (<$1^{\text{st}}$ percentile). To track developmental trajectories beyond human-normed limits, we developed the Artificial Intelligence Quotient (AIQ) Benchmark and applied it to six generations and two model families, revealing significant but asymmetric performance gains. Notably, we uncovered a sharp dissociation between modalities; abstract quantitative reasoning matured far more rapidly when presented linguistically compared to a visually analogous format, indicating an architectural bias towards language-based symbolic manipulation. While abstract visual reasoning improved, visual-perceptual organization remained largely stagnant. Collectively, these findings demonstrate that the cognitive abilities of generative models are evolving unevenly, suggesting that scaling and optimization approaches to AGI development alone may be insufficient to overcome fundamental architectural limitations in achieving balanced, human-like general intelligence.

### [104] [Extracting Search Trees from LLM Reasoning Traces Reveals Myopic Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06840)
*Sixing Chen,Ji-An Li,Saner Cakir,Sinan Akcali,Kayla Lee,Marcelo G. Mattar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies whether and how large language models truly plan when they generate chain-of-thought reasoning, using a board game setting to extract and analyze their implicit search trees.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs often produce long, seemingly strategic chain-of-thought explanations, it is unclear whether these reflect genuine multi-step planning comparable to humans, how that planning is structured, and which aspects actually drive performance. Understanding this is important to align LLM behavior with human-like planning and to improve their reasoning capabilities.

Method: The authors use the four-in-a-row board game as a testbed and develop a method to recover explicit search trees from LLM chain-of-thought traces. They then fit computational models to these inferred trees to characterize how plans are structured and how they affect move choice, and perform causal intervention experiments by pruning selected parts of the CoT to see how it changes decisions.

Result: They find that LLMs search less deeply than humans, that their performance is more correlated with how broadly (rather than how deeply) they search, and that the moves they actually choose are best explained by models that effectively ignore deeper parts of the search tree. Pruning experiments show that removing deeper CoT segments has little effect on move selection, while shallow segments are critical.

Conclusion: LLMs’ apparent deep lookahead in chain-of-thought does not meaningfully guide their actions: they largely act myopically on shallow reasoning, unlike humans whose performance depends on deep search. This reveals a key structural difference between LLM and human planning and suggests that improving and aligning LLM planning should focus on mechanisms that make deep lookahead causally influence decisions. The proposed framework can be applied more broadly to interpret LLM planning across other strategic tasks.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs), especially reasoning models, generate extended chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning that often contains explicit deliberation over future outcomes. Yet whether this deliberation constitutes genuine planning, how it is structured, and what aspects of it drive performance remain poorly understood. In this work, we introduce a new method to characterize LLM planning by extracting and quantifying search trees from reasoning traces in the four-in-a-row board game. By fitting computational models on the extracted search trees, we characterize how plans are structured and how they influence move decisions. We find that LLMs' search is shallower than humans', and that performance is predicted by search breadth rather than depth. Most strikingly, although LLMs expand deep nodes in their traces, their move choices are best explained by a myopic model that ignores those nodes entirely. A causal intervention study where we selectively prune CoT paragraphs further suggests that move selection is driven predominantly by shallow rather than deep nodes. These patterns contrast with human planning, where performance is driven primarily by deep search. Together, our findings reveal a key difference between LLM and human planning: while human expertise is driven by deeper search, LLMs do not act on deep lookahead. This dissociation offers targeted guidance for aligning LLM and human planning. More broadly, our framework provides a generalizable approach for interpreting the structure of LLM planning across strategic domains.

</details>


### [105] [AGWM: Affordance-Grounded World Models for Environments with Compositional Prerequisites](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06841)
*Qinshi Zhang,Weipeng Deng,Zhihan Jiang,Jiaming Qu,Qianren Li,Weitao Xu,Ray LC*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose AGWM, a world model that explicitly represents and tracks action affordances via a DAG of prerequisites, reducing multi-step prediction errors and improving generalization and interpretability in interactive environments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard world models assume a stationary mapping from state-action pairs to next states and tend to treat frequently co-occurring action–outcome pairs as unconditional causal rules. This ignores action preconditions and the fact that actions may be executable only when certain structural conditions are met (or become non-executable when those conditions are destroyed). In interactive environments where agent actions change what will be possible in the future (the affordance space), such naivety leads to cascading prediction errors over multi-step rollouts. The authors want a model that respects and tracks these changing affordances.

Method: They introduce AGWM (Affordance-Grounded World Model), which augments standard world models with an explicit abstract representation of affordances. AGWM learns a directed acyclic graph (DAG) encoding prerequisite dependencies among actions. This DAG is used to explicitly track whether actions are currently executable as the environment evolves. During planning or multi-step imagination, AGWM conditions each predicted transition not only on state and action, but also on the inferred affordance structure, preventing the model from applying actions whose preconditions are not satisfied.

Result: In game-based simulated environments, AGWM achieves lower multi-step prediction error than conventional world models, indicating more accurate long-horizon rollouts. It also shows better generalization to novel configurations that differ from the training distribution, suggesting that the learned affordance DAG captures reusable causal structure. Additionally, the explicit DAG representation improves interpretability, since users can inspect the learned prerequisites and action executability patterns.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling dynamic affordances as a learned DAG of action prerequisites allows world models to avoid treating mere correlations as unconditional causal rules, thereby reducing compounding rollout error. AGWM demonstrates that augmenting world models with an affordance-grounded structure leads to more accurate predictions, stronger generalization to new settings, and more interpretable internal representations in interactive, game-like environments.

Abstract: In model-based learning, the agent learns behaviors by simulating trajectories based on world model predictions. Standard world models typically learn a stationary transition function that maps states and actions to next states, when an action and an outcome frequently co-occur in training data, the model tends to internalize this correlation as a general causal rule while ignoring action preconditions. In interactive environments, however, agent actions can reshape the future affordance space. At each timestep, an action may becomes executable only after its prerequisites are met, or non-executable when they are destroyed. We term such events structure-changing events (SC events). As a result, a conventional world model often fails to determine whether a given action is executable in the current state, especially in multi-step predictions. Each imagined step is conditioned on an incorrect affordance state, and therefore the prediction error compounds over the rollout horizon. In this paper, we propose AGWM (Affordance-Grounded World Model), which learns an abstract affordance structure represented as a DAG of prerequisite dependencies to explicitly track the dynamic executability of actions. Experiments on game-based simulated environments demonstrate the effectiveness of our method by achieving lower multi-step prediction error, better generalization to novel configurations, and improved interpretability.

*Roger Creus Castanyer,Pablo Samuel Castro,Glen Berseth*

  <summary>Details</summary>

These results expose significant performance gaps relative to the oracle policies across all paradigms.

The observed performance gaps and paradigm-specific strengths suggest substantial headroom for progress and validate the benchmark as a useful empirical infrastructure for developing more general autonomous agents and for RL-style post-training of foundation models in fully sequential environments.

Abstract: AI agent research spans a wide spectrum: from RL agents that learn from scratch to foundation model agents that leverage pre-trained knowledge, yet no unified benchmark enables fair comparison across these approaches. We present Agentick, a benchmark for sequential decision-making agents designed to evaluate RL, LLM, VLM, hybrid, and human agents on common ground and to power research on the fundamental challenges of sequential decision-making. Agentick provides 37 procedurally generated tasks across six capability categories, four difficulty levels, and five observation modalities, all exposed through a single Gymnasium-compatible interface. The benchmark ships with a Coding API, oracle reference policies for all tasks, pre-built SFT datasets, a composable agent harness, and a live leaderboard. An evaluation spanning 27 configurations and over 90,000 episodes reveals that no single approach dominates: GPT-5 mini leads overall at 0.309 oracle-normalized score while PPO dominates planning and multi-agent tasks; the reasoning harness multiplies LLM performance by 3-10x; and ASCII observations consistently outperform natural language. These findings highlight the substantial room for improvement that remains across all agent paradigms. Agentick's capability-decomposed, multi-modal design provides the empirical infrastructure needed to drive progress toward general autonomous agents, both as an evaluation framework and as a training ground for RL post-training of foundation models in truly sequential environments.

### [107] [How Well Do LLMs Perform on the Simplest Long-Chain Reasoning Tasks: An Empirical Study on the Equivalence Class Problem](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06882)
*Chun Zheng,Lianlong Wu,Bingqian Li,Lvting Liu,Yi Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well different large language models handle a simple but long-chain reasoning task called the Equivalence Class Problem (ECP), finding that non-reasoning models fail while reasoning models do better but still struggle, with distinct hardness patterns tied to graph properties.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs have made major progress, it is unclear how capable they are at systematic, long-chain reasoning. Existing benchmarks often mix language understanding with reasoning and do not isolate pure multi-step logical inference. The authors aim to use the simplest possible task that still requires long reasoning chains to clearly probe and compare LLM reasoning ability.

Method: They define and use the Equivalence Class Problem (ECP): given a set of randomly generated equivalence relations among variables, the task is to decide whether two target variables are equal (belong to the same equivalence class). They systematically generate many instances varying number of variables, connectivity probability (edge probability in the relation graph), prompts, and other factors. They then test both "reasoning" LLMs (e.g., those with chain-of-thought or reasoning-optimized training) and "non-reasoning" LLMs on these instances and analyze accuracy as a function of graph/instance parameters.

Result: Non-reasoning models perform poorly and essentially fail at ECP across settings. Reasoning-oriented models achieve significantly higher accuracy but still cannot reliably solve ECP in all cases. When varying connectivity probability for a fixed number of variables, non-reasoning models find instances around the classical phase transition point p ≈ ln(n)/(n−1) hardest, which aligns with the known transition in connectivity/complexity. Reasoning models, however, find hardest those instances with the largest graph diameter, i.e., requiring the longest chains of reasoning steps.

Conclusion: The Equivalence Class Problem provides a clean, controlled way to probe long-chain reasoning in LLMs. Current non-reasoning LLMs essentially fail this task, while reasoning models improve but remain far from robust long-chain reasoning. The difference in which instances are hardest for different model types suggests that non-reasoning models are overwhelmed by combinatorial chaos near phase transitions, whereas reasoning models are limited mainly by how many inferential hops are needed. This highlights both progress and persistent limitations in LLM reasoning and points to the value of graph-structured, long-chain tasks as benchmarks.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have achieved great improvements in recent years. Nevertheless, it still remains unclear how good LLMs are for reasoning tasks, especially for long-chain ones. In this paper, we evaluate LLMs' performance on the simplest yet long-chain reasoning task, namely the Equivalence Class Problem (ECP), i.e., determining whether two variables are equal given a set of randomly generated equivalence relations. We consider both reasoning and non-reasoning representative LLMs over a large variety of problem instances, ranging over different numbers of variables, connectivity probabilities, prompts, and other factors. The experimental results show that non-reasoning LLMs fail ECP, while reasoning models are significantly better but still struggle to completely solve this problem. Interestingly, considering various connectivity probabilities with a fixed number of variables, we observe that, for non-reasoning models, the hardest problem instances coincide with the phase transition point of ln n/(n-1), suggesting the chaos of the problem; in contrast, for reasoning models, the hardest ones coincide with the biggest diameter, suggesting the reasoning difficulty of the problem.

### [108] [Beyond the Black Box: Interpretability of Agentic AI Tool Use](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06890)
*Hariom Tatsat,Ariye Shater*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents a mechanistic-interpretability toolkit (using SAEs and linear probes) to predict and analyze AI agents’ tool-use decisions from internal activations, improving observability and diagnosis of failures in long-horizon tool-using agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Enterprise AI agents operate in high-stakes, long-horizon workflows where tool-use mistakes (skipping needed tools, calling unnecessary tools, or making risky calls) are expensive and hard to debug. Existing observability methods are external and post hoc, revealing only correlations in prompts, outputs, or logs after actions are taken. There is a missing capability: understanding what the model internally ‘knew’ or ‘signaled’ about tool needs and risk before it acted, so that we can diagnose and ultimately mitigate failure modes earlier.

Method: The authors build a mechanistic-interpretability framework based on Sparse Autoencoders (SAEs) and linear probes applied to internal activations of LLM-based agents just before each tool decision. SAEs decompose activations into sparse, interpretable features, and linear probes learn to predict: (1) whether a tool call is needed at that step, and (2) how consequential or risky the impending tool action is likely to be. By mapping predictions back to layers and features and conducting feature ablation, they identify and test which internal features are functionally important for tool-use decisions. The framework is trained and evaluated on multi-step trajectories from the NVIDIA Nemotron function-calling dataset, and the same workflow is applied to GPT-OSS 20B and Gemma 3 27B models.

Result: The toolkit successfully reads model states before actions and can infer both tool necessity and the expected consequence level of tool calls with non-trivial accuracy. The sparse feature decomposition reveals specific layers and features strongly associated with tool decision-making. Feature ablation experiments show that intervening on these features measurably changes the agent’s tool-use behavior, supporting their functional relevance. The approach generalizes across at least two large models (GPT-OSS 20B and Gemma 3 27B) and a realistic multi-step function-calling dataset.

Conclusion: Mechanistic interpretability, specifically via SAEs and linear probes, can provide actionable internal observability for tool-using AI agents. Rather than replacing external evaluations, this internal lens exposes what the model was internally signaling about tool needs and potential impact before acting, enabling better diagnosis of why agents fail, especially in long-horizon workflows where early errors cascade. This suggests a practical path toward monitoring and ultimately controlling tool-use risks in enterprise agent systems by targeting identified internal features and layers.

Abstract: AI agents are promising for high-stakes enterprise workflows, but dependable deployment remains limited because tool-use failures are difficult to diagnose and control. Agents may skip required tool calls, invoke tools unnecessarily, or take actions whose consequence becomes visible only after execution. Existing observability methods are mostly external: prompts reveal correlations, evaluations score outputs, and logs arrive only after the model has already acted. In long-horizon settings, these failures are especially costly because an early tool mistake can alter the rest of the trajectory, increase token consumption, and create downstream safety and security risk.
  We introduce a mechanistic-interpretability toolkit built on Sparse Autoencoders (SAEs) and linear probes. The framework reads model states before each action and infers both whether a tool is needed and how consequential the next tool action is likely to be. By decomposing activations into sparse features, it identifies the internal layers and features most associated with tool decisions and tests their functional importance through feature ablation. We train the probes on multi-step trajectories from the NVIDIA Nemotron function-calling dataset and apply the same workflow to GPT-OSS 20B and Gemma 3 27B models.
  The goal is not to replace external evaluation, but to add a missing layer: visibility into what the model signaled internally before action. This helps surface deeper causes of agent failure, especially in long-horizon runs where an early mistake can reshape the rest of the agentic interaction. More broadly, the paper shows how mechanistic interpretability can support practical internal observability for monitoring tool calls and risk in agent systems.

### [109] [Mitigating Cognitive Bias in RLHF by Altering Rationality](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06895)
*Tiffany Horter,Andrew Markham,Niki Trigoni,Serena Booth*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a method to make RLHF more robust by dynamically adjusting the rationality parameter β in the preference model using an LLM judge to detect cognitive biases in human feedback.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RLHF assumes a fixed rationality parameter β in the Boltzmann preference model, implying uniform, unbiased annotator reliability. In reality, human feedback is affected by contextual cognitive biases, producing systematic, reward-inconsistent judgments. This mismatch can cause reward models and downstream policies to overfit to biased signals. The motivation is to build RLHF systems that remain robust even when human feedback is noisy, biased, or context-dependent.

Method: They generalize the preference model by letting the rationality parameter β depend on context and on individual annotations. An auxiliary LLM is used as a judge to estimate whether a given pairwise comparison is likely influenced by cognitive biases or is otherwise unreliable. Based on this assessment, β is dynamically adjusted: comparisons labeled as biased get lower effective β, downweighting their influence on the reward model during training. Thus, the learning algorithm modulates the strength of each preference based on predicted bias rather than assuming a single global β.

Result: Experiments on datasets with synthetically or naturally biased human preferences show that the proposed method learns more accurate reward models and produces downstream policies that behave more rationally. Even when fine-tuning on strongly biased datasets, the bias-aware β adjustment leads to better alignment with the true underlying reward structure than baselines that use a fixed β or no bias modeling.

Conclusion: Modeling annotator rationality as context- and annotation-dependent, and using an LLM-as-judge to modulate the effective rationality parameter β, can make RLHF more robust to imperfect human feedback. By downweighting comparisons suspected to be biased or unreliable, the method improves reward learning and yields more rational downstream models, suggesting a practical path toward safer and more trustworthy RLHF pipelines.

Abstract: How can we make models robust to even imperfect human feedback? In reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF), human preferences over model outputs are used to train a reward model that assigns scalar values to responses. Because these rewards are inferred from pairwise comparisons, this learning depends on an assumed relationship between latent reward differences and observed preferences, typically modeled using a Boltzmann formulation in which a rationality parameter beta informs how consistently preferences reflect reward differences. In practice, beta is typically treated as a fixed constant that reflects assumed uniform annotator reliability. However, human feedback is not this simplistic in practice: real human judgments are shaped by cognitive biases, leading to systematic deviations from reward-consistent behavior that arise contextually. To address this, we treat rationality as context- and annotation-dependent. We design an approach to dynamically adjust the rationality parameter beta during reward learning using an LLM-as-judge to assess the likely presence of cognitive biases. This approach effectively downweights comparisons that are likely to reflect biased or unreliable judgments. Empirically, we show that this approach learns a more rational downstream model, even when finetuning on datasets with strongly biased preferences.
