### [110] [Self-Programmed Execution for Language-Model Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06898)
*Luke J. O'Connor*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes self-programmed execution (SPE), where the language model’s own output serves as the orchestrator program instead of relying on a fixed external controller, enabling fully self-orchestrated agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing language-model-based agents depend on a fixed, hand-designed orchestration program that dictates how the agent transitions between steps (e.g., tool calls, memory updates). This rigid controller limits flexibility and constrains the kinds of behaviors agents can exhibit. The authors aim to remove this bottleneck so that the model itself fully determines its own control flow and orchestration strategy.

Method: The author formalizes SPE using the framework of “agentic machines,” defining SPE states as states from which a model completion can load any state of an embedded copy of the machine, meaning there is no fixed, externally imposed turn-to-turn policy. To make this concrete, they design Spell, a Lisp-like language in which the model’s output is an executable program that can inspect, edit, and re-evaluate itself. Spell ensures that side-effectful operations (such as model/tool invocations) are structured so that when a program is modified and re-run, previous side effects are not duplicated or replayed. The harness only evaluates the Spell program and does not impose its own orchestration logic, leaving all orchestration decisions to the model.

Result: Using existing frontier language models that have not been trained specifically for SPE or Spell, the experiments show that these models can still successfully operate within the SPE framework. They can write and modify Spell programs to manage their own execution, and they complete challenging agentic tasks within this self-orchestrated setting. This empirically demonstrates that current LMs are capable of functioning as agents without relying on a hand-written external controller.

Conclusion: The work shows that language models can be turned into agents whose control flow is fully governed by their own generated programs rather than by a fixed orchestrator, via the proposed SPE paradigm and the Spell language. This opens a path toward training models that learn sophisticated self-orchestration policies directly, raising new questions about what kinds of control strategies and behaviors such models might develop when explicitly optimized for self-programmed execution.

Abstract: At the heart of existing language model agents is a fixed orchestrator program responsible for the state transition between consecutive turns. This paper introduces self-programmed execution (SPE), an agent architecture in which the model completion is itself the orchestrator program, and the harness evaluates this program but does not impose its own orchestration policy. I formalize this idea using agentic machines: an SPE state is one from which a model completion can load any state of an embedded copy of the machine, meaning that it is subject to no fixed turn-to-turn orchestration policy. Realizing SPE in practice is nontrivial because the same data is both model context and executable program. I therefore introduce Spell, a Lisp-based language in which programs can edit and re-evaluate themselves, and effectful expressions like model invocations are structured such that re-evaluating an edited program does not replay its side effects. Experiments with existing models, not trained for SPE or Spell, show that frontier models can operate in this regime and accomplish challenging agentic tasks. These results demonstrate how an LM can act as an agent without any fixed orchestration policy, and they raise the question of what self-orchestration strategies might be learned by a model trained for self-programmed execution. Code is available at https://github.com/lukejoconnor/spell .

It is designed to represent and learn from diverse or conflicting behaviors while maintaining tractable computation, and is evaluated on a grid-world benchmark against existing constraint inference baselines.

Result: On a standard grid-world benchmark, MOCI achieves better predictive performance than existing constraint inference baselines, meaning it more accurately explains or predicts expert behavior, while preserving competitive computational efficiency. This empirically validates its ability to simultaneously recover common constraints and individual preferences from heterogeneous demonstrations.

Conclusion: MOCI is an effective and practical framework for constraint inference in multi-expert settings, capable of accurately recovering shared constraints and distinct expert preferences even when demonstrations are heterogeneous and conflicting. Its improved predictive accuracy and reasonable computational cost make it suitable for real-world applications where aligning reinforcement learning agents with safety constraints must account for diverse human behaviors.

Abstract: Constraint inference is widely considered essential to align reinforcement learning agents with safety boundaries and operational guidelines by observing expert demonstrations. However, existing approaches typically assume homogeneous demonstrations (i.e., generated by a single expert or multiple experts with identical objectives). They also have limited ability to capture individual preferences and often suffer from computational inefficiencies. In this paper, we introduce Multi-Objective Constraint Inference (MOCI), a novel framework designed to jointly extract shared constraints and individual preferences from heterogeneous expert trajectories, where multiple experts pursue different objectives. MOCI effectively models and learns from diverse, and potentially conflicting, behaviors. Empirical evaluations demonstrate that MOCI significantly outperforms existing baselines, achieving improved predictive performance, and maintaining competitive computational efficiency on a standard grid-world benchmark. These results establish MOCI as an accurate, flexible, and computationally practical approach for real-world constraint inference and preference learning tasks.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Conclusion: The study shows that classical planning concepts, such as hierarchical decomposition and generalized policies, can be successfully integrated into LLM-based agents. Automatically learned, parameterized components and a semantic retrieval mechanism enable strong generalization to new tasks and unseen applications, significantly improving both accuracy and robustness, especially for open-source LLMs. This suggests a promising direction for building more capable and efficient autonomous LLM agents through dynamic policy learning and reuse.

Abstract: We present a dynamic policy-learning approach that combines generalized planning and hierarchical task decomposition for LLM-based agents. Our method, Hierarchical Component Learning for Generalized Policies (HCL-GP ), learns parameterized policies that generalize across task instances and automatically extracts reusable components from successful executions, organizing them into a component library for compositional policy generation. We address three challenges: (1) learning components through automated decomposition, (2) generalizing components to maximize reuse, and (3) efficient retrieval via semantic search. Evaluated on the AppWorld benchmark, our approach achieves 98.2% accuracy on normal tasks and 97.8% on challenge tasks with unseen applications, improving 15.8 points over static synthesis on challenging scenarios. For open-source models, dynamic reuse enables 62.5% success versus near-zero without reuse. This demonstrates that classical planning concepts can be effectively integrated with LLM agents for improved accuracy and efficiency.

### [113] [Optimal Experiments for Partial Causal Effect Identification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.06993)
*Tobias Maringgele,Jalal Etesami*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to optimally choose a limited set of experiments, before seeing any outcomes, so as to maximally tighten bounds on a causal query that is only partially identifiable from observational data. It formalizes this as a max-potency optimization problem, proves it is NP-hard, develops methods to compute the value (potency) of individual experiments using polynomial programming, and introduces graphical pruning rules that massively cut down the search space. The approach is validated on synthetic networks, benchmarks, and a real NHANES case study on physical activity and diabetes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In many causal inference problems, especially with unmeasured confounding, target causal effects are only partially identifiable, so one can only derive bounds rather than a point estimate from observational data. Running experiments can shrink these bounds, but experiments are expensive and there are many possible intervention choices and combinations. Practitioners therefore need a principled way to decide, in advance and under a cost budget, which experiments are guaranteed to provide the largest possible tightening of the bounds on the causal quantity of interest, across all data that might be observed. Existing work lacks a general, computationally grounded framework to measure and optimize this worst-case informational gain from candidate experiments.

Method: 1. Formalization: Define the "max-potency" problem. For a given causal graph, target query, and set of candidate interventions with costs, define epistemic potency of an experiment as the worst-case reduction in the width of the identified bounds on the query that the experiment can guarantee, taken over all compatible outcome realizations. Pose the selection of a cost-constrained subset of experiments that maximizes total potency as an optimization problem.
2. Complexity analysis: Prove the max-potency problem is NP-hard via reduction from the 0-1 knapsack problem, showing that choosing experiments under a budget with maximum guaranteed benefit is computationally difficult in general.
3. Potency evaluation: Extend the polynomial-programming framework of Duarte et al. (2023) to compute epistemic potency in discrete causal models. For each experiment, formulate polynomial optimization problems that capture the pre-experiment and post-experiment feasible regions for the query and derive the guaranteed bound tightening.
4. Graphical pruning: Develop two structural (graph-based) criteria that can discard many candidate experiments before any heavy optimization.
   - Path-interception rule: A new condition exploiting district structure in the causal graph to identify interventions whose potency is provably zero. This can be checked in linear time in the graph size.
   - Identifiability-based rule: Use the ID algorithm to test whether a query becomes identifiable under an experiment; if so, categorize these experiments as ID-pruned and use their properties to simplify the subset search.
5. Search-space reduction: Show that experiments that render the query identifiable are combinatorially inert in the subset selection problem, allowing a super-exponential reduction in the number of experiment subsets that must be considered.
6. Empirical evaluation: Run simulations on Erdos-Renyi graphs and 11 standard bnlearn Bayesian network benchmarks to quantify how much pruning is achieved and to demonstrate computational gains.
7. Case study: Apply the end-to-end pipeline to NHANES observational data to design optimal experiments to better estimate the causal effect of physical activity on diabetes incidence.

Result: 1. Theoretical results:
   - The max-potency experiment selection problem is NP-hard via reduction from 0-1 knapsack.
   - A general polynomial-programming-based procedure is provided to compute epistemic potency for discrete causal models.
   - Two new graphical pruning criteria are derived: a path-interception rule (implying zero potency in certain structural configurations) and an identifiability-based rule using the ID algorithm.
   - Experiments that render the query identifiable are shown to be combinatorially inert in the subset selection, enabling drastic reductions in the number of subsets considered.
2. Empirical results:
   - On Erdos-Renyi random graphs and 11 bnlearn benchmark networks, the combination of the path-interception and ID-based pruning rules discards 50–88% of candidate experiments on average without solving any polynomial programs.
   - The resulting search over experiment subsets is reduced super-exponentially compared to the naive power-set search.
   - In the NHANES case study, the method successfully identifies a set of experiments on physical activity (and possibly related variables) that are optimal, under the model and budget, for tightening bounds on the causal effect of physical activity on diabetes.

Conclusion: The paper introduces a rigorous framework for choosing cost-constrained experiments that are maximally informative, in a worst-case sense, for tightening bounds on partially identifiable causal queries. It establishes that the problem is computationally hard, but shows that combining polynomial-program-based potency evaluation with carefully designed graphical pruning rules makes it tractable in many realistic settings. The graphical criteria eliminate a large fraction of unhelpful experiments and dramatically shrink the subset search space. Empirical tests on synthetic and benchmark networks, as well as a real-world NHANES application, demonstrate that the proposed approach can efficiently guide experimental design for causal effect estimation when only partial identifiability is available from observational data.

Abstract: Causal queries are often only partially identifiable from observational data, and experiments that could tighten the resulting bounds are typically costly. We study the problem of selecting, prior to observing experimental outcomes, a cost-constrained subset of experiments that maximally tightens bounds on a target query. We formalize this as the max-potency problem, where epistemic potency measures the worst-case reduction in bound width guaranteed by an experiment, and show that this problem is NP-hard via a reduction from 0-1 knapsack. Building on the polynomial-programming framework of Duarte et al. (2023), we give a general procedure for evaluating epistemic potency in discrete settings. To control the super-exponential search space, we introduce two graphical pruning criteria that depend only on the causal graph and the query: a novel path-interception rule that exploits district structure to certify zero potency in linear time, and an identifiability check based on the ID algorithm. On Erdos-Renyi random graphs and 11 bnlearn benchmark networks, the two criteria together prune 50-88% of candidate experiments on average without solving a single polynomial program. For the general subset search, we show that ID-pruned experiments are combinatorially inert, yielding a super-exponential reduction in the number of subsets evaluated. We close with an end-to-end demonstration on observational NHANES data, selecting optimal experiments for estimating the effect of physical activity on diabetes.

</details>


### [114] [Adaptive auditing of AI systems with anytime-valid guarantees](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07002)
*Siyu Zhou,Patrick Vossler,Venkatesh Sivaraman,Yifan Mai,Jean Feng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a statistically rigorous framework for auditing generative AI via highly adaptive testing with very small sample sizes, using anytime-valid hypothesis tests based on betting (SAVI) to both search for and certify the absence of failure modes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Auditing generative AI systems for rare or subtle failure modes is expensive and time-consuming, so practitioners use flexible, adaptive testing strategies (choosing new test cases on the fly, stopping whenever they think they've seen enough). However, this adaptivity breaks standard statistical assumptions, making it unclear what conclusions are valid (e.g., whether a model is robust or an auditor is effective) when only 10–50 annotated examples are available.

Method: The authors formulate auditing as a pair of 'dueling' hypothesis tests: (i) the model's null says there is no failure mode whose performance falls below a target threshold; (ii) the auditor's null says that they possess a sampling strategy that is guaranteed to uncover such a failure mode if it exists. They use Safe Anytime-Valid Inference (SAVI), representing the auditor's process as 'testing by betting' and deriving e-processes (anytime-valid evidence measures) for both hypotheses. This supports fully sequential, adaptive sampling and stopping rules without inflating type-I error.

Result: Theoretically, they show that under sufficient auditor power, the two dueling hypotheses become asymptotically inverse: passing a stringent audit (i.e., failing to find a failure mode despite adaptive search) actually certifies that the model is robust at the desired threshold. Empirically, across simulated and/or real auditing scenarios, their SAVI-based procedures maintain anytime-valid type-I error, outperform conventional pre-specified tests in efficiency, and often enable statistically valid conclusions with as few as ~20 annotated cases.

Conclusion: Highly adaptive audits of generative AI systems—where auditors flexibly choose what to test next and when to stop—can still support rigorous statistical guarantees if framed with SAVI-based e-processes over dueling hypotheses about model robustness and auditor power. Under appropriate conditions, a passed adaptive audit certifies global robustness, and in practice these methods are both valid and sample-efficient, substantially reducing annotation requirements compared to traditional fixed-design testing.

Abstract: A major bottleneck in characterizing the failure modes of generative AI systems is the cost and time of annotation and evaluation. Consequently, adaptive testing paradigms have gained popularity, where one opportunistically decides which cases and how many to annotate based on past results. While this framework is highly practical, its extreme flexibility makes it difficult to draw statistically rigorous conclusions, as it violates classical assumptions: the number of observations is typically limited (often 10 to 50 cases) and decisions regarding sampling and stopping are made in the midst of data collection rather than based a pre-specified rule. To characterize what statistical inferences can be drawn from highly adaptive audits, we introduce a hypothesis testing framework from two 'dueling' perspectives: (i) the model's null that asserts there is no failure mode with performance below a target threshold versus (ii) the auditor's null that asserts they have a sampling strategy that will uncover a failure mode. Leveraging Safe Anytime-Valid Inference (SAVI), we formalize the auditor as conducting 'testing by betting', which translates into simultaneous e-processes for testing the dueling null hypotheses. Furthermore, if the auditor is sufficiently powerful, we prove that these two hypotheses are asymptotically inverses of each other, in that passage of a stringent audit does in fact certify the AI system as being globally robust. Empirically, we demonstrate that our proposed testing procedures maintain anytime-valid type-I error control, outperform pre-specified testing methods, and can reach statistically rigorous conclusions sometimes with as few as 20 observations.

### [115] [Behavior Cue Reasoning: Monitorable Reasoning Improves Efficiency and Safety through Oversight](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07021)
*Christopher Z. Cui,Taylor W. Killian,Prithviraj Ammanabrolu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Behavior Cue Reasoning, a method where language models emit special token cues right before certain behaviors, enabling better oversight and control during reasoning without hurting task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Oversight of LLM reasoning is hard because harmful or misaligned behavior often only appears at the end of a long reasoning chain, making real‑time monitoring and intervention difficult and costly. The authors want a scalable way to make internal reasoning more observable and controllable so that external monitors can intervene earlier and more efficiently.

Method: They train LLMs to emit dedicated "Behavior Cues"—special token sequences—immediately before particular implicit or explicit behaviors. These cues act both as signals that an external monitor can read and as levers that can be used to steer behavior. They then fine‑tune a weaker external monitor with reinforcement learning to operate on a compressed stream consisting mainly of these cues, and also design a near‑optimal rule‑based monitor that uses cues to decide when to prune or redirect reasoning. Experiments are conducted across two model families and three domains, including complex math and safety‑sensitive settings.

Result: With only the compressed cue‑based view, a weak RL‑trained monitor can prune up to 50% of otherwise wasted reasoning tokens in complex math problems while preserving performance. In a safety environment where too many constraint violations cause failure, the rule‑based monitor combined with Behavior Cue Reasoning recovers safe actions from 80% of traces that would have ended with unsafe actions, improving overall success from 46% to 96%. These gains occur without degrading the core reasoning performance of the base models.

Conclusion: Behavior Cue Reasoning makes LLM reasoning more monitorable and controllable by having models surface structured cues before critical behaviors. This enables more efficient and effective oversight, including early pruning of unproductive reasoning and recovery from otherwise unsafe reasoning trajectories, without sacrificing task performance. The work illustrates a path toward scalable oversight where the model being monitored is explicitly trained to reason in ways that are easier to oversee.

Abstract: Reasoning in Large Language Models (LLMs) poses a challenge for oversight as many misaligned behaviors do not surface until reasoning concludes. To address this, we introduce Behavior Cue Reasoning for making LLM reasoning more controllable and monitorable. Behavior Cues are special token sequences that a model is trained to emit immediately before specific implicit and explicit behaviors, acting as dual purpose signal and control levers. When fine-tuning a weaker external monitor with Reinforcement Learning for reasoning oversight, a compressed view of only information surfaced by Behavior Cues is sufficient signal for the monitor to prune up to 50% of otherwise wasted reasoning tokens in complex math problem solving. When leveraged by an almost optimal rule-based monitor in an environment where excessive constraint violations results in failure, \ours allows for the recovery of safe actions from 80% of reasoning traces that would otherwise end with the proposal of an unsafe action, more than doubling the success rate from 46% to 96%. Through evaluation across two model families and three domains, we show that \bcreasoning improves reasoning monitorability and controllability with no cost to performance. More broadly, our work progresses scalable oversight by demonstrating how the monitored model itself can be trained to reason more tractably to oversight.

### [116] [The Context Gathering Decision Process: A POMDP Framework for Agentic Search](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07042)
*Chinmaya Kausik,Adith Swaminathan,Nathan Kallus*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a decision-process framework and practical mechanisms to manage LLM agents’ working memory so they can gather and use context more efficiently in large environments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents often operate over environments much larger than their context windows (e.g., big codebases or databases). Without explicit support, their internal working memory becomes fuzzy and lossy, leading to repeated searches, loops, and early stopping because they can’t reliably track what has been explored and what remains. The authors want a principled way to model and improve this search and context-gathering behavior.

Method: They formalize the problem as a Context Gathering Decision Process (CGDP), a kind of POMDP where the agent’s goal is to refine its belief state to isolate task-relevant information. They interpret LLM behavior as approximate Thompson Sampling within this CGDP and introduce a predicate-based representation that turns the LLM’s implicit search state into explicit, modular elements. Based on this, they design: (1) a persistent predicate-based belief state that tracks discovered information and bounds the context while still supporting multi-hop reasoning; and (2) a programmatic exhaustion gate that detects when further search is unlikely to be useful and halts it. These are implemented as plug-and-play interventions around existing iterative LLM agents.

Result: In experiments across four methods and three QA domains, replacing the LLM’s implicit internal state with the CGDP-style predicate belief state improves multi-hop reasoning accuracy by up to 11.4%. The exhaustion gate reduces token usage by up to 39% without reducing task performance, demonstrating more efficient search and context usage.

Conclusion: Viewing the LLM agent loop as a Context Gathering Decision Process yields a structured way to design improvements in how agents search and manage context. Predicate-based explicit belief states and programmatic exhaustion detection can be added modularly to existing agents, improving reasoning quality and efficiency without interfering with their core capabilities. This suggests CGDP is a useful conceptual and practical framework for building more reliable, efficient LLM agents in large environments.

Abstract: Large Language Model (LLM) agents are deployed in complex environments -- such as massive codebases, enterprise databases, and conversational histories -- where the relevant state far exceeds their context windows. To navigate these spaces, an agent must iteratively explore the environment to find relevant information. However, without explicit infrastructure, an agent's working memory can degrade into lossy representations of the search state, resulting in redundant work (e.g. repetitive looping) and premature stopping. In this work, we formalize this challenge as the Context Gathering Decision Process (CGDP), a specialized Partially Observable Markov Decision Process, where an agent's objective is to adaptively refine its belief state to isolate the necessary information for a task. We model an LLM's behavior as approximate Thompson Sampling within this CGDP, and introduce a predicate-based method that decomposes an LLM's implicit search into explicit and modular operations. We then derive two plug-and-play interventions for iterative LLM agents: a persistent, predicate-based belief state that bounds context while preserving multi-hop reasoning, and a programmatic exhaustion gate that halts unproductive search without premature stopping. Across four methods and three question-answering domains, we empirically validate that replacing an LLM's implicit state with our CGDP-motivated belief state improves multi-hop reasoning by up to $11.4\%$; while the modular programmatic exhaustion detection saves up to $39\%$ of tokens without any degradation in agent performance. Ultimately, we argue that framing the LLM agent loop as a CGDP can guide the design of modular, non-interfering improvements to agentic search harnesses.

### [117] [2.5-D Decomposition for LLM-Based Spatial Construction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07066)
*Paul Whitten,Li-Jen Chen,Sharath Baddam*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a neuro-symbolic “2.5-D” pipeline that lets an LLM only plan in 2D while a deterministic executor handles height, greatly reducing 3D placement errors in block-building tasks and achieving near-ceiling performance on benchmarks and edge hardware.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous systems that physically build structures from language need precise 3D spatial reasoning. Current LLMs systematically mis-handle coordinates in 3D block placement, causing avoidable construction errors. The authors want a way to keep using LLMs’ strengths in language and high-level planning while eliminating classes of predictable geometric mistakes that arise when the model directly outputs full 3D coordinates.

Method: They introduce a neuro-symbolic pipeline based on “2.5-D decomposition.” The LLM is restricted to planning in the 2D horizontal plane (x–y positions and high-level actions), while a deterministic executor computes all vertical (z-axis) placements using column occupancy and physical constraints like gravity. This removes the need for the LLM to reason about height explicitly. They evaluate the pipeline using GPT-4o-mini and other baselines on the Build What I Mean benchmark, perform controlled ablations to isolate the effect of 2.5-D decomposition, and test transfer to edge hardware (Nemotron-3 120B on Jetson Thor AGX) and to another dataset (IGLU collaborative building tasks).

Result: On the Build What I Mean benchmark (160 rounds), GPT-4o-mini with the proposed pipeline reaches 94.6% mean structural accuracy over 12 runs, very close to the benchmark’s effective upper bound of 97.6% due to inherent architect-side errors. This surpasses GPT-4o without the pipeline (90.3%) and the best prior system (76.3%). Ablation experiments show that 2.5-D decomposition alone yields a 50.7 percentage point gain in accuracy, indicating it is the main driver of improvements. The same pipeline, without prompt changes, achieves 94.5% accuracy when run locally with Nemotron-3 120B on an NVIDIA Jetson Thor AGX. A transfer experiment on 500 IGLU collaborative building tasks shows similar benefits, indicating the method generalizes beyond the initial benchmark.

Conclusion: Confining the LLM to 2D planning and offloading deterministic vertical reasoning to a symbolic executor (“2.5-D decomposition”) largely eliminates a major class of 3D coordinate errors in language-driven construction tasks. This neuro-symbolic design achieves near-ceiling performance on a standard benchmark, outperforming stronger LLM baselines and prior systems, and transfers robustly to edge hardware and other building tasks. More broadly, the work argues that systematically removing deterministic dimensions from the LLM’s output space is an effective, general strategy for autonomous construction and assembly problems where physical constraints fix some degrees of freedom.

Abstract: Autonomous systems that build structures from natural-language instructions need reliable spatial reasoning, yet large language models (LLMs) make systematic coordinate errors when generating three-dimensional block placements. We present a neuro-symbolic pipeline based on \emph{2.5-D decomposition}: the LLM plans in the two-dimensional horizontal plane while a deterministic executor computes all vertical placement from column occupancy, eliminating an entire class of errors. On the Build What I Mean benchmark (160 rounds), GPT-4o-mini with this pipeline achieves 94.6\% mean structural accuracy across 12 independent runs, within 3.0 percentage points of the 97.6\% ceiling imposed by architect-agent errors that no builder-side improvement can address. This outperforms both GPT-4o at 90.3\% and the best competing system at 76.3\%. A controlled ablation confirms that 2.5-D decomposition is the dominant contributor, accounting for 50.7 percentage points of accuracy. The pipeline transfers directly to edge hardware: Nemotron-3 120B running locally on an NVIDIA Jetson Thor AGX matches the cloud result at 94.5\% with no prompt modifications. The underlying principle, removing deterministic dimensions from the LLM's output space, applies to any autonomous construction or assembly task where gravity or other physical constraints fix one or more degrees of freedom. A transfer experiment on 500 IGLU collaborative building tasks confirm the effect generalizes beyond the primary benchmark.

### [118] [TeamBench: Evaluating Agent Coordination under Enforced Role Separation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07073)
*Yubin Kim,Chanwoo Park,Taehan Kim,Eugene Park,Samuel Schmidgall,Salman Rahman,Chunjong Park,Cynthia Breazeal,Xin Liu,Hamid Palangi,Hae Won Park,Daniel McDuff*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: TeamBench is a benchmark to test whether multi-agent systems truly coordinate when their roles are enforced by OS-level access controls, rather than just by prompts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-agent agent systems assign different prompts to different roles (e.g., planner, coder, reviewer), but there is usually no hard enforcement of what each role can see or do. As a result, a team can get a high pass rate even if one agent implicitly does all roles' work. The authors want a way to measure genuine coordination and the effects of enforcing role separation.

Method: They build TeamBench, a large benchmark (851 templates, 931 instances) where tasks are split into three roles: Planner (access to full spec, no editing), Executor (can edit workspace but lacks full spec), and Verifier (can certify final answer but has restricted access and no editing). These capabilities are technically enforced by the operating system, preventing any role from having full end-to-end control. They compare multi-agent teams using only prompt-level role separation vs teams where role separation is sandbox-enforced. They also run ablation studies (e.g., removing the verifier) and a 40-session human user study with the same role separation to observe behavioral patterns.

Result: Prompt-only and sandbox-enforced teams have similar task pass rates overall, but prompt-only setups cause much more role leakage: the verifier tries to edit executor code 3.6× more often. Verifiers incorrectly approve 49% of submissions that fail an automatic deterministic grader, and removing the verifier can increase partial scores. Team effectiveness is conditional: teams help when a single agent does poorly, but can hurt when a single agent already does well. The human study reveals different interaction patterns: solos work through tasks directly, human–agent pairs often devolve into quick rubber-stamp approvals, while human–human teams invest more into coordinating missing information between roles.

Conclusion: Enforcing role separation via OS-level controls reveals that many apparent multi-agent coordination benefits are overstated when evaluated only with pass rates and prompt-defined roles. True coordination patterns and failure modes—such as over-trusting verifiers, role leakage, and conditional value of teaming—become visible only under stricter role separation and richer behavioral analysis. Benchmarks like TeamBench are necessary to properly evaluate multi-agent team designs and to understand when teams actually help or hinder performance.

Abstract: Agent systems often decompose a task across multiple roles, but these roles are typically specified by prompts rather than enforced by access controls. Without enforcement, a team pass rate can mask whether agents actually coordinated or whether one role effectively did another role's work. We present TeamBench, a benchmark with 851 task templates and 931 seeded instances for evaluating agent coordination under operating system-enforced role separation. TeamBench separates specification access, workspace editing, and final certification across Planner, Executor, and Verifier roles, so that no role can read the full requirements, modify the workspace, and certify the final answer. Prompt-only and sandbox-enforced teams reach statistically indistinguishable pass rates, but prompt-only runs produce 3.6 times more cases where the verifier attempts to edit the executor's code. Verifiers approve 49% of submissions that fail the deterministic grader, and removing the verifier improves mean partial score in the ablation. Team value is also conditional. Teams benefit when single agents struggle, but hurt when single agents already perform well. A 40-session human study under the same role separation shows that our benchmark exposes interaction patterns that pass rate misses. Solo participants work through the task directly, human participants paired with agents often collapse into quick approval, and human teams spend more effort coordinating missing information across roles.

### [119] [Online Allocation with Unknown Shared Supply](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07080)
*Tzeh Yuan Neoh,Davin Choo,Mengchu Yue,Milind Tambe*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces and analyzes an online resource allocation model where a central hub allocates an unknown, limited supply to multiple locations facing sequential demand without backlogging, and proposes a near-optimal algorithm with provable guarantees and learning-augmented extensions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Many practical systems, such as humanitarian logistics and vaccine distribution, must decide how to preposition scarce resources across locations before actual demand is realized. Stockouts cause irreversible service loss, and decisions must be made online as demand unfolds, under transportation and lost-sales costs. Existing inventory and online allocation models do not fully capture this combination of shared global supply, no backlogging, fixed-charge shipping costs, and uncertain, sequential demand. There is a need for a formal model and algorithms that capture these features and provide performance guarantees, while also being able to leverage imperfect demand forecasts common in practice.

Method: The authors define the Online Shared Supply Allocation (OSSA) problem: a central hub with a finite, unknown total supply must allocate inventory over time to multiple sites experiencing sequential demand, with fixed-charge transportation costs and penalties for lost sales, no backlogging, and where shipping now only hedges against future demand. They design a deterministic online policy called GPA (a threshold-proportional allocation rule) and analyze its competitive ratio versus an offline optimal policy that knows the entire demand sequence. They prove that GPA is a 4/3-approximation up to an additive error independent of total supply. They then prove tight lower bounds showing that no online algorithm (even randomized with knowledge of total supply) can beat this 4/3 factor or avoid an additive error term. Finally, they extend GPA to a learning-augmented setting that incorporates external forecasts (e.g., experts or ML models) and analyze robustness: the algorithm benefits from accurate advice but maintains worst-case guarantees under poor advice. They validate performance using synthetic and real-world data experiments.

Result: The deterministic GPA policy achieves a competitive ratio of 4/3 relative to the offline optimal solution, with an additive-error term that does not scale with total supply. Lower-bound constructions show this 4/3 ratio is tight and that the additive term cannot be eliminated, even for randomized algorithms with knowledge of the total supply. The learning-augmented version of GPA is able to leverage high-quality forecasts to improve empirical performance, while preserving robustness if the forecasts are arbitrarily bad. Empirically, on both synthetic and real-world datasets, GPA outperforms natural baseline policies, especially in regimes where the global supply is scarce.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the OSSA model provides a realistic framework for many resource prepositioning problems with shared, limited supply and no backlogging. The proposed GPA policy is provably near-optimal, with tight competitive guarantees that cannot be fundamentally improved in this setting. Incorporating learning-augmented advice further enhances practical performance without sacrificing robustness. Overall, the approach offers both strong theoretical foundations and empirical advantages for real-world applications such as humanitarian logistics and vaccine distribution, particularly when supply is scarce and demand is uncertain.

Abstract: Many real-world resource allocation systems, such as humanitarian logistics and vaccine distribution, must preposition limited supply across multiple locations before demand is realized while stockouts incur irreversible service losses. To study this, we introduce the Online Shared Supply Allocation (OSSA) problem, a stateful online model in which a central hub allocates a finite, unknown supply to multiple sites facing sequential demand under fixed-charge transportation costs and lost-sales penalties. Unlike classical make-to-stock or make-to-order inventory models, OSSA precludes backlogging and replenishment only hedges against future demand. To tackle OSSA, we propose a deterministic threshold-proportional policy GPA and prove that it achieves a $4/3$-approximation to the offline optimum up to an additive term independent of the total supply. We complement this with matching lower bounds showing that the $4/3$ ratio is tight and that the additive-error dependence is unavoidable, even for randomized algorithms that know the total supply upfront. Finally, we develop a learning-augmented extension to GPA that principally incorporates imperfect forecasts (e.g., from human experts or ML models) commonly available in practice, enabling us to exploit high-quality advice while being robust against arbitrary bad ones. Synthetic and real-world experiments show that GPA outperforms natural baselines with global supply is scarce.

The gains are especially large in cases where different tools provide conflicting predictions, indicating that ARMOR effectively exploits their complementary information rather than being misled by disagreements.

However, the performance of individual tools varies substantially across reactions, making it difficult for any single tool to consistently perform well across all cases. This raises a critical challenge: how to effectively leverage multiple tools to obtain more accurate feasibility predictions. To address this, we propose ARMOR, an agentic framework that explicitly models tool-specific utilities, adaptively prioritizes tools, and further resolves the potential tool conflicts to produce the final prediction for each reaction. Unlike existing approaches that rely on simple aggregation or heuristic assignment over various tools, ARMOR organizes tools into a hierarchy that prioritizes top-performing tools and defers others when needed, characterizes their strengths through tool-specific patterns, and resolves conflicts via memoryaugmented reasoning. Extensive experiments on a public dataset demonstrate that ARMOR consistently outperforms strong baselines, including single-tool methods as well as various tool aggregation and tool selection approaches. ### [121] [Switchcraft: AI Model Router for Agentic Tool Calling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07112)
*Sharad Agarwal,Pooria Namyar,Alec Wolman,Rahul Ambavat,Ankur Gupta,Qizheng Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agentic AI systems that use external tools are powerful but expensive, and developers often default to large models, overspending on inference. Existing routing methods are tailored to plain chat completion and do not optimize for tool-using agents, where costs include both model choice and token-intensive reasoning. There is a need for a router specifically optimized for tool-calling scenarios so that systems can stay accurate while reducing inference cost and latency.

Method: The authors design Switchcraft, an inline model router for agentic tool-calling. It operates under a latency budget and chooses among multiple models with different costs and capabilities. They construct an evaluation framework across five function-calling benchmarks and train a DistilBERT-based classifier that predicts the lowest-cost model that will still be correct on a given tool-use query. The router is integrated into agentic workflows to decide in real time which model to invoke for each request.

Result: On the five tool-calling benchmarks, Switchcraft reaches 82.9% routing accuracy, which matches or exceeds the accuracy of the best single model used in the pool while reducing overall inference cost by 84%. This translates to more than $3,600 savings per million queries. The experiments also show that larger models do not always outperform smaller ones on tool-use tasks, and that models that seem cheaper per token can create higher total costs when they trigger long, token-heavy reasoning traces.

Conclusion: Switchcraft demonstrates that a specialized router for agentic tool-calling can maintain or improve correctness while drastically lowering inference cost. The findings challenge the assumption that bigger models are always better for tool-using agents and highlight the importance of considering total interaction cost, not just per-token price. This work offers a practical path for cost-aware deployment of agentic AI systems in real-world settings.

Abstract: Agentic AI systems that invoke external tools are powerful but costly, leading developers to default to large models and overspend inference budgets. Model routing can mitigate this, but existing routers are designed for chat completion rather than tool use. We present Switchcraft, the first (to the best of our knowledge) model router optimized for agentic tool calling. Switchcraft operates inline, selecting the lowest-cost model subject to correctness. We construct an evaluation framework on five function-calling benchmarks and train a DistilBERT-based classifier, deployed under a latency budget. Switchcraft achieves 82.9% accuracy -- matching or exceeding the best individual model -- while reducing inference cost by 84%, saving over $3,600 per million queries. We find that larger models do not consistently outperform smaller ones on tool-use tasks, and that nominally cheaper models can incur higher total cost due to token-intensive reasoning. Our work enables cost-aware agentic AI deployment without sacrificing correctness.

### [122] [AdaTKG: Adaptive Memory for Temporal Knowledge Graph Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07121)
*Seunghan Lee,Jun Seo,Jaehoon Lee,Sungdong Yoo,Minjae Kim,Tae Yoon Lim,Dongwan Kang,Hwanil Choi,SoonYoung Lee,Wonbin Ahn*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AdaTKG introduces adaptive, interaction-aware entity representations for temporal knowledge graphs using a simple per-entity memory updated online via a shared exponential moving average, enabling better reasoning and generalization to unseen entities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing temporal knowledge graph methods use static, parameter-bound entity embeddings that do not explicitly capture or update based on the sequence of interactions an entity experiences, limiting their ability to model evolving behavior and handle unseen entities.

Method: Model each entity as an adaptive process equipped with a dedicated memory state that is updated every time the entity appears in a time-stamped fact. Implement the memory update as a learnable exponential moving average with a single globally shared scalar controlling how much new interaction information overwrites historical memory, rather than separate learnable parameters per entity. Use these dynamic, memory-augmented representations for temporal reasoning tasks, updating them online as new interactions are observed.

Result: Across extensive experiments on standard temporal knowledge graph benchmarks, AdaTKG consistently outperforms existing TKG baselines, showing that adaptive per-entity memory improves predictive performance and temporal reasoning quality.

Conclusion: Adaptive, interaction-driven entity representations via a simple, shared-parameter exponential moving average memory are effective for temporal knowledge graphs, yielding better reasoning performance and enabling straightforward generalization to entities not seen during training.

Abstract: Temporal knowledge graphs (TKGs) represent time-stamped relational facts and support a wide range of reasoning tasks over evolving events. However, existing methods produce entity representations that are static at the entity level, in that each representation is a function of learned parameters only and retains no trace of the interactions in which the entity has participated. In this paper, we depart from this static view and propose that each entity be modeled as an adaptive process whose representation is refined every time the entity participates in a fact. To this end, we propose AdaTKG, which maintains a per-entity memory that is updated with every observed interaction, with the memory accumulating online and predictions improving as more interactions arrive. Specifically, we instantiate the memory update as a learnable exponential moving average governed by a single shared scalar instead of using learnable parameters for each entity, enabling AdaTKG to handle entities unseen during training. Extensive experiments confirm consistent gains over TKG baselines, demonstrating the effectiveness of adaptive memory. Code is publicly available at: https://github.com/seunghan96/AdaTKG.

Conclusion: Adversarial evaluation via AEB reveals that reinforcement learning for empathy can yield models that behave more robustly and responsively under manipulative user behavior, but this does not necessarily correspond to better explicit tracking of users’ emotional states. The observed gap between emotional responsiveness (final behavioral score) and ECS suggests a dissociation between externally observable empathetic behavior and legible internal state-tracking within this family of simulators. The authors emphasize that these findings should not be taken as evidence of true internal understanding or readiness for clinical use, but rather highlight the limits of current training and evaluation methods for empathetic AI under adversarial conditions.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning from verifiable emotion rewards RLVER has produced language models with strong empathetic performance, evaluated on benchmarks that assume cooperative, honest users. We construct the Adversarial Empathy Benchmark AEB and introduce the Emotional Consistency Score ECS to evaluate empathetic robustness under adversarial conditions. AEB comprises six psychologically grounded adversarial trajectory types with discriminative reward structures that penalize formulaic responses; ECS formally disentangles a model's capacity to track user emotional states from its capacity to improve them. In a controlled experiment across eight scenario-matched conditions (think and no-think conditions on 2 RLVER models, and 2 base models (Qwen 1.5B and 7B) with 480 adversarial dialogues), RLVER-PPO-Think substantially outperforms the same-scale untuned baseline (0.963 vs. 0.761, \(p<0.001, r=0.688\)), with zero dialogue collapses and 47\% higher hidden-intention detection. However, ECS remains nearly flat and is not significantly different for RLVER-PPO-Think versus Base-7B-Think (\(p=0.650\)): RL training improves emotional responsiveness without measurable gains in observable state tracking. We interpret the ECS--FS (Final Score) gap as a behavioral/legibility dissociation inside this simulator family, not as evidence about internal understanding or clinical readiness.

SREGym is architected as a modular, extensible framework that orchestrates fault and noise injectors across stacks. SREGym currently includes 90 realistic, challenging SRE problems. ### [125] [Repeated Deceptive Path Planning against Learnable Observer](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07174)
*Shiyue Cao,Pei Xu,Likun Yang,Lei Cui,Shizhao Yu,Shiyu Zhang,Yongjian Ren,Xiaotang Chen,Kaiqi Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Repeated Deceptive Path Planning (RDPP) and a new method, Deceptive Meta Planning (DeMP), for agents that must repeatedly travel while hiding their true destination from observers that learn over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional deceptive path planning assumes static, non-learning observers, which is unrealistic for real-world adversaries that can learn from historical trajectory data in domains like critical goods transportation or sensitive operations. There is a need to understand how to sustain deception when the observer actively updates its prediction model over repeated interactions.

Method: The authors formalize RDPP, where an agent interacts repeatedly with an observer that updates its predictive model based on observed trajectories. They analyze why existing DPP methods fail in this setting and show that simple incremental adaptation leads to an accumulating lag behind the observer. They then propose Deceptive Meta Planning (DeMP), a two-level optimization framework: (1) an episode-level planner that adapts the agent’s path within each episode to the observer’s current prediction model, and (2) a meta-level learner that, across episodes, infers how the observer updates its model and adjusts the agent’s planning strategy to anticipate and counter those updates.

Result: Experiments in multiple environments indicate that DeMP maintains deception more effectively than existing DPP baselines when facing learning observers, significantly improving deceptive performance while keeping path cost (e.g., length or resource usage) competitive. The method reduces adaptation lag compared to purely incremental approaches and retains high performance even as the observer continues to learn from new data.

Conclusion: Modeling observers as adaptive learners, rather than static predictors, is crucial for robust deceptive path planning in repeated interactions. The proposed DeMP framework shows that combining fast per-episode adaptation with slower meta-level learning can sustain deception against learning adversaries, offering a more realistic and effective approach for privacy and security in multi-agent systems.

Abstract: We study the problem of deceptive path planning (DPP), where an agent aims to conceal its true destination from external observers. While existing work assumes static, non-learning observers, real-world adversaries-such as in critical goods transportation or military operations-can adapt by learning from historical trajectories. To address this gap, we introduce Repeated Deceptive Path Planning (RDPP), a new formulation that explicitly models learnable observers. We show that existing DPP methods fail under this setting, as they cannot adapt to evolving adversarial predictions. While incorporating observer previous predictions into updates enables some adaptation, such incremental updates cause accumulative lag that degrades deception. To this end, we propose Deceptive Meta Planning (DeMP), a two-level optimization framework that combines episode-level adaptation, which enables short-term policy adjustment to counter updated observer, and meta-level updates, which leverage cross-episode feedback to capture how observers update their models and accelerate adaptation in future episodes. In this way, DeMP mitigates the accumulation of adaptation lag, enabling sustained deception against a learning observer. Experiments across environments demonstrate that DeMP significantly outperforms existing approaches in RDPP while maintaining competitive path cost. Our results highlight the importance of modeling repeated interactions with learnable adversaries, providing new insights into deception and privacy in multi-agent systems.

### [126] [Three-in-One World Model: Energy-Based Consistency, Prediction, and Counterfactual Inference for Marketing Intervention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07199)
*Junichiro Niimi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a unified “world-model” for marketing decisions where a Deep Boltzmann Machine learns latent consumer beliefs that support prediction, consistency checking, and counterfactual analysis, and they show it yields better heterogeneous treatment effect estimation than common causal ML baselines in simulated data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing marketing and machine learning models either focus on prediction or on language, and typically do not jointly model how latent consumer traits, evolving internal states, and explicit firm interventions interact over time in a way that supports both prediction and reliable counterfactual reasoning. There is a need for a single framework that can (1) represent stable latent heterogeneity, (2) track time-varying effects of actions like price and promotions, and (3) answer causal what-if questions about marketing policies without re-learning or breaking the representation.

Method: They design a Three-in-One world-model architecture centered on a Deep Boltzmann Machine (DBM). The DBM is trained on demographics, time, and lagged actions and outcomes to learn a frozen latent ‘belief’ representation of each consumer. On top of this fixed belief they attach lightweight task-specific adapters. The shared belief is then used for three tasks: (i) evaluating the consistency of observed behavior with the model using the DBM’s free energy; (ii) predicting future outcomes via the adapters; and (iii) performing counterfactual inference by holding the belief constant and only changing the action input passed to the adapters. They validate the approach on simulated panel data where true latent parameters (price sensitivity, promotion responsiveness, and base preference) are known.

Result: In the simulation, the adapter-based predictors achieve visit and purchase AUC performance comparable to a strong multilayer perceptron benchmark. However, when estimating heterogeneous treatment effects, the proposed architecture substantially outperforms standard meta-learners (S-learner, T-learner, X-learner, DR-learner) and a Causal Forest fitted on the same raw features, with the largest advantage observed in a confounded price-promotion intervention setting. Additionally, analyses using free-energy clamps show that the model assigns higher energy (i.e., penalizes) hypothetical purchase paths that lack prior promotional exposure, and this penalty varies systematically with the consumer’s latent base preference as theoretically expected.

Conclusion: A Deep Boltzmann Machine can learn latent belief states that disentangle underlying consumer traits in a way that is robust to counterfactual manipulation and useful across multiple tasks. This shared belief representation allows a single world-model architecture to combine consistency evaluation, accurate prediction, and superior heterogeneous treatment effect estimation for marketing interventions, suggesting a promising substrate for decision support in data-driven marketing applications.

Abstract: Marketing decisions reflect the interaction of latent consumer heterogeneity, time-varying internal states, and explicit interventions, a structure that current prediction- and language-oriented models do not capture in a unified manner. We propose a Three-in-One world-model architecture in which a Deep Boltzmann Machine (DBM) learns a frozen belief representation from demographics, time, and lagged actions and outcomes, with lightweight task-specific adapters attached on top. The same belief supports three tasks within a single framework: (i) energy-based consistency evaluation through the DBM's free energy, (ii) outcome prediction through adapters, and (iii) counterfactual inference by holding the belief fixed and varying only the action input given to the adapter. Using a controlled simulation in which the latent price sensitivity, promotion responsiveness, and base preference of each consumer are known, we show that the adapters match a strong MLP baseline on visit- and purchase-AUC while recovering heterogeneous treatment effects substantially better than S-, T-, X-, and DR-learner meta-learners and a Causal Forest baseline built on the same raw features, with the largest gap on a confounded price-promotion intervention. Complementing this, free-energy clamps systematically penalize counterfactual purchase trajectories that lack prior promotional exposure, and the penalty itself depends on the latent base preference in the expected direction. These results indicate that DBM beliefs disentangle latent traits in a form that survives counterfactual queries, providing an integrated world-model substrate for marketing intervention.

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes HMACE, a multi-agent LLM-based evolutionary framework that collaboratively designs heuristics for NP-hard combinatorial optimization, achieving better solution quality with fewer tokens than existing LLM methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based heuristic design methods use rigid, single-agent workflows that limit exploration, lack long-term memory utilization, and often converge prematurely to local optima. There is a need for a more flexible, autonomous, and collaborative architecture that can better explore the heuristic space, reuse past experience, and improve the trade-off between solution quality and token cost on combinatorial optimization problems.

Method: HMACE reframes heuristic search as an organizational design problem and uses a heterogeneous multi-agent collaborative evolution framework. Each generation in the evolutionary process is decomposed into a loop with four specialized LLM agents: (1) a Proposer that explores strategy ideas; (2) a Generator that turns strategies into executable heuristics; (3) an Evaluator that empirically assesses candidate heuristics; and (4) a Reflector that updates an archive-based memory. The framework uses behavior-aware retrieval to recall relevant past heuristics, lightweight filtering to discard weak candidates early, and fitness-based archive updates to maintain a diverse, high-quality pool of heuristic behaviors, thereby guiding exploration efficiently and avoiding redundant evaluations.

Result: On benchmark combinatorial optimization problems—TSP, Online BPP, MKP, and PFSP—HMACE shows a better quality-efficiency trade-off than state-of-the-art single-agent and multi-agent baselines. In controlled LLM-based comparisons, HMACE attains the smallest average optimality gaps on TSP and Online BPP (0.464% and 0.223%), while consuming only 0.13M and 0.42M LLM tokens, which is substantially fewer than competing methods.

Conclusion: HMACE demonstrates that modeling heuristic design as a heterogeneous multi-agent organizational process can significantly improve both the effectiveness and efficiency of LLM-driven combinatorial optimization. The coordinated roles, archive-backed memory, and behavior-aware mechanisms enable more diverse, targeted exploration and reduced redundancy, leading to better solution quality with lower token usage than existing LLM-based heuristic design approaches.

Abstract: Large Language Models have recently emerged as a promising paradigm for automated heuristic design for NP-hard combinatorial optimization problems. Despite this progress, existing LLM-based methods typically rely on monolithic workflows constrained by rigid templates, thereby restricting memory-guided exploration and triggering premature convergence to local optima. To design an autonomous and collaborative architecture, we introduce HMACE, a Heterogeneous Multi-Agent Collaborative Evolution framework that reconceptualizes heuristic search as an organizational design problem. HMACE decomposes each evolutionary generation into an autonomous, role-specialized loop with four coordinated agents: a Proposer for strategy exploration, a Generator for executable heuristic synthesis, an Evaluator for empirical assessment, and a Reflector for archive-backed memory update. By coupling behavior-aware retrieval, lightweight candidate filtering, and fitness-grounded archive updates, HMACE guides the search toward diverse and promising heuristic behaviors while avoiding redundant evaluations. Extensive evaluations on representative COPs, including TSP, Online BPP, MKP, and PFSP, show that HMACE achieves a favorable quality-efficiency trade-off compared to state-of-the-art single-agent and multi-agent baselines. In the matched LLM-driven reference comparison, HMACE achieves the lowest average gaps on TSP and Online BPP (0.464\% and 0.223\%, respectively), while requiring only 0.13M and 0.42M tokens for the two tasks, substantially fewer than the compared baselines.

### [129] [MEMOREPAIR: Barrier-First Cascade Repair in Agentic Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07242)
*Yang Zhao,Chengxiao Dai,Mengying Kou,Yue Xiu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MemoRepair, a method to safely and efficiently repair derived memory artifacts in agentic systems when their sources change or are invalidated, preventing stale or incorrect information from influencing future behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In agentic systems, memories such as summaries, embeddings, cached outputs, and learned tool procedures are derived from earlier artifacts and then reused across tasks. When a source artifact is deleted, corrected, or becomes incompatible due to tool/API changes, its descendants can continue to exist and influence decisions, leading to subtle, persistent errors. There is no principled way to propagate these changes through the entire web of derived artifacts without either over-deleting useful content or leaving stale information in place. The authors aim to formally characterize this "cascade update" problem and design a repair mechanism that is both safe (no reliance on invalidated information) and cost-efficient (avoiding unnecessary recomputation).

Method: The authors formalize the "cascade update problem" as operating over a memory store where artifacts have provenance relationships (predecessor-descendant links). They introduce MemoRepair, a barrier-first cascade-repair contract. When a repair event occurs (e.g., a source is invalidated), MemoRepair: (1) withdraws affected descendants from visibility, (2) reconstructs candidate successors using only still-valid support plus repaired predecessors under the current interfaces, and (3) republishes only those successors that form a validated predecessor-closed set. This framework translates the repair-choice question into a scalar optimization problem balancing repair benefit and cost. Mathematically, they show that selecting the optimal set of artifacts to republish (the publication problem) reduces to a maximum-weight predecessor-closure problem, which can be solved exactly via a single s–t min-cut computation on a suitable graph representation of memory provenance and repair costs.

Result: On ToolBench and MemoryArena benchmarks, and assuming complete influence provenance is available, MemoRepair eliminates exposure to invalidated memory artifacts (reducing it from 69.8–94.3% under baselines without cascade repair to 0%). Relative to a naive exhaustive "Repair all" strategy that attempts to repair every affected artifact, MemoRepair recovers 91.1–94.3% of all possible validated successors while reducing normalized operator (repair) cost from 1.00 down to the 0.57–0.76 range, demonstrating that it maintains safety while significantly lowering repair effort.

Conclusion: MemoRepair provides a principled, contract-based solution to the cascade update problem in agentic memory systems. By treating repair as a controlled transition from invalidated descendants to validated, predecessor-closed successors, and by reducing the publication decision to an efficiently solvable min-cut problem, it achieves zero exposure to invalidated memory while preserving most of the value of derived artifacts at substantially lower repair cost than exhaustive strategies. This suggests that robust, provenance-aware memory management is both tractable and practically beneficial for tool-using and memory-augmented agents.

Abstract: Agentic memory evolves across tasks into durable derived artifacts: summaries, cached outputs, embeddings, learned skills, and executable tool procedures. When a source artifact is deleted, corrected, or invalidated by tool or API migration, descendants derived from that source can remain visible and steer future actions with stale support. We formalize this failure mode as the cascade update problem, where repair targets the visible derived state of the memory store. We present MemoRepair, a barrier-first cascade-repair contract for agentic memory. A repair event induces a controlled transition from invalidated descendant state to validated successor state: affected descendants are withdrawn before repair, successors are constructed from retained support and staged repaired predecessors under the current interface, and republication is restricted to validated predecessor-closed successors. This contract induces a scalarized repair-selection problem for a fixed repair-cost tradeoff. We show that the induced publication problem reduces to maximum-weight predecessor closure and can be solved exactly by a single s-t min-cut. Experiments on ToolBench and MemoryArena show that, with complete influence provenance, MemoRepair reduces invalidated-memory exposure from 69.8-94.3% under systems without cascade repair to 0%. Compared with exhaustive Repair all, it recovers 91.1-94.3% of validated successors while reducing normalized repair-operator cost from 1.00 to 0.57-0.76.

</details>


### [130] [EnvSimBench: A Benchmark for Evaluating and Improving LLM-Based Environment Simulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07247)
*Yi Liu,TingFeng Hui,Wei Zhang,Li Sun,Ningxin Su,Jian Wang,Sen Su*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces EnvSimBench, a benchmark to systematically evaluate and improve large language models’ ability to simulate interactive environments for training AI agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Training scalable AI agents needs interactive environments that react accurately to agent actions. Hand-crafted simulators are costly, inflexible, and limited in diversity. Replacing them with LLM-simulated environments is attractive but currently rests on an untested assumption that LLMs can reliably simulate environment state and feedback. In reality, LLM environments hallucinate, become logically inconsistent, and allow hidden state drift, which corrupts rewards and undermines agent training. There was no formal way to define, measure, or systematically improve this “environment simulation ability.”

Method: The authors (1) formally define Environment Simulation Ability (EnvSim Ability) as a measurable capability of LLMs; (2) build EnvSimBench, a benchmark with 400 samples across 167 diverse environments, each with verifiable labels and difficulty stratified along three axes; (3) run systematic evaluations of state-of-the-art LLMs on this benchmark to characterize their failure modes; and (4) design a constraint-driven simulation pipeline that uses structured constraints to guide LLM environment generation and updates, reducing hallucinations and costs while improving the quality and yield of simulated environments.

Result: Benchmark evaluations show a “state change cliff”: current LLMs perform almost perfectly when the environment state does not change, but performance collapses when multiple state variables require consistent simultaneous updates. The proposed constraint-driven simulation pipeline reduces hallucinations, increases environment synthesis yield by 6.8%, and lowers simulation costs by more than 90%.

Conclusion: EnvSim Ability is a distinct, critical capability for LLMs used as environment simulators, and current models have major weaknesses when handling complex state changes. EnvSimBench provides a rigorous diagnostic benchmark and shows that constraint-driven pipelines can substantially improve reliability and efficiency of LLM-based environment simulation, laying groundwork for scalable agent training with simulated environments.

Abstract: Scalable AI agents training relies on interactive environments that faithfully simulate the consequences of agent actions. Manually crafted environments are expensive to build, brittle to extend, and fundamentally limited in diversity. A promising direction is to replace manually crafted environments with LLM-simulated counterparts. However, this paradigm hinges on an unexamined core assumption: LLMs can accurately simulate environmental feedback. In practice, LLM-simulated environments suffer from hallucinations, logical inconsistencies, and silent state drift failures that corrupt agent reward signals and compound the construction costs that the paradigm was designed to eliminate. To address this gap, we propose EnvSimBench with four contributions: 1) We provide the first formal definition and operationalization of Environment Simulation Ability (EnvSim Ability) as a quantifiable research objective. 2) We construct EnvSimBench, a rigorous benchmark covering 400 samples across 167 diverse environments, equipped with verifiable labels and fine-grained difficulty stratification along three axes. 3) Systematic evaluations reveal that all state-of-the-art language models suffer from a universal state change cliff: they achieve near-perfect accuracy on tasks when the environment state remains invariant, yet fail catastrophically when multiple states need simultaneous updates. This finding exposes EnvSim Ability as a critical yet largely unaddressed capability gap. 4) We design a constraint-driven simulation pipeline that substantially reduces hallucination, boosts environment synthesis yield by 6.8%, and cuts costs by over 90%. Overall, EnvSimBench serves as both a diagnostic framework and a practical optimization path for reliable LLM-based environment simulation, establishing a foundation for scalable agent training. Code and data are available at https://github.com/cookieApril/EnvSimBench

### [131] [Can Agents Price a Reaction? Evaluating LLMs on Chemical Cost Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07251)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of LLM agents in scientific tool use, particularly in chemistry, lack rigorous, judge-free ground truth and typically rely on curated examples, experts, or LLM-as-judge, making it hard to quantify real performance and failure modes. There is a need for a realistic, automatically scorable benchmark that stresses the full tool-use pipeline from grounding chemical identities to final numerical cost estimates, including robustness to noisy inputs.

Method: The authors define a concrete task: given a reaction description, an agent must identify the chemicals, query a frozen snapshot of supplier pricing, select valid purchasable packs, normalize quantities, and compute the total procurement cost. They build ChemCost, a dataset of 1,427 reactions linked to a static price snapshot with 2,261 chemicals and 230,775 supplier quotes, enabling exact numerical ground truth. They design evaluation metrics for scalar accuracy and stage-wise diagnosis (grounding, retrieval, procurement, arithmetic), and construct noise-injected variants of inputs by perturbing aliases, quantities, missing fields, and formatting to test robustness. They then evaluate several LLM agents (frontier, open-weight, chemistry-specialized) with tool access on this benchmark.

Detailed stage-level analysis reveals common failure modes in parsing the reaction, integrating retrieved evidence, choosing valid purchasable packs, and reliably using tools over multiple calls, indicating that tool access alone does not guarantee robust task completion.

Conclusion: ChemCost provides a rigorous, automatically scored benchmark for scientific tool use in chemistry, highlighting that current LLM agents—even with powerful tools—are brittle and error-prone in realistic procurement cost estimation tasks. The benchmark and analyses underscore the need for more robust parsing, evidence integration, tool orchestration, and error handling methods for LLM agents in scientific and industrial settings.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have become increasingly capable as tool-using agents, with benchmarks spanning diverse general agentic tasks. In chemistry, recent agents can plan syntheses and invoke domain-specific tools, but evaluations often rely on curated demonstrations, expert assessment, or LLM-as-judge scoring rather than exact, judge-free ground truth. We address this gap with chemical procurement cost estimation, a practical task in which an agent must ground chemical identities, retrieve supplier quotes, select valid purchasable packs, normalize quantities, and compute cost from a reaction description. We introduce ChemCost, a benchmark of 1,427 evaluable reactions grounded to a frozen pricing snapshot covering 2,261 chemicals and 230,775 supplier quotes, supporting scalar scoring and stage-level diagnosis of grounding, retrieval, procurement, and arithmetic failures. To evaluate robustness, we further construct controlled noise-injected views that perturb chemical aliases, quantity expressions, missing fields, and input formatting. Experiments with frontier, open-weight, and chemistry-specialized LLM agents show that tool access is necessary but insufficient for solving the task. The strongest agents reach only 50.6% accuracy within 25% relative error on clean inputs and degrade substantially with realistic noise. Stage-level analysis further shows that failures arise from brittle parsing, ineffective evidence integration, invalid pack selection, and non-convergent tool use.
