### [132] [Structured Role-Aware Policy Optimization for Multimodal Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07274)
*Bingqing Jiang,Difan Zou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes SRPO, a role-aware token-level reinforcement learning method that improves evidence-grounded multimodal reasoning by refining GRPO’s sequence-level rewards into role-specific token credits for perception and reasoning tokens.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RL from verifiable rewards methods like GRPO operate at the sequence level and treat all tokens uniformly. In multimodal reasoning tasks, this makes it hard to know if a correct answer truly relies on the appropriate visual evidence, because the reward does not distinguish between perception (grounding in the image) and reasoning (deriving the answer). The authors aim to improve credit assignment so that models learn to both look at the right visual content and reason from it reliably, without extra reward models or teacher models.

Method: They decompose structured LVLM outputs into two functional token types: perception tokens that extract or describe visual evidence and reasoning tokens that logically derive the final answer from that evidence. Building on GRPO, they introduce Structured Role-aware Policy Optimization (SRPO), which converts the sequence-level advantage signal into role-aware token-level advantages. For perception tokens, they compute importance via self-distilled contrasts between outputs under original versus corrupted visual inputs, capturing visual dependency. For reasoning tokens, they measure consistency with the already-generated perception tokens. These role-specific signals are then combined using a shared trajectory-level baseline to produce positive token weights that rescale each token’s update magnitude while keeping the original GRPO reward, sign, and optimization direction intact. The approach does not require external reward models or separate teacher policies.

Result: On multiple multimodal reasoning benchmarks, SRPO-trained LVLMs show better evidence-grounded reasoning compared to vanilla GRPO and other baselines. The models more reliably base their answers on relevant visual content and exhibit improved reasoning quality across diverse tasks, indicating more faithful multimodal understanding.

Conclusion: Role-aware, token-level credit assignment is crucial for reliable multimodal reasoning in LVLMs. By separating perception and reasoning roles and reweighting token updates using self-distilled, on-policy contrasts—while preserving the original GRPO reward structure—SRPO enhances evidence-grounded reasoning without extra supervision. This suggests future multimodal RL work should move beyond uniform sequence-level rewards toward structured, role-aware optimization schemes.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning from verifiable rewards (RLVR), especially with Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), has shown strong potential for improving the reasoning capabilities of large vision-language models (LVLMs). However, in multimodal reasoning, final-answer rewards are typically assigned at the sequence level and do not distinguish the functional roles of different tokens, making it difficult to determine whether a correct answer is supported by task-relevant visual evidence. In this paper, we revisit multimodal RLVR from the perspective of role-aware token-level credit assignment, where structured responses are decomposed into perception tokens for extracting visual evidence and reasoning tokens for deriving answers from that evidence. Based on this perspective, we propose Structured Role-aware Policy Optimization (SRPO), which refines the sequence-level GRPO advantage into role-aware token-level advantages without changing the reward function. Specifically, SRPO assigns role-specific credit by using self-distilled on-policy contrasts: perception tokens are emphasized according to their visual dependency under original versus corrupted visual inputs, while reasoning tokens are emphasized according to their consistency with the generated perception. These role-specific signals are further unified through a shared trajectory-level baseline, yielding positive token weights that adjust relative update magnitudes while preserving the original GRPO reward and optimization direction, without requiring external reward models or separate teachers. Experiments across diverse multimodal reasoning benchmarks show that SRPO improves evidence-grounded reasoning, highlighting the importance of moving beyond uniform sequence-level credit toward role-aware optimization for reliable multimodal reasoning.

### [133] [Signal Reshaping for GRPO in Weak-Feedback Agentic Code Repair](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07276)
*Jia Li,Yuxin Su,Ting Peng,Hailiang Huang,Yuetang Deng,Michael R. Lyu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper improves code-agent reinforcement learning with weak feedback by reshaping reward signals so that GRPO’s group-wise comparisons reflect true semantic task success, leading to higher compile-and-semantic accuracy and more efficient trajectories.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In code-agent RL, feedback at rollout time typically indicates whether code compiles or runs but does not fully capture whether it satisfies the intended semantic task. Standard GRPO relies on comparing trajectories within groups using these imperfect signals, which can mis-rank behaviors, mis-assign credit within trajectories, and compare non-equivalent rollouts. The authors aim to understand and fix how such weak, execution-based signals undermine effective learning and to design a minimal modification that makes GRPO’s comparisons semantically meaningful without changing its core algorithm.

Method: The authors use an agentic compile-fix setting and standard GRPO, then introduce a structured signal-reshaping scheme. They distinguish three requirements: (1) outcome rewards must recover semantic ranking across trajectories, (2) process signals must localize credit within trajectories, and (3) rollouts for a given prompt must be execution-comparable. They implement these via: (a) compile-and-semantic layered rewards that jointly reflect compilation success and semantic correctness while preserving GRPO’s group-normalized advantage mechanism; (b) step-level process scores that modulate update strength outside the group reward normalization, to assign credit more precisely within a trajectory; and (c) failure-cause-aware rollout governance that filters or adjusts rollouts so within-group comparisons are valid. They also run controlled ablations, including binary rewards and token-level distillation, to isolate the contribution of each component.

Result: With the full signal-reshaped GRPO setup, strict compile-and-semantic accuracy rises from 0.385 (base model zero-shot) to 0.535. Using only layered outcome rewards improves performance over binary rewards, which collapse intermediate compile-only solutions and weaken trajectory control. Adding process-score weighting on top of layered rewards further increases accuracy from 0.48 to 0.53 and shortens average evaluation steps from 23.50 to 17.02, indicating more efficient trajectories. A comparison to privileged-prompt token-level distillation shows that such supervision primarily enforces local token-level distribution alignment; in long tool-use trajectories its signal is diluted by many non-critical tokens, so it cannot substitute for semantically informed outcomes, process-level credit, or carefully governed within-group comparability.

Conclusion: Reshaping weak execution-based signals is critical for making GRPO effective in code-agent RL. By layering compile and semantic rewards, adding separate step-level process scores, and enforcing failure-aware rollout governance, the authors preserve GRPO’s core structure while making its intra-group comparisons align with true task semantics. This yields substantial gains in compile-and-semantic accuracy and efficiency, and demonstrates that naive binary rewards or token-level distillation cannot provide the rich, trajectory-level feedback needed for robust code-agent learning.

Abstract: Code-agent RL often receives weak feedback: rollout-time signals are reliable and executable, but capture only necessary or surface conditions for task success rather than the target semantic predicate. Using agentic compile-fix as the setting, we study signal reshaping for standard GRPO under such feedback. Our central claim is that GRPO's within-group comparison is meaningful only after three kinds of signals are reshaped: outcome rewards recover semantic ranking, process signals localize intra-trajectory credit, and rollouts from the same prompt remain execution-comparable. We operationalize these conditions with a minimal signal-reshaping construction that leaves GRPO's group-normalized advantage construction unchanged: compile-and-semantic layered rewards reshape trajectory ranking, step-level process scores outside group reward normalization reshape within-trajectory update strength, and failure-cause-aware rollout governance reshapes within-group comparability. Experiments show a clear end-to-end gain: full signal-reshaped GRPO improves strict compile-and-semantic accuracy from the base model's zero-shot $0.385$ to $0.535$. Controlled comparisons further explain the source of this gain: binary rewards remove the compile-only middle tier and degrade trajectory control; on top of layered rewards, process-score weighting further improves accuracy from $0.48$ to $0.53$ and reduces average evaluation steps from $23.50$ to $17.02$. As a boundary comparison, privileged-prompt token-level distillation mainly optimizes local distributional alignment; in long tool-use trajectories, this signal is diluted by non-critical tokens and cannot replace outcome semantics, process credit, or within-group comparability.

Existing approaches often entangle opponent modeling with prediction, relying on implicit contextual reasoning and limiting adaptability in dynamic interactions. To this end, we propose Structured Opponent Modeling (SOM), a two-stage opponent modeling framework that distinctly separates opponent model construction and opponent prediction. At the construction stage, SOM employs a Structural Causal Model (SCM), a graph-based formalism for representing dependencies among variables, to capture directed links between opponents' observations and actions, yielding an explicit and structured opponent representation. At the prediction stage, the LLM performs structured reasoning along clear pathways derived from the SCM, improving both prediction accuracy and stability. ### [135] [When Stored Evidence Stops Being Usable: Scale-Conditioned Evaluation of Agent Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07313)
*Jiaqi Shao,Yiyi Lu,Yunzhen Zhang,Bing Luo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new way to evaluate long-term memory agents that focuses on how their reliability changes as irrelevant history grows, rather than only on static accuracy or retrieval scores.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of memory agents mostly report snapshot metrics, like accuracy on a fixed dataset or retrieval quality, which fail to capture how well agents cope as more irrelevant interaction history accumulates. In realistic deployments, agents operate over growing logs where relevant evidence is sparse among many irrelevant sessions. There is a need for an evaluation protocol that measures whether evidence remains practically usable as memory scales and irrelevant content grows, under realistic constraints like limits on the number of memory calls an agent can make.

Method: The authors introduce a scale-conditioned evaluation protocol where, for each query, the true task-relevant evidence is kept fixed but an increasing number of irrelevant sessions is added to the memory. They track how an agent’s behavior and performance evolve along this growth trajectory. The protocol is applied to existing benchmarks (LongMemEval and LoCoMo) and multiple memory interface types (flat, planar, hierarchical). It defines and logs four diagnostics: (1) budget-compliant reliability, i.e., accuracy when the agent respects a specified limit on memory calls; (2) tail memory-call burden, characterizing how many calls are needed in difficult, large-scale conditions; (3) failure-regime decomposition, breaking down types or causes of failure as scale grows; and (4) a usable-scale boundary, the largest scale at which the agent maintains target reliability.

Result: Using this protocol, the authors find that degradation in performance with growing memory is nuanced and depends on the combination of agent, memory system, and interface. On LongMemEval, HippoRAG respects a two-call memory budget but its budget-compliant reliability still drops by 16–20 percentage points as irrelevant sessions increase, indicating that staying within budget does not guarantee stable reliability. For LiCoMemory, failure patterns vary strongly by base agent model: with Qwen3-8B, the system exceeds the allowed memory-call budget as scale grows, whereas with Qwen3-32B and Qwen3-235B it remains reliable within the tested scale range. These results reveal heterogeneous failure modes rather than a single uniform “memory fails at scale” phenomenon.

Conclusion: The study argues that claims about scalable memory for agents should be conditional and multi-dimensional, specified in terms of the agent model, the memory interface, the range of memory scales tested, and the interaction budget (e.g., allowed memory calls). Their scale-conditioned protocol and four diagnostics provide a more informative way to characterize when and how memory remains usable as irrelevant history accumulates, moving beyond coarse snapshot metrics to a trajectory-based view of memory robustness under growth.

Abstract: Memory-agent evaluations report fixed-snapshot accuracy or retrieval quality, but these scores do not show whether evidence remains usable as irrelevant sessions (sessions not annotated as task-relevant evidence for the query) accumulate. We present a scale-conditioned evaluation protocol for agent memory under evidence-preserving growth: for each query, task evidence is held fixed while irrelevant sessions are added. The protocol logs agent--memory trajectories and reports four diagnostics: budget-compliant reliability, tail memory-call burden, failure-regime decomposition, and the usable-scale boundary where reliability falls below the target. Applied to LongMemEval and LoCoMo across flat, planar, and hierarchical memory interfaces, the protocol shows reliability loss is not a single phenomenon. On LongMemEval, HippoRAG stays within the two-call budget but loses 16--20 percentage points in budget-compliant reliability as irrelevant sessions are added; LiCoMemory's observed failures depend strongly on the agent, with Qwen3-8B exceeding the budget while Qwen3-32B and Qwen3-235B remain reliable in the tested range. The result supports a framework for making scalable-memory claims conditional on agent, interface, scale range, and interaction budget.

### [136] [Implicit Compression Regularization: Concise Reasoning via Internal Shorter Distributions in RL Post-Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07316)
*Chen Wang,Hexuan Deng,Yining Zhang,Yuchen Zhang,Jionghao Bai,Zhaochun Li,Ge Lan,Yue Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Implicit Compression Regularization (ICR), a reinforcement learning regularization method that reduces overthinking in LLM reasoning by using the shortest correct rollouts as an implicit target, yielding shorter answers without sacrificing accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards improves LLM reasoning but often leads to overthinking, where models produce unnecessarily long reasoning traces. Existing solutions like length penalties risk accuracy loss and underthinking, while early-exit methods assume that much of the reasoning can be safely truncated, which is not always valid. A better way is needed to compress reasoning traces without hurting correctness.

Method: The authors analyze training dynamics of compression in RL and observe how the correlation between response length and accuracy evolves, transitioning from negative (shorter answers more accurate) to positive (underthinking). They formalize overthinking and underthinking via this correlation sign. Using this, they propose Implicit Compression Regularization (ICR), an on-policy regularizer that constructs a virtual shorter distribution based on the shortest correct responses within rollout groups. This implicit distribution provides a compression signal that pushes the policy toward concise but correct trajectories, without explicit length penalties or hard truncation rules.

Result: During training, ICR keeps the length–accuracy correlation in a favorable regime where shorter responses stay better aligned with correctness, avoiding drift into underthinking. Empirically, across three different reasoning-focused LLM backbones and several mathematical and knowledge-intensive benchmarks, models trained with ICR generate shorter responses while maintaining or improving task accuracy. This improves the overall Pareto frontier between answer length and accuracy compared to baselines.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that modeling and leveraging the length–accuracy correlation during RL training provides a principled way to control overthinking. Implicit Compression Regularization effectively uses the shortest correct rollouts as a self-supervised compression signal, enabling LLMs to reason more concisely without sacrificing performance and offering a stronger accuracy–length trade-off than existing compression techniques.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards improves LLM reasoning but often induces overthinking, where models generate unnecessarily long reasoning traces. Existing methods mainly rely on length penalties or early-exit strategies; however, the former may degrade accuracy and induce underthinking, whereas the latter assumes that substantial portions of reasoning traces can be safely truncated. To obtain a compression signal without these limitations, we revisit the training dynamics of existing compression methods. We observe that the length--accuracy correlation is initially negative but continually increases during compression, indicating that shorter responses are initially more likely to be correct but gradually lose this property as the policy moves toward underthinking. Based on this observation, we formalize overthinking: a negative correlation indicates an overthinking regime, while a positive one indicates underthinking. When overthinking, the shortest correct responses are shorter than the group-average response length in expectation, making them natural compression targets already present in on-policy rollouts. We therefore propose \emph{Implicit Compression Regularization} (ICR), an on-policy regularization method whose compression signal comes from a virtual shorter distribution induced by the shortest correct responses in rollout groups, guiding the policy toward concise yet correct trajectories. Training dynamics show that ICR maintains a better length--accuracy correlation during compression, indicating that short responses remain better aligned with correctness instead of drifting toward underthinking. Experiments on three reasoning backbones and multiple mathematical and knowledge-intensive benchmarks show that ICR consistently shortens responses while preserving or improving accuracy, achieving a stronger accuracy--length Pareto frontier.

Experiments on multi-dimensional ordinary differential equation benchmarks demonstrate that DoLQ achieves superior performance compared to existing methods, not only attaining higher success rates but also more accurately recovering the correct symbolic terms of ground truth equations.   <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large reasoning models often get the final answer right but via incorrect or unreliable intermediate steps, which is dangerous for high‑stakes use and hard to evaluate. Existing fixes depend on external verifiers or many sampled solutions, which are costly and don’t scale. The paper aims to directly align a model’s own confidence with the correctness of each reasoning step so that the model can self-assess and streamline its reasoning without extra verifier models or heavy sampling.

Method: The authors propose CASPO (Confidence-Aware Step-wise Preference Optimization), which extends Direct Preference Optimization to work at the level of reasoning steps and token-level confidence. They construct step-wise preferences based on which intermediate steps are logically correct and iteratively train the model so that higher token-level confidence is assigned to correct steps and lower confidence to incorrect ones, without a dedicated reward model. For inference, they introduce Confidence-aware Thought (CaT), which uses the calibrated confidence scores to dynamically prune uncertain or low-confidence reasoning branches during multi-step reasoning, designed to add only O(V) latency where V is vocabulary size.

CaT reduces the number of explored reasoning branches while maintaining or improving accuracy, leading to more efficient inference.

Conclusion: Aligning a model’s token-level confidence with step-wise logical correctness via CASPO substantially improves the reliability and efficiency of multi-step reasoning without relying on external verifiers or reward models. Confidence-aware Thought (CaT) demonstrates that such calibrated confidence can be used online to prune poor reasoning branches with negligible overhead, outperforming tree-search baselines at scale. The released step-wise dataset with confidence annotations enables further research on fine-grained reasoning reliability and confidence calibration in large reasoning models.

Abstract: Large reasoning models often reach correct answers through flawed intermediate steps, creating a gap between final accuracy and reasoning reliability. Existing alignment strategies address this with external verifiers or massive sampling, limiting scalability. In this work, we introduce CASPO (Confidence-Aware Step-wise Preference Optimization), a framework that aligns token-level confidence with step-wise logical correctness through iterative Direct Preference Optimization, without training a separate reward model. During inference, we propose Confidence-aware Thought (CaT), which leverages this calibrated confidence to dynamically prune uncertain reasoning branches with negligible O(V) latency. Experiments across ten benchmarks and multiple model families show that CASPO consistently improves reasoning reliability and inference efficiency. CASPO scales to Qwen3-8B-Base and surpasses tree-search baselines on AIME'24 and AIME'25 without using reward-model data. We also release a step-wise dataset with confidence annotations to support fine-grained analysis of reasoning reliability. Code is available at https://github.com/Thecommonirin/CASPO.

*Xingtong Yu,Zhongwei Kuai,Chang Zhou,Xuanting Xie,Renhe Jiang,Xikun Zhang,Hong Cheng,Xinming Zhang,Yuan Fang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing reasoning-acting frameworks for LLMs work well on text but do not directly address graph-structured data, where information is distributed across nodes and edges and encoded in both topology and latent representations. Graph reasoning requires not only grabbing relevant evidence from the graph but also managing and refining an evolving context over multiple steps, which current graph learning methods and retrieval schemes handle poorly.

Method: The authors propose GraphReAct, a framework that defines an explicit action space over graphs. It includes (1) topological retrieval actions that follow local structural dependencies to gather neighborhood information, (2) semantic retrieval actions that fetch non-local but representation-similar nodes as evidence, and (3) context refinement actions that summarize, distill, and reorganize the accumulated multi-step context into a more compact representation. Reasoning proceeds by interleaving these actions, moving from context expansion (retrieval) to context compression (refinement) during multi-step inference.

Result: Across six benchmark graph-learning datasets, GraphReAct achieves consistently better performance than state-of-the-art baselines. The experiments show that combining topological and semantic retrieval with explicit context refinement yields stronger multi-step reasoning over graphs than existing models that lack such a reasoning-acting mechanism.

Conclusion: Reasoning-acting can be successfully extended from text to graphs by designing graph-specific action spaces and explicit context refinement. GraphReAct’s interleaving of reasoning with both retrieval and refinement actions enables more effective multi-step inference on graph-structured data and delivers state-of-the-art results on multiple benchmarks, suggesting that structured reasoning-acting is a powerful paradigm for graph learning.

Abstract: Reasoning-acting frameworks enhance large language models (LLMs) by interleaving reasoning with actions for dynamic information acquisition. However, extending this paradigm to graph learning remains underexplored. Graph data is inherently structured, with information distributed across nodes and edges and encoded through both topology and latent representations. As a result, effective reasoning over graphs requires not only retrieving informative evidence from the graph, but also progressively refining the accumulated context during multi-step inference. In this work, we propose GraphReAct, a graph reasoning-acting framework that enables step-by-step inference over graph-structured data. Specifically, we design a graph-based action space with two complementary retrieval actions: topological retrieval, which captures local structural dependencies, and semantic retrieval, which accesses non-local but relevant evidence in the representation space. These actions dynamically expand the reasoning context. To further support multi-step reasoning, we introduce another type of action, context refinement, which distills and reorganizes accumulated information into a compact representation. By interleaving reasoning with both retrieval and refinement actions, our framework enables a progressive transition from context expansion to compression. Extensive experiments on six benchmark datasets demonstrate that GraphReAct consistently outperforms state-of-the-art methods, validating the effectiveness of reasoning-acting for graph learning.

### [141] [Offline Policy Optimization with Posterior Sampling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07393)
*Hongqiang Lin,Dongxu Zhang,Yiding Sun,Mingzhe Li,Ning Yang,Haijun Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes PSPO, a Bayesian model-based offline RL method that uses posterior sampling and constrained policy optimization to balance generalization from OOD data with robustness to model exploitation, achieving better benchmark performance than prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In offline model-based RL, learned dynamics models are often queried on out-of-distribution (OOD) states and actions. These OOD regions may reflect real but rarely observed dynamics that could help policy generalization; however, they also allow the policy to exploit model errors, leading to poor real-world performance. Existing methods typically resort to strong pessimistic regularization to avoid these exploitation errors, which protects against failure but prevents learning from potentially useful OOD transitions. The paper is motivated by the need for a principled way to quantify dynamics model uncertainty so that the agent can safely leverage dynamics-consistent OOD data without over-penalizing it, thereby improving both robustness and generalization.

Method: The authors introduce Posterior Sampling-based Policy Optimization (PSPO), which models environment dynamics within a Bayesian framework. They infer a posterior distribution over dynamics models that explicitly encodes model fidelity (uncertainty) across the state-action space. Using posterior sampling, they generate rollouts under sampled dynamics, and then perform policy optimization subject to constraints that restrict how aggressively the policy can exploit regions with high model uncertainty. Concretely, they: (1) formulate Q-value estimation under posterior sampling as a stochastic approximation process and analyze its convergence; (2) decompose the policy optimization into a series of constrained subproblems, each ensuring conservative, monotonic policy improvement; and (3) selectively leverage OOD transitions that are consistent across posterior samples, while downweighting or constraining updates in regions where the posterior indicates low model fidelity.

Result: Theoretically, the paper establishes that the Q-value estimator under posterior sampling converges via stochastic approximation analysis. It further proves that the sequence of constrained policy optimization subproblems yields monotonic policy improvement until convergence. Empirically, on standard offline RL benchmarks, the proposed PSPO algorithm outperforms state-of-the-art baselines in terms of final return, demonstrating better use of OOD transitions while remaining robust to model exploitation.

Conclusion: PSPO provides a Bayesian, posterior-sampling-based approach to model-based offline RL that explicitly quantifies and utilizes model fidelity. By integrating posterior sampling with constrained policy optimization, it achieves a better balance between generalization from OOD data and robustness to model errors than prior pessimistic methods. Both theory and experiments indicate that PSPO converges reliably, improves policies monotonically, and delivers superior performance on standard benchmarks, suggesting it is a promising direction for reliable offline model-based RL.

Abstract: A fundamental challenge in model-based offline reinforcement learning (RL) lies in the trade-off between generalization and robustness against exploitation errors in out-of-distribution (OOD) regions. While OOD samples may capture valid underlying physical dynamics, they also introduce the risk of model exploitation. Existing methods typically address this risk through excessive pessimistic regularization, which ensures robustness but often sacrifices generalization. To overcome this limitation, we propose Posterior Sampling-based Policy Optimization (PSPO), which formulates dynamics modeling as a Bayesian inference process to derive a posterior that explicitly quantifies model fidelity. Through the integration of posterior sampling and constrained policy optimization, our method leverages dynamics-consistent OOD transitions for generalization while ensuring robustness against model exploitation. Theoretically, we formulate Q-value estimation under posterior sampling as a stochastic approximation problem and establish its convergence. We decompose policy optimization into a sequence of constrained subproblems, demonstrating that solving these subproblems guarantees monotonic improvement until convergence. Experiments on standard benchmarks validate that PSPO achieves superior performance compared to state-of-the-art baselines.

### [143] [Efficient Data Selection for Multimodal Models via Incremental Optimization Utility](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07488)
*Jinhao Jing,Qiannian Zhao,Chao Huang,Zhan Su*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes OST, an optimization-based data selection framework for training large multimodal models that improves performance and efficiency over heuristic and judge-based methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scaling large multimodal models with synthetic data faces a quality-quantity trade-off: more data often means more noise and toxicity, which can hurt performance. Existing solutions like LLM-as-a-Judge help filter data but are computationally expensive and not very interpretable. The authors aim to find a more efficient, principled, and interpretable way to select high-utility training samples for multimodal reasoning tasks.

Method: They reformulate data selection as an incremental optimization utility ranking problem. Using a lightweight proxy model, they simulate a single optimization step for each candidate training sample to estimate its marginal utility (how much that sample would improve the model). Samples are then ranked by this estimated utility and subsets (e.g., top 20%, top 50%) are chosen for fine-tuning large multimodal models. This avoids manual semantic heuristics and heavy judge models.

Result: On multimodal mathematical reasoning benchmarks with Qwen-series models, OST achieves Pareto-optimal efficiency. Selecting the top 50% of samples cuts training cost by 43% and total time by 17% while outperforming a strong LLM-as-a-Judge baseline by 1.8 points. Under a fixed compute budget, training only on the top 20% subset yields a 5.6-point gain over LLM-as-a-Judge, beats heuristic scoring baselines like DEITA, and exceeds the Full-SFT baseline by 8.8 points.

Conclusion: Optimization-grounded, single-step utility-based data selection can significantly improve both the effectiveness and efficiency of large multimodal model training. By focusing on the marginal utility of each sample, OST not only outperforms judge-based and heuristic methods but also mitigates negative transfer and performance degradation caused by noisy or toxic synthetic data.

Abstract: The scaling of Large Multimodal Models (LMMs) is constrained by the quality-quantity trade-off inherent in synthetic data. Previous approaches, such as LLM-as-a-Judge, have proven their effectiveness in addressing this but suffer from prohibitive computational costs and lack of interpretability. To bridge this gap, we propose One-Step-Train (OST), a framework that reformulates data selection as an incremental optimization utility ranking problem. Instead of relying on semantic heuristics, OST estimates the marginal utility of each sample via a simulated single-step update on a lightweight proxy. Experiments on the Qwen series across multimodal mathematical reasoning benchmarks demonstrate that OST achieves Pareto-optimal efficiency. By selecting the top-50 subset, OST reduces training costs by 43% (and total time consumption by 17) while surpassing the strong LLM-as-a-Judge baseline by 1.8 points. Furthermore, under a fixed compute budget, our method using only the top-20 subset achieves a 5.6 point gain over LLM-as-a-Judge, improves upon heuristic scoring baselines like DEITA, and outperforms the Full-SFT baseline by 8.8 points. Notably, while Full-SFT suffers from performance degradation due to noise, our optimization-grounded approach effectively identifies toxic samples, successfully reversing the negative transfer frequently observed in complex reasoning tasks.

In this work, we propose a novel SFT-free training paradigm that significantly enhances the performance of small-scale models. We first present the initial systematic integration of generalized knowledge distillation into the GUI agent domain via Guided On-policy Distillation. By incorporating oracle reference trajectories together with a dynamic retrieval mechanism, our method reduces hallucinations and mitigates the cognitive misalignment inherent in multi-solution GUI tasks. Building on this foundation, we further introduce a Multi-solution Dual-level GRPO framework that jointly aligns macro-level subtask planning with micro-level execution matching, thereby improving exploration in long-horizon GUI agent scenarios. In addition, we construct an automated data generation pipeline to synthesize GUI task trajectories with rich multi-solution annotations. Extensive experiments show that our method achieves state-of-the-art performance among lightweight models while remaining competitive with substantially larger-scale models across all benchmarks. *Yuval Aroosh,Ayal Taitler*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Model-Driven Policy Optimization (MDPO), which adds adaptive, model-informed stochastic exploration to differentiable planning to handle ill-conditioned optimization landscapes in nonlinear and hybrid domains, leading to better performance than deterministic planners and model-free baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Differentiable planning uses differentiable models of system dynamics to optimize decision-making via gradients, but in highly nonlinear or hybrid discrete-continuous domains the optimization landscape is ill-conditioned, with flat regions and sharp transitions. This causes gradient-based methods to get stuck in poor local optima or fail to explore effectively. There is a need for a principled way to introduce and control exploration during differentiable planning to better navigate such challenging landscapes.

Method: The authors propose Model-Driven Policy Optimization (MDPO), which injects stochastic noise into the action space during the differentiable planning optimization process. Unlike fixed-noise approaches, MDPO uses access to the differentiable model to adapt the noise magnitude over time: it computes gradient-based sensitivities of the trajectory objective and uses these to derive a time-dependent exploration profile. This dynamically adjusts how much noise is added at each timestep and across optimization iterations, aiming to encourage exploration where it is most beneficial and reduce it where the objective is highly sensitive or well-optimized.

Result: On benchmark tasks in nonlinear and hybrid discrete-continuous domains, MDPO outperforms deterministic differentiable planning methods, including a noise-free version of MDPO and other state-of-the-art differentiable planners. It also surpasses model-free reinforcement learning baselines such as PPO in solution quality. The experiments show consistent gains in challenging settings and suggest that adaptive exploration improves convergence to better optima.

Conclusion: MDPO demonstrates that model-informed, adaptively scaled action noise can substantially improve differentiable planning in complex decision-making problems with difficult optimization landscapes. By leveraging gradient-derived sensitivity to distribute exploration across timesteps and optimization iterations, MDPO escapes poor local optima and finds higher-quality solutions than both deterministic planners and standard model-free methods. Analysis of the learned noise profiles provides insight into how exploration is allocated during learning, supporting the value of adaptive stochasticity in planning-based control.

Abstract: Differentiable planning enables gradient-based optimization of decision-making problems by leveraging differentiable models of system dynamics. However, in highly nonlinear and hybrid discrete-continuous domains, the resulting optimization landscapes are often ill-conditioned, with flat regions and sharp transitions that hinder effective optimization.
  We propose Model-Driven Policy Optimization (MDPO), a framework that introduces stochastic exploration into differentiable planning by injecting noise into the action space during optimization. Leveraging access to the model, MDPO further adapts the noise magnitude based on gradient-derived sensitivity of the trajectory objective, yielding a time-dependent exploration profile. This enables improved exploration of the objective landscape and helps escape poor local optima via dynamic allocation of exploration across timesteps and iterations.
  Experiments on benchmark domains demonstrate that MDPO consistently outperforms deterministic differentiable planning, including both the noise-free variant of our method and available state-of-the-art implementations, as well as model-free baselines such as PPO, significantly improving solution quality across challenging nonlinear and hybrid settings. We further analyze the evolution of the adaptive noise magnitude across both time steps and optimization iterations, providing insight into how exploration is allocated during learning.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They introduce Generate-Select-Refine (GSR), an open-ended Bayesian optimization framework that alternates between (1) generating new tasks from a user-provided seed in a coarse-to-fine way, and (2) optimizing currently known tasks via a task-acquisition function that decides which task to evaluate next; they analyze its regret, showing that the extra cost of exploring multiple tasks is only logarithmic compared to standard single-task BO.

Result: In experiments on several domains — new product development, chemical synthesis scale-up, algorithm analysis, and patent repurposing — GSR is empirically evaluated and found to outperform existing LLM-based optimization methods on these open-ended problem families.

Conclusion: Allowing the optimization task itself to be generated and refined during Bayesian optimization, rather than fixed in advance, can be done in a principled way with only small theoretical regret overhead, and this yields practical gains across diverse real-world applications compared to current LLM-based optimizers.

Abstract: When applying Bayesian optimization (BO) to scientific workflow, a major yet often overlooked source of uncertainty is the task itself -- namely, what to optimize and how to evaluate it -- which can evolve as evidence accumulates. We introduce Generate-Select-Refine (GSR), a open-ended BO framework that alternates between task generation and task optimization. Starting from a user-provided seed task, GSR generates new tasks in a coarse-to-fine manner while a task-acquisition function schedules optimization. Asymptotically, it concentrates evaluations on the best task, incurring only logarithmic regret overhead relative to single-task BO. We apply GSR to new product development, chemical synthesis scaling, algorithm analysis, and patent repurposing, where it outperforms existing LLM-based optimizers.

The approach not only scales with additional cores but is particularly effective in domains where grounding dominates computation, thereby improving both runtime and the number of solvable planning tasks.

We build upon this line of work and extend it by developing and analyzing an execution model with two levels of parallelism: rule-level parallelism and grounding parallelism. ### [151] [Inference Time Causal Probing in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07631)
*Sadegh Khorasani,Saber Salehkaleybar,Negar Kiyavash,Matthias Grossglauser*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper proposes HDMI, a probe-free causal intervention method that directly steers language model hidden states using a margin objective on model outputs, and shows it yields more reliable causal control than prior probe-based methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing causal probing methods in language models typically rely on training auxiliary probe classifiers to define interventions on hidden states. This creates several problems: the interventions become tied to specific tasks or models, and probe predictions may be misaligned with the model’s own internal predictive geometry. The authors want a way to test and control causal effects in models that does not depend on such probes and that better respects the model’s native representations.

Method: They introduce Hidden-state Driven Margin Intervention (HDMI), a gradient-based technique that directly modifies hidden states based on the model’s own output probabilities. HDMI uses a margin objective that increases the probability of a target continuation while decreasing that of the original source continuation, without any auxiliary probe classifier. They also propose LA-HDMI (lookahead HDMI) for text editing: it backpropagates through the softmax embedding layer to adjust the current hidden state so that user-specified tokens become more likely in the next-step generation while maintaining fluency. For evaluation, they define metrics of completeness (does the targeted property change as desired?) and selectivity (are unrelated properties preserved?), and use their harmonic mean as an overall reliability score.

Result: Across experiments on the LGD agreement corpus and the CausalGym benchmark, using Meta-Llama-3-8B-Instruct and Pythia-70M, HDMI achieves consistently higher reliability scores (harmonic mean of completeness and selectivity) than prior causal intervention methods that depend on probe classifiers.

Conclusion: Probe-free, gradient-based interventions that operate directly on a model’s native output geometry—specifically HDMI and its lookahead variant—provide more reliable causal control over language model behavior than traditional probe-based approaches. This suggests that causal probing can be made both more general and better aligned with model internals by avoiding auxiliary classifiers and instead steering hidden states via margin objectives on the model’s own predictions.

Abstract: Causal probing methods aim to test and control how internal representations influence the behavior of generative models. In causal probing, an intervention modifies hidden states so that a property takes on a different value. Most existing approaches define such interventions by training an auxiliary probe classifier, which ties the method to a specific task or model and risks misalignment with the model's predictive geometry. We propose Hidden-state Driven Margin Intervention (HDMI), a probe-free, gradient-based technique that directly steers hidden states using the model's native output. HDMI applies a margin objective that increases the probability of a target continuation while decreasing that of the source, without relying on probe classifiers. We further introduce a lookahead variant (LA-HDMI) for text editing that backpropagates through the softmax embeddings, modifying the current hidden state so that the likelihood of user-specified tokens increases in next token generations while preserving fluency. To evaluate interventions, we measure completeness (whether the targeted property changes as intended) and selectivity (whether unrelated properties are preserved), and report their harmonic mean as an overall measure of reliability. HDMI consistently achieves higher reliability than prior methods on the LGD agreement corpus and the CausalGym benchmark, across Meta-Llama-3-8B-Instruct, and Pythia-70M.

### [152] [Learning to Communicate Locally for Large-Scale Multi-Agent Pathfinding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07637)
*Valeriy Vyaltsev,Alsu Sagirova,Anton Andreychuk,Yuri Kuratov,Konstantin Yakovlev,Aleksandr Panov,Alexey Skrynnik*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes LC-MAPF, a learning-based decentralized multi-agent pathfinding method that augments agents with a learnable local communication module to share information with neighbors, achieving better coordination and performance without losing scalability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent pathfinding is crucial for real-world multi-robot systems, but finding optimal solutions is NP-hard and existing scalable solvers are often suboptimal. Learning-based decentralized methods exist but typically treat agents as independent learners with limited or no communication, which can hinder coordination and performance. There is a need for a scalable, communication-enhanced, learning-based MAPF solver that generalizes to unseen environments and improves cooperation among agents without sacrificing efficiency.

Method: The authors model MAPF as a decentralized partially observable Markov decision process (Dec-POMDP) from each agent’s perspective. They design LC-MAPF, a pre-trained neural policy that incorporates a learnable communication module. At each time step, agents compute features from their local observations and exchange them with neighboring agents through multiple rounds of message passing. The communication module aggregates and propagates this information to refine each agent’s policy decisions, enabling better coordination. Training is done using standard learning paradigms (imitation or reinforcement learning), but with the added communication architecture to improve cooperation.

Result: Experiments on a variety of previously unseen test scenarios show that LC-MAPF consistently outperforms existing learning-based MAPF solvers, including both imitation learning and reinforcement learning baselines. The performance gains are demonstrated across multiple metrics relevant to MAPF quality (e.g., success rates, path efficiency, collision avoidance), indicating more effective coordination. Importantly, the multi-round local communication mechanism does not degrade scalability, in contrast to many communication-heavy methods which struggle with larger numbers of agents or larger maps.

Conclusion: Incorporating a dedicated, learnable local communication module into decentralized learning-based MAPF policies significantly improves agent coordination and overall solution quality while maintaining scalability. LC-MAPF serves as a generalizable pre-trained model that can be applied to unseen environments, suggesting that carefully designed local communication is a practical and efficient way to enhance real-world multi-agent pathfinding systems.

Abstract: Multi-agent pathfinding (MAPF) is a widely used abstraction for multi-robot trajectory planning problems, where multiple homogeneous agents move simultaneously within a shared environment. Although solving MAPF optimally is NP-hard, scalable and efficient solvers are critical for real-world applications such as logistics and search-and-rescue. To this end, the research community has proposed various decentralized suboptimal MAPF solvers that leverage machine learning. Such methods frame MAPF (from a single agent perspective) as a Dec-POMDP where at each time step an agent has to decide an action based on the local observation and typically solve the problem via reinforcement learning or imitation learning. We follow the same approach but additionally introduce a learnable communication module tailored to enhance cooperation between agents via efficient feature sharing. We present the Local Communication for Multi-agent Pathfinding (LC-MAPF), a generalizable pre-trained model that applies multi-round communication between neighboring agents to exchange information and improve their coordination. Our experiments show that the introduced method outperforms the existing learning-based MAPF solvers, including IL and RL-based approaches, across diverse metrics in a diverse range of (unseen) test scenarios. Remarkably, the introduced communication mechanism does not compromise LC-MAPF's scalability, a common bottleneck for communication-based MAPF solvers.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors aim to fill this gap by providing a principled, causality-grounded benchmark tailored to industrial robotics.

Method: The authors build FactoryBench by: (1) defining Q&A items aligned with Pearl's four causal levels—state (associational), intervention, counterfactual, and decision; (2) creating structured question templates that can be programmatically instantiated from time-series telemetry; (3) supporting five answer formats, including four deterministically scorable structured formats and a free-form format evaluated via an LLM-as-judge voting protocol; (4) introducing FactoryWave, a dense, multitask, multivariate sensor dataset recorded from a UR3 collaborative robot and a KUKA KR10 industrial arm; and (5) aggregating normalized episodes from FactoryWave, AURSAD, and voraus-AD to generate over 70k Q&A items. These results quantify a substantial performance gap between state-of-the-art models and the level of operational machine understanding needed in industrial settings.

Conclusion: FactoryBench exposes significant limitations of current time-series models and LLMs in understanding and reasoning about industrial robot telemetry, particularly for causal and decision-making tasks. The benchmark, along with the new FactoryWave dataset and scalable Q&A generation framework, provides a standardized and causally grounded testbed to drive progress in machine understanding for industrial robotics. Future model and method development can be evaluated on FactoryBench to track improvements toward reliable, operation-ready machine understanding.

Abstract: We introduce FactoryBench, a benchmark for evaluating time-series models and LLMs on machine understanding over industrial robotic telemetry. Q&A pairs are organized along four causal levels (state, intervention, counterfactual, decision) instantiating Pearl's ladder of causation, and span five answer formats: four structured formats are scored deterministically and free-form answers are scored by an LLM-as-judge voting protocol. We propose a scalable Q&A generation framework built around structured question templates, present FactoryWave (a dense, multitask, multivariate sensor dataset collected from a UR3 cobot and a KUKA KR10 industrial arm), and construct FactoryBench as a large-scale benchmark of over 70k Q&A items grounded in roughly 15k normalized episodes from FactoryWave, AURSAD, and voraus-AD. Zero-shot evaluation of six frontier LLMs shows that no model exceeds 50% on structured levels or 18% on decision-making, revealing a wide gap between current models and operational machine understanding.

### [155] [GASim: A Graph-Accelerated Hybrid Framework for Social Simulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07692)
*Xuan Zhou,Yanhui Sun,Hantao Yao,Allen He,Yongdong Zhang,Wu Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: GASim is a graph-accelerated hybrid multi-agent framework that speeds up large-scale social simulations by replacing slow LLM memory retrieval and sequential ABM steps with graph-based memory and message passing, while dynamically selecting which agents need LLM reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hybrid social simulators that combine LLM-based agents with numerical ABMs allow rich behaviors at scale, but they suffer from high latency and cost due to heavy LLM memory retrieval and sequential simulation updates. There is a need for a framework that preserves the expressive power of LLM agents while making large-scale simulations faster and more cost-efficient, especially for studying real-world social phenomena like public opinion dynamics.

Method: The paper proposes GASim, which uses three main ideas: (1) Graph-Optimized Memory (GOM) for core LLM-driven agents, replacing expensive LLM-based memory retrieval with lightweight propagation over a sparse memory graph; (2) Graph Message Passing (GMP) for ordinary agents, replacing traditional sequential ABM updates with parallel graph-based feature aggregation using a Graph Attention Network; and (3) Entropy-Driven Grouping (EDG), which dynamically partitions agents into core versus ordinary groups by using information entropy to detect agents in information-diverse neighborhoods that warrant LLM-level reasoning.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that GASim achieves about a 9.94× end-to-end speedup compared to a traditional hybrid LLM+ABM framework, while using under 20% of the baseline token consumption. Despite this aggressive optimization, the simulated outcomes remain well aligned with observed real-world public opinion trends, indicating that performance gains are not obtained at the expense of behavioral fidelity.

Conclusion: GASim shows that graph-accelerated memory and message passing, along with adaptive entropy-based agent selection, can significantly improve the efficiency and scalability of hybrid LLM+ABM social simulations while maintaining realism. This suggests a promising direction for building large-scale, cost-effective social simulators that still capture complex emergent phenomena.

Abstract: Large-scale social simulators are essential for studying complex social patterns. Prior work explores hybrid methods to scale up simulations, combining large language models (LLM)-based agents with numerical agent-based models (ABM). However, this incurs high latency due to expensive memory retrieval and sequential ABM execution. To address this challenge, we propose GASim, a graph-accelerated hybrid multi-agent framework for large-scale social simulations. For core agents driven by LLM, GASim introduces Graph-Optimized Memory (GOM) to replace intensive LLM-based retrieval pipelines with lightweight propagation over a sparse memory graph. For the majority of ordinary agents, GASim employs Graph Message Passing (GMP), substituting sequential ABM execution with parallel updates by fine-grained feature aggregation and Graph Attention Network. We further introduce Entropy-Driven Grouping (EDG) that coordinates this hybrid partitioning, leveraging information entropy to dynamically identify emergent core agents situated in information-diverse neighborhoods. Extensive experiments show that GASim not only delivers a substantial 9.94-fold end-to-end speedup over the traditional hybrid framework but also consumes less than 20% of baseline tokens, significantly reducing costs while preserving strong alignment with real-world public opinion trends. Our code is available at https://github.com/Jasmine0201/GASim.

### [156] [Finite-Time Analysis of MCTS in Continuous POMDP Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07703)
*Da Kong,Vadim Indelman*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Finite-time theoretical analysis of MCTS in POMDPs, with concentration bounds in discrete and continuous observations and a new algorithm Voro-POMCPOW.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MCTS methods like POMCP work well empirically in POMDPs, but lack rigorous finite-time guarantees because heuristic action selection (e.g., UCB) creates nonstationary, dependent samples. There is also a gap in theory for continuous observation spaces, where branching is infinite and value estimates are hard to bound.

Method: 1) For discrete observations, extend polynomial exploration bonuses within UCB-style action selection to the POMDP root node and derive polynomial concentration bounds on empirical value estimates. 2) For continuous observations, design an abstract partitioning framework and bound the loss due to partitioning. 3) Under mild assumptions, prove high-probability bounds on value estimates in continuous-observation POMDPs. 4) Instantiate the framework as Voro-POMCPOW, which adaptively partitions the observation space via Voronoi cells to keep branching finite while respecting the original observation model.

Result: The paper derives finite-time, polynomial concentration bounds on MCTS value estimates at the root for both discrete and continuous observation POMDPs. It also gives a finite-time bound on the error introduced by partitioning continuous observation spaces. The proposed Voro-POMCPOW algorithm empirically matches or competes with strong baselines while additionally enjoying the new theoretical guarantees.

Conclusion: MCTS for POMDPs can be equipped with rigorous finite-time guarantees despite nonstationarity and dependencies from UCB-style exploration. By augmenting UCB with polynomial bonuses and introducing adaptive Voronoi partitioning for continuous observations, the paper obtains high-probability bounds on value estimates and a practical algorithm, Voro-POMCPOW. The techniques are general enough to also narrow theoretical gaps for continuous-state MDPs.

Abstract: This paper presents a finite-time analysis for Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) in Partially Observable Markov Decision Processes (POMDPs), with probabilistic concentration bounds in both discrete and continuous observation spaces. While MCTS-style solvers such as POMCP achieve empirical success in many applications, rigorous finite-time guarantees remain an open problem due to the nonstationarity and the interdependencies induced by heuristic action selection (e.g., UCB). In the discrete setting, we address these challenges by extending the polynomial exploration bonus to UCB in POMDP setting, yielding polynomial concentration bounds for the empirical value estimation at the root node. For continuous observation spaces, we introduce an abstract partitioning framework and propose a finite-time bound on partitioning loss. Under mild conditions, we prove highprobability bound on value estimates in POMDPs with continuous observation space. Specifically, we propose Voro-POMCPOW, a variant of POMCPOW with f inite-time guarantees that adaptively partitions the continuous observation space using Voronoi cells. This approach maintains a finite branching factor while preserving the original observation generator. Empirical validation demonstrates that the proposed Voro-POMCPOW shows competitive performance while providing theoretical guarantees. Although our analysis focuses on continuous POMDPs, the techniques developed herein are also applicable to continuous MDPs, closing another gap on the MDP side.

Abstract: HTN planning is a variation of classical planning where, instead of searching for a linear sequence of actions, an algorithm decomposes higher-level tasks using a method library until only executable actions remain. On one hand, this allows one to introduce domain knowledge that can speed up the search for a solution through the method library. On the other hand, it creates challenges that go beyond those of classical state-space search. While recent research produced a number of heuristics and novel algorithms that speed up HTN planning, these heuristics are not yet as informative as those available in classical planning algorithms. Using the Pytrich planner on six standard total-order HTN benchmark domains, we evaluate heuristics generated by nine LLMs under domain-specific prompting and compare them against the TDG and LMCount domain-independent baselines and the PANDA planner. Our results show that LLM-generated heuristics nearly match the coverage of the best available HTN planner, while substantially reducing search effort on 83% of shared problems.

### [159] [Alternating Target-Path Planning for Scalable Multi-Agent Coordination](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07744)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing solutions for TAPF mostly rely on Conflict-Based Search (CBS), which tightly couples target assignment and pathfinding. This coupling leads to high computational cost and poor scalability, limiting practical use in large, real-world multi-agent systems. There is a need for a method that can handle many agents efficiently while still producing reasonably good solutions.

Method: They introduce an iterative refinement framework that separates target assignment from pathfinding. Starting with an initial target assignment, the framework repeatedly: (1) solves a MAPF instance using a modern, fast, suboptimal MAPF solver such as LaCAM, (2) uses feedback from the MAPF solution to identify bottleneck agents that cause delays or conflicts, and (3) refines the target assignment to alleviate these bottlenecks. This loop runs within a fixed time budget, trading optimality for speed and scalability.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that the proposed feedback-driven reassignment loop scales to significantly larger TAPF instances than the previous state-of-the-art CBS-based solver while keeping solution quality acceptable. It shows better scalability in terms of number of agents and problem size, with competitive path costs or completion times.

Conclusion: Decoupling target assignment from pathfinding and iteratively refining assignments based on MAPF feedback is an effective strategy for TAPF. The proposed framework represents a practical, scalable approach that can handle large-scale TAPF problems, moving the field closer to deployment in real-world multi-agent systems.

Abstract: The concurrent target assignment and pathfinding (TAPF) problem extends multi-agent pathfinding (MAPF) by asking planners to allocate distinct targets and collision-free paths to agents. Prior work on TAPF has relied exclusively on Conflict-Based Search (CBS), which tightly couples target assignment and pathfinding, resulting in compute-intensive, non-scalable solutions. In contrast, we propose an iterative refinement framework that decouples target assignment from pathfinding. Our framework builds on modern, fast, suboptimal MAPF solvers, such as LaCAM. Specifically, within a given time budget, it repeatedly solves MAPF for the current target assignment, identifies bottleneck agents via MAPF feedback, and refines the assignment. Empirical results show that feedback-driven reassignment loop is effective, enabling our framework to scale well beyond the reach of the state-of-the-art CBS-based solver while maintaining decent solution quality. This represents a solid step toward practical, large scale TAPF suitable for real-world setups.

This makes it unclear whether high scores reflect genuine policy compliance or reliance on superficial correlations. The authors aim to create an evaluation framework that exposes and tests the internal rule-conditioned decision process in content moderation.

Method: The authors derive a benchmark from publicly available platform moderation policies, formalizing 93 atomic rules and 92 typed relations between these rules across three high-risk policy areas. They construct 2,166 image-text cases whose correct moderation outcomes depend on context-sensitive application and interaction of these rules. RuleSafe-VL is organized into four diagnostic tasks that break down moderation into a rule-conditioned decision chain: (1) identifying which rules are activated by a case, (2) recovering how those rules interact, (3) judging whether available evidence is sufficient to decide, and (4) determining the final outcome when missing context is provided. Result: Across 10 evaluated models, recovering rule relations is the main failure point: even the best model achieves only 64.8 Macro-F1, and some safety-focused models perform extremely poorly, below 7 Macro-F1. Prediction of decision states (e.g., whether there is enough information to make a decision) is also unreliable, with a maximum of 64.5 Macro-F1. These results show that current models struggle with the rule-governed reasoning steps required for faithful policy application, despite often achieving strong scores on simpler label-matching benchmarks.

Conclusion: RuleSafe-VL reframes content moderation evaluation from simple final-label accuracy to a diagnostic analysis of rule-conditioned decision reasoning. The benchmark exposes specific weaknesses in how vision-language models identify applicable rules, reason about rule interactions, and judge decisional sufficiency. This more fine-grained evaluation reveals that high performance on traditional moderation benchmarks does not guarantee correct, policy-faithful reasoning, highlighting the need for methods and training approaches that strengthen models’ rule-based reasoning capabilities in safety-critical moderation settings.

Abstract: Platform content moderation applies explicit policy rules and context-dependent conditions to decide whether user content is allowed, restricted, or removed. Current multimodal safety benchmarks largely reduce moderation to matching predefined final labels, leaving this underlying rule structure untested. As a result, a high benchmark score reveals little about whether a model applies the policy correctly or arrives at the correct label through superficial cues. To evaluate this rule-governed process, we introduce RuleSafe-VL, a benchmark for rule-conditioned decision reasoning in vision-language content moderation. Derived from publicly available platform moderation policies, RuleSafe-VL formalizes 93 atomic rules and 92 typed rule relations, yielding 2,166 context-sensitive image-text cases across three high-risk policy families. Its four diagnostic tasks decompose moderation into a rule-conditioned decision chain. They identify activated rules, recover rule interactions, judge decision sufficiency, and resolve outcomes once missing context is supplied. Experiments on 10 frontier, open-source, and safety-oriented VLMs reveal rule-relation recovery as the dominant bottleneck, where the best model reaches only 64.8 Macro-F1 and some safety-oriented models fall below 7 Macro-F1. Decision-state prediction also remains unreliable, peaking at 64.5 Macro-F1. RuleSafe-VL shifts moderation evaluation from final-label scoring toward diagnostic assessment of rule-conditioned decision reasoning.

### [161] [Exact Regular-Constrained Variable-Order Markov Generation via Sparse Context-State Belief Propagation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07839)
*François Pachet*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper extends belief propagation methods for handling regular constraints from first-order to variable-order Markov models by operating on product states of context graphs and automata, enabling exact constrained inference and efficient data augmentation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing belief propagation (BP) methods can exactly handle regular constraints for first-order Markov chains, but many practical sequence models are variable-order/backoff Markov models (e.g., n-gram language models with backoff). Applying first-order constraint layers to such models merges distinct histories, misrepresenting their probabilities and support. There is a need for a principled way to do exact inference with regular constraints while respecting the finer-grained state space of variable-order models, and to reuse this structure for efficient reversible data augmentation.

Method: The authors identify the appropriate state space for BP when the generator is a variable-order Markov model: instead of using first-order Markov states, they use observed context states given by the trained context graph and then form the standard product with the regular constraint automaton. They formalize the mismatch between first-order constraint layers and variable-order generators, then construct a sparse product graph that avoids explicit expansion to all K-tuples. They analyze the complexity, showing linear-time inference in the sequence horizon for fixed context graph and automaton, and polynomial time in the number of reachable product edges. They also characterize how to use the same finite-state interface to support reversible data augmentation via inverse count lookup and clarify which generation-time backoff policies can be treated exactly under BP.

Result: The resulting framework performs exact inference for variable-order Markov models under regular constraints using belief propagation, without expanding the model to an equivalent high-order Markov chain over K-tuples. The method preserves the intended variable-order distribution, including distinct histories, while enforcing automaton-based regular constraints. It provides an explicit complexity bound tied to the product graph’s reachable edges. Additionally, the framework enables a reversible data-augmentation mechanism equivalent to materialized transposition augmentation but without storing all transformed sequences. The authors also delineate which stochastic backoff policies (e.g., singleton avoidance) must be explicitly modeled to claim exactness.

Conclusion: By replacing the first-order Markov state with context states in a product with a regular constraint automaton, the authors extend BP-based exact inference to variable-order Markov models in a computationally efficient manner. This approach maintains the correct conditional distribution under regular constraints, supports scalable, reversible data augmentation, and clarifies the requirements for exact probabilistic semantics when using generation-time backoff policies.

Abstract: Variable-order Markov models generate sequences over a finite alphabet by conditioning each symbol on the longest available suffix of the generated history. Regular constraints, by contrast, describe finite-horizon control requirements by an automaton: fixed positions, forced endings, metrical patterns, and forbidden copied fragments are all special cases. Existing exact methods already handle regular constraints with belief propagation for first-order Markov chains. The contribution here is the variable-order extension: identifying the state space on which the existing BP-regular machinery must be run when the generator is a variable-order/backoff model. A first-order constraint layer can enforce useful support conditions, but it computes future mass after merging histories that a variable-order generator deliberately keeps distinct. We formalize this mismatch and give the sparse construction obtained by replacing the first-order Markov state with the observed context state, then taking the standard product with the regular constraint automaton. For a fixed trained context graph and automaton, inference is linear in the sequence horizon; in general it is polynomial in the number of reachable product edges. This gives the correct variable-order distribution conditioned on regular constraints without expanding to all K-tuples. The same finite-source interface supports reversible data augmentation by inverse count lookup, matching materialized transposition augmentation without storing transformed corpora. We also separate exact BP inference from generation-time backoff policies, such as singleton avoidance, whose stochastic semantics must be made explicit if exactness is claimed.
