### [162] [AgentEscapeBench: Evaluating Out-of-Domain Tool-Grounded Reasoning in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07926)
*Zhengkang Guo,Yiyang Li,Lin Qiu,Xiaohua Wang,Jingwen Xv,Dongyu Ru,Xiaoyu Li,Xiaoqing Zheng,Xuezhi Cao,Xunliang Cai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AgentEscapeBench is a new escape-room-style benchmark to test how well LLM agents use external tools in complex, long-range, dependency-heavy tasks, revealing that both humans and current models struggle as dependency depth increases, especially models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of LLM-based agents focus mostly on short, familiar, or locally dependent tool-use scenarios and do not adequately test whether agents can robustly handle novel, long-range tool-use procedures with explicit dependency structures. The authors aim to fill this gap by creating a benchmark that stresses sustained, tool-grounded reasoning with deep contextual dependencies, which is closer to realistic complex workflows.

Method: The authors design AgentEscapeBench, a benchmark consisting of escape-room-style tasks represented as directed acyclic graphs over tools and items, imposing long-range dependency constraints. Tasks require agents to call real external functions, track hidden state that is revealed over time, propagate intermediate results correctly, and produce a deterministically verifiable final answer. The benchmark has 270 instances partitioned into five difficulty levels based on dependency depth and supports fully automated evaluation. They then test sixteen different LLM-based agents and human participants on these tasks and analyze trajectories to diagnose error patterns.

Result: On AgentEscapeBench, both humans and LLM agents show decreasing success as dependency depth grows, but LLMs degrade more sharply. Human performance decreases from 98.3% success at difficulty level 5 to 80.0% at difficulty level 25. The best-performing LLM model drops from 90.0% to 60.0% success over the same levels. Analysis of the agents’ reasoning trajectories indicates that most failures stem from losing track of long-range state, failing to follow clues consistently, and not correctly propagating intermediate results through the dependency graph.

Conclusion: AgentEscapeBench demonstrates that while current LLM-based agents are often competent at localized tool use, they still struggle with tasks requiring deep, long-range tool-grounded reasoning and robust state tracking. The benchmark provides an automated, diagnostic testbed that highlights these weaknesses and can guide future research and training methods aimed at improving agents’ general-purpose reasoning, action, and adaptation capabilities in complex, tool-rich environments.

Abstract: As LLM-based agents increasingly rely on external tools, it is important to evaluate their ability to sustain tool-grounded reasoning beyond familiar workflows and short-range interactions. We introduce AgentEscapeBench, an escape-room-style benchmark that tests whether agents can infer, execute, and revise novel tool-use procedures under explicit long-range dependency constraints. Each task defines a directed acyclic dependency graph over tools and items, requiring agents to invoke real external functions, track hidden state revealed incrementally, propagate intermediate results, and submit a deterministically verifiable final answer. AgentEscapeBench includes 270 instances across five difficulty tiers and supports fully automated evaluation. Experiments with sixteen LLM agents and human participants show that performance drops sharply as dependency depth increases: humans decline from 98.3% success at difficulty-5 to 80.0% at difficulty-25, while the best model drops from 90.0% to 60.0%. Trajectory analysis attributes model failures mainly to breakdowns in long-range state tracking, clue adherence, and intermediate-result propagation. These findings suggest that current agents can often handle local tool use but still struggle with deep contextual dependencies. We hope AgentEscapeBench can serve as a diagnostic testbed for measuring current agent capabilities and informing future training efforts toward more robust general-purpose reasoning, action, and adaptation.

</details>


### [163] [TraceFix: Repairing Agent Coordination Protocols with TLA+ Counterexamples](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07935)
*Shuren Xia,Qiwei Li,Taqiya Ehsan,Jorge Ortiz*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: TraceFix is a verification-first pipeline that automatically synthesizes, verifies, and executes LLM-based multi-agent coordination protocols using TLA+/TLC and PlusCal, significantly improving reliability and robustness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM multi-agent systems often suffer from unreliable, ad-hoc coordination that can lead to deadlocks, livelocks, and failures, especially as model capabilities vary. There is a need for principled, formally verified coordination mechanisms that can be generated and used automatically, while remaining efficient enough to verify and run at scale.

Method: TraceFix uses an LLM agent to synthesize a protocol topology as a structured intermediate representation from a task description. It then generates PlusCal coordination logic and uses the TLA+ model checker TLC to verify the protocol. If verification fails, counterexamples are used to iteratively repair the protocol until it passes. The verified process bodies are compiled into per-agent system prompts, and a runtime monitor enforces adherence to the verified topology by rejecting invalid coordination actions. Extensive experiments are run across 48 tasks and multiple baselines, ablations, and fault injection settings.

Result: On 48 tasks across 16 scenario families, all protocols reach full TLC verification; 62.5% are verified on the first attempt, and no task requires more than four repair iterations. Verification remains fast (under 60 seconds) even for state spaces spanning six orders of magnitude. In 3,456 runtime trials, topology-monitored execution with the verified protocols achieves the highest task completion rates (89.4% average, 81.5% full). When model capability is reduced, performance using verified protocols degrades at about half the rate of prompt-only and chat-only baselines. In a fixed-runtime paired ablation, TLC-verified protocols reduce deadlock/livelock from 31.1% to 14.1%, with the strongest benefit under fault injection.

Conclusion: Verification-first synthesis of LLM multi-agent coordination protocols is practical and effective. By combining LLM-generated protocol structures with formal verification in TLA+/TLC and enforcing them via a runtime monitor, TraceFix substantially improves task success, robustness to model degradation, and reduction of deadlock and livelock, all with modest verification overhead.

Abstract: We present TraceFix, a verification-first pipeline for Large Language Model (LLM) multi-agent coordination. An agent synthesizes a protocol topology as a structured intermediate representation (IR) from a task description, generates PlusCal coordination logic, and iteratively repairs the protocol using counterexamples from the TLA+ model checker (TLC) until verification succeeds. Verified process bodies are compiled into per-agent system prompts and executed under a runtime monitor that rejects out-of-topology coordination operations. On 48 tasks spanning 16 scenario families, all tasks reach full TLC verification; 62.5% pass on the first attempt and none requires more than four repair iterations. State spaces span six orders of magnitude yet verification completes in under 60 s for every task. A 3,456-run runtime comparison shows that topology-monitored execution achieves the highest task completion (89.4% average, 81.5% full) and that runtimes using the verified protocol degrade at roughly half the rate of prompt-only and chat-only baselines when model capability is reduced. A paired ablation under a fixed runtime shows that TLC-verified protocols cut deadlock/livelock (DL/LL) from 31.1% to 14.1%, with the largest separation under fault injection.

### [164] [The Limits of AI-Driven Allocation: Optimal Screening under Aleatoric Uncertainty](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.07979)
*Santiago Cortes-Gomez,Mateo Dulce Rubio,Carlos Patino,Bryan Wilder*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to optimally combine machine-learning-based risk scoring with costly on-the-ground screening to allocate scarce resources, showing it is best to directly target highest-risk units and screen those near the allocation margin, with larger gains when outcome uncertainty is high.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Algorithmic targeting using machine-learning risk scores is increasingly used in policy and humanitarian programs because it is cheaper and faster than traditional physical screening, but even perfect risk prediction cannot avoid misallocation due to irreducible outcome uncertainty. Policymakers face a trade-off between costly but accurate screening and cheaper probabilistic targeting under a limited budget, and lack guidance on how to optimally mix these tools to improve allocation efficiency.

Method: The paper develops a theoretical two-stage allocation model with a fixed coverage budget, where an algorithm provides conditional vulnerability probabilities for all units and a screening technology can reveal true vulnerability for a subset at a cost. It analytically derives the optimal allocation rule that decides which units to screen and which to allocate directly, based on their predicted risk and the cost-effectiveness of screening. The authors then perform empirical applications using data from income-based social protection and humanitarian demining in Colombia to quantify gains from the optimal policy and to study conditions under which screening and algorithmic targeting act as complements or substitutes.

Result: The main theoretical result is that the optimal policy directly allocates resources to the highest predicted-risk units and uses screening on units near the decision margin—those whose risk scores are close to the allocation cutoff—because learning their true status most improves allocation. The analysis further shows that the welfare and efficiency gains from incorporating screening increase with the level of aleatoric (irreducible) uncertainty in the population. Empirically, in the case studies of social protection and demining in Colombia, the proposed two-stage strategy yields substantial improvements in resource targeting compared to pure algorithmic allocation or pure screening, demonstrating practical relevance of the framework.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that algorithmic targeting and physical screening should not be viewed as competing approaches but can be optimally integrated in a two-stage framework that screens marginal cases and directly serves those with highest predicted vulnerability. This hybrid strategy improves allocation efficiency under budget constraints, particularly in environments with high irreducible uncertainty. The empirical illustrations indicate that many real-world programs could significantly enhance impact by redesigning their targeting protocols according to the derived principles, carefully balancing screening costs against the gains from more accurate allocation.

Abstract: The rise of machine learning has shifted targeted resource allocation in policy and humanitarian settings toward algorithmic targeting based on predicted risk scores. This approach is typically cheaper and faster than traditional screening procedures that directly observe the latent vulnerability status through physical verification. Yet, even access to the true conditional vulnerability probability cannot eliminate misallocation: aleatoric uncertainty over individual vulnerability status is irreducible, and probabilistic targeting inevitably misallocates some resources. In this work we study how screening and algorithmic targeting should be optimally combined in a two-stage allocation framework where a screening stage observes true outcomes for a subset of units before a final allocation stage assigns the resource under a fixed coverage budget. We show that the optimal strategy screens units at the margin of algorithmic allocation, while directly targeting the highest-risk units. Furthermore, we empirically characterize when screening and algorithmic targeting act as complements or substitutes: efficiency gains from screening grow as the aleatoric uncertainty in the population increases. We illustrate our framework with applications in income-based social protection programs and humanitarian demining in Colombia, where the tension between screening costs and allocation efficiency is operationally consequential.

</details>


### [165] [Abductive Reasoning with Probabilistic Commonsense](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08011)
*Joseph Cotnareanu,Chiara Roverato,Han Zhou,Didier Chetelat,Yingxue Zhang,Mark Coates*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces PACS, a probabilistic neurosymbolic framework that models variation in human commonsense beliefs to improve abductive reasoning with LLMs and formal logic solvers, outperforming existing reasoning methods on several benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing neurosymbolic methods combine LLMs with formal logic solvers to enhance reasoning, but formal solvers lack commonsense knowledge. Current fixes ask LLMs to provide missing commonsense assumptions, implicitly treating them as universally agreed facts. However, human commonsense is not uniform; it varies among individuals, which is crucial when we want systems to reflect what most people would believe rather than a single, brittle assumption set. There is a need for a principled way to model and aggregate this diversity of commonsense beliefs in logical reasoning.

Method: The authors propose a probabilistic abductive commonsense reasoning framework called PACS. PACS uses an LLM together with a formal logic solver to generate multiple proofs, where each proof is interpreted as a sample from a different hypothetical individual's set of commonsense beliefs. These proofs constitute probabilistic observations over possible belief configurations. PACS then aggregates the conclusions drawn from these diverse proofs to estimate the likelihood that a statement would be judged true or false by most people. This neurosymbolic algorithm thus explicitly treats commonsense premises as latent, variable factors and uses sampling plus aggregation instead of a single deterministic assumption set.

Result: Across multiple reasoning benchmarks, PACS is empirically shown to outperform several strong baselines, including chain-of-thought prompting, previous neurosymbolic methods that integrate LLMs with logic solvers, and search-based reasoning approaches. The improvements suggest that explicitly modeling variation in commonsense beliefs yields better alignment with collective human judgments and more robust abductive reasoning.

Conclusion: By reframing commonsense reasoning as probabilistic abduction over diverse individual belief sets, PACS provides a more realistic and effective way to integrate LLMs with formal solvers. The work demonstrates that capturing variability in what different people consider "commonsense" can significantly improve reasoning performance, indicating that future neurosymbolic systems should incorporate population-level belief modeling rather than assuming a single, universally shared commonsense knowledge base.

Abstract: Recent efforts to improve the reasoning abilities of Large Language Models (LLMs) have focused on integrating formal logic solvers within neurosymbolic frameworks. A key challenge is that formal solvers lack commonsense world knowledge, preventing them from making reasoning steps that humans find obvious. Prior methods address this by using LLMs to supply missing commonsense assumptions, but these approaches implicitly assume universal agreement on such commonsense facts. In reality, commonsense beliefs vary across individuals. We propose a probabilistic framework for abductive commonsense reasoning that explicitly models this variation, aiming to determine whether most people would judge a statement as true or false. We introduce Probabilistic Abductive CommonSense (PACS), a novel algorithm that uses an LLM and a formal solver to sample proofs as observations of individuals' distinct commonsense beliefs, and aggregates conclusions across these samples. Empirically, PACS outperforms chain-of-thought reasoning, prior neurosymbolic methods, and search-based approaches across multiple benchmarks.

</details>


### [166] [Learning CLI Agents with Structured Action Credit under Selective Observation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08013)
*Haoyang Su,Ying Wen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces new methods to improve reinforcement learning for command line interface (CLI) agents through better context selection and credit assignment, and releases a benchmark dataset (ShellOps) for CLI tasks in code repositories.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: CLI agents must operate over large, evolving codebases via shell commands, but current RL approaches underuse the rich structure of CLI actions and struggle with two core challenges: selectively observing relevant parts of a large filesystem and assigning sparse terminal rewards to the many actions in long trajectories. There is a need for methods that leverage CLI-specific structure to make learning more sample-efficient and robust.

Method: The authors formulate shell-driven information extraction and file editing tasks and tackle two bottlenecks. For selective observation, they propose σ-Reveal, an inference-time mechanism that chooses a token-limited context from the filesystem for the CLI agent. For credit assignment, they introduce Action Advantage Assignment (A^3), an RL method that maintains the complexity of standard agentic RL while using CLI-native structure: it builds turn-level advantages from (1) episode-level relative feedback, (2) AST-based residuals of action sub-chains, and (3) tree-level trajectory margins. They also design ShellOps, a suite of verifiable CLI tasks embedded in repository environments, to evaluate these techniques.

Result: σ-Reveal improves the agent’s ability to select task-relevant evidence under a constrained token budget, while A^3 provides more accurate turn-level credit assignment compared to naïve RL baselines that rely solely on sparse terminal rewards. On the ShellOps benchmark, these methods yield better performance on shell-based information extraction and file editing tasks than prior approaches that do not exploit CLI structure for observation and credit assignment.

Conclusion: Exploiting the inherent structure of CLI actions and trajectories enables more effective RL for CLI agents. By combining structured context selection (σ-Reveal) with structured credit assignment (A^3), the authors mitigate key bottlenecks in large-codebase interaction. The ShellOps dataset establishes a reproducible benchmark for future work on CLI-based coding agents and suggests that further leveraging structural signals in both the environment and action space is a promising direction for agentic RL.

Abstract: Command line interface (CLI) agents are emerging as a practical paradigm for agent-computer interaction over evolving filesystems, executable command line programs, and online execution feedback. Recent work has used reinforcement learning (RL) to learn these interaction abilities from verifiable task feedback, yet few methods exploit the native structured attributes of CLI actions as learning signals. Beyond this underused action structure, CLI learning also couples two bottlenecks for coding agents. First, the agent must identify task-relevant evidence in a large codebase from partial observations. Second, sparse terminal rewards must be assigned to the actions that shape a long multi-turn trajectory. We study these bottlenecks through shell-driven information extraction and file editing tasks. For selective observation, we introduce $σ$-Reveal, an inference-time mechanism that selects token-budgeted context for the same CLI. For credit assignment, we propose Action Advantage Assignment ($\mathrm{A}^3$), a native agentic RL method that preserves the algorithmic complexity of standard agentic RL. $\mathrm{A}^3$ constructs turn-level advantages from episode-level relative feedback, abstract syntax tree (AST) based action sub-chain residuals, and tree-level trajectory margins. To further evaluate this problem setting, we construct ShellOps, a verifiable dataset suite covering CLI tasks in repository environments.

### [167] [Reason to Play: Behavioral and Brain Alignment Between Frontier LRMs and Human Game Learners](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08019)
*Botos Csaba,Sreejan Kumar,Austin Tudor David Andrews,Laurence Hunt,Chris Summerfield,Joshua B. Tenenbaum,Rui Ponte Costa,Marcelo G. Mattar,Momchil Tomov*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper compares large reasoning models (LRMs), reinforcement learning agents, and a Bayesian model on a complex game-learning task with concurrent human fMRI, finding LRMs best match human behavior and brain activity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To determine whether modern AI systems, particularly frontier large reasoning models, can learn abstract rules and plan in novel environments in a human-like way, and whether they provide good computational accounts of human learning and decision making.

Method: Participants played novel video games requiring rule discovery, hypothesis revision, and multi-step planning while undergoing fMRI. The authors trained and evaluated different AI agents – LRMs, model-free and model-based deep reinforcement learning agents, and a Bayesian theory-based agent – on the same games. They jointly assessed: (1) game-playing performance, (2) similarity to human learning trajectories, and (3) ability to predict human brain activity patterns. They also performed targeted manipulations to isolate whether brain alignment came from in-context game-state representation versus downstream planning/reasoning.

Result: Frontier LRMs most closely reproduced human behavioral patterns during game discovery and predicted human brain activity substantially better (about an order of magnitude) than both model-free and model-based RL agents across a wide range of cortical and subcortical regions. These findings were robust to permutation controls. Manipulation analyses indicated that the high brain alignment is primarily driven by the models’ in-context representations of the game state rather than their explicit planning or reasoning outputs.

Conclusion: LRMs provide a strong computational model of human learning and decision making in complex, naturalistic game environments. Their internal representations align closely with human neural activity, especially during rule discovery and early learning, suggesting they capture key aspects of human-like abstract knowledge acquisition and use. This positions LRMs as promising tools for cognitive modeling and for understanding the neural basis of flexible problem solving.

Abstract: Humans rapidly learn abstract knowledge when encountering novel environments and flexibly deploy this knowledge to guide efficient and intelligent action. Can modern AI systems learn and plan in a similar way? We study this question using a dataset of complex human gameplay with concurrent fMRI recordings, in which participants learn novel video games that require rule discovery, hypothesis revision, and multi-step planning. We jointly evaluate models by their ability to play the games, match human learning behavior, and predict brain activity during the same task, comparing a suite of frontier Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) against model-free and model-based deep reinforcement learning agents and a Bayesian theory-based agent. We find that frontier LRMs most closely match human behavioral patterns during game discovery and predict brain activity an order of magnitude better than both reinforcement learning alternatives across cortical and subcortical regions, with effects robust to permutation controls. Through targeted manipulations, we further show that brain alignment reflects the model's in-context representation of the game state rather than its downstream planning or reasoning. Our results establish LRMs as compelling computational accounts of human learning and decision making in complex, naturalistic environments. Project page with interactive replays: https://botcs.github.io/reason-to-play/

### [168] [VecCISC: Improving Confidence-Informed Self-Consistency with Reasoning Trace Clustering and Candidate Answer Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.08070)
*James Petullo,Sonny George,Dylan Cashman,Nianwen Xue*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: VecCISC speeds up confidence-weighted self-consistency for LLM reasoning by filtering redundant or low‑quality reasoning traces via semantic similarity, cutting token usage roughly in half while keeping or improving accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Weighted majority voting methods like Confidence-Informed Self Consistency (CISC) improve reasoning accuracy over standard self-consistency, but they are expensive because each candidate reasoning trace requires an additional critic LLM call to obtain a confidence score. This cost limits the practicality and scalability of such methods, especially when many samples are needed or when using large, expensive models. The authors aim to retain the accuracy benefits of CISC while substantially reducing its inference-time computational and monetary overhead.

Method: The authors introduce VecCISC, an adaptive framework that leverages semantic similarity between reasoning traces. First, multiple candidate reasoning traces are generated as in self-consistency. VecCISC then uses vector-based semantic similarity (e.g., embeddings) to identify traces that are (1) semantically equivalent to others (redundant), (2) degenerate, or (3) hallucinated or low-quality. Only a filtered subset of diverse, non-degenerate traces is passed to the critic LLM for confidence scoring, after which weighted majority voting is applied as in CISC. This reduces the number of expensive critic calls without substantially altering the decision rule of CISC.

Result: Across five challenging and widely used benchmarks covering mathematics, chemistry, biology, commonsense reasoning, and humanities tasks, VecCISC achieves similar or better accuracy compared to standard CISC while significantly reducing compute cost. Specifically, it cuts total token usage by about 47%, which directly translates into lower latency and monetary cost for inference-time reasoning with LLMs, without sacrificing performance.

Conclusion: VecCISC shows that semantic-similarity-based filtering of reasoning traces is an effective way to make confidence-weighted self-consistency more efficient. By eliminating redundant and low-quality candidate solutions before critic evaluation, it preserves or improves CISC’s accuracy while nearly halving token consumption. This suggests that vector similarity can be a practical tool for scaling inference-time reasoning strategies in a cost-conscious way across diverse domains.

Abstract: A standard technique for scaling inference-time reasoning is Self-Consistency, whereby multiple candidate answers are sampled from an LLM and the most common answer is selected. More recently, it has been shown that weighted majority voting (e.g. Confidence-Informed Self Consistency (CISC)), which assigns a confidence value to each candidate answer and chooses the answer with the largest accumulated score, tends to be more accurate on a wide range of popular benchmarks. In practice, weighted majority voting necessitates calling a critic LLM on each candidate's reasoning trace to produce the answer's confidence score. This secondary series of LLM calls greatly increases the overhead and cost of weighted majority voting, despite its potential performance benefits. To reduce this expense, we propose VecCISC, a lightweight, adaptive framework that uses a measure of semantic similarity to filter reasoning traces that are semantically equivalent to others, degenerate, or hallucinated, thus decreasing the number of candidate answers that must be evaluated by the critic. To ensure adequate experimental thoroughness, we evaluate VecCISC on five challenging, widely-adopted datasets spanning the domains of mathematics, chemistry, biology, commonsense reasoning, and the humanities. Our results demonstrate that VecCISC reduces the total token usage by 47%, while maintaining or exceeding the accuracy of CISC.

</details>
